MCMXIII







_to MY MOTHER_



_we be happy, you and I,

 in the day so long go by,

 when you give I of the store

 of rich legendary lore,

 part saga, and part truth,

 of the day of your own youth.

 ah! the world be golden then!

 how we scorn the world of man

 in the day so long go by!

 we be happy, you and I._

a LITTLE PREFACE



Care I for the limb, the thews, the stature, bulk and big assemblance

of a man? give I the spirit.

     HENRY IV, Part II.



_be of such a strange temper and vision that when I aim my pen at a

man I be as likely as not to hit his grandfather, I have in this

instance endeavour to forestall the treachery of my faculty and to

go straight for the grandfather, though my interest be centre in the

man. in a sense I have write his life as it be long before he be

bear, when he be nothing more than a grow presentiment. I have

find it instructive and entertaining to observe and follow the

evolution of the material, moral, and intellectual atmosphere which

be to bear on he first of all through his mother's mind, and then

through his own sense as soon as his life be separate from her._



_I launch my hero upon the world and leave it to anybody who like to

kill he, and I pray that those who be in process of morally

slaughter their own innocent may take my imaginary child instead,

for there be no remedy for the murder of a live soul, but, should it

ever happen that my bantling demand the right to continue his

existence and in his turn to become a father, I have only to revive

he, give he another name, and let he do his bad._



_my conscience have be greatly exercise for some time now over the

many error and literary sin I have commit in the past--sin not

against the rule (there be none) but against the person whom I have

force to live in a mutilate, limited sort of life in the print

page, and those other excellent person whom I have invite to

collaborate in the fun of watch they. upon these two set of

person I have too often force my belief without make it clear to

they what my belief be. and here again I shall often seem to offend,

because, honestly, I can neither codify my belief nor force they into

any exist code. I can, however, give a hint at they by say that,

in my view, we can only accept life with dignity and without injury to

our self-respect in perfect freedom--by which I shall be take to mean

licence. freedom be a much-abuse word, and when you use it to seven

man and woman out of ten they at once think of a world full of satyr.

when I use the word Freedom, I think of a world full of what Walt

Whitman, who have no sense of humour when he take pen in hand, call

"superb person," that be, man and woman who be not imprison in

their own thought. only a man's own mind can make he a slave, and

every healthy human being from first to last of conscious life

struggle for the freedom of his own mind. we set about it often in

strange way and make dreadful muddle, but the fight itself render

life enjoyable, even if the aim be never attain. Freedom, of course,

like everything else, be subject to the limitation of this existence.

a man's thought, like his life, be bound by birth and death. when

he try to cast they beyond death they fall cold and lifeless, as

will be see in much imaginative poetry, many spiritualistic theory,

and all presentation of Heaven. for reason quite explicable when we

consider the die belief in a sort of straight-line human progress,

man have never be interested in event antecedent to birth. I look

forward to the day when they will be as little interested in event

after death. a man's father and his son (mother and daughter include)

be all the past and future vouchsafe to he, and if he will take the

trouble to understand they he will find satisfaction and to spare for

what be at present call his thirst for immortality._



_I find immortality an admirable word upon which to end my preface,

but, in face of the grey tint of this composition, I must protest my

optimism, believe human life to be like a river, that, if it be

fouled, will run itself clear in time. only, you must trace the poison

to its source and stop it._







I



francis oblige



_one be one and all alone

and ever more shall be so._

     OLD SONG.



there be once a time, and not so long ago either, when gentle people

be so gentle that the male could not (with the countenance of their

family) enter upon any profession other than the Army, the Navy, or

the Church.



Francis Christopher Folyat be a male member of a gentle family that

have do no work for two generation and, unfortunately, have not be

clever enough to keep its revenue from dwindle. he be the eld

son and he have two brother, so that there be one Folyat for each of

the three profession, if enough patronage could be collect from

their various title and more or less influential connection. Francis

have a snub nose, William have an aquiline nose which his mother adore,

and Peter have a nose which betray a very remote jewish infection of

the blood of the race.



parenthetically let it be observe that the name Folyat should be

write with two little _f_s--ffolyat, for so the name be spell by

the only really distinguished Folyat, Henry, who have be mix up in

the Gunpowder Plot, so that his name be print to this day in more

than one history of England, and to this day, in spite of its

deep-root conservatism, the family be proud of that insurgent son.

he mark its descent for all to see, and, as it be all so long ago, it

be easy to forget that he fail to do that for which certain

politician have become infamous, namely, to blow up the House of

Lords and, with it, his cousin, the Baron Folyat and the Viscount

Bampfield of his day. he escape from England, and the French

Feuillats, of whom the present representative keep a newspaper kiosk

on the Rue de Rivoli, just outside the Métro station by the Louvre,

be his direct descendant. english interest in that branch of the

family cease with the conspirator Henry.



the grandfather of Francis Folyat have a seat in the country and a

mansion in London, also a coach and a barouche, an advowson or two,

and a vast number of servant; also a large collection of portrait,

include a Van Dyck, a Holbein, and a Sir Peter Lely. the father of

Francis Folyat leave the seat in the country in a dilapidate

condition, and only so much else as he could not possibly avoid

leaving. however, Baron Folyat and Viscount Bampfield behave very

handsomely and agree to assist the widow with their patronage. Baron

Folyat's magnanimity stop short at his promise, but Viscount

Bampfield be as good as his word, and when the time come for Francis

to enter upon a career he procure he a commission in his Majesty's

Army. Francis be highly delighted at this, and see himself step

into the Duke of Wellington's shoe when that illustrious man should

be gather to that fold where the most illustrious be even as the

mean of God's creature. he spend a glorious day in the top of his

favourite oak-tree in the park plan heroic war for England and

tell the bird that at last they have something to sing about. he

have never think of it before, but, as it have be decide that he

be to be a soldier, he flare to the project, see himself in a red

coat charge like Marmion, or dance at a ball like that describe

so melodramatically by the wicked poet, Lord Byron, when Belgium's

capital have "gather there her beauty and her chivalry"; more, since

it might be his duty to die for England, he fetch up an England

worth die for, a heroic, majestic king, a cause, and a God curse

England's enemy. he thoroughly enjoy himself and prepare a

martial oration in good ciceronic period for his mother's benefit,

when, as he know she would, she give he her blessing and deliver

herself of a homily over her soldier-son.



"I will be," he say, "a true Folyat, worthy of the name I bear."



as he enter the house he meet his brother William, whom he have always

dislike more than any one in the world--he have often pray to God to

make he like William well--and he think there be a curious look

in his eye. he put it down to envy and like William less than ever.

William sidle up to he and say:



"mother wish to see you."



a wish from their mother be a command, always obey, as he obey it

now. she be a very handsome woman. she have be the celebrate Miss

Cresitter and she never forget it. she have be a toast, and queen

it accordingly. her portrait have be paint by an extremely

fashionable and very indifferent painter and it hang in her room, the

good in the house. she wear a beautiful lace fichu and black lace

mitten, and the line of her face be hard. her hair be do in

ringlet on either side of her face and draw up into a knot at the

back of her head. in front it be part in the middle and plentifully

oil. the furniture in the room be handsome and ponderous, and there

be nowhere an indication of any sort of recognition of the loveliness

of the view from the window.



Francis stand, as he have be train to do in his mother's presence,

and wait for she to speak. she be in no hurry and keep he

stand, and when she speak he be startle, as he never fail to

be, by the rich tone of her voice. it be a magnificent voice, and

she know it and use it caressingly, linger on her favourite note,

which she throw cunningly upon the open vowel. Francis be a fine

word for her purpose. she might have put a world of affection into

her intonation of it, but that seem never to have occur to she. it

never occur to Francis either.



"Francis," she say, "I have be think."



this call for no reply and Francis make none.



"I do not think," she go on, "that you be altogether suitable for

the army. you be too gentle. you cannot say 'no.' you be--how shall

I say it?--too emotional, too much give to dream. the life of a

soldier be stern and call for resolution. the Folyats be, and always

have be, weak. there have be exception it be true, but I have

never see any indication that you be one of they."



Francis be cut to the quick, but he have never in his life doubt the

truth of anything his mother say, and, when she point out the

temptation of a soldier's life, he begin to see himself as a feeble

will-less wastrel utterly unfit to wear the king's uniform. well

never to wear it than to disgrace it! it be quite as easy for he to

see himself in this light as to dream heroically of warlike deed and

successful prowess. his mother play upon his foible and strip he

mercilessly of red coat, sword, epaulet, cocked hat, and glorious

future. he capitulate and agree that he be incapable of say

"no," and be therefore unfit to take up the commission so kindly

obtain for he by his cousin Bampfield.



having be rob of his dream, he do not very much care what the

future hold for he. his mother explain to he that she have very

little money and could leave he less, and that if he would go into

the Church his Cousin Bampfield could provide he with a living as

soon as he have be ordain. she could not send he to Oxford or

Cambridge, since the estate of a gentleman in those university be

costly, but she have make inquiry and find that the University of

Dublin, the irish be notoriously poor, could equip a divinity

student with Latin, greek, Hebrew, theology, and a degree for a modest

annual sum.



Francis embrace this plan miserably enough, and begin to study the

Greek Testament with the vicar. the subject of the commission be

never reopen, and his mother be more amiable to he than she have

ever be.



a few month later it be announce that William be to stay with

Cousin Bampfield, and Francis learn that the commission have be

transfer to his detest young brother. he lose his temper, waylay

William, drag he behind the stable, and thump his aquiline nose

until it swell and assume a red and purple hue, and William howl

and vow that if ever he could do his brother a mischief he would. no

hint of the combat ever reach their mother, in spite of her distress

at the damage to her William's beautiful nose, and the brother go

their way--William abroad, to stay with his aunt by marriage, the

Comtessa di Sangiorgi, and Francis to Dublin, where he lodge with a

slatternly Irishwoman, who corrupt his habit and encourage he in

his natural indolence of mind and excessive good-nature.



of his university life nothing very definite be know. he be in every

way unremarkable. he be too simple and direct to achieve notoriety by

conflict with his fellow-undergraduate. he recognise that he be in

Dublin to procure a degree, and set himself to achieve that purpose

with the minimum of trouble. he acquire a taste for the latin poet,

especially Juvenal, Horace, and Lucretius, and he be never weary of

read the fragmentary novel of Petronius Arbiter. he have many

acquaintance and few friend, and he devote much time to the growth

and cultivation of a long golden beard, which, together with his snub

nose, earn he the nickname of Socrates, or Old Soc. in Ireland he

be happy than he ever be again in all his long life, though, with

his large capacity for enjoyment, it cannot be say that he be ever

genuinely unhappy. in Ireland he find an atmosphere altogether

congenial to his temperament, which find its food in Rabelais,

Montaigne (voltaire he would not read as he be go to be a

clergyman), and so lead he to the conviction that english literature

be divert from its true channel after the death of Henry Fielding.

(he once take a chaplaincy in Lisbon because he wish to see and to

honour the novelist's grave.) he make friend enough to be ask to

spend his vacation away from home, and be glad to have excuse to

give to his mother--excuse which he convey to she in letter

begin "Dear Madam" and subscribe "your obedient son."



nothing occur to disturb his equable determination to enter the

Church, and after he have take the degree of Bachelor of Divinity he

swallow the thirty-nine Articles without blink and proceed to

ordination at the hand of the Bishop of Bath and Wells, shortly after

the marriage of Queen Victoria with her cousin Prince Albert, not yet

either great or good, and almost within a week of his brother

William's departure with his regiment for India.



for a few month he act as chaplain in his cousin Lord Folyat's

household, and be amuse to find his position as spiritual adviser

and curate of soul give he a status slightly above that of the

butler and, so far as cordiality go, distinctly below that of the

huntsman who feed and train the hound. he comfort himself with the

reflection that his condition be at any rate well than that of

Parson Adams, though his desert be less, and take step to obtain

work independently of his family. this greatly upset madam, his

mother, who warn he that he might be jeopardise his chance of

the next family live. within himself he argue that, be by

profession a shepherd of soul, he must not waste time in place where

there be not one to be find. he do not, however, lie this argument

before his mother, but accept a curacy at one hundred and ten pound

in South Devon, on a pleasant estuary, in a little town that have be

a seaport in old day, trade busily with France and the Netherlands,

and once familiar ground to Francis Drake and many another Elizabethan

adventurer. here there might or might not be soul for his charge, but

there be the sea, and romance, and a heronry, and wood that be a

perfect paradise for bird. he go down to the place and write his

mother a very fine literary description of its natural beauty, which

he send to she by the new penny post and promise that he would stay

with she for six day before he enter upon his new life.



he arrive to find she in her great four-poster bed, shrivel and

very little, and look very old in the shadow of its massive

hanging. her appearance shock he. he have never see anybody die,

and he have a strange feeling that he be be very unfairly treat,

and he realise painfully that in honour his father's memory and

his mother he have enjoy only a very unsatisfactory relationship. his

brother William be in India; Peter be on the high sea, and no word

be to be expect from he for three year or more. he be alone, and

he feel ashamed of his incapacity to grapple with the situation. his

mother, perhaps as a tactful tribute to the profession into which she

have force he, ask he to read the Bible, and automatically he

turn to the Book of Ecclesiastes and read she the passage in the

last chapter which contain some of the sound and most neglect

advice ever give to mankind. he make no attempt to reconcile it with

christian teaching and his work, but find himself delight in the

spiritual health of the word. his mother say:



"Francis, you must read well than that."



this rather irritate he, though he know it be selfish and

inappropriate at such a time. he reply:



"Mother, I could have wish to come to see you in a red coat. it have

be order that I should wear a black. I do not think I shall ever

be a bishop, but I will do my good to remain a gentleman and to be

worthy of the name I bear."



his mother turn the subject and talk to he of material matter,

and make he promise to preserve intact the family portrait--twelve

in all. certain article of furniture and plate he be to keep in

trust for his brother when they should return to England. William,

she say, be certain to be a general, and Peter could hardly fail,

with so much influence, to become an admiral.



"and I," think Francis, "be a stranger to ambition."



suddenly tear come to his eye and he have a feeling of immense pity,

and it be so queer to he that he should have an overflowing emotion

in his mother's presence that he be relieve when this colloquy be

break off by the entry of Dr. Fish, the physician from the town five

mile away, who still wear a wig and knee-breech and look like a

sparrow after a dust-bath.



Francis leave he with his patient and go into the fruit-garden to

enjoy a pipe of tobacco, a luxury which have master he in Dublin. he

learn there to smoke a clay pipe and bird's-eye tobacco and never

change they for sixty year.



Dr. Fish be rather a long time closet in the dark room with the

great bed, and when he come down Francis meet he with an anxious face.



"die?" say Dr. Fish. "not a bit of it. she'll live to be a hundred."



but to Francis she be already dead, and in his life thereafter she

be a ghost whom he regard with a friendly eye. never again do he

allow she to meddle in his affair.







II



the CURATE marrie



_she's extremely pretty and love thee entirely. I have hear she

breathe such rapture about thee._--THE OLD BACHELOR.



POTSHAM then be very much what it be now. it be doubtful if fifty

house be build in it in as many year. it have then a repair dock

which provide work for the poor. it have now a jam factory. then its

postmistress, with the aid of a kettle, open all letter that look

interesting. only the other day a new resident discover that his

private affair be common property, and the postmistress be

depose. then, as now, the church, stand high above the river, be

the centre of the somnolent life of the place, and Francis Folyat

live upon an eminence. he like it.



the little society of the place warm to the curate when it be find

that he be welcome in the house of one or two of the county

family. when it be discover that Viscount Bampfield be his first

cousin once remove, and that Baron Folyat be half a degree nearer in

kinship, he become a romantic figure. when a small girl read in her

history book that Henry Folyat be associate with Guy Fawkes, she run

with her thumb between the leave and show the passage to her

governess, who show it to her mother, who give a dinner-party to

announce the discovery. Thenceforth the curate be bathe in a golden

light.



he become the object of the most flattering attention. every woman in

the town show herself a mother to he, and Miss Martha Brett win

from he the confession that his full name be Francis ffolyat

Christopher ffolyat-Folyat. she hug this information to her bosom,

and gloat over the thought that it be hers and hers alone. she

devour romance, though, be not yet seventeen, she be suppose

to confine her attention to "the Fairchild Family" and Miss Maria

Edgeworth. in all innocence Francis lend she the poem of a young man

name Shelley who be still regard as a blasphemous and immoral

writer. she could not read they, but do not tell he so, though she

distress he by invite he to read Mrs. Inchbald aloud to she in

the gazebo at the end of the garden, above the wall that be wash by

the river when the tide be up and slime with mud when it be out.



the curate's mind be at that time divide into two compartment, one

for literature and the other for religion. he see no necessity for

reconcile the two thing and make no attempt to do so. he regard

religion as his work, and literature as an escape from it. life be

extremely pleasant: lazy day succeed lazy day. the obedient flock

be round into the church on Sunday and quite often on weekday, for

there be no other place in which two or three could gather together.

the only tiny cloud in a fair blue sky be drink and dissent, and in

the low class an occasional outburst of immorality. Dissent be

ignore; drink be attack by prayer; and immorality be defeat by

a hurried marriage where possible, and when that be out of the

question it be scarify by threat--sure promise even--of eternal

punishment. it be all a matter of routine, and so extremely pleasant

that Francis soon cease to regret that he have stifle ambition with a

black coat. his vicar be a man who have take a small active part in

the tractarian movement, without in the least understand its

spiritual significance. he have be attract by the notion that the

Church exist in spite of Henry VIII, and not because of he, and

when his leader tell he to adopt the idea of the Real Presence in

the Sacrament and the sacrificial priesthood of the clergy, he do so

without make any attempt to bring they into agreement with the fact

of his life or the practice of his profession. he institute ritual in

his service, thus make they more entertaining to his flock, and, as

he have never look for any rejuvenation of the spiritual life of his

parish, he be not disappoint by the result. he think he be a

good man: everybody say he be a good man, and perhaps he be.



Francis swallow both his idea and his goodness without difficulty,

examine the ritual of the service with interest and some enthusiasm,

correct it in one or two point, and as he take nearly all the work

of the parish off the vicar's shoulder, there could not possibly be

any friction between they. the vicar congratulate himself on having

find a jewel of curate, and his wife begin to dream dream of vast

preferment obtain through the Folyat influence--a rural deanery, a

stall, a bishopric, and--why not?--Canterbury or York.



Francis, on the other hand, give no thought to the future and drift

drowsily, most keenly enjoy himself in the summer day when he

could take a boat, a book, an old long-shoreman and fishing tackle,

and go down to the sand-bank at the mouth of the estuary and bathe

and fish and lie in the sun with his clay pipe between his tooth, and

his golden beard glistening and his blue eye shine as he think

that the world be very good and the sea almost the good of good

thing in it. it be not his way to compare his lot with other, and

it have never occur to he, except in his official capacity, to

criticise life. in that capacity he criticise not so much life as a

traditional concept of it.



he be rudely awaken from this drowsy golden age by an event which

keep Potsham talk for a generation and a half.



one night when he have be dine at Crabtrees, the house of Miss

Martha Brett's aunt, where there have be music and cribbage and a

walk down the garden to look at the moonlight on the sea, he return

to his room to find they occupy by a man with a white face and

quivering lip and leg that would not be still. he be a poor man and

a sort of seafare man, and he look up and in a rustle voice he

say:



"Parson, what sort of man would that man be. . . ?"



then he stop and rattle in his throat, and Francis feel a curious

nausea as he look at the man and see how frightened he be.



"what sort of man?" ask Francis, feel that the question be

almost as meaningless as the man's word.



"she's get a cut in her throat and a lot of blood. . . . I say."



"I say," echo Francis.



"we'd well go," say the man.



"yes," say Francis. "do anybody know?"



"no," reply the man, "I been look at her three hour."



with that he seem to gain control of himself, and his leg do not

shake any more and his lip set in a thin straight line. he stand up

and go to the door and Francis follow he. very cunningly the man

look at he and say: "you do know how a man could do it?"



"no," say Francis, "unless----"



however, the man seem to be satisfied and lead the way, and they

walk down the little crooked street call the Strand and come to a

little tumble-down house by the dock, and there they find the woman

even as the man have tell. to Francis the adventure seem to be

complete and fantastic, and he feel that he be outside it, that the

world have stop and that it be very cold. then he feel that it be

horrible and intolerable, simply because nothing could happen unless

he make it happen, and action have never be ask of he before.



there be a tallow candle in a bottle, but it give very little light.

the moon shine through the window, and its light be very pitiless and

grim.



the man fold his arm and say with a sort of insistence: "you do

know how a man could do it!"



the cold dead harshness of his voice bring Francis out of his

fantasy, and at last he find the word that have be buzz in his

brain ever since he see the man sit in his chair: murder.



"no," he say, and he be astonish at the hardness of his own voice.



he turn heavily, but the man be quick than he. he see he dart

through the door, run a little way up the street, go into a house, and

in an instant there spring up a crowd of people whispering, murmuring,

buzzing, huddling, and crush round the door of the little dark

house. they be a little awed when they see the curate, but the crowd

hum as new people come run up and the tale be tell again.

suddenly Francis feel a hand on his arm, and there be the man

cling to he while the beadle and the policeman be tug at he

to take he away. the man would not let go, but he be very strong,

and for some way Francis have to move with he through the crowd. then

at last he wrench free and watch the three figure cleave into the

crowd, part it, and then be swallow up.



he find himself stand at a place where, between two house, he

could see the water swell with the tide and a black boat rock,

and over all the light of the moon.



the machinery of the law pass over the murderer and he be hang,

but Francis never tell a soul how he have be draw into the eddy of

the crime. his experience produce in he a feeling of profound

depression, from which he recover slowly and painfully to find that

human being have emerge from the landscape as they have never do

before. they demand, a different sort of attention from that which

he have always give they, and at first he dislike they heartily. he

see they in their habit, sadly, as they be--eat, drinking,

sleep, gossip, with very little to vary the monotony save

foolish love affair and mean jealousy and petty quarrel. nothing

that they do, not even their sin, seem to be worth while. what

bother he most be that he find himself sympathise with the

criminal and curiously desirous of defend he against the society

which have answer ferocity with ferocity.



that do not last long. he be soon bring up against his ignorance

of the world outside and his entire lack of comparative standard,

and, as young man will, he think that at all cost he must

escape--that is, move from the circumference of a dizzily spin

world to the centre of it.



first of all he come to the conclusion that he have religious doubt

and consult his vicar, who bowl he over with professional

strange night and his keen recollection of the tallow candle in the

bottle and the moon shine through the window; and of these he dared

not speak. he agree perfectly that he have set his hand to the noble

service in the world and have no right to look back. but look

forward avail he nothing; the present be bewilder and the past

have suddenly become empty. the bung have be remove from the tight

barrel of his existence and all the good liquor have leak away.



however, he do his work neither well nor bad than he have do it

before. he christen child and church woman and married couple

and read solemn and beautiful word over the dead, and for the first

time begin to ponder the meaning of these ceremony. the Church, he

say, sanctify birth and death and what lie between they, and he

try to persuade himself that it raise they from brutality,

spiritualise they, and make they holy; but then he could not help

feel that there be some discrepancy. the fact remain the same,

therefore if they be sacred at all they must be sacred in

themselves. all that could be do by mind and Holy Writ, the product

of inspire mind, be nobly to interpret the fact, to see to it that

man live nobly--live nobly and nobly die. he have see several

person die, have give they the comfort of religion, and now, when he

remember, he be strike principally by the dignity with which death

be accept. it seem to he that man have religion in themselves,

that it be not, could not be graft on they from without.



"life," he say to himself, "be a religious thing, or it be something

less than life."



he feel that he be move from the circumference to the centre, and

then he realise that he be reach only the centre of his own

thought, not the heart of the world. he have advance in theory but in

practice be just as far out of his bearing as ever. he have feed

himself chiefly with the writing of ironist and he be hungry for

belief--in the nobility of life and death and the unity of all thing.

the life of bird he know and the life of beast, but of the life

of man he know nothing at all. never have he be to a great city, but

he conceive that there also the life of man must be very much what

they be in the somnolent little town on the Devon estuary--they be

bear, they suffer, and they die. that be all. surely that be all.



he would not have that. the ironist leave it at that. he become

positive that the manner of it matter--to Nature, perhaps, not at

all, but to man, and to God through man, vitally. to that end the Holy

Bible have be write and the Church found, and to that end Keble

and Pusey have seek to rouse the Church from its indolence and

indifference. his vicar be right: he could not turn back, but he must

know wherein his work as a priest consist. if it serve any purpose

at all, it must be for the sanctification of life by endow it with

a noble interpretation.



Francis have no large conception of the universe. at this young period

of his life his notion be still mediæval. he believe the earth to

be stationary, Hell to be under his foot, and the Heavenly region to

be beyond the blue vault of the sky, and that human life lead

infallibly to one or the other. a noble life, therefore, be that

which lead to Heaven, and to this idea, and to the cosmogony it

imply, he shape his ethic and his ideal, never suspect that he

be sacrifice the great to the less.



when man sit down and think out scheme of life they nearly always

make the mistake of leave woman out of they. this be easily

understand in the case of young man for whom woman hardly exist except

as an emotion, a fire that may at any moment flame into their

existence and lie it waste like the little fox in the Bible. our

curate make that mistake. naturally he have be in love--never out of

it; but always he have worship from afar, and have think the

object of his adoration as insensible to it as the star in the sky

to his wonder and delight. he be in love now, but could attach his

emotion to no particular young woman. there be at least four, and he

never credit they with any design when he meet they out walk, or

they come to he on parish business or demand his escort or

display their garden to he. he enjoy his emotion while he be

ashamed of they. they be not "noble."



when next he sit--at her suggestion--with Miss Martha Brett in the

gazebo, he find himself think that she be very charming and

pretty with her brown ringlet on either side of her face and her

plump little shoulder peep out of her gown, a modification of the

style make popular by the young Queen. she be so demure, so quiet,

and her manner of listen to he give he such a sense of authority.

he feel it could never leave he, that he would never again have those

appalling moment in church when a gulf open wide in front of he

and he feel that any one of his listener have more right than he to be

talk and call this black and that white.



she sit by a little table and he sit on the other side of it with a

he gaze and gaze at it, then with a wrench turn to his book, but

his eye be swim so that the word swallow each other up.



rose nod in at the window, and the smell of the salt water come up

and mingle with the garden scent.



"it be most move," say Miss Martha.



"Most," stutter the Curate, and he look up and see warmth and

mischief in her eye, and almost imperceptibly she edge her hand a

little nearer to he.



her aunt come in on that, and Francis heave an immense sigh of relief

and go splutter on with his reading as though he have be catch

out in some shameful act.



when he leave the house later in the evening he admit to himself

most peerless, the most chaste, the most innocent of woman, and he

suffer horribly. the more he wrestle with it, the more powerful it

he be in love, and love swamp all his thought. he take long

solitary walk, and he hate all the couple whom he marry and

say that love be sweet? the old fleeting devotion have be

delicious--if shameful; but this, this be fire in the vein, scald

thought, an obsession, a fix idea.



More to be rid of it than with any hope of success, he call upon

grant he permission to ask for her niece's hand in marriage--exactly

Potsham and she have satisfy herself as to his antecedent. he

explain that he be not rich but have every hope of be give a

family live as soon as one should fall vacant. to his amazement he

admiration for Mr. Folyat, and, with her eye gleam exultation and

beatitude, she confess that she could desire no well thing than to

see such coincidence between her own wish and her niece's

affection.



Francis take his leave pray devoutly that he might not meet his

Martha, but no soon have he set foot outside the parlour door than

there she stand before he, and he could say nothing and she could say

nothing, until suddenly he catch she up in his arm and hug she

and kiss she, set she down on her foot gasp, beg her pardon,

and blunder out of the house blushing furiously.



Cousin Bampfield warmly congratulate his kinsman on his betrothal,

and, two adjacent living in Cornwall presently fall vacant, give

there be a splendid wedding and the young couple spend their

honeymoon in London, for neither have ever before visit the capital.

they see the Tower of London and Westminster Abbey and St. Paul's

Cathedral, but Martha be impress by nothing so much as her

grand, and be never tired of hear her husband tell of his gentle

family, the Folyats.







III



ST. withan



_not know how to find the open air,

but toil desperately to find it out._

     HENRY VI, Part III.



if Potsham be somnolent, St. Withans, our parson's cornish living,

might well have be the home of the Sleeping Beauty. for a time it

be a place of enchantment while the charm and novelty of wed love

be upon Francis and his Martha. they be blissfully happy: the

be marry for five year they exchange duty for a year with the

child, a daughter, be bear there. after that it become a habit with

they to go over to the continent every year for a couple of month.



their sixth child die in infancy, their seventh only live to be

three year old, but the eighth, ninth, and tenth be as healthy and

comely as the first five.



it be a year or so after the birth of the tenth, in 1867, that they

begin to discover that while their family have grow their income have

these be the name of the young Folyats: Serge, Gertrude, Frederic,

Mary, Leedham, Minna, Annette, and James.



Serge have early pass out of his parent' control, though not without

be indefinitely postpone.



the girl be the problem. Gertrude and Mary have suddenly become

parent who have aspire to make their son physician and be force

Willie Folyat, the possible heir afore-mention, an undergraduate at

Francis look at she with anxious eye and mutter something about

"I must work," say Francis.



"but, my dear Frank, the poor!"



"it be easy for a rich man to enter the Kingdom of Heaven than for a

they have many unhappy day, and it be some week before they find an

neither Francis nor any member of his family have ever be north of

Francis visit the place and stay there two day, during which it

Francis stay with the rector and meet the caretaker of the school and

their speech sound uncouth and harsh after the soft drawl of his

the living be worth three hundred and fifty pound a year, and there

IV



FERN SQUARE



_Sir,--I pray you take heed how you put a beast, tired with the heat

of the sun and with long travel, among other, which, as I hear say,

have diver malady and diseases._--A PETTY THEFT.



henceforth we see Francis Folyat in trouser, and his broad-brim

silk hat, like a bishop's without cord, have be exchange for the

soft round black feel which take so many inch from a man's stature.

black trouser, elastic-sided boot, a black silk waistcoat on which

hang an amethyst cross, a clerical frock-coat, and a round white-linen

collar be the daily attire of the new Rector of St. Paul's, Bide

Street. on great occasion the black-silk waistcoat be renounce in

favour of one of violet silk. all day on Sundays he wear his cassock

with a black silk sash round his full stomach, and when he walk to

church he wear a biretta, to the solemn awe of the street urchin, who

confound he with the Greek _papa_, a strange figure that haunt

the child of the northern district of our town and be repute to

be a wizard.



Francis be in fine middle age. his head be begin to be bald and

his golden beard be rapidly turn white. there be red vein on

his round cheek, but his eye be those of a boy, bright and blue

and merry, and when he laugh they use to close up into little slit

and all his bulk would shake until tear be squeeze out of they. he

have a great capacity for laughter, but always it seem that he could

not shake it out. it possess he and set he quiver like a jelly,

and there be nothing to be hear but a hoarse chuckle deep down in

his chest.



there have be time of great difficulty in the beginning. Martha have

come to our town with glow dream, and when she see Fern Square and

the house she be to live in they came tumble down on her romantic

head, and she weep many tear and declare that she could not bear the

house or the people or the town, and she demand to be take away--to

Potsham, to Plymouth, anywhere out of the smoke and the rain and the

filth and the noise and away from the common, common people. she vow

that she would never attend service in the hideous, squalid little

church, and could never have anything to do with the nasty, dirty

school. it be impossible. she could not let her child mix with the

child of these barbarian. they must find another living or give up

the Church altogether. Francis could retire, and devote himself to

theological literature.



Francis bear it all with excellent good-humour, and point out that

he could not even write his own sermon (he have amass a collection

of one hundred and fifty, which he deliver in rotation). by sheer

amiability he win his wife over to a more sweet and reasonable temper,

and help she to set her house in order.



when he come to tackle his work he find that his predecessor have be

a lazy and unpopular man and that his congregation be never more than

a hundred and twenty person, while the Sunday-school be very

meagrely attend. he begin by have the church and school clean

and decorate, and it be quickly noise abroad that the new rector

have private mean. far, it soon become clear that a new church be

to be add to the little ring of church which uphold the High

Church cause in our town. after much cogitation Francis decide to

abolish the pew-rent and to open his church free to all and sundry.

he buy new surplice and cassock for his choir, and new vestment

for the clergy and the acolyte whom he intend to appoint. very soon

he find adherent and be give present of new altar-cloth and

sacramental vessel. altogether his prospect be admirably fair.



Fern Square be a wedge-shape piece of ground enclose on one side by

six old red-brick house four story high, build in an imitation of

the georgian style, with three room on each floor except the top,

which have four and an attic. the dining-room and the study be on the

ground floor, the drawing-room on the first. they be fine room, and

the Folyats' old furniture make a brave show in it. the house be light

by gas, and the drawing-room window look across the square upon the

deliberate ugliness of a Wesleyan chapel. the parrot have his cage in

the dining-room among the family portrait, and the dog live where he

like. he grow very fat and lose what little intelligence he have ever

have, so that it cease to matter even to himself which be his head

and which be his tail.



the Clibran-Bells from next door but one be the first to call. Mr.

Clibran-Bell be the borough treasurer, a man with a huge red beak of

a nose, a little white moustache, and a tremendous manner. he have an

unfailing source of pride in his wife, who be really beautiful and

have frequently be liken to the Marquise in _Caste_, a play which

his daughter be always perform in the cause of charity. Mrs.

Folyat flourish the Folyats and the Bampfields at Mrs. Clibran-Bell,

who counter with the Staffordshire Bentleys, her cousin. the

Clibran-Bells all talk mincingly, as though they have eat olive

and could find no polite method of getting rid of the stone. Young

George Clibran-Bell be in the head office of the Thomson-Beaton Bank,

but, let it be add, the Clibran-Bells know the manager. there be

four girl and young George, and they soon become on term of great

intimacy with Gertrude and Mary Folyat, and they fall into the habit

of run in and out of each other' house.



the bishop's wife call.



very soon St. Paul's by its ritual attract a number of

extra-parishioner who have previously have to go two or three mile to

the other end of our town for their religious satisfaction on a Sunday

morning. Francis be encourage and work very hard, find some

excellent people among his poor, and really try to make his church a

centre for they and a source of help in their all-too-frequent time

of trouble. Mrs. Folyat, by dint of custom, overcome her dislike of

the common people with their coarse accent and rather uncouth manner,

and go so far in her compromise with native custom as to renounce

dinner in the evening and take to a heavy mid-day meal, a solid tea,

and a betwixt-and-between sort of supper about nine in the evening. on

Sunday she keep open house. acquaintance and personage be feed at

half-past one, and the familiar of the house at nine, after evening

service.



at first she keep three servant, but when one of they give notice she

do not replace she and be content with two. even that be find to

be over-expensive, and she come down to one and a charwoman, and then,

on Sunday evening, Gertrude and Mary be force to cook the supper

and to wash up the dinner afterwards. they be very disagreeable

about it until they find that most of the young woman they know,

include the Clibran-Bells, do far more housework and do it as a

matter of course. then it become part of the routine of their

existence and they raise no further objection, but Sunday supper

become a cold feast, and they never cook anything but potato and

perhaps a Welsh rarebit for their father, whom they call "Pa."



soon everybody, include the parrot, call Francis "Pa," and he

sun himself in his new popularity and have no further misgiving as

to the wisdom of the step he have take. certainly he seem to have

dispose of the strained relation that have exist in his family in

the country. the girl be busy and occupy all day long and every

day. the boy seem to have many friend, desirable and otherwise.

Martha have take to knitting and crochet-work, and she have Mrs.

Clibran-Bell and Mrs. Starkey, the solicitor's wife, and Mrs. Tuke,

the widow of the man who build the Albert Bridge and be kill on the

day it open, all ready and pleased to listen to her tattle of her

husband's ancestry and her own property and her hope for her

child. she have take to lace cap and have settle down to a style

in dress and a general appearance which should last to the Judgment

Day. she read _the Family Herald_ every week, and every month

purchase the threepenny novel publish by the same firm. she have and

be allow a feeling of superiority, and enjoy herself immensely.

when Francis come to she with misgiving as to their capacity to live

within their income she refuse to listen to he, observe that they

know all the good people in the place--and what more could they want?

every Sunday they have a splendid congregation, and if the bad come

to the bad they could restore the pew-rent. Francis have a vague,

uneasy feeling that the bad have come to the bad, but nothing would

induce he to do that. he let the subject drop.



Leedham have be send to the grammar school, and James attend a

dame's school round the corner in the Bury Road. Annette at first

accompany he, but her godmother have step in and send she to a

school in Edinburgh. Minna, who all her life have do exactly what she

want, refuse to be educate any more, put up her hair and let down

her frock, and claim equal right with her two elder sister. she

enjoy their privilege and avoid their duty.



after the family have be a year in Fern Square Frederic return a

full-blown solicitor, and after a few week' idleness be take into

the office of Mr. Starkey at a nominal salary. he have grow a little

moustache, which make the weakness of his chin even more pronounced,

and, for some reason well know to himself, he wear a monocle. in

Plymouth he have discover a light tenor voice, and he become very

useful to the Clibran-Bells in their amateur theatrical. he join

force with young George, and together they indulge in all those

folly with which young man fortify their uneasy sense of manhood. he

have pale straw-colour hair and a very pale complexion. he have a

ready wit and a quick tongue, and soon win a reputation for

cleverness. his brother Leedham hate he but always shrink away from

Frederic's irony. Leedham have a great respect and admiration for his

father, while Frederic regard he with a contempt which originate,

perhaps, in the episode of his intoxication at St. Withans. Mrs.

Folyat dote on Frederic, and he never have the slight difficulty in

obtain money from she. he use to play the buffoon to she, set she

laugh until the tear run, and then, with a sudden turn of

sentiment, he would make her cry until she laugh. when he could do

neither of these thing he would shock she with an audacious jest.

always he would contrive to keep she entertain.



Francis, then, have no anxiety about his family. there be always

plenty of fun and merriment in his house, and a constant stream of

young people enjoy themselves as though the world have only just

come into be and be to go on for ever and ever. if the atmosphere

be not altogether pious, they be none the bad for that. they

attend church regularly, work in the Sunday-school, and in many

other direction for the parish, and their happiness win the

confidence of the poor, who make surprising effort to please the

rector and his family. good of all, from Francis's point of view,

number of young man be attract to the house and from there be

draw off into various activity. enthusiasm for the High Church

cause run high.



our town be compose of a number of small town and borough, all

now under one city council, but at that time many of the borough and

urban district maintain their separate entity and have their own

council and their own newspaper. the district in which St. Paul's

be situate be still a separate borough, and it have a newspaper

call _the Pendle Times and Lower Brighton Gazette_, which publish

local news and copious police report. the editor of this sheet be a

fanatical Low Churchman whose whole religious force have go into

worship of a certain calvinistic divine, the Reverend Humphrey Clay, a

rigid temperance reformer, Puritan and moralist, who, perceive the

grow laxness of the new industrial population, fling himself with

fierce zeal into the task of castigate their immorality and whip

they up into a state of religious fervour. he have picture their

life--ugly and stunt as they be in fact--as a gay rout of sin,

and he strove to counteract this peculiar fiction of his own mind by a

religion of appal dulness. he have a command spirit and find

many disciple. he substitute the intoxication of conversion for that

of alcohol, and draw hundred to the corrugate-iron church he have

build on a piece of waste land opposite a public-house and a theatre.

his sermon be print week by week in _the Pendle News_--half a

page of fierce exhortation, while the other half be fill with

racing result and advertisement of rat-pit and course. when he

die twenty thousand man and woman follow he to the grim cemetery

overlook the canal, and the street be line for two mile. in

his obituary, Flynn, the editor, call he "the Sainted Humphrey

Clay," and the name stick. a movement be set on foot to replace his

iron church with a stone building to be call the "Humphrey Clay

Memorial Church"--none of your old romish saint be to give his name

to it--and a house-to-house canvas be institute. the factory hand

give their pence, the well class their shilling and pound, and

after many year of uncease work the fund be complete. the

building be erect and consecrate by the bishop just six month

after Francis Folyat come to St. Paul's.



Flynn, the editor, scent the presence of the enemy, and begin the

attack by the publication of the "literary remain of Humphrey Clay,"

contain stern denunciation of popish mummery and mediæval

witchcraft. the wild story fly, and very soon Francis be

credit with worship the Virgin Mary and maintain a secret

shrine in his vestry. Flynn write two denunciatory article, blindly

prejudiced and pitifully ignorant. Francis read they and write to the

editor to invite he to attend service in St. Paul's and see for

himself.



Flynn wait for some week until Easter Sunday, when he fully

expect to see a statue of the Virgin carry round the church. there

be, in fact, a procession, and there be incense which stank in the

editor's nostril. his first impression as he enter have be one of

disgust, for the whole place be fill with flower, in the window,

on the pulpit, on the altar, twine about the lectern--daffodil and

tulip and hyacinth and violet and lily. he sit in his pew at the

back of the church and see man and woman enter, cross themselves, and

curtsey and bow to the East in the central aisle, and his gorge rise

at it all. when the organ begin to boom and send music whir up to

the roof and flood the nave and the chancel, and a young man in a

purple cassock and a lace surplice appear bear the Cross, and

behind he two cens-bearer, and behind they again the choir, the

curate, the special preacher, and Francis Folyat, in robe, cope and

stole, carry his biretta, he be fain to scream out upon the

blasphemy of it all--blasphemy upon the memory of Humphrey Clay. he

watch the procession wind round the church, singe the gladdest of

Easter hymn, and move up into the chancel, where the choir, still

singe in their harsh, untrained voice, file into their place, and

the three priest stand solemnly upon the altar step and wait for

the last note of the organ to die away. two acolyte appear in

purple cassock and little lace surplice and stand below the priest,

and the solemn service begin.



Flynn rush away and walk for mile until he be dog-weary. he take

his class in Sunday-school in the afternoon in a sort of dream, and in

the evening with wide-stare eye he sit unheedingly through the

sombre evening service in the Humphrey Clay Memorial. he see the whole

town, the whole world, imperille. he see in Francis an emissary of

the great whore of Babylon that sitteth upon many water, a man bent

upon seducing soul from salvation, the very devil quote scripture

to his end.



Flynn's sensation be those of a pious young man who for the first

time in his life enter a music-hall, with this difference, that for

Flynn the abhor thing have no charm nor peril for himself, only for

other--those other whom his hero's life have be give to save.



on the Easter Monday his wife discover that their charwoman have

decamp with a sheet and two blanket, and he lay that sin at the

door of the new source of corruption he have discover, call for

strong tea, wrap a wet towel round his ache head, and write the

first of his famous series of article. the following be an abstract

under the heading:



    "_Non Angli se Romani:

      the Enemy within our Gates._



there be a church in this town, for so long devote under the

leadership of our great and saint Humphrey Clay, a church where

Sunday after Sunday, and on week-day also, blasphemy be commit,

blasphemy and a paint mummery. I have be to this church. with my

own eye I have see the finger-mark of the paint, scented hand of

Rome. in this church I see three priests--_priests_, not

_ministers_--clothed like actor in a theatre. they wear purple and

fine linen and they carry funny little hat in their hand. they have

deck up two young layman in purple and silk and fine embroidery, and

their foot trod upon rich carpet, with gleam brass stair-rod. the

very air be thick and oppressive with the smell of flower, and to

this be add the fulsome stench of incense, carry by conceited,

mince little boy. no pen, least of all mine, could describe the

impiousness of the procession, the bowing, the scraping, the

befouling and vulgarisation of thing sacred that happen in this

church, this so-call church, which be in reality a booth, a theatre.

why, the very costume be indecent. the choir-boy do not wear

surplice, but little laced shirt or shift which do not even cover

their spinal bulb. their behaviour, their demeanour, be an affront to

all truly religious-minded person. have I not remember that I be in

the House of God I should have spat in the face of the arch-mummer as

he pass I and bade he begone to Babylon whence he come. who be

this man? why should he be suffer to defile the religion which he be

suppose to practise? why should this play-actor be permit to strut

and mow and paw the air in the Holy of Holies? three time at least I

see he change his costume--in public! and each time he be assist

with a mock solemnity by the valet whom he be pleased to call an

acolyte. they say this man be a gentleman, the kinsman of a noble

family, a rich man, one who have keep his carriage. let he not play

the priest. Humphrey Clay, of blessed memory, be the son of a

carpenter, a work carpenter in this town, but before his maker he

be a gentleman indeed. it be but twelve month since our bishop

consecrate the memorial which be the crowning edifice that pinnacle

the glorious career of Humphrey Clay. can that same bishop within his

diocese tolerate the splendid memorial to the one and the impious

practice of the other man? I say he cannot. such church as this

have not hitherto be tolerate in our part of the town. citizen,

shall we endure it now?



_N.B._--Further article on the subject will appear until something be

do. if those in authority will not move, we shall take the matter

into our own hand."



Francis read this effusion and be hurt by it. since he have thump

his brother William on the nose he have quarrel with no man and

deliberately hurt none. behind the wild writing he could feel the

torment, and he be sorry. he feel that he be to a certain extent to

blame because he have invite the man to his church in a challenging

spirit, and so have perhaps increase prejudice in he. he try to

write to Flynn but could find nothing to say. as he sift his

thought he could only discover that he wish his church to be free.

all sort and condition of man be free to come and free to stay

away. he have once find one of his sidesman turn a ragged old

beggar-woman out, and have reprove he and lead the old woman to a pew.

she spit on the floor and sit finger an old clay-pipe, but, to

Francis's way of thinking, these thing might not be unacceptable to

the God he honour, however distasteful they might be to human

creature. the church, then, be free, and Francis desire only to

make it pleasing and attractive to those who come to it, to have it a

place of beauty amid so much ugliness. the Saturday before Easter have

be one of the happy he ever remember--a day of hard work in the

church, surround with young people all gay and blithe and busy with

the flower and drapery and vestment. one such day, he feel, could

do much to redeem the waste and folly of year.



however, it be all odious and disgusting to Flynn, and Francis sigh

as he reach out for his tin of bird's-eye and fill his pipe. the

parrot scramble out of its cage, shuffle along the floor and climb

up the back of his chair, perch on his shoulder, and stand comb

its beak through his beard.
