hostility



_Thou lift I up above those that rise up against I; thou hast

deliver I from the violent man._

     PSALM xviii. 48.



the dead play a not altogether disproportionate part in the affair of

the living. there be so many more of they. the thought would be

desperate but for the reflection that in all probability the most

numerous of all be the unborn. the Creator may at any moment get

tired of the eternal monotonous repetition of birth and death, but no

man or woman will ever believe that. we get joy out of it, and his be

the sum of all our joy--the dead, the living, and the unborn.



Humphrey Clay, for all the grimness of his word and work, must have

be a joyous man, for his spirit be very powerful and rouse many

man to action. true, their action be all ugly, but that come from

their stupidity and the squalor of their surrounding. there be no

country on the north of our town for thirty mile--only smoke brick

and mortar and tall chimney and colliery stack. on the south you

must go seven mile before you will find a truly green field, and most

of we be quite old before we can make such a pilgrimage, and then

clear air and tree and stream and sky and the song of bird be

thing as separate from our life as our dream. they be almost a

show to we. our great holiday be whitsun-week, and then each church

take its child in wagonette and char-à-banc out into the near

semblance of green country, where they wander and play and laugh and

squabble and be feed until they can hardly stand. it be call a

"treat," and it give they a new zest for the street and their

adventurous, strangely independent life.



the roman Catholic church organise procession which meet in the

centre of the town and wind through the street, the little girl in

white and the little boy in the good they can muster.



in his fourth article Flynn exhort Francis to be an honest man and

take his flock to join they. in the meanwhile there have be appeal

to the bishop, who refuse to move in the matter, be convince,

from what he have see, that there be nothing uncanonical in the

conduct of the service at St. Paul's. he like Francis, and if he

could not altogether approve of the mean, the result be eminently

satisfactory. as a result of Flynn's campaign there be hardly ever a

seat to be have in St. Paul's on Sundays, and some of the most noted

preacher in our town and the surround district be glad to appear

in the pulpit.



Flynn's paper be do very well out of it. all sort of people

rush into the fray and fill his column for nothing, and when his

supporter take to interrupt the service at St. Paul's with

vehement protest the other paper take the matter up, and Francis

find a sort of greatness thrust upon he. he refuse to see

reporter, and tell one persistent Scotsman that it be Flynn's

affair, not his, and that he have no intention of move against Flynn.

he receive many letter denounce he as Anti-Christ, and many more

proclaim he the one Spiritual Hope of the North of England. More

than one of his correspondent enclose poem.



Martha be all in a flutter, and be quite sure that Francis be on

the point of be make a bishop. he be invite to preach to the

judge when they come on assize, and she have no doubt that that would

be the first step. Francis have no such illusion. he be not ambitious

for promotion. he take out Sermon no. 112 and deliver it with the

full consciousness that it be profoundly dull. Flynn come to hear it,

take shorthand note, and print an abstract without comment.



this official recognition provoke exasperation, and on the follow

Sunday as Francis be walk in cassock and biretta to his church he

be accost by a gloomy-face individual with a sandy complexion, who

call he a "spawn of Rome," and when Francis smile at the

grotesqueness of the expression he stoop down and pick up a

handful of dung and fling it in his face. Francis go on his way amid

the hoot of little boy and the jeering of woman.



a few day later the window of his house be break and the voice of

Flynn in _the Pendle News_ rise to a triumphant scream. two policeman

be mount on guard in Fern Square, and the attention of the

malcontent be transfer to the school in Bide Street. the

railing be tear down and the furniture of the door wrench away.

Roughs and hooligan join in, and one Sunday all the door of the

church be find to be screw up, and the congregation stand in the

street, while from the church step Francis read the service and

deliver the first extempore sermon of his life. he be tremble

with emotion and his voice crack, and hardly a soul could hear he,

and he break down altogether when the people sing



    _Rock of Ages, cleft for I,

     let I hide myself in thee. . . ._



a few day later the authority make the mistake of arrest Flynn

on a charge of incite to violence. the prosecution fail, but Flynn

have the satisfaction and the bitterness of martyrdom, and he return

to the assault with new frenzy.



meanwhile at home there have be a new development. Leedham, the third

son, the one stolid member of the family, have upset his mother by

announce his intention of leave school and our town and go out

to the Brazils. he have make the acquaintance of a family who have

connection out there, and he have be fire by their description of

Rio de Janeiro. his real reason be a heartfelt desire to get away

from Frederic, but of that he say nothing. he observe, with much

justice, that he be not do any good at school and would probably

learn no more if he stay there another two year. (the school be

conduct on the principle of force the bright boy and leave the

dull one to pick up what they could.) far, he argue that if he

have to earn his own living, the soon he begin the well. through

his friend, he say, he could obtain a post in a bank in Rio, and he

would rather be in a bank there than in our town.



Francis be inclined to approve, but Martha weep. like so many

mother, she have no notion of her real relation with her child, and

live in a fantasy in which she be the perfect mother who adore and

be adore by they. More than once to Mrs. Clibran-Bell she have say:



"there be nothing that my child do that they do not tell I."



and Mrs. Clibran-Bell, be of much the same type, believe she, and

together they glow with rapture over this miracle of domesticity.



Leedham have very little imagination or capacity of invention, and,

like his father, have rather a disconcerting way of accept the fact

of his existence for well, for bad. he know that he be unhappy at

home, feel that he be go to be a great deal more unhappy, and see

nothing but the necessity of get away.



"Darling Leedham," say his mother, "how can you think of enter

upon vulgar commerce!"



"what else be I to do?"



"but think of your name! a Folyat in a bank!--a clerk! and with your

christian name too!"



(the Earldom of Leedham be the title which Minna miss sharing when

she jilt Willie Folyat.)



"George Clibran-Bell be in a bank," say Leedham.



"but, darling, how can you leave your mother? how can you think of

it?"



"People have to leave their mother soon or later."



"but you love your mother?"



"of course," say Leedham sturdily, "but I want to go."



"you cannot go without your father's consent."



"no."



"very well, then."



and that seem to end the interview.



Leedham see his father first and come straight to the point.



"I want to go to the Brazils."



"I know. your mother be very much upset by it."



"that's not the point."



Francis agree.



"the point be, what be I go to be if I stay?"



"you might be a clergyman or--or----"



"I don't want to be a clergyman."



"a doctor, then?"



"can you afford it?"



"no," say Francis, and the admission bring his opposition tumbling

down. they discuss way and mean, and Francis delight in his

boy's practical good sense and independence, though he have a feeling

of pity and shame that he have not come to know he well before.



"thank you, sir," say Leedham. "and please, will you ask mother not

to cry over I?"



"you cann't expect she not to feel it."



"no, I suppose not. but I want she to be glad too."



"well," say Francis, "I'm glad and I'm proud of you. I wish----"



the thought of Frederic come to he and he say no more.



Mrs. Folyat cry in public at every possible opportunity, and she

come in for a great deal of sympathy. Frederic, who have always use

Leedham as a butt, and thoroughly dislike the idea of lose he, do

his good to make he feel a callous brute. but Leedham be excited and

exalt at the prospect of adventure, though he have no one on his side

but his father and the boy James, who gaze at he with large envious

eye and promote he to heroic rank.



during his last few week Leedham spend many hour in the study with

his father, and they have long friendly talk all about nothing, in

which they skirmish round the new affection that have spring up

between they.



on his last Sunday night there be a farewell supper. Mabel and Jessie

Clibran-Bell be there and Gertrude and Mary and Minna. Frederic be

out, and the boy James have be in bed all day with a cold catch in

crawl along the roof in his night-gown from his attic-window to the

attic of the boy next door. he have be thrash for do it--but

when the boy next door have lay in a feast of sardine and raspberry

jam the temptation be too great, and he scramble over in the pouring

rain, sit for two hour in his wet night-gown and then sleep in it.



with Frederic away Leedham could talk, and he brag of how he would

return in ten year and buy a carriage for his mother and re-build his

father's church and set James up in life and bring jewel for Minna.

(he be fond of Minna.)



"but suppose you marry?" say Mary.



"not I," say Leedham.



"I expect," remark Francis, with a chuckle, "he'll marry a

Portuguese."



"Frank! how can you!" protest Martha. "the Portuguese be

Catholics!"



"perhaps she'll be rich," throw in Minna.



"and beautiful, with dark languish eye," add Mabel Clibran-Bell.

and in a few minute they have create the future Mrs. Leedham and,

rather maliciously, endow she with a furious temper.



Leedham take all the chaff in good part and make himself especially

amiable to his mother.



Mary go upstairs with some supper for James and the talk turn on

Flynn, and everybody wonder what he would do next.



"I hate that Flynn," say Martha.



"oh, come!" reply Francis, "he's fill the church. I couldn't have

do it without he."



"but it be horrid," say Mabel Clibran-Bell.



"certainly; but Flynn be get what he want and I be get what I

want. both his people and my people be more enthusiastic than they

would be otherwise."



"Father say," put in Jessie Clibran-Bell, "that he be get

libellous."



"let he," return Francis.



"wouldn't you proceed against he?"



"not I. I don't think the clergy should squabble even in the Law

Courts."



"but," say Martha, "it would be a case for Frederic."



Mary return say that James be not in his room and nowhere in the

house. she have call through the window to the boy next door, but

there be such a terrific wind her voice be blow away. there be

two chimney-pot blow down in the square.



Mrs. Folyat go white and her lip tremble. they all look from one

to the other. Leedham leave the room and they hear the front-door

bang, and the wind moan in the chimney.



Francis rise to his foot and move towards the door. Mary run upstairs

again, and Gertrude put the parrot's cloth over his cage because he

be begin to scream. come a ring at the door, and presently

Leedham appear with his hair blow into his eye and his face very

pale and his tooth chatter. he turn to his father and say:



"come!"



Mrs. Folyat faint.



Francis turn sick at heart and go out into the passage. the front

door be open and the gas be flicker in the wind, so that it be

very dark. there be two man hold a little white bundle between

they.



the boy James have be blow from the roof and they have find he on

the pavement below. he be quite cold, and it be impossible to tell

how long he have be there.



the house be full of whispering and the guest withdraw, steal

away like ghost. Leedham stay to look after his mother. they

carry the boy upstairs and lay his poor break body on the bed in

Mary's room, and Francis fumble out and along the street to beg the

doctor to come at once. there be nothing to be do. thinking be no

use. tear seem foolish. it be only mechanically that Francis

turn to his God and say, "Thy will be do."



the boy be bury in the grim cemetery over by the canal. the

parishioner club together and erect a little marble cross above

his grave. they want to express their sympathy, and the very poor

send pathetic little wreath of ivy and hideous wax monstrosity and

horrible crosse of iron filagree. the beauty and charm of the boy

be discover after he be dead, and for a little while the house in

Fern Square be a sort of temple in his honour. his belonging be

gather together and partition, and Leedham take with he to Rio de

Janeiro his little brother's christen mug and spoon.



Mrs. Folyat be prostrate with grief, and the shock to her nerve make

she for a long time a valetudinarian. she be just recover when

there come the crown act of brutality.



Flynn be silence for a space, but it be strangely whisper among

his follower that in St. Paul's mass be be say and candle light

for the dead.



Francis have encourage the more devout among his parishioner to use

the church for private meditation and prayer. he himself, in his

grief, spend many hour there, and this find interpretation in the

report that he be institute the confessional. Flynn do not stop to

examine the accuracy or probability of the rumour but hurl

thunderbolt. a gang of rough set on Frederic one day, and he come

home with his clothe tear and muck and his face bloody. urge by

his wife, and much against his own inclination, Francis write to Flynn

and beg he to confine his attention to himself. he say:



"I be a priest, but I be proud, and if there be to be suffer as the

consequence of my action I would rather bear it on my own shoulder."



Henceforth Francis be know as the Proud Priest.



one of the most fanatical of Flynn's follower discover that the boy

James be bury not twenty yard away from the angel-guard tomb of

Humphrey Clay, and this, when bruit, fall like a spark upon the dry

mind of the most ignorant member of the faction. on a dark evening

in November they go up to the cemetery, overturn the little marble

cross, efface the name James Matthew Folyat, and scatter the

wreath and flower.



Mrs. Folyat take to her bed. the ringleader be discover and

arrest, and Francis appear in court, very pale, obviously near

breaking-point, and in a very low voice say that he do not wish to

prosecute. there be a wave of sympathy for the unfortunate rector of

St. Paul's. Flynn's paper be boycott of advertisement and he fall

into low water. he have ruin himself in the struggle and he have

almost drain Francis of courage and faith in human-kind. he clung

obstinately to his work, but be dog by a sense of the futility of

it all and, in his bad moment, see it only as a mechanical

sanctification of birth, marriage, and death. Humanity seem so

primitive--just a base struggle for existence and satisfaction in

existence, and silly devastating squabble about form. he realise

dreadfully what a gulf lie between himself and his wife, and he strove

desperately to bridge it, only to discover that she be unconscious of

any disparity and have a diabolical skill in coat any uncomfortable

fact with a romantic fiction so that it become as a pearl upon her

shell. he blame himself for it, and be kind to she and fight

against the exasperation which her prattle arouse in he. have no

friend to whom he could turn--all the man he know defer to his

cloth and treat he as a creature apart--he try to find sympathy

and interest in his daughter and Frederic, his remain son. they

be absorb in their youth and their dream and folly, and seem to

be afraid of he. he watch they, but soon find that he be spy

upon they. the one thing he have to love be the memory of the boy

James, who become ever more radiant to he, and he use to watch the

going out and coming in of the boy next door and think he a

splendid fellow, and regret all that he have miss when his own boy

be alive.



for many, many day life seem to stand still. there be dull

routine, Sunday succeed Sunday. gradually gaiety creep once more

into the house in Fern Square, but it seem to Francis so remote--as

remote as the woman upstairs, who complain and complain and yet

could babble of fashion and the weather and money and the young man

who came court her daughter.







VI



FREDERIC'S friend



_by my troth, Cony, if there be a thousand boy, thou would'st spoil

they all with take their part._

     the KNIGHT of the burning PESTLE.



at the age of twenty-four Frederic be earn twenty-five shilling a

week as a manage clerk in Mr. Starkey's office in Hanging row. he

be fairly punctual in the morning, having hire Minna to rout he out

of bed at eight o'clock, and he would lounge through the morning until

one o'clock when he would disappear for two hour for lunch and coffee

and domino in a smoky cellar call the Mecca Café. in the afternoon

he would work furiously from three to five so as to have something to

show for his day, and in the evening he would come to life. a sort of

swagger would come into his bearing and a pinkish tinge would come

into his pale cheek and a new light into his blue-green eye. he have

discover that in winter his light tenor voice could be make to earn

about thirty shilling a week, and together with a spotty-face youth

in his office who sing comic song (with patter) he go up and down

our town and district giving "I'll sing Thee Songs of Araby" and "to

Anthea" and "there be a Lady Passing by," and win much applause

which invariably go to his head and make he very drunk. he sing

under an assumed name, and no one at home know what he be do

except Minna, whom he bribe with cigarette to hold her peace. (she

use to lock herself in the bath-room and smoke they out of the

window.) when occasionally his mother complain that he be never at

home in the evening he use to say that he be rehearse. at

interval he use to take part in private theatrical with the

spotty-face youth or other of his friend. the piece generally give

be the farce of Madison Morton, or _the blind Beggars_, or some

amateur musical play. there be a Gentlemen's Musical Society which

have a little hall in Oswald Street in the centre of our town. Frederic

and the spotty-faced youth be member, though the Society have fall

on evil day and its entertainment have become rather broad. for the

most part they be smoking-concert, not unlike the Caves of Harmony

that use to be in London, but the air be purify occasionally by a

lady' Night, when the lion roar as gently as any sucking dove,

and give innocuous theatrical entertainment to which the member

bring their daughter. Frederic become a shine light in the

performance, and the member' daughter fall in love with he and

write he ridiculous letter of admiration, which he gulp down

without blink.



it be at the Gentlemen's Concert Hall that he first meet James Lawrie,

the dramatic critic of our weekly newspaper who write under the name

of "Snug," and have some public reputation as a writer of elegant

poetry, and an immense fame among journalist and actor and

theatrical musician and painter as a composer of bawdy verse. this

man be a Scotsman, a hard drinker, and he be say to know every

verse that Robert Burns ever write by heart, and also to have many

poem that have never be print. he use to write notice of the

little performance in the Gentlemen's Concert Hall, and, as he could

be very scathing, the actor use to fawn upon he and flatter he.

the spotty-faced youth introduce he to Frederic one night, and the

old man--he be not above fifty-five, but he have always be Old

Lawrie--shake he warmly by the hand and say:



"I'm proud to meet your father's son, sir."



that rather stagger Frederic, to whom it have never occur that his

father might be admirable. Old Lawrie see that he be a little take

aback and he scowl and go on:



"come, come! not ashamed of your father, be you, heh? my son be,

but then my son be respectable. that's what's the matter with they,

they're respectable and safe. Safe's a good word for a gag. you can

bring your lip together on it hard."



"I be at school in France," reply Frederic with a flush of timidity

under his paint. he have just come from the dress-rehearsal on the

stage, the play be _still water run Deep_.



"if you like France," say Old Lawrie, "you willn't like this curse

hole. you'll die in it. I've never be to France myself, but I've

read their book. they pull everything to bit with their brain.

nothing leave. they're a well lot than we be. get no moral, but who

have? we pretend to have 'em. they don't. know your burn? it's in

print, so I see no reason why I shouldn't speak it:



    _o Lord! yestreen, Thou ken, wi' Meg--

     Thy pardon I sincerely beg,

     o may it ne'er be a livin' plague

          to my dishonour,

     An' I'll ne'er lift a lawless leg

          again upon she.

     maybe Thou let the fleshly thorn

     Beset thy servant e'en and morn,

     lest he owre high and proud should turn

          'Cause he's sae gift:

     if sae, Thy hand maun e'en be bear,

          until Thou lift it.

     but, Lord, remember I and mine

     Wi' mercy temp'ral and divine

     that I for fear and grace may shine

          Excell'd by nane,

     an' a' the glory shall be Thine,

          amen, amen._



Old Lawrie be a fine man as he declaim the verse. his eye flash

and his voice come big from his chest, and when he have do he turn

to Frederic and say:



"that's burn, and out of Shakespeare there isna a healthy spirit in

the world. Burns and Shakespeare--both of 'em poor man and straight

from the earth, and 'll give ye all the cultured dandy in the world

for a farthing gift. so you're play at play-act, young man?

that's what nine-tenth the world be for ever do in its daily life.

they can' I a disgusting old man, but they should hear what I can' they

when my tongue's loosed and my mind's eye see vision. I live in

Hell, but I've a Heaven in my brain. . . . your father's a good man to

go his own way with the dirty Lutherans and the filthy Puritans

yelp at his heel. you'll not be go in for the professional

play-act?"



"no," say Frederic. "I sing."



Old Lawrie clap his old silk hat on his head, take his blackthorn

stick in his hand and give a shout of laughter. he pat Frederic on

the shoulder, purse his lip and hum through they strangely and

vaguely as though he be turn over a morsel of music on his

tongue, and then he break into verse and say:



    _o youth it be a pretty thing,

       a wild rise in the bud.

     but it must die with the pass Spring

       all trample in the mud.

     we've heavy foot in our town,

       rough shod with iron band.

     virginity go topple down

       Befouled with loutish hand.

     o spring be smoke in our town,

       and life's a dirty scrum,

     the angel weep to see God's frown

       and we make Hell to hum._



he turn away after this impromptu and join a bibulous-look

individual with white hair and an enormous face, Joshua Yeo, his

editor, and the near approach to a friend that he have.



Frederic turn to the spotty-face youth and find he grin

vacantly.



"quite balmy," say the spotty-face youth.



"I think he's splendid," return Frederic, amazed at his own

enthusiasm.



"want a new coat," say the spotty-face youth. "he's wear that ever

since I've know he, and it's green with age. he get fight

roar blind once a month, and his son lock he up. I know one of

they--Bennett Lawrie--a bee-yooti-ful young man, high-Church and all

that. may have be to your governor's show. the old man's a

Presbyterian as much as he's anything. he use to be in a bank, same

bank that Randolph Caldecott use to be in. but he quarrel,

quarrel with every one."



"do you think he make that up--about youth and our town?"



"come easy to he. when he's drunk he talk blank verse. he be run

in once, and he harangue the beak like Mark Antony at Cæsar's

funeral."



they be call back to the stage, and Frederic find that he be not

nearly so pleased with himself in his part as he have be. he begin to

think the play foolish and shoddy.



after the rehearsal he have an appointment with the spotty-face youth

to meet two girl on Kersley Moors, a high, dark, treeless common just

outside the northern suburb. from Kersley the road run into the town

past the bishop's palace, and here nightly young man and maiden

foregather and stalk each other and exchange mysterious

greeting, sometimes stop and talk, sometimes pass and

disappear down the dark lane that enclose the bishop's huge

garden. the spotty-face youth, who have be impress by Frederic's

braggadocio of the thing that be much well do in France, have

introduce he to this exchange and mart of foolish emotion and

transitory affection. they go there in search of pleasure and

adventure, and they generally find they, though more puny and debase

than they be prepared to admit. they go there now only half

believe that the girl with whom they have make their assignation

would turn up, for they have seem so superior to the usual quarry.

they do not know their name or where they live. it be enough for

they that both the girl be pretty and responsive to such wit as

they could produce.



the spotty-face youth's father be a doctor, and he have three

brother, and on the way he regale Frederic with tale of their

escapade and the narrow squeak they have have, and the great score it

be to have a father who be a man of the world and understand these

thing. he become so foul-mouthed that Frederic stop he.



"if you don't shut up I shall go home."



"right ho!" say the spotty-face youth. "only I do think you have a

well sense of fun. you don't seem to mind Old Lawrie talk about

Burns and Meg."



"that's different. that's poetry."



"be it?"



"yes. like 'Lucrece' and 'Venus and Adonis.'"



"well. they're pretty hot."



"oh! shut up."



Frederic do not rightly understand why he be so indignant with his

companion. he be conscious of a difference between the two

thing--frank acceptance and fumbling--but he could not put his finger

on it, nor could he discover why for the first time in year of folly

he should feel a sense of shame. it grow on he as they walk up the

Kersley Road and, by the second lamp-post past the bishop's gate, see

the two young woman arm-in-arm pacing slowly in front of they.



"bag I the tall one?" say the spotty-face youth.



before Frederic could reply they have come up with the girl and his

companion have greet they with a tag from the pantomime of the last

winter.



"we think you wouldn't come," say the tall girl.



"no fear," say the spotty-face youth. "trust I when I've spot a

winner."



Frederic set the pace and they walk briskly up the road to Kersley

Church. he hardly say a word, but his companion keep up a run

fire of facetious chatter. at the church the tall girl and Haslam

(that be the spotty-face youth's name) walk on and disappear

into the darkness of the moor after arrange to meet again at ten.

Frederic be leave under the lamp-post with the other girl. she be

very little and slight, and she be rather poorly dress. she look

shyly up at Frederic, and he say:



"you're very pretty."



"I like you well than the other one."



"you see," say Frederic, "I be at school in France."



"oh! France. be they very wicked in France?"



"it depend what you mean by wicked. no more than they be here."



"I suppose you willn't tell I your name."



"I don't mind," say Frederic, with a sudden flow of honesty. he have

so often be Snooks and Jones and Walker. "it's Folyat. Fred Folyat."



"Mine's Lipsett, Annie Lipsett. it's a silly name."



it seem such a silly name to Frederic that he could find nothing to

say, and there come a dead silence between they. she be offend at

last and move away and he have almost lose sight of she in the

darkness of the moor when he run and catch she up. they pass

through the post that fill the entrance to the moor, and Frederic

put his arm round her hard little waist. she stop. he stop and

kiss she and they walk on.



there be lover (and bad) everywhere, and as they creep slowly

forward they hear sigh and silly giggle and voice murmur. it

be very dark and the cloud hang low and the wind be a little cold.

they find a place to sit where through tree they could see the

light of the house. Frederic sit a little away from she and with his

cane prod into the ground.



"I wonder where the other be," say Annie Lipsett.



"do it matter?"



"no."



they be silent for a little, then Frederic remember old Lawrie,

and he purse up his lip as the old man have do and croon a little

to himself. then, suddenly, he ask:



"be you happy?"



he do not wait for she to reply but go on:



"I'm wonder why we mess about with it. what's the good of it, all?

who be you? I don't know. who be I? you don't know. we live in a

beastly dirty town and we wander about like lose soul. and because we

be lose soul we take anything that come along--you I, I you. be it

good enough? it's all wrong. but what's right? . . . it's fun, I

suppose. Fun! . . . but what else be there?"



he take the girl's hand.



"I tell you what. I'm damned sorry for you."



"of course," say Annie Lipsett. "of course, you're a gentleman."



and Frederic laugh. he tell himself that he be an idiot, and that

all _that_ be not his affair. he have bring this girl here, just as

all the other young man in the place have bring all the other young

woman, to forget, to escape for a little while, to lose all thought of

the beastliness of life down below in the town. it _be_ beastly.

everything be so dirty, and everybody be so poor and so tired. . . .

he take the girl in his arm and hold she very close and whisper

silly talk to she, and soon she be sigh and lie and nestle to

he.



they be ten minute late in their return to the lamp-post by the

church, and Haslam and the tall girl be very ill at ease and silent.

Annie Lipsett clung to Frederic's arm and they walk down to the

bishop's palace. there they part. Frederic kiss she and she clung

to he. the tall girl lead she away and they vanish into the night.



as they turn their face homeward Haslam say:



"Gawd! I have have a rummy time. I couldn't touch she without she

start on the cry game. Sha'n't see she again."



"no. I suppose not," say Frederic.



Haslam look at he.



"well. you're a caution, you be!"



"I'm a bit of a swine, the same as you," retort Frederic.







VII



young WOMEN



_our own precedent passion do instruct we

 what levity be in youth._

     TIMON of ATHENS.



young people must for ever be try to fall in love, and in this

ancient sport young woman be every bit as active as young man. for

the matter of that, the great part of humanity remain adolescent in

this affair, that be, hem in by a thick-set hedge of prejudice and

unsatisfie emotion and convention and childish theory, so that they

be for ever in a state of uneasy curiosity about love, always ready

to put salt on its tail, but unable to come within reach of it. for

that reason they be always confuse love with be in love, an

active state of living which can be permanent with an emotional

condition which must be transitory.



Mrs. Folyat have the most beautiful illusion about her household. she

be not entirely deceive by they when she come face to face with

herself, but in her relation with her husband, her friend and her

daughter she always exhibit the most profound faith in they. though

her daughter be grow woman she never trouble to discover the

state of their mind, but assume their innocence and purity, and she

never refer back to her own state of mind at the same age and the

same maiden condition. in short, she burke the difficulty and the

responsibility, though she be secretly alarmed at their slowness in

find husband. she have no notion of their find any career

outside marriage, and take no step to prepare they even for that.



there be a constant stream of young man pass through the house,

and they all seem to do their good to fall in love with Gertrude and

Mary, but they either fall victim to Minna, who play with they and

squeeze their young heart dry between her finger and thumb, or they

disappear and be catch in the toil elsewhere. there be so many

young woman and so few eligible young man. they flirt, they dance,

they pay visit to the theatre, and Gertrude sing and Mary play

her violin, but nothing happen. it be very annoying. the

Clibran-Bell girl do not marry either, but there be no comfort in

that. they have such large nose; and they be not folyat. they have

not the charm of high gentility. . . . neither Gertrude nor Mary be

pretty, but they could be amusing and they seem to attract

attention. Minna be decidedly pretty, with a wide delightful grin and

a mocking humour. the most serious and solemn young man be devote

to she. they be always propose to she, but she always refuse they

or become engaged to they for about a week. her betrothal hardly ever

seem to survive the visit to their family. she invariably seem

to see they in caricature, and have amass quite a large collection of

mental picture of north-country family who receive she at high tea

and welcome she with shy effusiveness.



Mrs. Folyat be fonder of Minna than of her other daughter. she be

easy to get on with and much less expensive. Mary and Gertrude have

acquire the habit of visit relation in the South much rich than

themselves, and every year they demand an exorbitant outlay on

clothe, and they come back rather scornful of life in Fern Square and

rather rebellious at have to resume their household duty or work

in the church and Sunday-school. also, for a time, they would assume a

lofty tone with the young man of their acquaintance, and they use to

prick at Frederic and tell he he be become provincial. Minna use

to lash they with her tongue, deal out the wicked malice with

the most urbane good-humour, and deliberately annex any young man whom

they bring to the house. she call Gertrude "Mother Bub" and Mary

"Mottle-tooth." from their superiority in year they affect to

ignore she, but they lose no opportunity of annoy she and upset

her plan for her own comfort and enjoyment.



the money-cloud have grow dark over Francis. it seem impossible to

make his expenditure acknowledge even a bow acquaintance with his

income. he have credit with the tradespeople but it be abuse. fifty

pound have become a large sum of money to he, because the payment of

it mean dislocation of his finance. Frederic be always send in

small bill that be too large for his slender earning. the

girl--Mary and Gertrude be still call "the girls"--were always

want money. Annette in Edinburgh hardly ever write without want

money, and Mrs. Folyat seem to have no notion of the decrease

elasticity of his resource. it be perfectly clear to he that a

change must be make, and quickly. he go into his account, find

that he owe three hundred and fifty pound--nearly a year's

stipend--and write the figure down on a scrap of paper and lay it

before his wife.



"we owe that," he say. "it's a lot of money."



Mrs. Folyat turn the piece of paper round and round in her finger,

and Francis stand above she pull at his beard.



"it's a big sum," he say.



Mrs. Folyat pull out her handkerchief and begin to whimper, as she

always do when Francis be masterful.



"I'm sure," she say, "I'm sure it's not my fault. I'm sure I wish

we'd never come to this hateful pace. I don't know why we do."



Francis feel a gust of exasperation.



"we come here," he say, "to marry the girl. they're not married."



Mrs. Folyat see reproach in what be only a statement of fact, and she

protest with some vehemence. the failure of her daughter hurt she.

she feel it as keenly as Sarah, the wife of Abraham, feel she

barrenness, for she see life altogether in term of marriage, romantic

marriage. her own life have fall into the line lay down by the

fiction with which she refresh herself--as a girl she have dream of

a romantic lover--he have come--a parson, a creature of noble

birth--and she have marry he. she have bear he a truly biblical

number of child and look for they to follow a similar destiny.

she have regard it as a thing that happen automatically, for she

be in mind a child, and life be to she a toy present to she by a

beneficent creator, already wound up and prepare to go indefinitely.

when apparently it run down she could do nothing but weep and make

thing as uncomfortable as possible for those near she. she hate

fact, and Francis, her husband, have the most odious habit of plump

they down in front of she.



always before when they have be present with any financial

difficulty they have sell a house at Potsham, for the reduction of

their private income by twenty or thirty pound have seem no great

matter. but they have already sell five house, and the loss of one

hundred and fifty pound a year have, as Francis now point out,

play a considerable part in bring they to their present quandary.

he be loth to sacrifice another house and more income, and nervously

propose that they should raise the required sum by sell some of

their valuable china and perhaps a piece or two of Martha's jewellery.

she hardly ever wear her jewellery, but she love to hoard it, and

whenever she be particularly pleased with her woman friend she use

to reward they by display the content of her treasure-drawer,

jewel, old lace, silk and brocade and fan, acquire and

inherit--thing valuable and trumpery all lie higgledy-piggledy.



her husband's suggestion act like salt rub into the wound

occasion by his statement of fact. she ask why she should always

be the sufferer for the delinquency of her family, and almost

persuade herself that she be their scapegoat. she go back over the

year and rake over the ashe of old resentment and grievance, even

go so far as to disinter the sacrifice of her carriage at St.

Withans.



"the fact remain," say Francis, "that we owe a large sum of money. I

be a clergyman, and my house should be free of the sordid trouble

that beset the laity. it be not free of they and I be ashamed."



"very well, then," say Martha, "let we sell everything, spend

everything--the girl will do that easily enough--and then go into the

workhouse."



"please be reasonable," rejoin Francis. "we must pay our debt and

reduce our expenditure. if necessary, the girl must go out and earn

what they can."



"the girl!"



"there be no shame in honest work, whatever it may be."



"but they will _never_ marry if they work."



"half the woman I marry be work woman."



"I willn't discuss it. you have never be the same since we come to

this hateful place."



"I be think chiefly of Mary. she could teach music. and Annette

have have a well education than the other. she could . . ."



"what?"



"she could obtain a situation as a governess."



"a governess! Annette! a governess!"



in Mrs. Folyat's eye to send your daughter out as a governess be a

confession of poverty. there could be no gloss it over. of course

the clergy be miserably pay, but Francis have always rise superior

to that reproach in public opinion by the general belief in the

amplitude of his private mean. it could be little short of disaster

then to confess to inadequacy. and a governess! poor Annette! though

to be sure when she be a child her godmother have look at she sadly

and observe that she must assuredly be prepare for a convent. she

be so plain--a remark which Minna have never cease to brandish over

poor Annette's head whenever their will clash. . . .



Francis at length cut short his wife's protestation with a sigh and

say:



"my dear, I'm sorry. that's the position. we have to swallow it. we

cann't give the girl the opportunity they ought to have. we must let

they fight their own way. at present anything be well than the sort

of life they be lead. we'll sell another house, but that shall be

the last. we'll make a fresh start. be patient with I, my dear."



"and be _i_ to tell Mary?"



"no. I'll do that, and I'll find a family for Annette."



Francis go away feel that there be a great deal to be say for

the celibacy of the clergy. other man, of course, do not see so much

of their family, and perhaps, for that reason, could understand they

well, be well friend with they, and not so acutely conscious of

their irritate peculiarity. the relation between a father and

daughter should be a very beautiful thing, and indeed there be

moment when the house in Fern Square be a place of happiness and

affectionate unity. only--only, there be the future. Martha grow

more and more helpless, and the household duty and responsibility

devolve more and more upon Gertrude and Mary, and they lose their

bloom.



Francis have a vague feeling of injustice which be harshly in

disaccord with his professional teaching of acceptance--"Whatever be,

be right" and "it's all for the good." at any rate there be still

abundant laughter in his house, and that be well than the grim

smile which be all these Northerners would for the most part allow

themselves. the day of violent opposition be go, but the Puritans

still look askance at the Proud Priest--for the nickname clung--and

his family. the grocer with an off-licence round the corner spread

tale of the large quantity of beer that be consume in the

parson's house.



Mary take the suggestion very well, and soon she have five pupil,

little boy and girl, whom she teach to fumble on the piano and to

extract horrible noise from the violin. she go to their house and

enjoy make new friend. Annette be bring home from Edinburgh at

the end of the term and be find a situation with an ironmaster's

family name Fender. she have one pupil, a little hunchbacked girl who

alternately adore she and bully she. Annette be very happy. at

home she have be so mercilessly tease by Minna that she be glad to

get away. the Fenders live in Burnley, ten mile away, and in summer

they move to a lovely house they have in Westmoreland, high-perch on

a hill look down on Grasmere and Rydal. she read enormous

quantity of novel, and devour the pound and pound of sweet and

chocolate that be lavish on her pupil. once a week she write

dutiful letter to her parent and surreptitiously she begin to write

a novel in the manner of Mr. James Payn. she write three chapter, and

then find the labour of write too exhausting and continue the

story mentally in her many idle moment.



at home in Fern Square the conduct of Gertrude have be cause some

astonishment and alarm. for five consecutive Sundays she fail to put

in an appearance at morning service, and once she neglect she

Sunday-school class. when question about it--she be a woman in

year, but Mrs. Folyat be not the mother to relax her authority until

it be wrest from she--she reply that she be make a tour of the

High Churches in our town--with a friend. the answer be find

satisfactory, but Minna look into the fact and find that her elder

sister have spend every one of those five sunday in St. Saviour's,

where there be a young acolyte who have a beautiful profile and

soulful eye. he wear the most exquisite garment, and his expression

be as near monkish as anything you can find in the Church of England.

his face be lean and pale, and his whole bearing be mournful in the

extreme.



for two sunday after the inquisition Gertrude go to service in St.

Paul's. on the third she disappear again, and Minna plead

headache, watch the other go off with their prayer-book and Sunday

clothe, and then hurry to St. Saviour's, a little church build on a

slag-heap above the jewish quarter. she creep in just before eleven

and find Gertrude sit far up near the step of the nave gaze in

rapt and religious devotion at the young acolyte as with almost

theatrical solemnity he perform his rite. if he be conscious of

she he give no sign. with an almost yearning intensity he creep

noiselessly about minister to the priest. Gertrude's great moment

come after the sermon when, the churchwarden and sidesman move

lugubriously from pew to pew, the acolyte come down to the altar step

and stand with a large brass plate in his hand wait for the

offertory. he stand there proudly with his pale face upturned, his

whole soul seem to be bear aloft on the hymn sing by the

congregation. on this occasion it be:



    _o God our help in age past,

       our hope for year to come;

     our shelter from the stormy blast,

       and our eternal home._



Minna have come in a mischievous spirit, but even she be impressed.

there be a soulfulness in the young man, the look of one hopelessly

atone for all the sin of the world, and, above all, there be

artistry in his movement. everything that he do seem to be

immensely important and pregnant with meaning. when he stoop and the

churchwarden and sidesman lay their little bag in the great brass

plate, he do it with the air of one accept a worthless gift for

the grace of the giving. to he at least it do seem to be true that

it be more blessed to give than to receive. his humility be so great,

so move, that Minna wish she have put sixpence into the bag instead

of a penny.



she could not see Gertrude's face, but she be familiar enough with

she back to be able to gauge her feeling. Gertrude have rather a poor

figure, with high shoulder and a very short waist. now her shoulder

be high than ever, and she be lean forward and her elbow be

tremble ever so slightly. Minna smile and think maliciously of

all she know about Gertrude, and that be not a little.



before the service be over she leave the church, and be lie in the

study with a wet handkerchief over her head and a volume of Tennyson

on her knee when the rest of the family come home from St. Paul's and

Gertrude from St. Saviour's.



"where do you go to, my dear?" ask Mrs. Folyat.



"oh! to St. Benedict's," reply Gertrude. "they have the most lovely

altar-cloth I ever see. but the curate intone very badly."



"as badly as pa?" ask Minna.



"that's impossible," say Francis with a long chuckle.



there be some chatter, circulation of gossip get at the church door,

and then with some anxiety Mrs. Folyat look across the long table at

her husband and say:



"be you go to tell they, Frank?"



Francis have his mouth full and could only say "Hum! ha!"



"what be it?" Frederic turn a little pale and wonder what be

come. his misdeed, take collectively, be very trivial, but he

know from experience that any one of they take singly, rob of its

context and place under the scrutiny of other eye, would assume

gigantic proportion.



"have all the Folkestone Folyats die and leave we all their money? or

have uncle William come back from India with a gigantic fortune?" Minna

be rush wildly ahead on all the strange possibility when

Francis finish his mouthful and clear his throat.



"no," he say. "I have hear from your brother Serge."



"Serge!" say Mary.



"Serge!" say Gertrude, snatch from her tender dream.



"be he rich?" ask Minna.



"I don't know. he talk of come home."



"where be he?" this come from Frederic.



"he write from Durban in South Africa."



"oh! then of course he's a millionaire. Hurrah! he'll buy Frederic a

partnership, and I a husband--catch I marry a poor man--and Mary

a genuine Strad, and Gertie a--an acolyte."



Gertrude flush hotly and look dagger across the table.



"he merely write that he be come home, as though he have only be

away a week."



"some of you child can hardly remember he," say Martha.



Minna say she could just recollect his put she into a bassinette

and let she go fly down a hill into a pond.



Francis go on:



"he send your mother some water-colour drawing."



"any good?" ask Frederic.



"I think they're quite good. but I don't know anything about these

thing."



"I'll take they to old Lawrie. he'll know," say Frederic.



"Lawrie?" murmur Gertrude.



"yes. D'you know he?"



"no. no."



Minna wink at Frederic. he have often talk to she about old Lawrie,

and she have discover that the name of the young acolyte at St.

Saviour's be Bennett Lawrie, old James' third son.



"I say," say Frederic, "do Serge know we're here?"



"no. the letter be forward from St. Withans."



"don't you think you ought to let he know what he's in for?"



"I cann't do that. he give no address."



"it willn't matter to he if he's rich," say Minna, and they all fall

to and rummage their memory for recollection of Serge as a boy.

Minna invent lavishly and suddenly she shout:



"do he say whether he'd get a wife?"



"I bet it's a blackamoor like old Nicholas Folyat," say Frederic.



"even if she be black," say Mrs. Folyat solemnly, "if he be marry

to she she will be my daughter-in-law and I shall receive she."



the conversation take on a broad complexion which be more permissible

in the family circle than in the print page.



that evening Frederic take Serge's drawing with he and seek out

old Lawrie in the Arts Club, where always on a Sunday evening there

be a gathering of old warrior and choice spirit--Joshua Yeo, Elihu

Beecroft, the painter, Peter Maitland, who write pantomime, and

Warlock Clynes, the photographer, and B. J. Strutt, the manager of the

old theatre, where, as a young man, Henry Irving have be a member of

the stock company. they be smoke and drinking and yarning. they

have vast store of anecdote of the great Bohemians in London.

Beecroft have twice have picture in the Academy, and B. J. Strutt have

begin life as a call-boy at the Haymarket Theatre. Old James Lawrie

have be to London three time and have shake hand with J. L. Toole

and Helen Faucit, and Clement Scott have send he a copy of his

ballad, of which he have produce many gross parody.



the club be simply three room in a dark block of office--a bar, an

eat room, and a smoke-room. Frederic be show in by the grubby boy

whom he find at the door read a penny "blood," and he stand

foolishly in the middle of the room realise dreadfully that old

Lawrie do not remember who he be.



"Mr. Lawrie. . ." he say.



"eh?"



"I--I--my name's Folyat. I--I act. you ask I to--to look you up

here some day."



"eh? oh, yes. come and sit down. what'll you have? I cann't pay for

your drink, but some one will."



Frederic sit down, and the little group of old man be embarrass by

his presence.



"so. . . so you act, do you? here's B. J. Strutt. get he to give you

a job in his next pantomime."



"I'm--I'm not a pro," say Frederic. "I'm a solicitor." and, as he

say it, he feel that it be a small thing to be among these free man

who practise the art. Frederic be a chameleon who take his colour

from his surrounding. he have a queer capacity for enthusiasm, which

come and go and be altogether beyond his control. he drink a little

whiskey and he feel that he be in the company of very wonderful

being. they talk of thing and man that be glorious dream to

he, and they speak of they with such ease and familiarity, like

giant play marble with the mountain. his own little celebrity,

which have be very dear to he, dwindle into nothing, and it be to

protect himself that he produce Serge's drawing and begin to talk of

his brother.



Beecroft take the drawing and look they through. he have a huge red

beard and a glisten bald head and round spectacle that make he

look like a benevolent spider. he clap his hand to his bald pink

head and with immense fervour say:



"by God!"



"be they good?" rasp Lawrie.



"no. damn bad."



Frederic feel very small.



"I don't know," say B. J. Strutt. "I like that one all yellow in the

foreground and blue in the distance. and I like that one with the

nigger file through the orange-tree with the pinky-white house

beyond."



"so do I," cry Beecroft. "I like 'em all. the man cann't draw, but he

can feel colour and big distance and lot and lot of air."



Frederic begin to feel well. the old man gather round the drawing

and give grunt of satisfaction. (they have be very bored all the

evening and be glad of something to interest they).



"where be he?" say Beecroft. "this brother of your."



"in Africa. he's come home."



"he's a genius. do you know that? a genius."



"be careful, Beecroft," put in Lawrie. "there have be about twenty

man of genius--real genius--in forty, or be it sixty?--thousand

year."



"a genius," reiterate Beecroft. "we'll give a show. you can ram he

down their throat week by week, Yeo."



"it's no good run a genius in this place," growl Lawrie.--He be

always discover poet and see they go to ruin.--"They don't want

genius. they're so used to imitation."



"at any rate," protest Beecroft, "they haven't have anything like

this for year, and I don't think we ought to let 'em off."



"my brother's come home now. I'll tell he what you say. it's

rather funny. I haven't see he since we be boy, and then he be

much old than I."



"we'll have a show," repeat Beecroft. "he's a local man?"



"we ben't bear here. but my father's be rector of St. Paul's, Bide

Street, for some year now."



"that's good enough. the stink rotter here like to think they've

have a hand in anything produce in the place--if people talk about it

enough. have some more whiskey?"



Frederic be beyond say "no." the drink go to his head, inflate

he, and he offer to sing. Strutt play his accompaniment, and

they keep he at it for an hour until he be hoarse, and they shout

the chorus in crack, beery voice.



it be very late when Frederic leave the club, after shake hand all

round and promise tearfully to bring his brother, the genius, as

soon as he arrive. he forget the drawing altogether, and old Lawrie,

be the sober of the party, gather they together and take they

home with he.







viii



serge



_it's a queer place, and indeed I don't know the place that ben't._

     the ARAN ISLANDS.



SERGE FOLYAT land at Plymouth on a wet autumn day and walk to St.

Withans rejoice and declare in his heart that England be the most

beautiful country in the world. he see no reason to alter his opinion

as he walk north until he come to the outcrop of industrialism in

the plain of Cheshire.



it take he ten day to walk from St. Withans to our town, and six of

they be wet. he love the soft english rain and the rich green of

the countryside and the glorious gold and red of the sodden autumn

leave, and when, for the first time, he see the black and grey of

South Lancashire sky and the dark chimney rise out of the dense

mass of building he could be glad of they too. all the same he

wonder what strange whimsey could have take his father out of the

soft southern air into such menacing harshness. however, it do not

greatly exercise his mind. he have never trouble to find reason for

his own folly and accept those of other with as good a grace.



he be a man a little above middle height, tan and brown, with

bright blue eye like his father's, and a close clip golden-brown

beard, turn grey at the corner of the jawbone. he love talk,

and engage all whom he meet or catch up on the road, and nearly

always leave they more cheerful than when he encounter they. when he

be alone he talk or sing to himself in a very loud voice. he be

not look for adventure and meet with none. his clothe he carry in

a sack on his back, and he have a great stick in his hand, a pocketful

of tobacco, and a calabash pipe. other possession he have none. he

walk very fast and sometimes cover forty mile in a day.



in the evening of the tenth day he enter the town by the Derby Road

and follow his nose and the tramline until he come to St. Thomas'

Church. there he ask for Fern Square and receive no response. he

stop a dismal-look man and ask he. the stranger gape at he

and say:



"A'm a stranger 'ere, my sen."



his next enquiry provoke a long answer in a language so uncouth that

he could make nothing of it. he follow the tram-line again and

wander vaguely until he come to a cross-road from which he could see

the Collegiate Church stand velvety-black against a sooty sky, with

a railway bridge and the dome of a station beyond. he see a tram

label Pendle and follow it. the road lead he under the railway

bridge, past a sequester market and a sort of fair with booth and

swing-boat and a cocoa-nut shy and a merry-go-round. he stop and

watch the dirty mournful-look people take their pleasure, and

the sight rather depress he. a little far on he have to pass

through the place in which these people live, and under the

factory where they work. he like the hum and business of it all,

and he like the slatternly grubby little shop.



there be a place where the road skirt a height, and from the road a

public park stretch down to the oily-black river wind through

flat. beyond the river gleam the reservoir of the mill, steam

under the humid air. beyond they again be hill cover with house,

and away to right and leave a forest of tall chimney. over all hang a

pall of mist and smoke, a rail edge the road, and here Serge stand

and gaze at the queer degrade beauty of it all. there be hardly a

blade of grass in the park, none at all on the flat by the river.

tree and plant be stunt. down in the park, on the bench,

saunter down the path, hide behind the bush, he could see

lover, and that comfort he.



he move on singing to himself and swinge his stick, and presently

he come to a wide place over against a washing-machine factory. the

road here be finely broad, but it be flank on either side by mean

little house and forlorn little shop. it make the slow ascent of a

long hill, and although there be plenty of traffic--tram, cab,

dray, lorry--it look empty and desolate. there be not a tree in

sight.



Serge stop a man with a sandy moustache and a complexion like a

suet-pudding and ask his direction to Fern Square.



"I'll take you there," say the man. "what number?"



"five," answer Serge.



"Mr. Folyat's." Serge nod.



"he's a good man be Mr. Folyat, and that kind to the poor, and they

don't need to go to his church neither. he and the roman priest,

Father Soledano, they do a lot of good, and there's a deal of good

need do, there be. he give I a job when I come out o' prison."



"oh! you've be in prison?"



"a month ago, I come out."



"what for?"



"'spicious character. the p'leece put a jemmy in my carpenter's bag

and find it there. Mr. Folyat 'e speak for my repu-character, but you

cann't say nothing agin the p'leece. there it be, and I 'ad to do my

six month. here we be. you look like a sea-fare man."



"good-night," say Serge.



"good-night." and the man shamble off.



Serge stand gaze at the door and then he turn and look over the

square at the Wesleyan Chapel. a factory hooter buzz. from the

inside of the house come the wailing of a violin.



Serge knock at the door Minna open it and stand peer out at

he.



"Hullo!" say Serge. "which be you? Mary?"



"my father ben't in," reply Minna.



"all the well. you don't remember I, and I've be think of you

as a baby. I'm serge."



"Serge!"



he step in. Minna rush away, and he hear she call all over

the house:



"Serge have come! Serge have come!"



there be a pattering of foot on the stair, a banging of door, and

presently Gertrude, Mary, Mrs. Folyat and Minna come down upon he. he

catch his mother up in a great hug and squeeze the breath out of

she, and she stand talk and cry while he kiss Gertrude and

Mary on the cheek and Minna full on the lip.



Mrs. Folyat lead the way to the dining-room, and he sit at the table

and they round he, and they devour he with their eye. he look

from one to the other. he think that Gertrude look sly, Mary

plain, but he like the mischief in Minna's eye, and she have a wide

friendly grin and a dimple in her cheek. his mother be so much old

than he have imagine she and he want to tease she out of it. she be

wear a white woollen shawl, and she have shove her spectacle up on

to her forehead when Minna break in upon her reading. the room be

dark and rather oppressive and none of the window be open.



Minna light the gas and pull down the blind.



"well!" say Mrs. Folyat, "you _have_ take we by surprise."



"I mean to," reply Serge. "I go to St. Withans first. I don't

know you'd go. I walk on here."



"walk!" this come from Mary.



"yes. it's a nice cheerful hole you've come to live in."



"horrible!" say Minna.



"dreadful!" cap Mrs. Folyat. "but your father would come. he say

his place be among the poor."



Gertrude and Mary exchange glance, but say nothing. Serge notice

it and try another topic.



"how's all the child? how many be there?"



"oh! my dear." Mrs. Folyat feel for her handkerchief.



Minna answer for she.



"Annette's away. she's a governess with some rich vulgar people. James

be dead. he fall from the roof. Frederic be out. he always be. we're

in. we always be. and that's the lot."



"what about Leedham?"



"I forget Leedham. he's in Rio, in a bank. be you rich, Serge?"



Serge feel in his trouser pocket and produce four sovereign, three

shilling and ten copper. he lay they out neatly on the red baize

table-cloth.



"that's all," he say.



Minna laugh and count out the money. Gertrude and Mary and his

mother look at Serge in dismay.



"I don't know," say Serge. "if this place ben't full of money,

there's no excuse for it."



"it's a queer place," say Minna, "and not so much money in it as all

that. what you've get would be wealth to most of father's people."



"your father," put in Mrs. Folyat, "say his place be among the poor.

I'm sure he get what he want."



Serge feel that she be fish for his opinion. he give it.



"I meet a man," he say, "who bring I to the door. he say my father

be very good to the poor. he be a wretched devil who have just be

let out of prison."



"Sam Dimsdale. that's his name." Minna heap Serge's money up into

little pile.



"how's Frederic?" ask Serge.



"Frederic's a solicitor," reply Mrs. Folyat with a little show of

pride.



conversation flag until Mrs. Folyat ask Gertrude and Mary to get

the supper, and then Serge insist on help and ask if he might

cook an omelette. Mrs. Folyat bid he stay with she.



he sit opposite she and she fix her spectacle and look long at

he. then she say:



"you're like your father, but there's a look in you too of my mother.

what be you go to do?"



"do? I don't know. I've spend all my life try thing and leave

they before they leave I."



"it be a terrible blow to we, your leave the Navy like that."



"be it? it's so long ago now, but I be rather surprised at it

myself. I be sick of the water and pretend to defend England's

shore when nobody seem to want to attack they."



"but you be only a boy."



"I sometimes think I shall never be anything else. I cann't stand the

thing man do. they waste such a lot of time over they."



"but you must work."



"I suppose so. but I shall have to ask you to feed I for a little."



"oh! your father willn't say no to you. he never say no to any one."



"there's consistency in that."



"your father be not the man he be. we have have terrible time, my

dear. too dreadful. the people in this town."



"why don't the girl get marry?" ask Serge.



"my dear," answer his mother, "there be so few man whom one would

like to see they marry."



Mary and Gertrude return, and just then Francis come in. Serge go

up to he and kiss he, and Francis say "God bless my soul." when

he realise who it be he shake his son warmly by the hand and go on

say: "I'm glad to see you, glad to see you, glad to see you." and

he chuckle inside he and make Serge sit down, and stand look at

he, take he in, and go on:



"something like a prodigal son, eh, Martha? only the queer thing be

that I feel it be I who ought to say 'I be no more worthy to be call

thy father!'"



Martha protest, and they sit down to supper.



Francis sit absolutely silent at the head of the table and Martha

prattle and tell Serge all the family news, all the death, and all

the content of all the will, especially those by which neither she

nor Francis have benefit, and how Willie Folyat have win his case and

become Earl of Leedham, and how Minna have be practically engaged to

he once and might have be a countess but for her folly.



"I couldn't have bear Willie for a husband. he be so mushy," say

Minna.



"you might have leave he and get a handsome settlement," suggest

Serge.



"oh, no! the title carry very little money."



"left he! serge!" Mrs. Folyat apostrophise he.



Minna wink at Serge and say:



"you're not married, I suppose?"



"no."



they eat cold ham and pickle and Gruyère cheese and captain'

biscuit. Francis drink toast and water and Serge dispose of two

bottle of beer. he look round at the family portrait and drank

their health.



"I wonder," he say, "how they like see we here?"



"I often sit with they," reply Francis, break his silence, "and I

fancy they be snob and like be in a place where they can feel

themselves immeasurably superior."



"some of they," remark Mrs. Folyat, "be worth at least a hundred

pound."



"I find myself rather like this place as I walk here," say

Serge. "but I find myself wonder what happen to all the

suppress vitality of the people in it. how many people be there?

there must be half a million. what do they all do? their work cann't be

very satisfying. do they produce child at an appalling rate? or be

there any artistic outlet? there cann't be, or it wouldn't be so ugly.

I suppose there's a lot of crime and a lot of mess. I must have a look

at it. do they have frightful disease, and ben't it rather a mockery

spread the Gospel of Christ in such a place?"



"Serge!" Mrs. Folyat be unable to follow what he say, but she be

hurt at the mention of one whom she have always regard as her Saviour

at the supper-table.



"have I shock you, mother? I'm sorry," say Serge. "you're all so

different from what I have be think you for year and year and I

find it difficult to say anything. you're not exactly full of news

about yourselves, and my thought run away with I. that's bad."



"you haven't become an infidel I hope." Mrs. Folyat be rather

querulous. "you go to church in Africa?"



"I be lie reader to the Bishop of Bloemfontein for six month."



"ah!"



that reassure Mrs. Folyat, and she turn to her food again. she

enjoy eat, and take very small mouthful and nibble at they in a

most genteel fashion. Francis on the other hand eat hurriedly in large

gulp and have always finish his plateful before everybody else.

Serge suddenly find their method of eat intensely interesting. he

too love eating--he have revel in English cooking after his year

in Africa--and it be pleasant to find that he have something in common

with his father and mother, though, instinctively, he know that he

must not talk about it.



Francis rise from the table and take up pipe and tobacco. Serge

produce his calabash and fill it.



"you don't smoke cigarette?" ask Francis.



"no."



"Frederic do. beastly habit."



Mary and Minna clear away the thing from the table and Gertrude

disappear upstairs. Francis sit by the fireplace and say nothing.

Mrs. Folyat remain in her chair at the end of the table and say

nothing either. Serge blow ring and cloud of smoke into the air and

stretch his leg. outside it have begin to rain, and the water

gurgle in the gutter.



"how long have you be here?" ask Serge.



for a long time it seem that he be to receive no answer, but then

Mrs. Folyat in a ventriloquial voice, without the small expression

in her face and without turn her head say to her husband:



"how many year have we be here, Frank?"



"a good many. nine, ten--more."



"it seem more than that."



again there be a silence, and Serge glare at the gas-jet until black

spot swam in front of his eye. a gust of indignation sweep through

he, and he bring his fist down on the table with a bang.



"look here!" he almost shout, "this ben't good enough! ben't you

glad to see I? I've come home to you after nearly twenty year, and

here you be as silent and gloom-stricken as though I'd rise and

confront you from the grave. . . . do you remember how I blubber

when I leave you at the rectory gate at St. Withans? a boy's grief be a

little thing, but it's keep you warm in my thought all these year.

. . ."



he stop. he see that his mother be mop at her eye and her

hand be fumble at the tablecloth, and she seem very old and

pitiful to he then, and he know that he must not hurt she. his father

seem not to have hear he and go on suck at his pipe, which he

smoke with great skill so that the blue smoke only come from the bowl

and his mouth at long interval. he look all beard and spectacle,

impersonal and unexpressive, sit there by the fireplace, and yet

there be humour in his very bulk. Serge feel that he have make an

error in tactic, a blunder in manner. these people, his father and

mother, be not to be take by assault. they have rampart and

bulwark against all comer, perhaps against each other, and their

inmost life be not to be lay bare for the first clamorous

belligerent. he realise that his mother's tear be defensive

weapon--a shower of greek fire and boiling pitch. they be very

effective, and they drive Serge back blistered and wound, but also

they rouse the devil of obstinacy in he and make he resolve to

stay in the queer dark house so full of shadow and to fight with all

his might against its oppressive atmosphere, and to win his way

through to the heart of the old woman, his mother, and the bulky,

silent, bearded man, his father.



he lean forward and take his mother's hand and fondle it. he

squeeze it like a lover, and give a funny little laugh deep down in

his chest.



"I'm sorry," he say.



she give his hand a convulsive, sudden little pressure, and he begin

to talk about himself and his adventure. how he have wander in

America and work his way across to Cape Town and go up-country

hunt elephant; and how he have fight in the Zulu War and teach

dutch girl English on a boer farm, do anything and

everything--prospect for gold, diamond; cheat and be cheat;

thrash and be thrash; and as he tell the smoke come fast from

his father's bowl and pipe, and at last he tell how he have take to

paint picture for a living, because he be starve in Kimberley,

and how he make enough money to pay his passage home, and come because

he want to see green England again and the people with whom he have

be happy as a boy.



"do you get the picture I send you?"



"oh, yes," say Francis. "I think some of they very good."



"I'm glad of that. I don't know much about it, but they say out there

the colour be fine. one or two be send to London and a picture

swell there write to I about they."



Mrs. Folyat say she think she would like to finish her book before

she go to bed, gather her shawl about her shoulder, tell they not

to be late, give serge her cheek to kiss, and wander from the room.

Serge open the door and close it after she.



Francis lay down his pipe. he grunt once or twice and then lean

forward in his chair and say:



"I don't think I realise before that my child be grow man and

woman. it make a difference. one lose the right to interfere, if one

ever have any. what be you go to do?"



"if possible I shall become a painter. if it's impossible--there be

plenty of other thing to do."



"and where will you live?"



"here. I've get four pound and a few shilling and the clothe I

stand up in and my drawing."



"I'm not a rich man."



"you haven't pay out a penny for I since I be fifteen."



"that's true."



"give I a couple of year' board and lodging and a hundred a year and

I'll pay you back every penny as soon as I can."



"I cann't give you a hundred a year."



"how much have Frederic have?"



"Frederic? a good deal. . . . More than I could afford. your mother's

very fond of Frederic."



"shall I tell you what will happen if you don't take my offer? I shall

stay, and go on stay until you suddenly realise that I have be

here for year."



"how do you know that?" ask Francis, a little uneasily.



"the house be like that. I'm rather like that myself--sometimes. I

suppose it's in the blood. we get into false position--we're

intelligent enough to know that they're false, but we're not strong

enough to break away. ben't it so? it's call good-nature. don't

everybody call you a good-natured man? they do I. a damned good sort

they call I--man I hate too--but it only mean that I'm easy and

don't make situation painful by demand a clear issue."



"ben't that what you're do now?"



"only because we're both good-natured man and there willn't be any issue

at all if I don't. I've come home. I'm interested. thing be go to

happen in the house, and I want to be in at the fun. I may be useful."



"what sort of thing?"



"I don't know. who do? what matter be that they should happen.

. . ."



Francis begin to chuckle, and Serge throw back his head and laugh,

though there be nothing particular to laugh at, and yet it be very

strange to he to be sit opposite a man and try to get at he

and salute his soul, and that man his father. their conversation

seem to he like two cogged wheel in a machine miss their clutch

and whiz round separately. they go on talk, but finally

admit the futility of it, exchange tobacco and sit in silence,

enjoy it and each other. Francis find company in his eld son,

and it be very pleasant just to sit and look at he, he be so strong

and clean and healthy.



Frederic come in very late and find they sit there and the room

full of smoke. Serge rise and take his thin nervous hand in his great

paw and say:



"hello! Frederic. I'm serge."



"how be you?" return Frederic. "go to stay long?"



"about two year."



"the devil you be. I've just be talk to some man about you. I

show they your drawing. one man say you're a genius. what do it

feel like?"



"be a genius? I don't know. but I imagine it's like be an

ordinary person--only more so. you look rather wash out."



"oh! I've be work hard. I'm tired."



"you're very late," say Francis.



"yes. I don't think you'd be sit up. all the woman in bed? where

be you to sleep?"



"I don't know."



"where be he to sleep, pa?"



"I don't know. I cann't wake your mother up, and the girl will be

asleep."



Serge laugh.



"I don't see anything to laugh at," mutter Frederic. "I don't want

you in my bed."



"I'll sleep on the sofa," say Serge. "I be laugh because it be

so like the Folyats."



Francis take a book in his hand and roll out of the room. Serge

remove his collar and cover his foot with his coat and lie down on

the sofa. Frederic stand tug at his little golden-red moustache

and look down at he.



"good-night," he mutter, and go away.



"God!" say Serge. "what a weak chin he have."

     ant, BEES and WASPS.



FREDERIC have omit to make any mention of the fact that he have lose

his brother's drawing, and have brush the thought of they aside. he

have a comfortable memory and a convenient conscience which never

worried about his lapse and misdemeanour until they be know or in

danger of be know to other people. then he live in dread of the

application of official morality and indulge in a perfect orgy of

self-torment, grind himself between the upper and nether millstone

of his own laxity and the rigid code with which his upbringing have

imbue he. he have have a qualm or two about Serge's drawing, but it

be not until his brother appear on the scene that he begin to think

their loss might be serious--that be, fraught with unpleasant

consequence to himself. he be essentially amiable. Disagreement hurt

he, and he would go to any length to avoid an unprofitable quarrel.

on the other hand if a squabble seem to lead to immediate gain he

would rush at it head down.



in the morning he be out and away while Serge be still in the

bathroom splash and roar at the top of his voice. he spend the

morning in his office write letter to Beecroft and Strutt tell

they that his brother have come home with a stock of drawing well

even than those he have show they, and letter from London man about

they. he have no clear purpose in do this, but be fill with a

vague notion that if the first drawing be irreparably lose he be

make some amend.



in the lunch interval he go round to the Arts Club and ask the

off with something under his arm that night. yes; it be a big, square

thing, because he have put Mr. Lawrie into a cab and it fall on to the

floor, and he pick it up and lay it on Mr. Lawrie's knee.



Frederic give the boy a penny, get Mr. Lawrie's address, and, as soon

of a terrace of four stucco house with Gothic window. it stand at

the corner, and a little bye-street lead down one side of it to a slum.

it have a little raise lawn, two laurel tree and a privet hedge in

front of it, and a wide asphalt path lead up to the front door, which

lie far back in a huge gloomy porch. the window look out on to

another row of stucco house with a shop at the corner which for the

time be be a laundry. opposite the laundry be a public-house. two

street meet a few yard along the road, and in the cleft of they be a

large red-brick house with its garden gate gleam with brass plate.

here live Dr. Haslam, the father of the spotty-face youth.



Frederic give a long tug at the bell and stand look stupidly at the

door, the low panel of which be scratch and dent with heavy

kick. a large tabby cat come and rub herself against his leg.



presently the latch be draw and the door be open about six

inch, and in the aperture there appear a long bony face,

incredibly line and wrinkle, and in it two burn-sorrowful eye.

the mouth of this face open, and out of it come a toneless mournful

voice say:



"what be it?"



"be Mr. Lawrie in?" ask Frederic.



"he be. but he's busy. be ye from the office? we'll be ready in ten

minute."



"I want to see Mr. Lawrie most particularly on a private matter."



"ye cannot see he."



"I must."



"what name?"



"Folyat. Mr. Frederic Folyat."



"I'll see."



the door be close to and Frederic, leave with the cat, stand try

be an awkward moment until he say:



"have you rung?"



"yes."



"she's a bit slow. she's get rheumatism in her foot. I know you, but

you don't know I. I've see you at your father's church. my name's

Bennett Lawrie. I'm in business. it's beastly."



"do you often go to our church?"



"I go to all the High church, when I can get away. I want to be a

clergyman, but I suppose I never shall be now."



"you'd well come and see we. we have supper on Sundays for anybody

who like to come."



"I'd just love to know your father."



"I want to see _your_ father."



"oh! _my_ father!"



the boy shy away on that, and again the door be open six inch.
