"will ye come with I now?" she say.



Frederic follow she down a long gloomy passage and into a large

strong coffee by his side. his brow be draw tight over his eye,

"the figure--give I the figure!"



the old servant take up a newspaper and read out a series of figure

"I'll be late if I don't take a cab," she say.

"now we'll have some poetry."



and he read half a canto of Spenser's "Faërie Queene" in a big

Frederic could make little sense of it and sit take in the room, the

when the reading be finish Frederic say:



"I'm sorry I interrupt you."



"not at all. I'd finish my work. I'm alone. I'm always alone in this

house. have you ever think how lonely a man can be in his own house?

. . . you've not? you don't think? may be you're wise, though, maybe

again, you're only young. well, I tell you, a man can be very lonely

in his own house, but not many be as lonely as I be. look at it

purely philosophically it's something of an achievement--in a negative

sort of way. I mean, not many man can have all that nature have

provide for a man and get nothing at all out of it. nearly all man

manage to get some picking for their vanity, but I don't even get

that. I get nothing. . . . so do hundred of thousand of people. they

cann't. but the difference between they and I be that they can pretend

and I cann't. nature's very wasteful. she produce far more man and

woman than she want. just a few be sound and really alive. the rest

be shadow. I'm a shadow. I don't know what you be. a sort of

betwixt and between I should say, just look at you. the real man

make loveliness, and the intelligent shadow have a sort of echo of it

and have a sort of reflect life through it. the rest wither away and

be dead year and year before they die. what I cann't stand though be

their damned cruelty. I cann't expect you to make much of that. I talk

like that for hour to old Tibby. she's the most real person in this

house, and the rest of we be stick to she like leech. she's have

no life of her own, haven't Tibby, and sometimes she will stand and

look at I and say, 'you're a wonderful talker, man,' and I'll say,

'I'm nothing else, woman.' queer people, we be. but we're all queer

in this place to go on live in the darkness and mist of it as we do.

I've meet your father. he's a comfortable man. it must be very pleasant

to have a comfortable man in the house."



Frederic do his good to follow he in his harangue, and, though he

could make very little of it all, he be interested and want he to

the boy Bennett come into the room, walk across to the book-shelf

by the window, take down a book and go out again without take the

the old man watch he with a sort of hunger, and he take a piece of

Frederic plunge into his own affair and say:



"do you remember some drawing I show you? my brother's come back. I

think he want they. the boy at the club . . ."



"I have they."



"you remember Mr. Beecroft say he be a genius."



"Beecroft's a sot and a fool. but they're good. thing see and feel.

I've give over ask for more than that."



he go to a cupboard and produce the portfolio. Frederic see it with

except for awful moment Frederic have always find life very pleasant

in the dark passage he meet Bennett wait for he. Bennett be very

Frederic feel irritated. the boy be so horribly in earnest. there be

the boy take his hand and press it violently. he have a cold, hard

he reach home to find Serge entertain a large party, include

"he's an acolyte at St. Saviour's. we've be to St. Saviour's once or

"my dear!"

"I'm prepared to like you all."



"you're quite right about this house, Serge. it be a prison. it's be

"how can I? I've no money; cann't make any--and I've no intention of

"Gertrude. she's in love with that young Bennett Lawrie. she go to

"oh! yes. she's a nagger."



Serge go up to Minna and put his arm round her waist and kiss she.

"what a pity you're my brother."



"I fancy a good many woman say that to their brother--when they don't

"have you know a lot of woman?"



"a good many."



"I think so. we seem to exist for you as individual. to the

"I hope so. but don't do anything dreadful just for the fun of it. it

ben't worth it."



Minna give a little purr of contentment and rub her cheek against

"you're so warm and friendly. I'm glad you come. but I can understand

people hate you like poison."



"they do, my dear, they do."



he kiss she again and she run happily away and upstairs.







x



SUNDAY supper



_he for God only._--MILTON.



PURITAN hostility to the proceeding in St. Paul's survive the demise

of Flynn and his newspaper and splutter into activity upon occasion,

as when Francis institute the Litany as a separate Sunday afternoon

service or allow his school-room to be use for musical

entertainment organise by the local nigger minstrels--(the annual

visit of Moore and Burgess give birth to numerous amateur troupes)--or

when in the jumble sale which be hold at interval he countenance

and even patronise raffle. one pretext be as good as another, but

the fundamental grievance be his friendship with Father Soledano, a

priest attach to the Roman Catholic Cathedral. the offence be

great in that he have no friend, hardly a kindly acquaintance among

the anglican clergy. they fight shy of he because he have incur

the disapproval of the dean, though their wife occasionally call on

Mrs. Folyat because she be still visit by the bishop's wife.



Father Soledano be an Irishman of spanish extraction, a little ugly

man, with a lame leg, stiff bristling hair recede from a knobby,

wrinkle forehead, little eye glow under bushy brow, a long upper

lip and a sensitive mouth, and a chin that look as though he have to

shave it every half hour.



the Roman Catholic Cathedral be about a mile away from St. Paul's,

and Father Soledano live in the priest's house in an asphalt court

that lie under its shadow. the surround district be inhabit

mostly by poor irish--ignorant, drunken, superstitious, and Jews,

whose morality be a reproach to the rest of the dweller in the

slum; and there be french people, and Bavarians, Lithuanians, not a

few Poles, and refugee from Russia. all these be sweep into the

various trade and manufactory--sweat tailor, sweat

shirt-making, sweat jam-making, sweat engineering. and Father

Soledano find his work of dig for their soul infinitely amusing.

several of his Catholics live over in the parish of St. Paul's, and

he have seek Francis out on hear a tale of his kindness to an old

woman who, by practise midwifery, support her daughter-in-law and

four child. this old woman be a Catholic, and every penny she

could spare be spend in buy image of saint. she have the Virgin,

and St. Peter, and St. Anthony of Padua, and a little Saint Catherine

of Siena. one day two of the man who have assault Frederic and

overturn the gravestone of the boy James, make a round of all the

catholic house they could discover and destroy all the image. when

Francis hear of it he go from house to house and be give a list

of all that have be destroy, and a week later he go round with

his chief sidesman carry a clothe-basket full of saint, and make

good every loss. when the aforesaid old woman find herself with a new

Virgin, a new Saint Peter, and a new Saint Anthony of Padua and a more

beautiful new Saint Catherine of Siena than she have ever think of in

her wild dream, she kneel down and begin to pray for the soul of

the English Father. and Francis kneel down and pray with she, and

all the child gather round and stare at their grandmother and

the portly bearded man kneeling there by the kitchen table, and their

mother begin to cry, and the old woman begin to cry, until Francis

lift she up and kiss she on the cheek. then he send the child

out to buy bread and jam and cake and they have a lovely tea together.



Francis do not tell the tale to anybody, but it be soon out all over

the parish, and there be much indignation. a few of the parishioner

leave the church and Mrs. Folyat be shock and affront. what

offend she most be that Francis have carry the image publicly and

openly through the street. be an inveterate gossip herself, she

could not endure be the subject of it, except it be flattering to

her vanity.



Father Soledano write to Francis and thank he, and Francis invite

he to come and see he. the invitation be accept, and the two man

find that they have many thing in common outside their profession,

and they have many a long talk about old Dublin day. Soledano be

amuse by the Anglican's easy-go optimism, and Francis be shocked,

interested, and stimulate by the priest's almost cynical pessimism.

they never discuss religion. to a certain extent they secretly

ally force in their work of deal with the moral and economic

difficulty of their poor, a certain substratum of whom be

ultimately catholic and anglican accord as they could win the

attention and sympathy of the district visitor or the Little Sisters,

though they hardly ever attend service either in the Cathedral or in

St. Paul's.



every now and then Soledano would come to supper at Fern Square on

Sundays, and it so happen that he be there when Bennett Lawrie

appear for the first time dress sprucely for the occasion with a

new suit and a very high collar and a blue bird-eye tie. the whole

family be present, having be to church in full force--Serge read

the Lessons--and they have sit down to their meal, having forget all

about young Lawrie, when there come a resounding peal at the bell. the

servant be out and Gertrude open the door to he. his face be

utterly tragic, and he could hardly find his voice to ask if Mrs.

Folyat be in. Gertrude admit he, show he into the

dining-room, where several people be talk all at once, and

disappear into the kitchen to fetch he plate, knife, and fork. it

be some moment before Frederic recognise he--two gas-jet in glass

globe do not give very much light--and Bennett suffer agony of

shyness and begin to wish he have never come. he see Francis open his

mouth and insert a large piece of cold beef and his beard wag as he

chew it slowly, and he rather resent it. he have romanticise

Francis, and have always picture he in his vestment very saintly and

impressive. the other man, Soledano, sit between Mrs. Folyat and

Minna, look much more like a saint. . . . Minna gaze at Bennett

with mischievous approval, and he think she very beautiful and cast

down his eye. Frederic say "Hullo" and tell his mother who Bennett

be, and Mrs. Folyat bade Serge and Mary make room for the young man

between they. Gertrude return with plate, knife and fork. Bennett

sit down at the place make for he, and conversation be resume and

flow on over he. it be chiefly concerned with food, and Mrs.

Folyat be very anxious to know what Father Soledano have to eat at the

priest' house. he tell she they eat very little meat and a great deal

of macaroni. Mary try to talk to Bennett but could get nothing out

of he but "yes" and "no." he like music but know nothing about it,

and have never be to a concert.



Serge tackle he. the directness of his question embarrass

Bennett, and the kindliness of his interest move he so that a lump

rise in his throat and he could hardly get out his reply. he have, he

say, be bear in the town and have hardly ever be away from it;

once to Scotland, where his father come from, and once to Westmoreland

and once to derbyshire. his family be so poor, you see, though they

have once be quite rich and live in a big house with a garden. he

could just remember the garden. he be nineteen and have be in

business since he be sixteen, first of all in a little office where

there be only one clerk, and then, by the influence of his uncle, in

a great firm of shipper where, if you do not earn very much, you

be at any rate safe. his mother be Low Church and his father be a

Presbyterian but never go to any place of worship. he have two

brother and two sister, but they be all old than himself and

don't care about the thing he care for, though one of his brother

sing in the choir at the Church of the Ascension, where they only wear

surplice and no cassock. . . . timidly he ask Serge if it be true

that he be an artist, and Serge laugh and say he be a sort of

middle-aged embryo.



"that must be splendid," say Bennett, wistfully. "I draw, but not

real thing, only dream and horrible grotesque. we start a family

paper once but the other wouldn't do anything, and I have to write it

all myself and draw all the picture, and they laugh at everything I

do, and I draw a picture of my mother be carry off by the devil

and they burn it. I write verse about the people in the office, but

they don't like they unless they're--you know--rather nasty. we cann't

smoke in our office and everybody take snuff. I think I'd like to

have be a clergyman."



he suddenly become conscious that Gertrude's eye be upon he and

that she be devour every word he say. he have recognise she as

the young woman who come so often to St. Saviour's, and he have think

about she a great deal. he have torture himself with the notion that

she might have come to see he, have even dream lofty romance in

which she figure as a mysterious lady of high degree who sweep he

off in a great carriage with two tremendous horse, and then have be

ashamed. it comfort he a little to know that she be the daughter

of the Rev. Francis Folyat, and that her attendance at St. Saviour's

could therefore only have be prompt by the high spiritual

motive. . . . all the same she be look at he exactly as she do

when he come down to the step of the nave and stand with the great

brass offertory-plate. he be wretchedly nervous, but he imagine the

Folyats to be a happy united family, and he bask in the warmth which

seem to pervade their house. he listen to their bantering

conversation and be very much afraid of they all except Serge.

Frederic seem to drink a vast quantity of beer, and he remember

story that he have hear of he in the office. like everybody else

who be interested in church matter, he be familiar with the fly

gossip concern the Folyats, and the ill-natured remark that be

current about the unmarried daughter. he think Minna more and more

beautiful, and Mary devote, and Gertrude--he could not disentangle

Gertrude from all the absurd thing he have think of she before he

know who she be.



Mrs. Folyat begin, as she always do in the presence of a newcomer, to

talk of the ancestor on the wall, and to tell the lurid story of

the Red Lady, who have know more than be ever write of the Monmouth

rebellion, and the Grey Lady who have such a violent temper, that,

lose it one day out drive with her husband in a high chariot, she

box his ear so that he lose his balance and fall out and break his

neck. she ramble on by way of Baron Folyat to Willie, now safely

establish as Earl of Leedham, and she declare, be thoroughly

warm to her subject, that fail heir male, and in the event of

the extirpation of two other branch of the family--and less likely

thing have happen--Serge would become heir, or his son, if he ever

have any.



Bennett be much impressed, as it be mean that he should be, and

begin to talk of his own ancestry. there be Lawries in Elgin as far

back as Robert the Bruce, and for hundred of year there have be

lawrie who be laird or in the ministry. Mrs. Folyat ask he who

his mother be, and Bennett reply:



"she be a Miss Smith. she marry my father when she be seventeen.

People don't seem able to marry so young nowadays."



"it be difficult, ben't it, Gertrude?" ask Minna.



the meal come to an end, and Francis ask Frederic to accompany he

to the study to discuss a theatrical entertainment that be in process

of organisation in aid of the restoration of the organ. Mary and Minna

clear away and Gertrude help her mother upstairs, carry she

spectacle, book and knitting-bag. Serge, Bennett, and Father Soledano

be leave in the dining-room. Serge and Bennett smoke and Father

Soledano begin to talk. Bennett be unused to drink beer. Serge have

ply he with it rather too generously in the frequent lapse in

their conversation, and the fume of it have go to his head so that

it feel very hot and large, while inside it his brain work with

unwonted swiftness and a hectic clarity. his cheek be flush and

they burn, but on the whole he find his new sensation very

pleasant, and there be a sort of splendour in be treat by these

grow man, an artist and a priest, as one of themselves. to Bennett

all artist be great artist--he be not his father's son for

nothing--and the priesthood be the noble and most exalt call

possible for man. he live from Sunday to Sunday. on Monday morning he

die and be bury in his office. on Saturday evening come a glorious

resurrection, and he rise to exalt height each Sunday morning when

he take the sacrament. he be an emotional creature and have no other

outlet.



he sit look from Serge to Father Soledano and from Father Soledano

to Serge as they talk, but take little account of what they say.

they be exchange impression of the town and speak of it in a

curious critical way that Bennett find difficulty in follow. he

know nothing of the machinery of the world. he be poor, and he have

accept it as axiomatic that poor people have to do work that be

distasteful to they. he have no notion of what that work result in,

or who profit by it. you go on work until you have enough to

marry, and then you marry and go on work until you die. his

brother be both bank-clerk, and he gather that their work be

even dull than his own, which consist in address envelope and

take message down into the warehouse where there be rough man who

be even poor than himself. they pack and unpacked bale of

cotton-good which be place on lorry and carry off to train,

which take they away to the sea and across the sea to Bombay and

Calcutta and Shanghai and Yokohama. there be many other process

go on in the office and warehouse, but that seem to be the

general principle--cotton come from America, be buy on the

Exchange, spin and weave in the mill near Oldham, bring to the

warehouse and dispatch--fully insure--through the complicated

machinery of the office. there be five partner in the firm and they

be all very rich. one of the employee, the head-clerk, have six

hundred a year, but he himself, Bennett, receive every week only

thirty shilling. many young man of his age be earn only half

that sum, and he be quite ready to admit, without thought or

examination, that he be worth no more to his employer. he do not

understand the machinery in which he play a part, do not want to

understand it, and do not find it sufficiently interesting. be

poor, he have to work, and the nature of the work be not his affair.

it absorb the great part of his life, but it be outside his work

that he be as really alive as he could be.



this visit to Fern Square be perhaps the great adventure of his

life. he have hear Father Soledano's voice drone on for some time,

and now he hear word that interest he.



"the middle class be begin to invest their saving in

industrial security. that be go to make thing bad than ever

for the poor, since it mean organised exploitation, dividend to be

pay as well as the profit of private enterprise. it seem to I that

man be invent machinery for make money and let they get out

of hand. a machine that no man can control or use for the purpose of

good be the most perilous engine of destruction."



"a machine," think Bennett, and at once there come to his mind the

street surround his office where all day long there be the thud

of machinery and thousand of man and woman be swallow up in the

morning by great black ugly building, and spew out again in the

evening, all, he suppose, as weary and listless as he feel himself on

every evening except Saturday.



"ben't that," say Serge, "ben't that what have happen with the

Church? . . . you don't mind my discuss it in that way? . . .

haven't it become a machine which take everything from man and give

they nothing? I fancy my father's Church don't meddle much, or, at

any rate, effectually, with politic, but yours be always struggle

after the temporal power which it have lose."



"that be because we believe that the Church and State should be one."



"I agree. and so they would be if the Church be really a Church, and

the State be really a state. I have never be to your church, but I

know that my father's be only an imitation, a fairly good imitation

and quite attractive, but it have nothing at all to do with religion as

I understand it."



"it depend what you mean by religion."



"I take it to mean the profound instinct in a human being, that

instinct of life which embrace and should direct all the rest."



"I agree, but it be impossible."



"why be it impossible?"



Bennett do not hear Father Soledano's reply. the dialogue have be to

he like the murmuring of mysterious voice in a dream, bear no

relation to his own actual experience. his own religion be so

axiomatic that the possibility of criticism, outside crude

condemnation, to which he be harden and accustom, have never

occur to he, and yet, now that it have happen, it be as

something remote, impious, but menacing and disturbing. that Father

Soledano should lend himself to such talk perturb he not at all,

for he have be bring up to believe that anything be possible for

the roman Catholic priesthood. he be conscious of resentment, and

tell himself that it be because these thing be be say in Mr.

Folyat's house. he be hurt, and childishly he wish to hand on the

pain to some one. he wait until Father Soledano's voice have die

down, and then he say, take no account of his word:



"it ben't for we to inquire into these thing. if we believe at all in

the authority and the divine origin of the Church we be bind by its

tradition and its--its dogma."



"I beg your pardon," reply Serge. "I forget that you be there. I

don't believe in the authority or the divine origin of the Church, and

I refuse to be bind by its tradition, which have always be, to say

the least of it, unhappy in its result, or its dogma, which seem to

I unsound and more or less contradictory of the spirit of the New

Testament."



"but--but . . ." Bennett pounce on Serge with an air of triumph,

brandish his point before prove it. "but what about moral?"



"that," say Serge, "be exactly where you and I part company. you

Christians have only evolve a morality which you apply to the affair

of other and not to your own. you have no standard of goodness--only

the wickedness of other people, a Pharisaic standard which would have

be repulsive to the man whom you choose to regard as your Founder.

my father's sermon, for instance--and they be like every parson's

sermon--begin by draw such a frightful picture of human wickedness

that when it be over his hearer feel that they be angel of goodness

in comparison. it's an old dodge, and I daresay Father Soledano make

use of it too."



"I do," say Father Soledano. "I do."



Bennett gape at he. he feel that he would burst into tear if this

go on any long, and indeed his eye be wet and his throat be so

dry that he could not speak.



"you like your religion?" ask Serge.



"it be my whole life." Bennett be surprise at the ferocity with

which he say this. he be stagger by Serge's answer:



"I be sorry for you. you will be badly hurt when life gobble you up

and give you other engrossing interest, which you will be

ill-equipped to tackle."



"come, come," say Father Soledano. "it be not fair. it be not fair to

come down from the general to the particular like that."



"I protest," answer Serge. "my whole indignation arise from the

unkindness and dishonesty of stuff young people, and ignorant

people, with generalisation."



"what else can you give they? they be not conscious of

individuality."



"I don't believe that, and even if it be so you ought to leave they

free to become conscious--if they can."



"the risk be too great."



"what risk?"



Bennett's mind have be move swiftly and partly by memory, partly by

intuition he come to this:



"People cann't do as they like."



Serge stand up suddenly and pace round the room.



"young idiot!" he say. "they can, and they do. ben't it your

experience, Father, that they do? the trouble be that with all these

foolish generalisation buzz in their head they be always do

the wrong thing, and do they in the wrong way, shuffle, and

sneak so as to hide away from the bogie you give they." he turn

to Bennett and ask: "have what you do and think on Sunday the

slight bearing on what you do and think on week-day?"



"it keep I from temptation," say Bennett so earnestly that there

be not the small hint of priggishness in he.



Serge take he by the arm and lift he clean out of his chair and

set he down with a jolt on to his foot.



"keep you from temptation, do it! How?--By run away from it."



Bennett be very angry. he raise his voice:



"if you have to live in my house you wouldn't talk like that. my

father's a drunken beast and my mother don't even try to understand

we. you'd believe in God if you live in our house. . ." he come to an

end suddenly. Serge pat he kindly on the shoulder.



"that's all right. that's all right. let's go upstairs and see what a

happy home be like, or perhaps you would prefer to go and talk to my

father and Father Soledano in the study."



"I'll go with you," say Bennett.



they go upstairs, and Father Soledano join Francis in the study.

in the drawing-room they find Frederic hold forth about the

performance in the school-room. the piece choose be _the Rose and the

Ring_, in a musical version.



Gertrude ask Bennett if he could sing. he reply that he could, and

Frederic graciously allow he the use of one of his song, "on, on,

my bark, dash through the foam." Bennett have a light baritone voice

with a curious harsh quality in the middle note, but he love singing

and really let himself go. when he have finish Mrs. Folyat look up

from her _Family Herald_ and say:



"very nice, very nice indeed. even Frederic do not sing it so well."



Frederic ask Bennett to take a part in _the Rose and the Ring_, and

he accept. Gertrude take he aside to show he his part, and Mary

produce her violin and play Mendelssohn's Songs without word for

half an hour, after which she produce a table and card and sit

play Bézique with her mother. (Mrs. Folyat declare that she could

not sleep without her game. no one else be allow to play card on

Sunday.)



Serge sit teasing Minna, and time fly.



there come a ring at the bell, and after a little interval a gaunt

figure in black stalk into the room, stand by the door, and say:



"Bennett, your mother say you're to come home."



Bennett rise to his foot at once, mutter good-bye, turn the colour

of a red peony and slunk out after the old Scotch servant.







XI



ART and drama



_each have an upper stream of thought

 that make all seem as it be not._

     PETER BELL the THIRD.



LAWRIE, Beecroft and Co. have not a monopoly in culture. our City

Fathers provide we with an art gallery, to which, with praiseworthy

regularity they add two Academy picture every year; the Town-hall

have be decorate by a Pre-raphaelite, and there be a whole network

of Free Libraries, all equip with thousand of book in a uniform

binding, and with the smell proper to Free Libraries. in the cold

weather they be always very full, in the hot weather they be

always very empty; but in the hot weather the accumulate smell of

the winter be distil and concentrate. for music we have two or

three series of concert during the winter month. they be chiefly

patronise by Germans and Jews, and the English brag about they. we

have a College of Music, and a School of Art in connection with the

municipal technical school. this institution be preside over by a

socialistic disciple of William Morris, who spend a great part of his

free time in design banner for Friendly Societies--Buffaloes, Free

Foresters, Hearts of Oak--and cartoon for Labour journal. it be

situate in a square which be typical of the town. in the centre of

it stand a huge ugly anglican church, and three side of it be

fill with a presbyterian chapel, a Wesleyan chapel, a baptist

chapel, a Secular hall, a Maternity Hospital and a Dental Hospital.

down a by-street be the headquarters of the Salvation Army, and down

another a large roman catholic church. quite near be the office in

which Bennett Lawrie work, and all round be slum, public-house,

brothel, a wedge of infamy between the work centre and the

outskirt. all round the anglican church in the centre of the square

run a wide pavement on which be wooden bench. here at night come

hundred of man, woman and boy who have no resting-place. they spend

half the night there until they be move on by the police, when they

go to a similar pavement with bench outside the Infirmary, meeting

half-way their comrade in misery who have be move on from that

place--a sort of general post. in the day-time the square be always

busy, for two main road meet in it, and tram-line from four

direction converge. near at hand be many cheap shop, and the

wife of the clerk come thither to make their daily purchase.



it be to this School of Art that Serge Folyat come as the result of

his exhibition, which be an almost unredeemed failure. Beecroft

bang the drum and old Lawrie blow the trumpet, but the local school

of artist be contemptuous, and declare that the new genius could

not draw. Serge quite agree. he sell ten of his picture, and go to

see the disciple of William Morris and arrange to attend eight

class a week, four in the afternoon and four in the evening.



he find the school very amusing, though at first his position be a

little difficult, for most of the student be very young and

inclined to look askance at a man with a beard turn grey and his

hair grow thin on the top of his head. the class be very cheap,

and he be able to pay for the first term himself and postpone

discussion as to future way and mean, reckon that in three

month' time his family would have digest and assimilate he, and

add he to the already large number of habit which make their

common existence tolerable. he work very hard both at home and at

the school, wrestle with the horrible difficulty of the human

body. he have an intuitive feeling that he would never be able to draw

hand, and he become very ingenious in conceal they.



the class at the school be mixed. there be a few serious

student of both sex, a great many who attend from the vanity of

talent, and some to whom study art be an occupation. a little

hunchback with a malicious intense face have be there for thirteen

year, and an old spinster of fifty-five have spend fifteen year

without ever pass an examination or take a single certificate.

she be extremely hopeful, and one of the most cheerful person in the

school. on the whole it be not cheerful. it lack spirit and

enthusiasm. many of the young man no doubt have a secret conviction

that they have a great destiny, but they be rather ashamed of it, and

only in rare moment of excitement do they dare to let it appear.

Theodore Benskin, the morrisian principal of the school, have be

enthusiastic at twenty-five, but he have stop there. however, he be

a good teacher of a mechanical sort. his business be to turn out

draughtsman rather than artist, and he succeed. Serge desire to

become a draughtsman, and he follow Benskin's direction, though all

the while he have a feeling of the grotesque in what he be do, and

be incline to think that a bushman's drawing on the wall of a cave

be of more value than all the finished study turn out under

benskinian rule. however, he be nettle by the failure of his

exhibition, and see that it be quite useless to take keen pleasure in

his work unless by the work he could communicate that pleasure to

other. he have no concise theory of art beyond a conviction that

unless it could create pleasure there be no excuse for it. as for

make money by it, there be a thousand easy way of do that,

way that leave more leisure and do not induce such profound

depression. it be all very well, he think, to gird, as do almost

everybody he meet, at the sordidness and grimness of the town in which

they live, but the most miserable of all the people in it be the

suppose artist, the man who frequent the Arts Club. they be all

man of talent, but none of they ever seem to have use their gift

to any purpose. they be perpetually curse the lack of appreciation

of their fellow-citizen, but they have never make any really serious

attempt to win they or to open up any new way for their mind. when it

come to the point their standard be those of the rich man, upon

whose caprice they live. like everybody else in the town they put up

with money as the sole channel of communication between one man and

another. Serge use sometimes to try to talk to the waif and stray

on the bench outside the church in the square, but he find they

nearly all brutalise and fuddle. they seem to have no thought

beyond the next meal, no programme beyond the next drink. they cadge.



among the student at the school be a young man whom Serge have mark

out from the very first moment. he be short, and have a large head,

dark hair, bright eye, and he be always merry. he have a joke for

everyone, and he be always in love with one or other of the

girl-student. Benskin be proud of he, for he win all possible

prize and be always solidly work. his name be Basil Haslam,

brother of that spotty-face youth who be Frederic's boon companion.

they make acquaintance quickly but do not become friend until they

both enter for a competition for a prize, the subject be a

sea-piece. Haslam win it, and protest with Benskin that Folyat's be

the good, because Folyat know about the sea and he don't.



he be delighted when Serge tell he that he have be a sailor.



"ah! that's it," he say. "that's it. I've never be anything. I can

just draw but I don't understand about man and how they live."



"that's not very difficult," reply Serge. "they be much the same

everywhere. they be all bear in the same way, and death have not many

variation. what lie in between be largely a matter of eat,

drinking, and sleep."



"and loving."



"just a few get as far as that. not many."



"but all of they seem to think about getting marry."



"that have surprisingly little to do with love. how much love do you

get in your own house?"



"not much. but then they think I'm queer. my father's a doctor. he

want I to be a doctor, but I've get a hundred-and-fifty of my own,

so I can do what I like. I shall go to London as soon as I'm through

here. it's no good be a painter here. they all think it's a joke, a

sort of excuse for do nothing."



"I know. they think picture be produce automatically--like

everything else."



"Old Benskin's automatic enough."



"exactly. he can work just as he can go to sleep, almost without

know that he's do it. it's a matter of habit. he's almost

forget how he use to despise that sort of thing."



"do you think he ever do?"



"of course, or his work wouldn't be as good as it be."



"I cann't understand people cease to be keen."



"I can. you only need to wobble a very little to come down on the

wrong side. then you're do for--in Hell. and after a bit you find

that you quite like it, except in awful moment when you realise that

after all it be Hell and that you might so easily have be in

Heaven."



"I know what you mean. you mean that the whole thing rest with

yourself. but it's rotten luck when you're weak and cann't help do

the wrong thing though you see the right thing the whole time."



"but we're all like that. we only go to Hell when we do the wrong

thing and pretend that it's the right."



"how do you find that out?"



"by a careful study of Hell and its inhabitant."



"then you don't mean the Hell one's people talk about?"



"no. I mean here and now, the world as it be. I'm not interested in

any other."



"neither be I. Hurray!"



this conversation be the first of many. Haslam use to wait for Serge

and walk with he as far as their road lay together. he be an

ambitious young man with his eye set on the road to London, not so

much because he be eager for fame and material reward as because he

be hotly impatient of art which stop short at Benskin and

Beecroft.



"but," Serge would say, "Benskin and Beecroft will both die."



"I know, but there'll be a new Beecroft and a new Benskin by that

time."



"that's true. we shall never be rid of they."



"I expect London be cram full of Benskins and Beecrofts."



"maybe, but there be more of the other sort there too."



"if I don't reach London by the time I'm twenty-seven I shall throw up

the sponge."



"why twenty-seven?" ask Serge, smile.



"oh! if a man haven't do something by the time he's twenty-seven he

never will."



"I'm a good deal more than that. . ."



"but you've do everything. you've make yourself. you're not really

any old than I be, and everybody here be so horribly old."



"yes, they all come to a bad and perfectly respectable end."



Haslam swing his fist in the air and shout indignantly:



"respectable! respectable! give I a list of any ten man live in

respectable suburban villa and I warrant you there'll be more

dishonesty and cowardly misdo in their life than in ten of the

so-call criminal class. I don't understand it. I do rotten thing

myself--who doesn't?--but I cann't shut my eye to they when they're

do. take my brother. he's a beastly idiot or an idiotic beast,

always get into scrape and shuffle out of they. by the time

he's thirty he'll still be do the same thing, but he'll have

learn how to prevent they come to the surface. he'll marry, settle

down, enjoy a comfortable income, be a pillar of the Church and a

smug, hard Pharisee like all the rest, with all his track carefully

cover up and his conscience have a splendid time go over they."



"I don't think it matter to any man," say Serge, "what his brother

be and be not."



"I know what you mean. it ben't worth while let out at brute like

my brother, but it's a great comfort to be able to do it

occasionally."



"Good Lord! my dear, we cann't do anything. we must all stew in our own

juice. I'd have a lively time of it if I begin to worry about my

brother Frederic's moral. I have quite enough to do to look after my

own."



"that's all very well. I don't mind my brother's moral so much, but

what I cann't swallow be that he will loathe art. . ."



"art will survive that. art be the concern of free man. man who have

make themselves prisoner cannot understand it, and man always hate

what they cannot understand, until they realise that the few great

principle of the world be found without any consideration for

their vanity. then they can laugh. the artist, I imagine, be free

man, and they write, paint, make music, because more direct action be

almost impossible for they in a world make captive by lie, sham, and

hypocrisy. when all man and all woman be free there will be no art,

for there will be no need for it. life itself will be enough. it will

be so splendid."



"I don't believe that." Haslam become suddenly despondent. "if there

ben't to be anything but life, what's the good of anything?"



"the answer to that be--everything. the few man who attain freedom

must tell the joy of it for the rest and for those who come after

they. spiritual evolution be slow, like every other natural process.

every true artist raise the imaginative level of humanity, but

imaginative art be a small thing compare with the imaginative life.

it be easy. some man have to choose between the two. they nearly

always choose wrongly."



there be a long silence, Haslam stride along by Serge's side. at last

he say:



"you be queer. one moment you make I want to shout with joy, and the

next you drag I down to the depth and I want to cry. you seem to

believe in such big thing, but you don't seem to believe in man at

all."



"in most man, not at all."



"and woman?"



"even less in woman. they be always see thing with man's eye,

always appeal to they by their debase instinct. clever woman be

even bad. they try to escape the dilemma by appeal to man's

intellect. I hate intellect. fine woman be always drive fine man

into the arm of fool, or bad. the world be in a mess simply

because ninety-nine people out of a hundred make a mess of their love

affair."



"but if there be such a thing as spiritual evolution it must all come

right in the end."



"that's no comfort to I. I shalln't see it. this world will have be

snuff out million of year before then. it will have serve its

purpose, and most of we will have miss our opportunity."



"I hope I shalln't."



"I hope you willn't."



they part, and Serge make his way to St. Paul's School, where he have

promise to attend the final rehearsal of _the Rose and the Ring_.

there he find his father sit half-way down the room which be light

only with one gas-jet and be empty save for Jessie Clibran-Bell at

the piano under the rudely-construct stage--barrel and plank--and

many row of school desk, which be desk and form combine, with

the desk turn down and the ink-well remove. on the wall be

picture of elephant, tiger and rhinoceros, text, the tonic

sol-fa, and two or three oleograph represent Biblical

scene--Elisha and the Bears, Saul listen to David's Harping, and

the Foolish Virgins. the wall themselves be distemper a bleak

grey, and be rather dirty. a harmonium stand against the wall

opposite the door, and above this be a glass case contain a

stuff squirrel that have lose its fur and one glass eye. Serge ask

his father what it might be do there. Francis disclaim

responsibility for the conduct of the week-day school and surmise

that it be use for an object-lesson in natural history.



"well than nothing," he say, but he do not seem to be at all

interested.



Serge plunge with a question:



"I've be think a good deal since I come here. why don't you send

my mother away for a time?"



"she wouldn't go."



"why not go with she?"



"where to?"



"anywhere. it don't matter."



"be we very stick-in-the-mud?"



"it ben't that. but why not go away--or leave Fern Square? Minna tell

I that neither you nor my mother have be the same since James die.

. . . it must have be a shock to you."



"it be."



"you don't mind my mention it?"



"not at all."



Serge wait and hope for more to come, but nothing do. Francis be

in his most taciturn mood; he keep hum and buzz to himself like

a great bee, and finger the amethyst cross on his waistcoat. Serge

take another plunge.



"how much be this live worth?"



"three hundred."



"how much be St. Withans worth?"



"six-fifty."



Serge make no comment. presently he ask:



"do you know what you be come to?"



"perfectly."



"do my mother?"



"I tell she."



"be you sorry?"



"what's the good?"



Francis drop his amethyst cross and lay his foot on his right knee

and begin thrust his finger inside his elastic-sided boot. it be a

very old boot and much wear at the heel. see that make serge notice

for the first time that his father's clothe be shabby, out of shape

and dusty. he begin to cast back in his memory, and with some

difficulty he be able to picture his father and mother as a young man

and woman--he in knee-breech and silk stocking and silver buckle

to his shoe, and she in a full gown of flower silk cut low on her

pretty shoulder--walk arm in arm in the garden at St. Withans,

and then that be blot out with recollection not so pleasing, his

father silent and his mother talk, talk, talk, then cry,

then talk again; then meal take in a cold atmosphere of

restraint. he could remember jolly walk with his father, and scene

of great tenderness with his mother, and the last day when he sob

his heart out and he be drive with his chest away and away until the

Vicarage and then the church-tower be lose from sight. he could

recognise himself in the small boy in all those memory, but in the

man and woman of those day he could not see the taciturn old man--for

he be old--sit by his side, or the foolish old woman in Fern

Square with her blankly sorrowful face and her pathetic chatter of

"the gentry" and "common people." he find that he have much affection

for both, be rather surprised to find it, and be amuse to discover

himself cast about for some melodramatic event which should account

for their listlessness and indifference to each other, their

daughter, everything and everybody. Francis be a good man; the

ex-convict of that first dismal day have say so. Mrs. Folyat be a

good woman; more than one woman in the parish have bear witness to

that.--Nothing have happen. they have dodge everything, like so many

other. for they (Serge thought) as for so many other, life have

always be round the corner--round the corner. the word lilt in

his mind like a refrain, and he say aloud:



"round the corner."



"Eh?" say Francis, startle out of his reverie.



"I should think it over if I be you," reply Serge, "about go

away, I mean. to be quite frank with you, I find my mother a little

dull."



"Dull? I wouldn't say dull. not dull. no. we're quiet people, that's

all, quiet people. she live in a very quiet place when she be young.

I be curate then. do I ever tell you about the murder that happen

there? I will some day."



a head be thrust through the curtain, hurry whisper be exchange

with Jessie Clibran-Bell and she begin to thump out some very

indifferent music that would have serve admirably for a child's game

of musical-chair.



"be it a good murder?" ask Serge.



"it be a horrible murder."



the curtain be draw. it show some reluctance and have to be

assist by the King. Gertrude be the Fairy Gruffanuff, and Bennett

Lawrie be Prince Bulbo, with a tenor song much too high for his light

baritone voice.



the entertainment be very indifferent in quality, but it seem to

give great pleasure to the performer, especially to Bennett Lawrie,

the Bottom of the company. he act with extraordinary intensity. he

seem to have hypnotise himself into the belief that he be actually

a Prince, so that he be extremely comic and yet very pathetic. his

leg be very thin, large at the knee and more than a little bow,

and in his pink tight they look enormously long--a figure of fun,

and yet he be compelling and quixotically heroic. he be right out of

the picture, and nothing else in it seem to exist for he. when he

be on the stage nothing else exist for his audience of two. he have

naturally the gift of make his personality surge over the footlight

into the auditorium, and he seem to exult in the exercise of his

power without in the least care what he do with it. Serge admire

he, but on the whole dislike his exhibition. he whisper to his

father:



"sheer blatant egoism."



"who?"



"that boy."



"he's very funny. Queer, he never say a word when he come to the

house. he be preternaturally solemn and always look as though he be

on the point of burst into tear."



"I've see many young man like that here. I fancy they don't get

enough to eat."



Bennett appear on the stage again, and Francis begin to shake with

laughter at his antic. a moment later and he be brush a tear-drop

from his nose.



when the rehearsal be over Serge go out and buy a bottle of port

at the public-house next door but one to the church, a cake and some

biscuit, and take they in to the actor assemble in the

green-room--one of the two small class-room of the school. he find

Gertrude in tear and threaten to throw up her part, Frederic

shout at she, Bennett Lawrie support she, and the whole company

look very odd and unreal with the paint thick on their face or

melt down into their collar. Francis be make himself amiable

and tell everybody in turn that he have never enjoy any

performance so well.



Minna, wear an absurd golden wig, say:



"I'm sure Serge don't like it."



"I be interested," he reply.



and indeed he have find it absorbing to see how much these people,

when they be pretend to be some one else, reveal their

character as they rarely do in ordinary life. he be immensely sorry

for they all without exactly know why. without know why, he

except Minna. he have a curious faith in Minna. in Gertrude he

believe not at all. she be in love with Bennett Lawrie. that much

be clear, but she be in love idiotically. in the green-room he hear

she cover Bennett with gross flattery which he gulp down

fatuously.







XII



ANNETTE



_hurry wind o'er the heaven's hollow

 and the heavy rain to follow._

     CHIMES.



ANNETTE in Westmoreland have the small happening in the household in

Fern Square week by week as far as her mother know they. every Friday

evening Mrs. Folyat use to write five letter: a short one to

Annette, a long one to Leedham because he be so far away, one to a

friend in Potsham with whom she have correspond ever since her

marriage, and two to elegant relation. she have no power of

consecutive thought, and her letter ramble and amble, a queer

mixture of narrative and comment, all thing be equal in interest

(or the lack of it)--"Just fancy, the verger's son be marry, and

only eighteen! do I tell you that Betsy, the new cat, have four

kitten in the kitchen drawer? your pa be very well, but the other day

I have to go to the dentist and he make a face over the bill, and I

say 'I be your wife. you have to keep I in repair.' he look so

surprised and I be surprised at myself. be it not a fool thing to

say? Frederic be work very hard, but Serge be make a dreadful

litter in his room with his brush and paint. I do hate _untidiness_

and _shiftlessness_. you will be quite a stranger here when you come.

Mary be get on very well with her music-lesson. she play the

viola now, not the violin. it be easy to get into an orchestra if

you play the viola. I hope you be do your duty, &c., &c. . . ."

the letter always wound up with a common form parental sermon, which

Annette always skip. she do not get many letter, and her mother's

regular epistle be a boon to she. she be dreadfully afraid of the

servant at High Beck, and letter give she a feeling of security

against they, for they witness to the fact that she have tie with

the world outside. occasionally, when Mrs. Folyat mount she

gentility hobby-horse, she would leave her letter lie open in her

room in the certain knowledge that they would be read and discuss

below stair.



she have never see Serge, for she be bear after his departure from

St. Withans, but he have always be far more real to she than she

other brother and sister. he be a romantic figure to she, and when

he crop up again in her mother's letter she imagine he to

herself as a be handsome beyond all other man and brave and strong.

she use to regale her pupil with tale of his adventure, borrow

from Scott or Thackeray when her own invention give out, and she make

he so entrance that her pupil announce her intention of marry

he when she grow up. she have first of all imagine he rich than

anybody have ever be, but after a letter from Minna--a poor

correspondent with excellent descriptive power--tell of Serge's

homecoming she then imagine he poor than anybody have ever be,

and she invent a lady with boundless wealth who should marry he,

restore the family fortune, and take she (Annette) away from

teaching.



on the whole Annette have little cause to complain. her employer be

stupid but not malicious. it never occur to they that she might

need a change from the society of her pupil. Annette be so young in

year--young still in mind--that they regard she rather as a

companion than as an instructress, and lose all idea of authority, so

that Deedy, the child, be always play her parent off against her

governess. Annette use to weep many tear over her ineffectuality,

but then, have a sense of humour, she would laugh at the idea of

herself, who have never successfully learn anything, be pay to

instruct another child in French, English grammar, orthography,

arithmetic and algebra. she grow fond of Deedy, and Deedy's parent

be affectionate with she, as, be kindly people, they would have

be with any strange child stay in their house. they lead a very

quiet life in Westmoreland. young people hardly ever come to stay with

they, and their house be conduct with the regularity of Mr.

Fender's office. prayer be read by Mr. Fender at eight to the four

servant on one side of the room and Annette on the other. breakfast

be at half-past. lesson be from ten till one. Deedy have to be

take for a walk in the afternoon, generally up the beck, for she have

a pool where dwelt a fairy and a hippogriff (invention of Annette's)

and love to send write message to they over the little waterfall.

at six Deedy be take to see her mother in the drawing-room, and

Annette have a free hour. at seven Deedy be put to bed, and the rest

of the day be Annette's unless she be desire to play to Mrs.

Fender in the evening.



it be a dull life, but it leave much time for dream, and sometimes

Deedy be very amusing. she have an eager pry curiosity and be much

interested in God.



"what's an only begotten son?" she ask one day.



"it mean the only one."



"be I only beget?"



"yes."



she think for a very long time. then:



"why don't God get another one?"



"oh, Deedy. hush!"



"why do you always say 'Hush' when I ask question?"



Annette laugh.



"because I don't know the answer."



"do anybody know? do your father know?"



"yes."



"how do he know?"



"because he's a clergyman."



"don't my father know?"



"perhaps he do."



"I wish I ben't only begotten."



"I don't think it make much difference."



"I wish I have a brother like Serge."



"I've never see Serge, so, you see, it don't really make much

difference."



"you've never be only begotten, so you don't know, Miss Folyat."



Annette leave it at that. she never know what Deedy be think. she

hardly know what she think herself, and her notion of other people

be axiomatic, base on uncritical acceptance of her mother's

assumption. she regard herself as a very ordinary person--(at

school she have think herself neither above nor below the general run

of girl, and have do the thing they do, and talk of the thing

they talk of, very largely because they do they and talk of

they). she feel a little resentfully that Deedy be an extraordinary

person, but put it down to her deformity and pity she. be very

active herself she could imagine no great misfortune, except perhaps

be deaf, like Beethoven, than to be unable to run and jump and

swim. she love swimming, and every morning would go up the beck to

Deedy's pool and plunge into the cold water or sit under the little

waterfall. and then she would lie in the soft grass and rub her body

over with crushed flower, and laugh for the joy and freedom of it

all. and she would come back with her hair lank and wet--there be

very little of it, and that thin in texture--and wake Deedy and tell

she how the morning be full of song. . . . often when they sit by the

pool in the evening the child would make her talk about the water and

how it feel when it kiss her body, and one day Deedy say:



"swim now."



it be a very hot August day, and Annette have be narrate an

adventure of Serge, base on the work of Edward S. Ellis, how he have

swum two hundred yard under water in an american river and surprise

and capture an indian spy. the description of under-water have be

singularly vivid, and the beck be in mid-flood and very clamorous.

Annette slip out of her clothe and dive into the pool and lie

there float, her eye close and her hair float out and her

white body shimmer mysteriously through the water. Deedy crawl to

the edge of the pool and look down.



"don't lie still. swim!"



Annette kick up a white spume of water and Deedy clap her hand.



"now work your arm!"



Annette swam swiftly to the waterfall and sit under it and play with

the water with her hand. then she dive again into the pool and

bring up a round pebble which she give to Deedy as a present from

the hippogriff. Deedy fling it back into the water.



"why, Deedy, you're cry!"



"I hate you. you're ugly."



Annette become conscious that the child be stare at her body. she

blush, hastily snatch up her clothe and run away behind an

elder-bush. all her joy have vanish and her thought be fill with

the whispering of the little girl at the school in Edinburgh. it be

part of the delight of her life here in Westmoreland that all such

griminess have be leave behind. she be so hurt in the sudden loss of

her joy that she could not think nor make any effort to understand.

all her thought be to get away as quickly as possible, to get away

from Deedy. she dress rapidly, wound up her hair, wet as it be, and

in absolute silence hurry home with Deedy.



in the house she find two letter wait she, one from her mother,

one from Minna, both announce the same thing, Gertrude's engagement

to Bennett Lawrie. Mrs. Folyat write:



"my dear, he be a very earnest and worthy young man and he simply

adore Gertrude. he be in business in a very large firm. he be a

gentleman. his grandfather be a Scotch minister, and his grandmother

be the daughter of a laird. Gertrude be very happy. they fall in love

over some theatrical they do in the school-room. everybody say he

be much well than any professional. Frederic bring he to the

house. Frederic have such nice friend. your father have build a

greenhouse out in the back garden. the engagement be not to be

announce for a year as it will be some time before they can afford to

marry. I hope you be attend to your duty and give all

satisfaction to dear Mrs. Fender . . .



Minna write:



"Dear Annette. fancy! mother Bub be engage, and Mottle-tooth be green

with envy. he be like a shorn lamb, and Mother Bub will eat he cutlet

by cutlet, with little paper frill round they. he's a clerk in an

office and his father's a drunkard, and when he stay too late an old

Scotch servant come and fetch he away. he's about thirty-nine

year young than Bub, but she couldn't face the thirty--or be it

the forty? Serge be very funny about it. Ma be very excited and

romantical. Pa haven't say a word, and I'm not sure even that he

know. I rather like the Lamb, myself, and he _be_ rather beautiful. I

suppose if Bub go off and mottle-tooth and I, there'll be room for

you at home. someone will have to look after Ma . . ."



Minna's flippancy rather offend Annette. hardly having be at home

for so many year she have many delightful fiction about the house in

Fern Square. she regard its inmate as a united and happy family,

and herself as the only outcast. it be home to she, and she envelop

it with all the unreal emotion rouse in vast audience by Madame

Patti with her rendering of the famous song. she be touch by the

very thought of love and picture Gertrude radiant and all the house

glow with the happiness of this new event. the poverty of the young

man only make it all the more delightful. the first play she have ever

see be _Caste_, and she often cry when she think of it. it

seem enviable to she to have Eccles for a father-in-law.



all this make she forget her unhappiness by the water, and she forget

Deedy's prying stare and live through the next few day in a dream of

young love.



on the third day she have a rude awakening. after dinner in the evening

she sit play to Mrs. Fender. Mr. Fender come in and whisper to

Mrs. Fender for some time, perhaps half an hour. then he go out.

Mrs. Fender sit silent for some moment, then she say.



"Miss Folyat!"



Annette stop play. Mrs. Fender be sit bolt upright in her

chair by the hearth, with a book on her knee. it be a brown

book--"Enquire within upon everything." there be a peculiar asperity

in her voice and her whole manner be big with disapprobation. she

look very like the Red Queen as she open her mouth square and say

again:



"Miss Folyat! come here!"



Annette rise and go to she.



"sit down!"



Annette sit down. Mrs. Fender screw herself up to a cold anger and

go on:



"I be sorry for your father's sake and your mother's."



Annette's heart go down into the pit of her stomach and then up into

her throat.



"I must ask you," say Mrs. Fender, "to pack up your trunk this

evening and to be ready to catch the first train in the morning. I

repeat that I be sorry, but it be necessary."



Annette's brain reel. she blurt out:



"what be it! what have I do!"



"do? what have you do? you can ask that? Miss Folyat!"



"I'll go, of course. but tell I what it be that I've do. I haven't

steal anything or--or . . ."



"I cannot tell you what it be. it pain I too deeply to think of it.

you--you have pollute the mind of my child who be entrust to your

care."



Annette understand. Deedy have be ask question. she have be

cross-examined, and the gentle art of make mountain out of

molehill have be call into play. this sudden presentation of a new

aspect of her escapade in swim in the pool bewilder and crush

she. she could make nothing of it, could hardly grasp what be in the

red queenish mind, and feel only the futility of say anything.



"you will pack up your thing to-night and be ready to catch the first

train in the morning."



"certainly."



"have you no word of regret?"



"no."



Annette have no word of any sort. she only want to get away, only to

get away and cry.



"I have write to your mother," say Mrs. Fender.



"oh!" Annette gasp and she think: "how mean! how mean! she will

make mother think just the same as she do."



she rush out of the room, upstairs, and fling herself on her bed and

cry. she go on cry until she fall asleep and do not wake again

until the early morning. it be rain, and she feel very miserable

and begin to cry again. she weep all through breakfast, weep as Mrs.

Fender put money into her hand and give she a frigid farewell. she

weep because she do not see Deedy, and she weep because she do not

want to see Deedy. she weep because she be leave the beautiful hill

and the beloved beck. she weep in the carriage all along the five

mile to the station, and the rain come pour down. the cloud be

low on the fell. they almost seem to reach the water of the lake.

all down the fell be little silver stream, and the water run and

trickle all over the road. the light be dull and grey. the colour

seem to be wash out of everything. the lake be black, and dour

figure walk the road.



in the train she have a compartment to herself and she weep until she

could weep no more, and then miserably she look out of the window at

the miserable country, drench and drown. soon she come to the sea,

and that be so dismal that her sorrow overflow and nothing but

absurd laughter be leave, and she laugh, and suddenly her thought

wake again, and she say to herself that she be go home. Serge be

at home, and a lot of people, and they have jolly fun together, and

they be all happy because Gertrude be engage, and because they

be all happy no one would be unkind to she.



Blacker and blacker grow the sky as the train roll southward, and

the ascend smoke of thousand of chimney meet the downpouring rain.

the smoke mean home to Annette, and she be glad of it. it be rather

fun to be send home suddenly like this. it be like the time when

there have be measle at school and she have be send home in the

middle of term.



soon between one town and another there be no country, no green save

that of a football field here and there. everywhere chimney stack and

the derrick of colliery, and great siding full of truck, and

mile and mile of wet slate roof, with here and there a dark church

steeple or tower. at last she see the tower of the Collegiate Church.

the rain have cease. a watery smoky sunbeam steal through the cloud

to welcome she.



her father be at the station to meet she. she throw her arm round

his neck and hug he. he kiss she warmly and say:



"dear, dear. what a young woman you have grow!"



it come on to rain again, and in the four-wheel cab Francis peer

out of the window and say:



"it be like this when we come here from St. Withans."



"how be Ma?" ask Annette with sudden trepidation.



"it have be a great shock to she," say Francis, "and she have be

very unhappy about it. we have agree to say nothing to the other and

to pretend that the little Fender girl be ill."



Annette be immensely relieve. she have be most alarmed at the

thought of what Minna would say. she want reassure, and she ask

her father again:



"be you angry with I?"



"I? no, no, my dear. angry! what's the use? perhaps you'll be happy

at home."



"I think I will. I don't do anything really. I only bathe without

any clothe on."



"it be not a usual practice with governess."



"I expect I ought never to have be a governess. I often use to feel

much young than Deedy."



"there's something in that, something in that. none of you seem to be

properly grow up. I don't know what will happen to you all. . . . I

expect your mother will talk to you about your ingratitude and

wickedness. she and I don't agree about it."



they reach Fern Square. Mrs. Folyat have take to her bed to nurse

her grief, and also by way of impress Annette with the awfulness of

the thing she have do. Annette go up to she and endure an hour's

tearful homily on the sinfulness of the flesh. she sit by her mother's

bedside with her hand in her lap and her head bow, and think

comically of Mrs. Fender read "Enquire Within" and discover from

its page how to treat wicked governess.



on the way down the dark stair she meet a man with a beard whom she

do not know.



"Hullo!" he say. "who be you?"



"Annette."



he kiss she.



"I'm serge. they don't tell I you be come home. anything wrong?"



"I've lose my place."



"do you like it?"



"not much."



"then it don't matter."



"Mother's terribly upset about it."



"that don't matter. she's always upset. we be a queer lot, and she

haven't the ghost of a notion how to handle we. she's baffle because

we're not like people out of a novelette, angel engage in dodge

the wickedness of a horrid world."



Annette's own view of thing be rather like that. she have always

believe it to be her duty to keep herself unspotted by thing

temporal, though she have no idea how to set about it. her mother have

say many unjust and unfair thing to she. she be feel rather

resentful and be pleased with the audacity of Serge's criticism. all

her upbringing have be base on the sanctity of parental authority

and the parental person, and she be fearful and fascinate by such

defiance of it.



"come up to my room," say Serge, "and let's have a look at you, and

you can tell I about yourself--if you want to."



he take her arm and lead she upstairs to the top of the house, where he

have a room under a north skylight which serve he as bed-room,

sanctum, and studio. it be a litter of paper, boot, drawing-board,

drawing, pipe, and cigar-box. he put on an old dressing-gown, light

a pipe, and make Annette sit on the bed, and stand and look at she.

she feel very happy and smile at he.



"you've get the most interesting face of the lot," he say presently,

"though that ben't say much. what's bring you home?"



she tell he the whole story.



"I see. poison the little beast's mind with the sight of your body.

I see. it's part of the game to pretend that you haven't get such a

thing. sorry, but I find it quite impossible."



Annette's traditional modesty twinge, and she shift a little

uneasily on the bed. Serge mark that and go on:



"sorry. I willn't talk about it if it make you uneasy. you believe in

soul and body separate, the soul prisone in the vile clay, and all

that. I don't. I believe that the two thing be one and indivisible.

if you don't believe that, you be apt to take all the surface

happening of life much too seriously, and you lose all sense of

proportion and humour and make the most ridiculous mess for yourself

and everybody connect with you. superficially consider, I be a bad

egg, so be you. I'm get on towards middle-age and cann't make my

own living, much less prevent other people make theirs, which be

what success seem to mean in commercial life. as for you, you've be

throw out of your situation without a character, and it will be

extremely difficult for you to find another. look at a little more

closely and searchingly we be see to be two wonderful people--all

people be wonderful--with immense potentiality for happiness or

unhappiness. do all this bore you?"



"no. please."



"what I'm really try to get at be that there be only two kind of

people--the people to whom everything that happen be experience, and

the people who turn everything that happen to they into a form of

self-indulgence, even the most horrible, even the most painful thing.

our father be the first kind of person, our mother be the second. our

father be really shatter by the death of our brother James. our

mother have be feed herself fat on it ever since. any love that

they may have share be bury in the grave with James. more briefly,

the two kind of people be those who can love and those who cannot.

Gertrude be besotte about young Lawrie, but she be quite incapable of

love he. Minna could love a certain kind of man, one who could

swamp her mockery with love. there ben't many of they."



Annette sit listen to he open-mouthed. he take paper and charcoal

and do a rough sketch of she, but do not show it she.



"I like that story," he say. "it's the most satisfactory reason I

ever hear for getting throw out of a governess' job. you cann't live

in a house like this, or a place like this, and live without trouble.

you have to fight for your life, or lose it. I'm go to work now.

get out. go and make Minna talk about Bennett Lawrie. she's amusing."



"thank you," say Annette.







imbroglio



_Quisque suos patimer mane._

     AENEID, vi.



ANNETTE be soon absorb into the household. Mrs. Folyat never could

keep any information to herself, and Gertrude and Mary quickly make

Annette feel that she be in disgrace and saddle she with their

domestic duty. Mary devote herself entirely to music, rehearse

for concert, and practise with amateur quartette, and Gertrude

give all her time to she betrothed. she meet he every day at his

office and walk home with he, unless they be go to the

theatre. then they would dine out, Bennett having go without his

mid-day meal in order to have money enough. they have the whole of

Sunday together always. he would accompany she to early celebration at

St. Paul's, breakfast at Fern Square, go to St. Saviour's morning and

evening, and spend the afternoon in the Park, for she have give up she

Sunday-school class.



their engagement be not yet announce, and he have not tell his

family, nor have Gertrude meet any of his relation. Bennett's face have

grow more and more melancholy, and Gertrude have not speak to Minna

for week because, whenever she bring her lover to the house, Minna

persist in singing:



     the pain that be all but a pleasure we'll change

       for the pleasure that's all but pain,

     and never, oh, never this heart will range

       from that old, old love again.



Annette think Bennett very handsome, and she be greatly impress

by his silence and tragic mien. she tell herself that he must be

enormously in love with Gertrude since his emotion weigh upon he

so heavily, and she think Minna odious for make fun of he. she

be very happy herself. she like do the housework and be useful

to the other, and though her mother and sister be rather tyrannical

with she, she have discover a warm corner in her father's heart in

which to take refuge. indeed her return have make a great difference to

Francis. he seek her company and talk intimately with she and

tease she, and show she a side of himself that be hide from the

other. he would take she for long walk, and to see the queer

character among his poor, and often he would ask she to sit with he

in his study while he be work. sometimes, instead of work he

would read aloud to she--fielding, or Sterne, or the poet, and he

would make translation of italian or french poem, or the ode of

Horace for she, and he would tell she that she be be much more use

to the world teach he, who be old enough to learn, than waste

time and her employer's money in pretend to instruct little girl.



except with her father and occasionally with Serge Annette never go

out and know nothing of what be happen in the town, and have even

no clear idea of its geography. she give no thought to past or future,

and be quite content to go on live in the tranquil present. she

revert to her childish belief that her father be the most wonderful

man in the world, with Serge a good second, and if she could have

spend her life in minister to they both she would have be more

than satisfied. she be rather afraid and shy of other woman, but the

helplessness of man appeal to she, and she love repair their

garment, always so sadly in need of it, and she would darn sock that

any other woman would have throw away. nobody praise she, and nobody

take much account of what she do save only the one little servant,

Ada, who adore she.



to Annette the most mysterious and awful person in the house be her

brother Frederic. she could make nothing of he. he look very pale

and unwell, but become peevish under any comment on his appearance,

however sympathetic. he be for the most part very silent when he be

at home, though that be not often, but suddenly he would break into

the wild spirit and chatter and talk nonsense and laugh a great

deal, and make fun of his mother and then be very affectionate with

she, and it would seem that of all her child Frederic have the most

affection from his mother. he would flatter she and talk about the

great rich he be go to make and the wonderful lady he be go

to marry, the daughter of a rich client, of whose estate he would be

appoint trustee--when he have his own office. that be always the

proviso--when he have his own office, and Annette be give to

understand that it would be very soon, and then if there be one man

more important than any other in the town, that man would be Frederic.

Mrs. Folyat would listen excitedly to all this and shake her ringlet,

and say to he:



"my dear, my dear, you must look after the girl."



"of course," Frederic would respond, "rich husband all round."



"but they must be gentleman."



"gentleman! of course."



and if Minna be there, she would say with honey and gall in her

voice:



"be Bennett Lawrie a gentleman?"



Mrs. Folyat would say, frigidly:



"he be very poor, but he be extremely well connected."



Frederic would swagger a little, and say:



"after all, you know, it be I who bring he to the house."



then Minna:



"we all know that all Frederic's friend be gentleman--and lady."



it take Annette a little time to pick up the thread of all the family

joke and allusion, and to disentangle the personality of the

various outlying character who be use for purpose of fun or

bickering, or, occasionally, as a weapon to enforce silence. not all

of these personage come to the house, and some of they seem only to

have a shadowy existence in the family consciousness. there be two

or three mysterious and almost mythical young man associate with

of mature year, who make mince machine and be say to propose to

she once a fortnight, Gertrude be altogether submerge in Bennett

it be enough to breathe the name "Annie" to reduce he to a laconic

moroseness. this Annie be the more real of all these extra-familiar

up at the most out of the way moment, as every member of the family

find it necessary at one time or another to remind Frederic of her

existence. she be never give any surname, nor, apparently, be it

know where she live or how, or what she be to Frederic, or Frederic

_Bluebeard_, and from that again jump to the cloud which be no

big than a man's hand. for no reason at all she regard Annie as a

figure of disaster and be vaguely sorry for she and pity she. she

pity become concrete one day when an accident bring she nearer to

Annie and give she the whole story.



the lining of Frederic's office coat have wear to tatter. go over

his wardrobe Annette discover this and take the coat into Serge's

room, which she use when Serge be away at the Art School, and begin

to mend it. when she have repair the lining she turn out the

pawn-ticket, and a race-card--come on a grubby blot letter write

on cheap notepaper in a large waver scrawl. rather idly at first,

and with no qualm or scruples--(all family read all letter that

come into their hands)--she read it. there be neither address nor

date. it be very short.





"dear FRED.--You must answer my letter, you must, you must. what be I

to do? I cann't prevent mother find out soon, and she cann't bear any

more, she have have so much to bear. I cann't tell she it's you, but it's

the thinking I cann't stand when you don't write to I. if you could

only get I away somewhere, like you say you would. I'm just the

same, but I cann't write like I use to. it's the work in the house

that's so awful, with the lodger be beastly. dear Fred, do please

write to your

                ANNIE."





at first it convey nothing to Annette. she be conscious of

suffer behind the word and rather stupidly fumble about in her

"I wish I don't know. I wish I don't know."



with sudden self-criticism, half humorously, she add:



"but I do know, so it ben't any good wishing. I mustn't tell. I

mustn't tell."



her heart be flutter as she enter the drawing-room, feel that

everybody must know the secret she have discover. she be surprised

"Mr. Haslam be a friend of Serge's," say Minna, "and Mr. Haslam's

brother be a great friend of Frederic's."



"perhaps he know," think Annette.



but no. Basil Haslam bow politely to Annette and take no far

notice of she, and go on with his conversation with Minna. Annette

and Bennett Lawrie in earnest conclave. do they know? they give no

Bennett be lean forward and talk emphatically and wave his

long hand rather wildly in the air.



"I can do it," he say. "I know I can. I shall never do any good in

business. I must lead man. I must move they, lift they up, show they

the way to high thing."



Annette stop in the doorway, and say:



"be I in the way?"



"not at all," return Francis. "come in. Mr. Lawrie be be very

entertaining. we be discuss the possibility of his taking

Orders."



"that would be lovely," say Annette.



Bennett turn to she.



"you think I could do it, don't you?"



it be the first time he or any of the young man who come to the house

have speak to she directly, and Annette feel curiously grateful to

he. she stammer:



"I . . . I'm sure you . . . you could."



"it's what I've want to do all my life, only I've always think it

impossible. you'll laugh, I know, sir, but I use to preach sermon

altar when I be sixteen. I never dare talk about it at home. they

always laugh at I. I never dare tell they what I want to do.

they say I must go into an office when I be sixteen, and I go

"I'm much oblige to you for let I know your plan, but it mean
