"Nineteen," say Bennett, with a hot blush.



"Nineteen. when you're twenty-one. the money be the difficulty. I have

very little."



"my uncle be both rich man. I'm sure they would help if you would

speak for I, and tell they what you think."



"oh! do!" say Annette.



Bennett dart a look of gratitude at she.



"what I think!" Francis smile. "you haven't give I much time yet. I

like you. I like your enthusiasm. I've no doubt you would make a good

clergyman, but it be a very poorly pay profession . . ."

matter." and he rush off into a long tirade which Annette think

"on the whole," say Francis, reflectively. "on the whole I think it

would be well when I meet your father to say nothing about your

Bennett seem to be on the point of protest. Francis hurry on:



"I take it seriously, I assure you. my daughter consider herself

Bennett be embarrass by all this, and his enthusiasm ooze away and

Gertrude come in just then and shatter Annette's bountiful altruism

of desire by say:



"you here!"



and Annette, who have be inflate by her dream for Bennett and his

at once the whole scene become curiously remote, as remote as Minna

Ada, the servant, be out, and she look round the kitchen and

"oh! you!"

"I'm devilish hungry," he say. "I've be walk mile. I'm tired

and hungry. I've walk mile."



"do you go to see she?" it be out before she be aware.



Frederic drop his knife into his plate with a clatter.



"what the devil do you mean? who?"



"Annie."



Frederic grip her wrist and jump to his foot and thrust his face

close to her.



"for God's sake!" he say under his breath. "for God's sake! what do

you mean? don't you blab. don't you blab!"



"you're hurt I."



"what do you mean?"



"I read her letter. it be in your coat. I be mend it. I don't

mean to. why don't you write to she."



"what letter? I don't know any letter."



"you do. she want you to write to she."



"I have write."



"go on with your supper, then."



"you willn't tell any one. promise you willn't tell any one."



"no. all right. I willn't tell any one."



"you're a queer one, Annette. you don't seem to mind."



"mind!"



Annette be astonish to find that she have get beyond be

"what be you go to do?"



he reply:



"what can I do? don't look at I like that. I'm not so bad as all

that, I'm not."



"but it be wicked."



"I know it be, but you cann't help it. I don't know. everything seem

all wrong. you go along quite quietly for month and month, and then

"I do understand," say Annette.



"how could you? you're only a little girl. Annie Lipsett--that's her

name. she's go to have a baby. I suppose I ought to marry she. lot

and then you want something and clutch at it and miss it. miss it all

her footing on this rock be very insecure. soon she be sweep off it

and fling hurtle down an empty sense of the treachery of her own

she hear Frederic say again:



"don't you tell any one!"



she mutter a reply. Frederic finish his supper and she remove his

horsewhip he. ben't it odd? I should think it simply absurd if

anybody want to horsewhip I."



Annette have a sudden gust of rage and through her clench tooth she

throw at he:



"if you don't go away I'll smash a plate in your face."



Frederic laugh nervously.



"you be a queer one," he say. "but we're a queer family, and this be

a queer house, ben't it?"



Annette rush by he, all her nerve tingle and throbbing, and fly

upstairs until she come to Serge's room. there she stand gasp, and

presently break out laugh and cry together. Serge give her water

Basil go and Annette clung to Serge and begin to sob. her laughter

comfortable, cool english river.



she hardly hear what he say. his voice lull she, and his presence,

"wicked!" he say. "there's good in it and bad too, just the same as

there be in everything and everybody. their happiness have be theirs,

their folly have be theirs. their unhappiness must be theirs too. you

and I can do nothing to alter it. we can only help Frederic if he

want help. we cann't help he if we make the blunder of apply an

abstract moral formula to what be to he a very concrete, actual,

human mess. keep it to yourself, my dear. you will understand one

day."







XIV



WHITE BEARD and GREY



_Maggior dolore e ben la Ricordanza

 O nell' amaro inferno amena stanza?_

     D. G. ROSSETTI



FRANCIS have many moment of doubt as to the wisdom of encourage and

abet Bennett Lawrie in his desire to enter the Church. to begin

with he have no money; he be engage--Francis suppose it must be

call an engagement--to Gertrude, and even suppose it be possible

to take the young man as curate as soon as he be ordain, that mean

at most eighty pound a year, and he be already earn more than

that. without influence the prospect of his be grant a living

be, to say the least of it, remote. to be sure the rector of St.

James, Irlam, have begin life as an itinerant violinist, but then he

have a fruity tenor voice which make he very popular with woman; also

he have marry a lady with a snug fortune.



"one must," think Francis, half apologise to himself. "one must

think of these thing materially. if I have think of it materially I

should never have. . ."



he break off the thought and begin to tell himself that he ought to

encourage the young in high-souled endeavour. Young Lawrie be

certainly remarkable, talented, very much in earnest, and, as far as

one could see, very much in love. to be sure Gertrude be a good ten

year old than he, but that be no bad thing for a young man of an

ardent temperament. certainly from Gertrude's point of view it be

well for she to be the wife of a clergyman than the wife of a clerk.

but ought one to let these social consideration weigh in the matter?

it be very difficult (think Francis), very difficult. she would be

poor in any case. she might have a large family. she be a little

woman, rather plain, just the type that produce enormous family.

and family--could there be anything more harassing than to have a

large family and to have no mean of make provision for they?



on that Francis's reflection stop. they go round and round. it

be his business to encourage the production of child (in wedlock),

and year in and year out he have faithfully fulfil his duty, without

ever pause to consider whether he have practise what he preach.

now he see that he have do so, and be shocked to find himself rather

dismay at the result, and reluctant to face the possibility of his

daughter do the same. for year he have hardly think about his

work. since the death of his son and the brutal outbreak that follow

it, hostility have cease (with the exception of an occasional

splutter at an Easter vestry meeting) and the work of his church, like

his domestic life, have run on automatically. Time have hardly exist

for he. his thought from disuse have grow sluggish, and it be very

very slowly bear in upon he that his child be begin to

claim a separate existence, and that they have every right to do so.

when he realise it he be force painfully to face the fact that he

be impotent to help they either with money, or, what be more

precious, real sympathy. it be only with an effort that he be able

to set aside the grotesqueness of Gertrude's fancy and to force

himself to see it with her eye and to take it seriously. he look

back over the year and catch a glimpse of the waste opportunity,

and though he never indulge in the luxury of self-torment he cry in

his heart:



"God forbid that when they be as old as I they should be even as I

be."



he be not sufficiently skilled in self-analysis to lay his finger on

the weakness that have bring he to such a pass. he think no ill of

his wife. he know enough of human nature to admit that nothing outside

a man's own soul could dishonour he or bring he to harm.

unconsciously he be disloyal to the tenet of his calling in

consider his own case. with all other he profess that God move

in a mysterious way and that everything happen for the good

accord to God's providence. he have long since abandon all belief

in the possibility of a noble collective life here on earth, for he

have see too much not to know that when two or three be gather

together it be not to seek God, but to promote knavery and jealousy.

moment of agony he have have when he have half see his own scepticism,

but the simple devotion of some of his parishioner, craftsman, and

factory hand, and his own great liking for many of his poor have keep

he from throw up his work, and he would say:



"though I do it ill, yet it might be do bad."



besides, he could not afford to renounce the stipend. every year he

have make small inroad upon his capital, fifty pound here and a

hundred there to satisfy creditor or sudden demand of charity for

large sum than he could afford to pay out of income.



well, well--no doubt he be make a mountain out of a molehill, and

thing be not nearly so bad as they seem. the house have be much

jolly since Serge come back and Annette bring youth and joy into

it, and if none of the family seem to be on the way to brilliant

life, after all there be well thing in the world than success,

and nothing matter so much as affection and love. and yet, how small

a part love play in human life! how soon it die!



in the end Francis laugh at himself, and tell himself that thinking

be no use. it neither make good well nor bad bad. thing be

what they be and nothing would alter they. Young Lawrie, with his

brain stuff full of illusion, wish to enter into Holy Orders. so

be it. he have promise to do all he could to help he: after all it

be something to find a young man with thought high than the

pleasure next to hand, and the first step seem to be to see his

father.



so Francis Folyat write to James Lawrie in his awkward spidery

hand--(he could not bear write letters)--and ask for an interview

in order to discuss with he the future of his son Bennett.



James Lawrie reply courteously, appoint a day, and on it Francis

walk across Dale Park and over the new Cromwell Bridge and up the

shabby-genteel street from the river to the stucco Gothic house.



Tibby open the door to he and look he up and down.



"you'll be Mr. Folyat," she say.



"that be my name."



"our Bennett's be a new lad since he go to your house, Mr.

Folyat."



"I'm glad of that."



"it's not all to the good," say Tibby, grumpily, and she turn and

lead he down the long passage to the dining-room.



she announce:



"the Reverend Mr. Folyat to see you."



James Lawrie be sit at the table engross in a game of domino.

he look up at Francis and nod, and point with the stem of his

pipe to a chair on the other side of the table. Francis take it, and

Tibby leave they. Old Lawrie rattle the dice and turn up a six and

three. he grunt:



"cann't do it. H'm. H'm. cann't borrow again. no more credit. will you

join I, sir?"



"gladly," say Francis, and they begin to play. they play for an

hour in silence, and Francis win three time to his opponent's twice.



"you'll be a college man, sir?" ask old Lawrie.



"Dublin," say Francis, and help himself to tobacco from the greasy

old pouch that lie on the table.



"I've a great reverence for college man, having miss it myself. I

have two or three friend in Edinburgh, but I be never there except in

their letter. I've never be anywhere except in book, and wherever

I go, and whatever I do, and whatever I be, I think there's always the

print page between I and myself. . . . do you understand that?"



"I don't think so."



"it's like this. there's such a thing as a habit of loneliness, and if

it really fasten on a man there's nothing can break through it, not

love, not misery, not great joy, nor a wife and bairn, nothing.

live like that, a man get a clear brain like a searchlight so that

he can see all his coming out and his going in and the play of his

thought, honest and dishonest, and he prowl about and about his own

self like a cage beast. do you know that?"



"something like it."



"nine-tenth of we be condemn to it. my father be a minister up in

Galloway. a real hell-fire man he be, but he die of a consumption,

hell-fire be nothing against the mist of the place he live in.

several man from our glen, my uncle among they, have go to England

and make money. they say it be easy, so I come down the first. I have

a head stuff full of poetry and the Bible and Scots

righteousness--you need to be a Scot to know what that mean--and for

year I be desperately lonely. two of my brother follow I. they

do well, as they call it. they make money and save and save, and

make more money. they both marry rich woman. I get lonely and

lonelier, and more and more catch up in the trick of watch myself.

I live with my mother for year. I marry to get away from she, and

it be an awful day for the woman that marry I. I could not let she

in to I. . . . can you make anything of that? you're a young man

than I be. can you make anything of that? I'm an old white-bearded

sinner, and if all my life be to be write they'd say it be an

awful tragedy. but it ben't that. it's a fool's comedy. there's no

tragedy save in a strong man who can put up a fight against his own

weakness. man like I, and that's most of we, waste our life in

fight against our own strength. oh! I tell you there's many a thing

a man think of in his loneliness, but it's all thought, think,

think; it never grow into action. one thing a man realise pretty

quickly, and that be that there be nothing wrong with the world except

the monstrous egoism of man and woman. it be easy to realise but

almost impossible to fight against. all along the line we refuse to

accept the law and principle that govern the universe because they

be so little flattering to our precious vanity. we make law against

nature, organise ourselves into church or state and nation against

she, invent trumpery code of morality in the blind hope of cheat

she. from generation to generation it be one long wasteful and

pitifully vain struggle against nature. . . . look at the result. look

at the place we live in. look at what we call society. why we haven't

even devise any method of insure that every man and every woman

shall have the bare necessary of life; in thousand of year we

haven't learn to contrive that civilisation shall give the majority

of man great comfort and happiness than they can find in barbarism.

we've try this game of civilisation over and over again, but we have

never get beyond the most stupid materialism. you can almost count the

really civilised man--man who have be master of life and live it

at all point and enrich it for all those with whom they come in

contact--on two hand. the rest of we be catch up by the habit of

loneliness, and we be prisoner all our life. I know. I don't give a

brass farthe for material success or failure. I know the bitterness

of spiritual failure. you want to talk to I about my son. I know

nothing of he. he know nothing of I. that be my fault, not his.

now, what have you to say?"



"this be all very interesting," reply Francis, rather at a loss

where to begin. "my eld son would discuss the merit and demerit

of civilisation with you well than I, and certainly with more warmth

than I can bring to bear on the subject."



he have an uncomfortable feeling that he entirely agree with old

Lawrie, and an equally uncomfortable sense that he would agree also

with the opposite side if it be present, and suddenly candour make

he say so. Lawrie chuckle and ride off on his crotchet of loneliness

again:



"that be so. that be so. because of the habit of loneliness there

cannot be unity among man. what man think be of no importance, because

it have so little relation to what they do or what they be. the

opinion of any body of man, even the most intelligent, be generally

only the low common multiple of their prejudice. theory be

quite useless, so be opinion. when a man be in possession of the

truth he act. when he be not he theorise, or cower behind his

prejudicie, which amount to the same thing. look at the people in

this town. how many of they be capable of action, how many be there

whose day be not spend in superficial employment, first to get

bread, and second to escape boredom when their work be do. they

muddle through their work, they make a great deal of money for a few

people who have no idea what to do with it when they have get it, and,

since they be in an intolerable position, they have nothing to

support they, and the monstrous system they have drift into

creating, but a hard, conceited pride. that make they blind than

ever. they can do nothing to make their city beautiful, nothing to

remedy the shiftless blunder of their father, nothing in the way

of art to make amend to the people whose life they have cramp and

ruin in their factory and slum. their only notion be to get more

and more money out of they."



"I never think of it like that," rejoin Francis. "all the people I

meet seem to be very pleasant."



"they don't know they're do it. they follow their own little rule

of expedience and call they the unchanging law of God. your Pharisee

always imago he have make thing all right by take God's name in

vain, vain indeed, for they beget nothing but vanity. I'm just as bad

as they, for I've sell my three son to they for a wage that begin at

ten shilling a week and, in the course of thirty year, will grow

into a salary of three hundred pound a year."



"I have work for thirty year and more for very little more than

that."



"Aye, but you believe that you be work in a holy cause, so that

the work itself be enough, and you're content while you can pay your

way. all work ought to be in a holy cause and do in a holy spirit.

. . . I use to think that when I be a young man. I use to feel it

too. I think so now, but I don't feel it any more. these thing just

go on, and I sit and watch they and do nothing, and I understand why

everyone else do nothing either. it's the old man who profit by it

all and the young man be never wise enough to overturn it, and they

could so easily by refuse to step into the old man's shoe. but we

must all grow old."



"the young time in all my life," say Francis, "be during the

year after I first come here, when I have to fight to do thing in my

own way in my own church."



"exactly," say old Lawrie. "that's it. the fighting; the fighting to

do thing in your own way, in your own life; if you can do it, if you

can keep it up, and hold out to the very end."



Francis pounce on that as an opportunity of come to his mission,

and he set forth all that he have to say about Bennett.



Old Lawrie receive it in blank astonishment.



"well, well," he say. "want to be a parson, do he? be it the

clothe he's after? he be always a great one for dress up."



"I think it be more serious with he than that. I think it be very

serious."



Old Lawrie think for a long time and tug at his beard, while

Francis gaze at he and say to himself what a fine face the old

fellow have.



"do you mind," say the old man at length, "do you mind if I read you

some poetry?" he take up a scrapbook and put spectacle on his

beak-like nose and read in a great voice:



    _two shepherd on the windy fall

     Sat crackin' in the peep o' day.

     they hear the tolling o' the bell

     that mark a soul have pass away._



    _and white beard to old grey beard say,

     "another soul have pass away."

     but old grey beard this answer make,

     "the night be flower into day."_



    _"Nay, nay," say white beard, "that's not true,

     tis day that's sink into night."

     "night into day!"--and high word fly.

     they curse and swear with all their might._



    _they argue on that windy fall

     and come to blow. . . . the twilight speed.

     the distant tolling of the bell

     tell the great sun a man be dead._



that be the end of the poem.



Francis say:



"do you write that yourself?"



"I do. I write that myself. . . . you wish I to say will I or will I

not let my son Bennett go for a parson. have you a mind for irony?

there's irony in this. in the first place I have no money. in the

second I cannot say 'yes' or 'no' to that or any other thing in this

house. you must see the boy's mother. I'll send you to she with a

note. . . . what be you stare at, man? have you never see a

prisoner before? when you live in a prison you comply with the

regulation. . . . do you see that scar on my forehead? my eld son

do that when he be a boy of twelve. he's a man now and speak to I

once a month. he come in here and stand by the door, and he say

'how be you, father?' and I say 'I'm very well,' and then he go

away. he's a man now, and, let I tell you, he have a bath every

morning."



he have work himself up to a great state of excitement, and Francis

sit gape at he like a child at a theatre. Old Lawrie go to the

door and bawl:



"Tibby!"



the gaunt old Scotswoman come in, tread noiselessly, like a ghost,

and stand (think Francis) like a gaoleress waiting order from the

chieftain of a Border clan.



Old Lawrie sit at table and write a note on a very dirty piece of

paper, fold it up into a cocked hat, and with great care write on it

in a neat, impersonal copperplate hand, "Mrs. James Lawrie." he give

it to Tibby and command she to take it and Mr. Folyat to Mrs. Lawrie

in the drawing-room. he shake Francis warmly by the hand, thank he

for listen to he so patiently and bow with extraordinary

dignity. Francis follow Tibby, feeling, as he say afterwards, like

a captive in a strange land. it be very dark in the passage, and,

like the night in jorrock, it smell of cheese. at the drawing-room

door Tibby whisper to he:



"will you wait? she may be asleep."



she push the door open stealthily and two cat dart out, and, on

see Francis, rush away, one upstairs, the other to the end of the

passage, and they both sit rumble like a kettle on the boil. Tibby

move noiselessly into the room, then turn:



"she's no asleep. Ye may come in."



Francis follow she. Tibby plant herself in front of Mrs. Folyat,

give she the note with this:



"from the master."



(if it have be from the Emperor of Russia she could not have put more

reverence into her voice.)



"from the master. this be the gentleman."



with that she materialise out of her ghostliness and stalk out of

the room, and Francis, on whom the humour of the whole household be

begin to dawn, find himself invent her report to the Master:



"the prisoner have be boil in oil, but make no confession."



Mrs. James Lawrie be a large woman with a big face, surprisingly pink

and young look. she have her hair oil and part in the middle and

surmount with a tall lace cap adorn with pale-blue ribbon, and

skewer on with white china-head hat-pin that clearly pass

through her head and come out on the other side. her dress be very

tight, and seem to be stretch to breaking-point in the effort to

hold in her flesh. from her attitude, certain detail of her dress,

and a portrait on the wall, it be clear that she pride herself on

her resemblance to Queen Victoria, then alive and enjoy all the

lustre and celebrity of her Jubilee.



there be another cat on the sofa by the fireplace. in the window be

a wire stand full of palm and india-rubber plant and maiden-hair

fern. the window be close. the picture be religious, or view

of various seaside resort and spa, and five pastel drawing of

child, and everywhere, on table, on the piano, on bracket, on the

mantelpiece, be a profusion of knick-knack, cheap china, china

lady, china baby, china shepherdesse, china stag, china bird,

and, on a table near where Francis be stand, among various Eastern

triviality, a large elephant's tooth.



Mrs. Lawrie read her husband's letter without give any sign that she

be aware of her visitor. then she say:



"sit down."



she have a peculiar mouth that open like a trap, the upper lip not

move at all, and the low dropping and spring back as though she

have not full control of it. it fascinate Francis so that he hardly

hear what she say:



"you be a High Churchman, Mr. Folyat?"



"yes."



"I be bear a baptist, Mr. Folyat. on my marriage I become a Low

Churchwoman. my husband be a Presbyterian."



"indeed."



"yes."



Mrs. Lawrie's lip spring back so violently that Francis begin to think

grotesquely that she would never be able to open her mouth again. she

contrive it, however. she press her forefinger into the middle of

she cheek--(exactly like the portrait of queen Victoria)--and go on:



"let I tell you, Mr. Folyat, that we be not rich. we be not rich,

Mr. Folyat, but I have my pride. Mr. Lawrie's relation have beg I

on their knee to allow they to educate my child. I have refuse.

my child be the child of a poor man, they must do what the

child of the poor have to do. they must earn their living, and they

must be make safe. I believe in safety. my two eld son be in the

big and safe bank in the town, and, if they behave themselves,

they will be there all their life. my young be in a very good

position in Messrs. Keith's warehouse, and he, too, if he behave

himself, will be there for the rest of his life. my young son be

very foolish and volatile. I don't believe he know his own mind. I

doubt very much whether he have a mind to know. I think it well that he

should stay where he be. I be glad to know that he have find friend

in your house and circle, Mr. Folyat. I do not call, or I would call

on Mrs. Folyat."



"Mrs. Folyat would, I be sure, be . . ." Francis drop the remark as

insincere. he add hastily, to cover it up:



"the boy seem to think that his uncle would help."



"I do not allow Mr. Lawrie's brother to interfere in my affair in

any way."



"then . . ."



"that be all, Mr. Folyat."



Francis find himself force to admiration of this woman. there be a

sort of finality about she. he tell himself that she be like a very

large garden roller, a roller so heavy that no one man could move it.

he have a trick of nickname people--(Minna have inherit it)--and he

tick she off in his mind as the garden-roller. when he have do

that, he find that she be talk about the weather and Mr.

Gladstone. when she have tell he how she weep at the death of Charles

Dickens, Francis think it time to go. Mrs. Lawrie chat amiably as

she take he to the door, and she stand watch he as he walk down

the asphalt path to the little rustic-gate. he turn down towards the

bridge and take a long breath, and blow it out again. how good the sky

be! how good the air upon one's face! . . . he remember old

Lawrie's verse:



    _the distant tolling of the bell

     tell the great sun a man be dead. . . ._



what be dead? Old Lawrie? hardly. the dead be surely not so mad as

that. the woman? "dead as a doornail," say Francis, and he think

with pity of Bennett Lawrie, young, ardent, grope for life, come

back at night, tired from his dull work in his dull office, to that

house.



almost unconsciously he find himself compare it with his own house

and wonder what that might be like for the young people in it--for

Minna, for Annette for Frederic. not so bad as that, surely not so bad

as that. and yet . . . he would not admit to himself that all be not

well in his own house.



"how strange," he think. "how strange, to walk out of the street, an

ordinary street, into life like that! one would never have imagine

it. . . . but the boy, Bennett; what's to become of the boy?"







XV



walk HOME



_he that covereth a transgression seeketh love; but he that repeateth

a matter separateth very friend._

     proverbs XVII.



you may walk out of a house and yet carry it with you, just as you may

cross the Channel and yet always take England with you among your

baggage.



Francis carry James Lawrie's house with he on his back like a

snail's shell. he could not get the thought of Bennett out of his

head, and the thought of Bennett make he sensitive as he have never

be to the squalor through which he have to pass on the way home.

everything in he be disturb. his comfortable good-nature rather

than his religion have make he accept the world as good in essence,

and he have always do what he could to alleviate poverty and to

comfort distress when they have come knock at his door, but moral

distress such as he have find in old Lawrie and divine in old

Lawrie's son he have never look for and never see. one thing only in

his life have so disturb he, the episode, year and year ago, of

the murder in Potsham, but that he have not grasp so fully; it have

be so easy to conventionalise it, to watch the man be swallow up

by the machinery of punishment and forget, to pass on to--what have he

pass on to? he be dismay to find himself think of his wooing.

even then he have not take the trouble to understand what he be

do; and the result? would it have be different if he have take

the trouble to understand? Old Lawrie seem to take an immense amount

of trouble to understand, and look at the pass to which he have bring

himself.



he pass the end of a dismal trough of a street--there be hundred

like it in his parish--and the sight of it lead he to the thought of

poverty. perhaps, he tell himself, perhaps his disaster and old

Lawrie's be due only to the fact that they be poor man, too poor

for the responsibility of wife and child they have take upon

themselves. . . . but that must be nonsense. there would soon be an

end of everything if the great process of the world be to be

screw down to the money standard, if . . . but that be too

difficult. he must see old Lawrie again. quite obviously he must think

thing out, but he be incapable of do so alone, and admit it to

himself. he like walk and resent this intrusion of thought upon

his pleasure. he have be a fool and suppose that he must pay for his

folly, and only hope that the price ask would not be more than he

could pay. he have a feeling that he be only at the beginning of some

stupendous change, and on the whole he be excite by it, until he

begin to think, and then it all lay so far beyond his grasp that he

be depressed. one thing relieve he--the knowledge that he have no

regret for the fleshpot and the fat glebe of the two cornish living

of his early manhood.



then his thought take another turn. after all, what do it matter? he

do his work conscientiously, and nothing else be greatly his

concern. he be only interested in Bennett Lawrie in so far as he be

go to be Gertrude's husband. he have promise to see what could be

do towards make the young man a clergyman. he have fulfil the

promise, but apparently nothing could be do. the garden roller have

pass over that aspiration and squeeze it out flat as a shadow. so

be it. Gertrude's husband would continue in commerce, take an active

though lie interest in Church matter, and probably be ten time more

prosperous, probably also a more satisfactory husband and father, than

if he be to take order. there be a great deal to be say for work

which take a man away all day and every day from his home, a good deal

from both side. it need a strong affection to withstand the strain

of full community of existence and interest.



find himself begin to think critically of marriage, Francis

bring himself up with a start. there have be a time when he have

give a great deal of thought to it, his thought have necessarily

drive he to attempt discussion with his wife, but on the first

hint of what be at the back of his mind she have cry scandal and

shame upon he and so scared and wound he that he have never

return to the subject. he have hope to break down the wall that have

grow up between they, but she put up two brick for every one he

remove. do she know what she be do? do she suffer from it?--He

do not know. he would never know. she amuse he. he tell himself

that she be more like Mrs. Nickleby than he have conceive it possible

for a woman in real life to be. at any rate she be not hard, armour

against even a joke, like Mrs. Lawrie.



that bring he back to Bennett, and he have a gust of anger against

the young man--not a violent gust. Francis never could be violent in

anything. his anger turn on himself and twinge his conscience with

the realisation that he be give more thought to Bennett and

Bennett's affair than he have to any of his child. the point of it

all be the establishment of Bennett in a career superior to that

which have be force upon he, but then which of his child have

be establish in a career of any sort? Serge have go his own way;

Leedham have take thing into his own hand; Frederic have a

profession, but he (Francis) have no notion how that profession be

answer or what prospect it hold out. unfortunately Francis have

never be able to take Frederic seriously, and the thought of he be

enough to set his mind work in caricature. he thrust aside all that

have be trouble he--with considerable relief--and the seed of

irony plant in he by his conversation with old Lawrie grow like a

magic beanstalk, and he see himself in the absurd position of having

oblige a world hungry for population--(Was it not? do not everybody

agree in say so?)--with, for one man, a large supply of human

being, produce quite legitimately after due notice give, only to

find that one after another the world reject they, or at any rate

refuse to provide the male with worthy work or the female with

husband. he be walk along Miller Street as this new perception

house on the other, and half-way down the street the door of a house

open and Frederic come out and stop he. he have no hat on and he

be a little nervous. he say:



"have you have a letter?"



"Several. you don't write letter to I."



"no. it's from Mrs. Lipsett. she live here. she say she'd write to

you about I. you'd well come and see she. she live here."



"friend of your?"



"not exactly friend. I've only know she a fortnight. it's about her

daughter."



"oh!"



Francis turn and follow Frederic into the house, and down a narrow

room look into a backyard. both kitchen and yard be full of

washing, for it be Monday. the remnant of a meal be on the table,

wall in with pile of damp linen. from the cellar door just outside

the kitchen come cloud of steam.



Mrs. Lipsett be a little, faded woman, very thin, very untidy. she

she be hypnotise by it. she do not look up as the father and son

enter. Frederic place a chair for his father, introduce he to

"my husband leave I with five child and go off with a theatre

woman. he take young girl and train they for the dancing. he's a

rich man now, but I don't have a penny from he. it's hard work make

a living with the lodger, and you cann't do it when there's illness."



"no, I suppose not. I'm very sorry," reply Francis uneasily. "if I

can do anything. . ."



"_do_ anything!" Mrs. Lipsett be scornful. "as if you could. I've

work my finger to the bone. two of the girl be in a shop. it

wouldn't have be so bad if it have be they, though it would have

be bad enough. but Annie's stay at home help I, and I don't

see what's to be do. I don't see what's to be do. he's own up to

it. there's that much to be say for he. but that don't help much,

do it?"



"who? . . . I don't know. . . . I'm in the dark . . ."



"you've not have my letter . . . !"



"letter?"



"yes. that tell you."



"tell I what?"



"what you ought to know."



"about whom? about what?"



"Him."



"your husband?"



"no. he and she."



Francis have learn patience in deal with his parishioner, who

be incapable of a direct statement. Mrs. Lipsett have no intention of

be mysterious. it only show that she could not bring herself to

"I cann't have it in the house. I cann't lose my lodger. it would

frighten the lodger."



"what would?"



Mrs. Lipsett look desperate.



"don't you know?"



"no, I don't," reply Francis, rather petulantly.



Mrs. Lipsett have rise to her foot. now she sink back again into her

chair and begin to cry. Francis prefer that to her incoherence.



"my good woman," he say. "you seem to be in some trouble. if I can

give you any consolation . . ."



"I be in trouble," moan Mrs. Lipsett. "I'm always in trouble. I've

never be out of trouble since I be bear. some people be like that

you know."



these reflection cheer she up perceptibly, and she ask Francis if

he would mind if she begin to cook the first floor's tea.



Francis begin to feel exasperate.



"my good woman," he say, "will you kindly explain what my son have to

do with all this, and why he have bring I here?"



Mrs. Lipsett have move to the table and take up an armful of linen.



"don't he tell you?"



"no."



Mrs. Lipsett drop her linen, run to the door and scream "Annie!"



a voice answer she.



"come here!"



Mrs. Lipsett turn to Francis, fold her arm and with her lip

tight pressed she worry out her word:



"not tell you, haven't he? leave I to make a nice fool of myself!

I've hear of you, Mr. Folyat! that innocent you be that you don't

know you're bear yet. . . ."



Annie come in and cut short anything else she might have to say.



"yes, mother?"



"ben't Mr. Folyat with you?"



"no. I think he be here with you."



"Sloped, have he? Sloped!--This be Mr. Folyat's father."



"good evening," say Annie.



an awkward silence come on the three of they, and all three thought of

Frederic with vary degree of wrath.



"my daughter . . ." begin Mrs. Lipsett.



"Mother!"



"tell he yourself then."



Annie blush.



"I cann't."



Mrs. Lipsett drop into the vernacular.



"eh! I be vex!"



Francis take his hat and rise with some dignity.



"I be sorry," he say, "but as neither of you seem disposed to

enlighten I . . ."



Annie stand between he and the door. she blurt out:



"it's Fred, Mr. Folyat. it ben't fair of he to leave you alone with

mother like that. we see you go by and he say he'd go and tell

you. I suppose he don't. he's like that. he mean well."



"mean well!" this from Mrs. Lipsett.



"please, mother!" Annie go on. "Fred ought to have tell you, Mr.

there's some thing you cann't help. we don't think, I suppose. but

it's come to that, that we've get to think. I'm go to be a mother

in three month, and Fred want to help as much as he can."



Francis sit down again.



"Frederic!"



"yes. the beauty! ben't you proud of he?"



Frederic! Francis be not so much shocked as amazed. he be only too

accustom to irregularity, large and small, but he have always

regard the victim of it as creature of another clay. automatically

by their offence they pass from one compartment of his mind to

another. where possible they be give benefit of clergy, but only as

one find a home for a stray dog. . . .



Mrs. Lipsett say:



"I say he ought to marry she."



Francis do not hear she. he be still try to grasp the fact, but

once more he find himself confront with the difficulty that he

could not take Frederic seriously. that Frederic should be, regularly

or irregularly, on the point of become a father strike he as comic

that in course of time the fate that have overtake himself should

overtake his son also. but also a man be usually give time to get

accustomed to the idea. in the ordinary course a man introduce a

young woman to his father and mother--(with a pang he think of Mrs.

Folyat's reception of this event!)--they be engage, married, and as

bluntly as possible the Church service announce the probable

consequence. everything go smoothly and one hope for the good. but

Frederic, the buffoon, the play-actor, have dispense with all this;

have, by a sort of conjure trick, inveigle he into a strange house,

and leave he with a very cool and collect young woman with a strong

accent and an angry mother whose speech be of the broad, and

he admit to himself that his one desire be to avoid have his

perspective)--and, with a very real feeling for the two woman, he

"I'm sorry. what do you want I to do?"



"you're not angry?"



Francis fall back with some relief on formula:



"I be deeply pain and grieve. . ." but then the new little

conscience there be develop in he cry out on his insincerity

and he be silent.



Mrs. Lipsett repeat:



"I say he ought to marry she. I say . . ."



"he don't want to marry I," say Annie. "he say he know he

couldn't make I happy."



"what's right be right," say Mrs. Lipsett. "can he afford to marry

she, Mr. Folyat? can you make he marry she?"

"it be not I who be to be consider, but these two woman. Frederic be

least of all to be consider."



he do his good to think of Frederic as a husband, but it be quite

"I be sorry, very sorry, extremely sorry. I will see my son. I will do

"promise," say Mrs. Lipsett, "be like pie-crust--make to be

"not mine," return Francis, as he bow himself out.

"I only want to get away, sir. I only want to get away."

"God bless you, my dear. you shall."

"surely she have suffer enough."



and he feel a purely masculine anger against Frederic, anger which

"after all," think Francis, "he be my son. I have have my share in

"I wonder what they've say to he. I suppose he's devilish angry."



and he fall to count up his income and his debt, and wonder

"I don't mean to leave you like that. I mean to have it all out in

"I don't think any explanation be necessary," reply Francis in a

curious toneless voice.



"by George! he _be_ angry!" think Frederic.



"I only want to know one thing," say Francis. "do you seduce the

young woman with a promise of marriage?"



Frederic steal a glance at his father. it be such an odd question

"there be never anything say of marriage from begin to end.

there never be in these case. it's so casual, you know. it seem to

dead earnest . . ."



"silence."



"sorry."



they walk on for a quarter of a mile.



"do anybody know?"



"only our two self, Annie and her mother--oh! and Annette."



"Annette!"



Francis be really angry. the thing have touch his new affection, the

"no."



"be you go to provide for she?"



"I'll do my good."



"after this do you expect I to allow you to stay in my house?"



"I'll clear out if you like."



"I do like."



"very well then. only you lose the right to interfere in the matter,

or in my affair in any way."



"I never have interfere in your affair."



"no. . . . you'll let I come and see my mother?"



that bring Francis up short. (Frederic know it would.) Frederic be

"I cann't let you do that."



"I must."



"I'm not go to have you interfere . . ."



"you will not marry she. I can conceive of no great misfortune

befall she than marriage with . . ."

"all the same I must see that she be not . . ."

"I'm glad of that. I'll leave it in your hand and neither of we will

"no," say Francis, profoundly ashamed.



Frederic begin to hum, and they walk on until they come to the Park.



Frederic say:



"I have a sort of feel you'd take it like that. you never let we

know much of what you're think and all that. I suppose you think

much as I despise myself, but what I most despise be the way I've let

"I know I should say the wrong thing," reply Frederic, more to

he be very tired. he feel as though he have be engage in a long,

the use of the word paramour strike Francis as particularly absurd,

physical and spiritual, the struggle to reach and maintain health;

and hatred only for hypocrisy and fraud and slovenliness.



"verily I say unto you, whosoever shall not receive the Kingdom of God

as a little child shall in no wise enter therein."



he put down the Bible and take up "Tom Jones," and remember an

"all the same," he say, "it be a long stride from Tom Jones to

with that he fall to thinking of the student in Dublin and the man of

old day, and wonder what might have become of they all and if they

have fare well or bad than himself.







XVI



MRS. FOLYAT dissect



_if you have marry a conscientious Bishop and make_ he _live in a

pig-stye--à la bonne heure!_

     JOHN RUSKIN



from be a governess with extremely small wage Annette become a

servant with no wage at all. a few month after her return to her

father's house, Ada, the cook-general, married (beneath she) and she

be replace by a gnomish child of sixteen who wear short dress and

have her hair do up at the back in a tight little bun. she talk an

entirely unintelligible language and delight the Folyat family on

the day after her arrival by say to Annette, who happen to be in

the kitchen:



"Eeh! Annie,"--never a "Miss" from a north-country girl--"Eeh! Annie,

will ye whack t' pot on t' table while I wash I 'ead?"



Annette oblige, and "whack the pot on the table" become the

family euphemism for get a meal ready.



Gertrude and Mary have gradually retire from active service--Mary with

well excuse than Gertrude--and the whole administration of the

household devolve on Annette. nothing be say to she about it, no

arrangement be make; it just happen, and nobody notice that it have

happen. from early morning when she prepare tea for her mother, to

late at night when she boil her chocolate, Annette be cook,

wash up, dust, make the bed, &c., and her only excursion,

except to church or the school, be to the shop to buy the

wherewithal to cook, wash-up, dust, &c. Nobody ever thank she: for

many week nobody remark that she be do so much, and then Serge

find she drag a heavy coal-scuttle up the stair to his studio,

relieve she of it and question she. after that, when he be at

home, he do what he could to assist she in the heavy work.



as for Mrs. Folyat, she be a very lily, in that she toil not

neither do she spin. when she think of it, she resent the decline

and fall of her kitchen from cook-housemaid and parlourmaid to the

sixteen-year-old hobgoblin, but, resentment be rather an active

state of mind, she avoid it by give no thought to the matter.



if Mrs. James Lawrie could be liken to a garden roller, Mrs. Folyat

could most nearly be say to resemble a mill-stone. she be of the

great and ignoble army of people who be neither good nor bad,

renounce their potentiality in either direction, and drag all those

to whom they cling--for cle they must if they be to remain above

ground--down to the low depth of impotence, than which there be no

bad state. she make herself comfortable with fiction and prefer

everything to truth. an amazing capacity she have for compelling other

to acquiesce in her self-deception by tickle their sentimentality

so that it rise in they like a flood of treacle and slop over their

imagination and critical faculty. have it ever occur to she to

exercise this power in print she might have become an enormously

successful novelist. she be to all appearance much love, and all

her acquaintance and many of those whom she call her friend always

speak of she as "dear Mrs. Folyat." she be never unhappy, but, on the

other hand, she be never happy. in all material matter she be a

furious optimist. she like eat and sleep and gossip and

go to the theatre and reading. if she could indulge in all these

seemingly harmless pleasure to the extent of her appetite it seem

to she that all be well with the world.



when she marry Francis, ambition be stir in she and satisfied.

through the long year at St. Withans she bear he child with great

regularity and also with the indifference of an automaton. she

regard herself as a perfect wife because she be faithful, and as a

perfect mother for no other reason than that she be a mother. when

her child offend she she chastise they, when they please she

she kiss and fondle they. on the whole she bring they up on the

principle of Rabelais' Abbé: _Fais ce que vouldra._ on that principle

also she conduct her own life, but, unhappily, she never want

anything much.



she believe herself to be a Christian. she be so familiar with the

Bible that it have absolutely no meaning for she. her memory be

astonishing, so that she do not need to read the book. her childhood

have be spend in an atmosphere of great piety, and she have absorb

the whole scripture from Genesis to Revelation, through the pore of

her skin rather than through her brain. what most nearly penetrate

her consciousness be, curiously enough, the prophecy of the end of

the world: _there shall be war and rumour of war_, and every now

and then she indulge herself in the luxury of terror, read sign

in everything. she be extremely superstitious and would never walk

under a ladder, nor sit thirteen at a table, and when a mirror be

break in the house or salt be spill or knife be cross, she

would see in the next disaster, great or small, the infallible

for that portend luck; alarm on an encounter with a cross-eyed

woman, for that bode no good. her mind be like a dusty empty room,

the door of which be seal with cobwebs, show that she have not

for many year pass out nor have any enter in. she be romantic and

picturesque, love the romance of fiction, and entirely oblivious of

the romance of fact. only twice in her life do she deliver herself of

utterance the least philosophical, and as, be what she be, her

sincerity must remain suspect, neither can be take as give a clue

to the inward working of her mind. these be they:



(1) long after Gertrude be marry and have live through her little

tragi-comedy she say:



"all man be beast. I marry the good of they, and he's a beast."



(2) when one of her grandchildren--(this be a digression we may

skirmish up and down the alley of time)--beset by philosophic doubt,

want to know what be go to happen to the world she make this

pronunciamento:



"the world will go on get bad and bad until the end of

everything come, just as the Bible tell you. there shall be war and

rumour of war, . . . &c."



at the back of her mind during all her adult life be the belief in

the proximity of the end of the world, and in her inevitable

translation to divine region, where, with her husband, she would live

an untroubled and unsexed life of uninterrupted habit. she take she

husband with she, partly because he be a clergyman and have a

prescriptive right to a heavenly mansion, but chiefly because, after

it be all very hazy, but it be towards this future that she turn

when she say her prayer morning and evening. this she do as

operation she devote herself to her public duty as rector's

wife--Bible class, mother' meeting, and mission work--and to the

cultivation of the near approach to a passion in her existence,

gentility. she spend many solitary hour in the drawing-room because

she could not sit with Francis in his study, as she dislike the smell

of tobacco and detest his allegiance to a clay pipe. she be hardly

ever know to stoop to enter the kitchen.



Withal her authority be never question, and she obtain from her

family, their friend and acquaintance, the homage and service she

expect.



she be a match-maker, and no combination of male and female be too

grotesque for she. she be delighted with Gertrude's engagement.

Bennett Lawrie's personality lend itself to sentimental heroic, and

she be more than a little in love with he herself--as a little girl

be in love with the first-comer. Minna's plurality in affair of the

heart baffle and annoy she, for in love she always look for

constancy. she have mark down Streeten Folyat for Mary, though,

beyond send a brace of grouse every August, he show little sign

of desire the more acquaintance of his cousin. . . . Annette and

scheme, but, go his own way, he ever evade she until, having

fail in her high flight, she come to look nearer home. the

Clibran-Bells have inherit money, and there be only one life between

they and a large fortune, so that all the girl would possess some

three hundred a year, while George would eventually be a man of large

mean, for the money come through Mrs. Clibran-Bell and avoid Mr.

occasion great excitement in Fern Square, and the Clibran-Bells

add another servant to their two. they also make a gift of two new

altar-cloth and a chalice to the church. one of the altar-cloth be

work by Jessie Clibran-Bell with embroidery and appliqué. she be an

accomplish needlewoman, have many little talent, and she be

intelligent and pious. she be the eld of the family and the most

nearly beautiful. her nose be straight and like her mother's, whereas

her sister have unfortunately go to their father for their nose and

Jessie be two year old than Frederic. she be just a little

austere in temperament, singularly pure and innocent in mind. the wave

the most beautiful lady in the world, the "very pinnacle of human

virtue." (the phrase be her father's, often on his lip, and Minna

always refer to Mrs. Clibran-Bell as "the Pinnacle.")



it may be ennoble and purify to idealise your womenkind, but if

your womenkind accept the position they be rather apt to believe,

with disastrous result, that it be more blessed to receive than to

give. certain it be that if Robert Clibran-Bell have an ideal, he never

have a wife, and that his child never have a mother.



Jessie Clibran-Bell in her simplicity believe that the Folyats have

all that she have lack. she be devoted to Francis, and when Mrs.

Folyat play her sentimentalist's game with she she be entirely

deceive, see in Mrs. Folyat a perfect hen of a mother and creep under

the more warmly do Jessie's fondness flow. they both enjoy

themselves thoroughly, and often meet in conclave in the Folyat

this new intrigue that she lose interest in Gertrude's affair and

devote herself to the snaring of Frederic.







XVII



FREDERIC snare



_there be a special providence in the fall of a sparrow._

     HAMLET



the snaring of man be the tame sport in the world. it be so

ridiculously easy. let but the female cast a favourable eye upon the

male and he be her--for as long as she be clever enough to keep he.

whether a prize so easily win be worth the keeping be a matter for

every woman to decide for herself. generally the matter be settle by

the advent of child, or by economic complication, or by fear of

public opinion. Desire wait upon vanity and vanity be the destroyer

of love. unhappily passion be so exceedingly rare that there would be

neither marriage nor give in marriage if man and woman do not

hoodwink themselves and each other. quite clearly the world would be

the well without the hoodwinking and the marriage result from

it, but, these be in the majority, and the ignoble art of

hoodwinking be pass on from generation to generation, and

commend by eminent divine and popular writer, and since woman

insist on get marry in all circumstance and at whatever cost of

degradation and disappointment, there be nothing to be do but to

grin and bear it and applaud every active protest that be make against

it.



these be the sentiment rouse in Serge Folyat when it be announce

that Frederic have enter upon an indefinite engagement to marry

Jessie Clibran-Bell.



quite other and not at all philosophical be the sentiment of

Frederic's father when the announcement be make to he exactly a week

after his visit to Miller Street, to the house of Mrs. Lipsett. he be

shocked and outraged, but as the announcement be make to he by his

take by surprise. she have make Frederic take she and Jessie to the

pantomime, and on the way home Jessie have steal her hand into her

and say:



"I be so happy."



and Frederic have add:



"yes. ben't she?"



and then she know! and Frederic be so proud and happy too. and so

brave and manly! he could not think of marry Jessie until he be

make three hundred a year. and don't Francis think it be time they

set Frederic up in a practice by himself?



Francis groan inwardly.



it would be delightful (continue Mrs. Folyat) to have Frederic

settle. of course he would only have a small establishment to begin

with, but when he have make his position, he would be able to live in

the good suburb on the south of the town and his son would go to

public school. Jessie be such a dear girl, as Francis would find

when he know she well, and she be so devotedly attach to

Frederic, and Frederic be so very much in love, so chivalrous and

attentive. nothing well could be wish for. Francis must really

consider the possibility of provide Frederic with an office of his

own.



"I'll think it over," say Francis. "if you don't mind, I would like

to sleep."



Mrs. Folyat continue her monologue for a quarter of an hour and

lull herself to sleep with the sound of her own voice.



Francis lie on his back stare into the darkness. his first impulse

be to go up to Frederic's room and have it out with he there and

then, but he could hardly do that without wake the woman sleep at

his side. also he have make it a rule never to act in any difficulty
