without a night's cogitation. the trouble be that this new

complication seem to he so hideous that he hate to think of it. in

the cause of morality, also for the sake of Jessie Clibran-Bell, he

ought to denounce Frederic and fle he out neck and crop. but common

sense bid he pause. what would be the result? a great deal of

wretchedness and misery in two house, and in all probability

Frederic's utter ruin.



already he be an accessory after the fact of Frederic's first

dishonour. could he become an aider and abettor of the second? or,

rather, having swallow the first could he reasonably strain at the

second? . . . he condemn himself for his weakness in palliate such

an offence for the sake of peace. then, rebound from

self-condemnation--(no man can keep it up for very long)--he tell

himself that it be not for the sake of peace but to save that poor

girl from a drudge life with a man out of her own class. then, in

justice, he be force to admit that the truth lie between the two.



his final conclusion, just as dawn begin to outline the window, be

that the world must be much less or more simple than he have think.

the effort of decide which the world be entirely exhausted he, and

sleep come at last.



in the morning he have a letter from his brother William, the first for

fifteen year, announce his return from India and settlement at

Sydenham, near the Crystal Palace, where he would be glad to see

Francis, his wife, or any of his child. how many be there? he,

William, have two.



Francis hand it over to his wife just as Frederic come down.



"ben't you go to congratulate Frederic, my dear?" ask Mrs.

Folyat.



Frederic look across at his father with malicious defiance in his

eye. Francis open another letter and ignore the question. Mrs.

Folyat return to the charge.



"my dear, Frederic be to be congratulate."



"I be as delighted," reply Francis, "as Frederic be himself."



Frederic viciously slice off the top of an egg. Mrs. Folyat seem to

be satisfied. she read William's letter.



"that will be very nice," she say. "Gertrude could stay with they on

her way back from the Folkestone Folyats."



Frederic go to the door and bawl peevishly to Annette to bring his

coffee.



"Annette," observe Francis, "be not a servant."



"I know," return Frederic, "but I cann't be late."



Annette appear with Frederic's coffee. he give she no thank, and

she return to cook breakfast for Serge, Minna, and Gertrude. (Mary

be away on a visit.)



"I think," say Francis, "I think Annette might be the first to stay

with William."



"Annette!" Mrs. Folyat sweep she out of consideration. "Annette! she

have no clothe."



Frederic gulp down his coffee and hurry away.



"it will be time," say Mrs. Folyat, "it will be time to think of

Annette when Gertrude and Mary and Minna be marry."



"and suppose they never marry?"



"of course they will marry."



Serge come down in Frederic's dressing-gown, and shortly afterwards

Minna and Gertrude follow he.



"any news?" ask Minna.



"my dear . . ."



Mrs. Folyat wriggle with excitement.



"my dear. what do you think? Frederic take I and Jessie to the

pantomime last night; I think it vulgar and most unsuitable for

child. and what do you think? Frederic and Jessie be engage."



"how clever of you, ma," say Minna.



"I! I be entirely take by surprise."



Minna grin:



"so be Wellington when he find he have win the battle of Waterloo."



Francis gather up his letter and the daily paper, a Conservative

organ, together with the _Church Times_, and turn to Serge.



"if you can give I a moment or two," he say, "I should like your

opinion on a matter of some importance."



"delighted," answer Serge.



five minute later Serge knock at the study door, go in, and find

his father at his desk write a letter. Francis lay down his pen and

turn.



"I want your opinion as a man of the world. I find myself in a

situation with which I be not competent to deal, and yet I must deal

with it."



"my experience be," say Serge, "that most problem solve themselves."



"this be a moral problem."



"moral problem crumble away under the pressure of time more easily

than any other."



Francis be not encourage. however, he go on:



"Frederic . . ."



"ah! I think it must be about Frederic."



"Frederic have propose to and be accept by Jessie Clibran-Bell."



"a very estimable young woman, though she have no sense of humour."



"Frederic be also entangle . . ."



"with the daughter of a lodge-house keeper."



"you know that?"



"yes. I know that."



"you can imagine then what pain and sorrow this must have cause I."



"yes. it be always distress to find fiction overturn by fact."



"you do not condemn Frederic?"



"it be surely one of the first principle of religion to condemn

nobody."



"true. true. but one must not encourage immorality."



"nothing encourage immorality so much as condemnation and

prohibition."



"be that how man of the world think of it?"



"I don't know. it be how I think of it."



Francis comb his finger through his beard.



"then . . . then, what be I to do?"



"it seem to I that the difficulty have already solve itself. Miss

Clibran-Bell be in love with Frederic. she will probably make he a

good wife. Frederic could not possibly marry the other girl. it would

destroy all her chance of marry a man whom she could love, honour

and respect . . ."



"but he have destroy her chance."



"not at all. she will be a soberer, a well and a more sympathetic

woman after this experience, if she be help through it and treat

with decent human feeling . . . Frederic be finish as far as she be

concerned."



"I tell Frederic he must leave my house. I go back on it."



"that be just as well. it would have make my mother very unhappy and

cause a bitter scandal in your parish. these thing be nobody's

affair until they be everybody's affair. the only sane course to

pursue be to see that they do not become everybody's affair!"



"what do you suggest?"



"do what you can for the girl and leave Frederic alone. no man can

trifle with his emotion with impunity. that be natural law, Divine

law if you like, infinitely more search than your law of crime and

punishment. the trouble with you people be that you think moral law

be a human invention. they're not. they be an inherent principle of

the universe, and we be as subject to they as we be to the weather.

this thing be Frederic's affair and his only. you and I know perfectly

well that he willn't look after the girl if he be leave to himself,

therefore you and I must interfere, for purely humane reason, as you

do with your parishioner, and as I do with any human trouble that I

happen to come across. you can give the girl a few pound to take she

down into the country. she'll be much well there, and you can allow

she, say, ten shilling a week until she get work or marrie."



"I be just write to she," say Francis. "I ben't sure whether it's

right."



"perhaps it ben't," reply Serge. "but at least it be practical."



"I be glad to have talk it over with you. should I say anything to

Frederic?"



"no. if you want to hurt he--though I don't see why you should--you

will do so far more by simply ignore he and take the affair out

of his hand."



"thank you. I'll write to the young woman."



"if you like I'll find a place in the country for she."



"that will be good of you. thank you."



this conversation with Serge relieve Francis enormously. he be like

a man who, after long hesitation at a cross-road have follow one way

for a mile or two, and then need reassure. he have already write

half his letter to Annie Lipsett. he thoroughly enjoy complete it.



Serge leave he at it and find his mother wait for he by the

dining-room door. she say she want to speak to he, draw he into

the room, and begin to cross-examine he as to what his business might

have be with Francis. he tell she it be nothing of any importance,

and then with a great deal of hesitation she come to her business.



"don't you think Jessie be just the very wife for Frederic, Serge?"



"the usual remark that she be far too good for he seem to be

peculiarly appropriate."



"Serge, do Frederic ever talk to you about himself?"



"only in his more light-hearted moment."



there be a moment's hesitation on Mrs. Folyat's part. then:



"Serge, there be an odious woman pursue Frederic. she be threaten

he. have he tell you?"



"no. but I know."



"oh! serge, please, please, cann't you save he from her clutch? I

have be so wretched about it. don't let he marry she!"



"that," say Serge with gusto, "that he shall not do if I can help

it."



"oh! Serge, thank you. . . . don't let Frederic know I tell you, and

don't say anything to your father. it would upset he so dreadfully."



"no. I willn't say anything to either."



"oh! serge. I shall be grateful to you as long as I live. why do

Heaven allow such creature . . . . ?"



"I must get to my work," say Serge. he kiss his mother and pat

her shoulder, and stay with she until she have dry her eye and

look up at he with a watery smile.



later in the morning, hear Annette in the next room, he call to

she, and when she come he ask she:



"do mother read father's letter?"



"she read any letter she can find. I don't think she can help it,"

say Annette, blush for her own lapse.



"wicked old woman," chuckle Serge. "would you like a day in the

country, one Saturday, Annette?"



"I should love it more than anything."



"you shall have it. you're the only person in this house who deserve

well of the world, and to taste the sweetness of thing. possibly

you're the only person who can."



"I would like," say Annette, "I would like to go to a river."



"so you shall, the very good river we can find."



"you're very good to I, Serge."



Annette be too busy to stay talk. Serge turn to his work and she

stride away.



* * *



as Francis have promise, so it be do. Serge find room for Annie

Lipsett in a not too dull village. her mother's lodger be tell that

she be run down and go away for a change, and would be away for

three month. they receive the intelligence with about as much

interest as though they have be tell that the ceiling need

whitewashing--as they do--and Annie go away. the only condition

that have be make be that she should not write to Frederic. her

mother shed a great many tear, but promise to come and see she once

a week and to be near she when her time come.



Frederic be receive with open arm by his prospective father, mother

and sister-in-law. the Clibran-Bells and the Folyats join in

rejoice over he, and he find himself doom to slavery. he

affect the attitude of the devoted swain, and every minute of his

day, outside his work hour, be give to Jessie, her mother, she

sister, her father, her brother, her cat. he go nowhere alone with

she. he go nowhere without she. . . . they be to be marry as

soon as he be earn three hundred a year. he look ahead and see

no prospect of it. he become very envious of people who be happy.







xviii



EXCURSION



_enter these enchant wood,

     you who dare!_

          the woods of WESTERMAIN



MRS. FOLYAT have her way--as when do she not?--and it be Gertrude,

equip cap-à-pie with new clothe, who go to stay with her uncle

William at Sydenham, near the Crystal Palace. therefore she be not of

the party which grow out of Serge's promise to take Annette into the

country on a Saturday. Annette have be unable to keep this entrancing

project to herself. Minna have half suggest, half demand, that she

should be of the party. to square the number Serge have ask Basil

Haslam, and Minna out of coquetry have invite Herbert Fry, Frederic's

quondam Plymouth comrade, who have turn up on legal business, which,

move slowly, have keep he many week, so that, to while away the

tedious hour, he have resume relation with she. he be still

"Apollyon," have an air of great prosperity, flattered Mrs. Folyat up

to the eye, so that he be altogether in her good grace, and she

entertain hope of his carry Minna back with he to London. (he

have tell Frederic, but not Mrs. Folyat nor Minna that he be marry.)

to pair with either Haslam or Fry, as the case might be, Mary be

include, and, in compassion for his forlornness in the absence of his

"old, old love," Bennett Lawrie.



Serge pay. Annette make up a great basket of provision which Bennett

Lawrie and Basil Haslam carry between they.



Less than an hour's journey take they to a great river where they

hire two boat--a double-sculler and a dinghy. Basil Haslam try to

manoeuvre Minna into the dinghy, but could not detach she from she

"Apollyon," and be force to relinquish the little boat to serge and

Annette, who jump into it while the rest be argue, push off,

and row away up stream, leave they to follow in the big boat.



"our party," say Serge, as he send the little boat skim over the

water, while Annette dip her finger over the side and let the

water gurgle up her arm.



"but I'm glad the other come," answer Annette. "that boy Lawrie

look so pale."



Serge make she take the rudder line and teach she how to steer.



"how red your hand be get," he say.



"it's the housework."



"what a shame!"



"oh! I like it."



"well than governesse?"



"oh! much, much well. it's home, you see. and, of course, there's

you. I often sit in your room when you're not there, and sometimes I

look at the thing. it must be wonderful to be able to--to draw."



"now, why?"



"I don't quite know, only when you come to beautiful place like this

it make you want to--want to . . ."



"well?"



"I don't quite know. . . . it's like grow . . ."



"that's quite good. I'd like to know what you think of I, Annette?"



"you're very puzzling. sometimes I think you don't take anything

seriously, but then I think it be because you be so different."



"how different?"



"not like Frederic."



out of the bank near they scuttle a vole, and along and into a hole

under the root of a willow. Annette watch he eagerly, and then

return to Serge, and say:



"don't let's talk about Frederic. I be so happy."



Serge begin to sing. he have very fine deep note, but his voice fail

he in the upper register, and whenever it crack he laugh, and

when he laugh Annette have to join in. he could never remember any

song through to the end, and he invent the most absurd word. then

over a long stretch, as he row, he sing a melancholy canoe-song in a

minor key that he have hear on the Zambesi. he sing it over and over

again.



"I like that," say Annette. "do you know, often when I'm in the

kitchen I think I'm in a boat sail away and away. it's like

dream, only it go on and on . . ."



"that's love."



"be it? . . . that's nonsense. I'm not in love."



"not _in_ love, my dear. but it's love all the same! your little soul

grow and expand, try to find an outlet, a channel that will

lead it to warmth and the sun . . ."



"you make I feel unhappy when you talk like that."



"you're wise than I be, Annette. you accept thing where I think

about they."



"we mustn't lose the other."



"we shalln't lose they. they'll have to come on until they find we. if I

think that Fry be row I'd take he ten mile, but I'm pretty

sure he ben't."



"you don't like he."



"no. do you?"



"no. but he's very pleasant."



"you can admire what you don't like?"



"I like to admire people. when I'm work it's pleasant to remember

the thing they do and say, and the way they say they."



"so you're a pleased and uncritical audience of the doing in Fern

Square?"



Annette dodge the question. she give a long sigh, and say:



"I be enjoy myself; but I like good be alone with you. it's such

a glorious day."



and then she begin to tell he some of the story she compose about

he for Deedy Fender's benefit. when she have do she add:



"of course, I never imagine anything like you."



"be you disappointed?"



"oh! no."



they come to a great wood grow down to the water's edge. Serge run

the boat into the bank and moor she. he fill his pipe and begin to

smoke, then lie back with his head on the little seat in the bow.

Annette sit with her hand in her lap, and they bask in the hot sun

and feel that it be very good. the bird be very merry in the

tree. in the tree the wind whisper song gather from the sea

only twenty mile away. over all blaze the sun. fly dance above

the water. all be harmony and peace.



round the bend of the river come the other boat. Bennett Lawrie and

Basil Haslam be row. Mary be steer, and on each side of she

be Minna and Herbert Fry.



Fry call out:



"you've lead we a nice dance. it be an hour past lunch time."



Serge grin and shout pleasantly:



"all the well for eat, my dear."



the big boat bump into the dinghy and moor alongside. the

luncheon-basket be haul out, and on the grass under the tree a

cloth be spread. they sit round it, and for some time be silent

until their hunger begin to be appease.



"at half-past three," say Serge, "I be go to bathe. will you join

I, Basil?"



Haslam assent.



"what about you, Lawrie?"



"I would like to, only I cann't swim."



"you can bob up and down in the shallow."



"I don't think I will," say Bennett miserably.



"some one," comment Minna, "must stay and look after we. you cann't

leave three sister alone."



"Fry will protect you from each other," say Serge.



"delight," rejoin Herbert Fry, with a gallant glance at Minna.



Mary say:



"this pie be perfectly delicious, Annette. you certainly make pastry

well than any of we."



"Mary's first remark to-day," say Minna, maliciously.



Mary, who have be most amiably dispose, relapse into silence, then,

feel that she be damp the general cheerfulness, she make

another effort and turn to Herbert Fry, and ask he:



"I suppose you find our town very dull after London."



Herbert Fry reply:



"of course, you know, London be the only place to live in."



"it obviously ben't that," say Serge, "since there be million of

people who don't live in it, don't want to live in it, have never be

there, and also many million who have never hear of it."



Minna be startle.



"Hullo, Serge! you go to defend our horrid, dirty town?"



"it don't need I to do that. it be quite satisfied with itself.

there be really something admirable about its hard, conceited pride.

we don't really belong to it, be parasitic. if we do, we should be

like the rest, blind ourselves with a tragic vanity."



"whether I'm a parasite or not," rejoin Minna, "I'm go to get out

of it as soon as I can."



"so be I," say Haslam. "I'm go to London at the end of the year.

I've only be there once, but it be a fine place, and no mistake."



"I've be there twice," say Minna. "Mary's be three time. Annette

never. have you be, Bennett?"



Bennett be rather take aback at be draw into the conversation.

he be rather shy of Minna.



"no," he say. "I've never be to London. my father have be. I don't

suppose I shall ever go. it's such a long way. it must be a wonderful

place. I've read a lot about it."



"I don't think they have nearly such good music as we have here . . ."

Mary have wait very patiently to produce the remark which have be in

her mind when she first speak. she do so with such a flourish that

she bring the conversation to an end. Serge wind it up with:



"we don't come into the country to talk of town."



"no," say Minna. "we come to have lunch, and a very good lunch it have

be."



she rise to her foot with a whimsical right-and-leave glance at Haslam

and Fry, as though she be hazard which to take with she. both

spring up together as she move away, but Haslam be the quick and

reach her side first. they disappear into the wood, and Fry

return sulkily to the rest of the party. Annette begin to gather the

plate, knife and fork to take they down to the water.



"shall I help you?" say Serge.



"no, thank you. I think Bennett might, as he's the young."



Annette have be feel very sorry for Bennett. he seem so

solitary, so much out of his element, so unable to cope with grow man

like Serge and Basil and the lordly Londoner, Fry. he accept she

invitation with obvious relief, take her burden, and carry it down

to the water's edge, under a willow trail its leave in the water.



Herbert Fry offer his escort to Mary, and she acquiesce, bridle.



Serge be leave alone. he lie on his back and gaze up at the

sky--blue, serene, cheering, and comfort. his body relax, and he

give himself up to the sweetness of the day's mood, not without a

final drowsy reflection:



"if such a moment of contentment as this be the high good, and,

since it can be procure at the cost of a little physical labour

reward by a solid meal, what's the good of all the rest? the answer

to that be that one cannot live alone. what a day for love-making!" he

laugh. "everything lead back to that."



he think of Herbert Fry fob off with Mary, and he chuckle. then

he think of Bennett Lawrie and Annette together by the water. he

raise himself up. he could not see they, but he could hear their

voice.



"what a day!" he say again, and add "for love-making."



* * *



down by the river Annette and Bennett be at first very shy of each

other. in silence she hand he the plate, and he dip they in the

water and hand they back to she and she dry they; then the fork,

and when they come to the knife, Bennett think:



"why cann't I say something?"



and Annette think:



"why cann't I say something?"



she look out along the shine river, slow-move under its green

bank; never a house, never a boat in sight, and Bennett be bend

down entirely engross in his occupation. it be his air of complete

absorption in everything he do and say (though he never do and

never say anything remarkable) that interest she and make she want

to know more of he.



at last, when they have finish, very timidly she ask he:



"be you go to be a clergyman?"



"I'm afraid not."



"oh! I'm sorry!" she remember very vividly his earnestness in her

father's study.



"it cost too much money, you know. and my mother don't believe in

I. it wouldn't be any good if she do, because there ben't any

money."



Annette could only say again:



"I'm sorry."



instead of move away, she sit down on the bank, and Bennett kneel

quite near she. seek to explain away her desire to stay, she say:



"it's so lovely here."



"it's not so beautiful as Scotland."



"or Westmoreland."



"have you be to Scotland?"



"I be at school in Edinburgh."



"my father come from Scotland."



they exchange the history of their respective father. his be a

mournful tale of a gradual descent into poverty, and he end:



"I suppose I shall be a clerk all my life, unless I run away and

become an actor."



"an actor?"



"yes. I should go to London. I might starve in the beginning, but I'd

be a great man in the end. I'd play Shakespeare. don't you love

Shakespeare?"



"I've never read any of his play."



"I'd like to read you some. I know some of the speech by heart."



and he deliver himself of the oration of Henry V before Harfleur.

when that be do he plunge into the address of Othello to the most

potent, grave, and reverend signior, warm to the word, lose

himself, and come to a triumphant close with: "this be the only

witchcraft that I use."



"who be she?"



"Desdemona. and in the end he smother she because a beast call

Iago tell lie about she."



"you do recite well."



"I couldn't recite badly to you."



"but what will . . . ?"



she be go to ask what Gertrude would do while he starve in

London, but she could not force Gertrude's name to her lip and she

break off the question, and cover her awkwardness by throw a twig

into the water and watch it float down the stream. Bennett seem

to know what she be go to say, for he become suddenly embarrassed

and his excited confidence ooze from he. he throw she back on

herself by ask:



"what be you go to do?"



"I--I don't know. just go on."



"I couldn't do that. anything's well than just go on."



"but it's different for you. you're a man."



"yes," say Bennett, please by the reflection that, after all, he be

a man. "yes, I suppose it be more difficult for a woman. but I shalln't

run away. I shall just go on and on be a clerk all the rest of my

life."



he be appeal to she for pity; in vain. Annette say, cheerfully:



"there must be thousand of man who be clerk, and they cann't all be

so wretched."



"some people don't mind, and the rest get use to it. I'm not like

that. I want to do thing. it ben't enough just to earn your living. a

navvy can do that. a horse do that, or a pony down in a mine."



"what else can you do?"



"you can fight against darkness, and ugliness, and cruelty, and

everything that make life horrible and ugly and terrify for

child."



"oh! for child!"



"yes. you don't know what my childhood have be like . . ." and he

draw a rapid picture of the loneliness of an imaginative child in a

dark unhappy house where no love be. "even now I'm often afraid of

the dark stair up to the attic where I sleep."



"please, please," say Annette, "don't talk of it any more. it have all

be so dark, and it be so lovely here."



"it's odd, but I've never talk like that to . . ."



he, like Annette, could not force Gertrude's name to his lip.



she begin to gather the knife and fork. then she stop and look

at he. their eye meet for a second, then his turn away.



"well?" he say.



Annette be a little troubled as she give he her answer:



"I do so want you to be happy."



she leave he on that and return to Serge. he be asleep, lie on

one side with his hand over his face. noiselessly she begin to re-pack

the basket. when she have do that she steal away into the wood, and

catch up by their happy mystery, their joy in the warm air, and the

sun she run down the first path she come to until she reach a little

place full of bracken. she fling herself down on the carpet of dead

leave and look along under the bracken stalk--the tiny forest

under the great--and watch the gleeful play of light and green

shadow. it be good to be alive and sweet to be alone.



by the river sit Bennett in an attitude of utter dejection. he try

to tell himself, as so often he have tell himself, that he love

Gertrude with a love that should defy death itself, but the idea wake

no echo in his heart. it melt not as be its habit. (it have melt

for so many, besides Gertrude, with the sick sweet longing of a boy.)

the image of Gertrude be cold. it glow not with its old brilliance

of colour. he feel curiously hollow; nothing in either head or heart

until he come to Annette's last word. she want he to be happy. he

would be. he would be. the word set he stir in a new way,

discover for he a new direction, and stiffen he up for the

journey with a sternness that he have never know before. he be half

afraid of himself and yet proud. he feel curiously detach,

independent, and strong to face all that have weigh on he so

crushingly. . . . he notice then that Annette have leave he, and he

go in search of she. he find Serge just wake up, and feel a

sudden alarm.



"Annette?" he say.



"I think she be with you."



"so she be. but she leave I only a few minute ago."



"well find she then. she cann't be go far. I'm go to bathe. no

sign of the other?"



"I haven't see they."



"all right. I'm go to bathe."



in a few second Serge have strip and run swiftly across the grass,

take a great leap head-foremost over a bramble-bush and splash into

the water. Bennett stand envy he. Serge look so strong, and he

move so beautifully and easily.



he think Annette must have go to look for Minna, and walk slowly

into the wood. he have only go a few yard when he half turn back.

he want to be alone. he half want to go and bathe with Serge, but

vanity forbid that, for he be ashamed that he could not swim. he

take Serge's prowess as a reproach to himself. that sting he into

move, and he wander down the path between the bracken until he

come to a rowan-tree in all the glory of its red berry. he stop

and pluck a handful, think he would give they to Annette. he

pass on until he come to a little clearing full of wild flower and

heather. these seem to he more beautiful than the berry. he fling

they away and fill his hand with heather and wild flower.



look up he could see the river shine through the tree and rich

green wood and blue hill beyond. he move towards the river.



presently he hear voice behind a hazel-tree and, peep, he see

Haslam and Minna sit hand in hand, he murmur, she smile. then

suddenly Haslam catch Minna to he and they kiss.



Bennett steal away, his heart flutter. what he have see send a

great emotion rush through he, but soon it wither and become

disgust. he feel a strange futile anger against the couple, an anger

so absurd that it mock he. he have idealise the whole of the Folyat

family, and to see Minna like that degrade she. he do not see she in

any ridiculous aspect. his conception of love be too boyishly lofty

for that, and yet beneath his anger and his feeling of outrage be the

sense of the ridiculous, which must accompany any intrusion into the

private affair of another.



Bennett have plenty of imagination, but he have not train it to run in

harness with his observation. his imagination have, so far, only

colour and inflame the theory he have imbibe during his education

concern human nature, and, as these theory nowhere meet the fact,

he be perpetually be shock by his observation. have, as yet,

no experience, his theory remain unassailed. he believe that he

love Gertrude Folyat with a pure and ennoble love, as a man should

love a woman; as, in fact, a man may love the Venus de Milo, a

creature of stone. a woman, accord to Bennett's docile acceptance

of trite theory, must be a goddess of beauty, purity, and chastity,

with never a worldly desire or think. the woman of his love, in

fine, must be the Virgin Mother.



that Gertrude be ten year his senior make it all the easy for he

to raise she to this exalt position in his idea. having achieve

this with she, without any reference to her wish or desire, he have

manufacture a halo for each of her sister as her attendant saint.

he have never kiss Gertrude except as a devout person kiss Saint

Peter's toe. he have dream of kiss, and have, with unholy joy,

conceive a horror of himself as a terrible and immoral young man, so

that his vanity also be implicate in this catastrophe of Minna's

downfall. what, at bottom, trouble he most of all be the obvious

truth that Minna kiss Basil Haslam because she like it.



Bennett have such a tussle with his reflection and emotion--he be

not far from call they "the devil"--that he break into a sweat, and

to seek air and coolness for his eye he make straight for the bank of

the river. he have advance only a few yard when he hear a voice

singing:



    _bury I deeply when I be dead,

     with, a stone at my foot and a cross at my head;

     and bury I deep that I ne'er may return

     to the scene of my true love--the brown scottish burn._



and he hear a splashing of water and, hide behind the huge trunk of

a beech, he look and see Annette swinge on the branch of a

chestnut tree, her foot dangle to the water and kicking and

splash. she be naked. her hair be wet and hang limp down to her

shoulder. she be as happy as a bird.



Bennett stand root. his heart, his whole be melt, and turn

away reflection, troubled emotion, all power of thought. he gaze

and gaze, and know that she be beautiful, swinge there under the

great leave of the chestnut. curiously he think that she be not so

very unlike a boy. he be fascinated. up and down she swing her

branch, scramble to her foot and dive. . . . the spell be break.

Bennett cover his face with his hand as he realise what he have

do. from the extreme of heat he turn very cold and shiver. he

find that he have let his heather and wild flower fall, pick they

up, and rush away, blindly. he lose himself and wander for a long

time before he find again the grassy plot where they have lunch. at

the same moment Minna and Basil Haslam return. Fry, Mary, and Serge

be sit, and Annette be busy boil the kettle for tea.

entirely oblivious of every one else Bennett go straight up to

Annette and hold out the wild flower and heather.



"I bring you these," he say, without look at she.



"the poor flower be dead," reply Annette, "but the heather be

lovely. thank you."



"thank you," echo Bennett.



Annette's hair be still down her back and wet. she catch he gaze

at it.



"I have such a lovely swim," she say.



"the wood," say Bennett, "be very beautiful."



Annette be really grateful to he for give she the flower. no one

have ever do as much for she before. she say:



"if you like you can row I home in the little boat."



Bennett be fill with alarm and he gaze miserably at she. he long

to accept, but he be terrify. he be rouse from his dilemma by

Basil Haslam, who, overhear Annette's remark, call out:



"the dinghy's mine and Minna's."



this he say for the benefit of Herbert Fry, who turn and look,

dog-like, upward at Minna.



a large chuckle escape Serge.



* * *



in the evening, as they turn westward under a glorious sunset,

Bennett elect to sit in the bow of the big boat. Fry and Serge

row, and Annette and Minna sit in the stern. Bennett dream

vaguely. his blood run warmly through his vein, his brain glow, and

the wind and the water sing to he. he be satisfied as he have never

be. when he think of Minna and Haslam it be with a drowsy,

delicious envy. to be together, gently glide down the river with the

evening shadow chase each other under the tree. to be together--in

a little boat--he and Annette . . . Annette . . . Annette . . .



in her lap Annette finger the heather and wild flower that Bennett

have give she and smile softly to herself. Serge see her smile, and

say:



"happy?"



"oh! yes."



to Bennett her voice sound distant and very lovely, and it seem to

he that she be speak to he, for he.



presently they pass the little boat nestle by the bank under a

plane-tree. Mary call out:



"you'll be late."



there come no reply.



they be late. it be half-past twelve before Minna reach home. the

household be asleep and Serge have stay up for she. he say:



"hardly wise to be so late."



"we miss the train."



"two or three. just as well you don't miss the last."



Minna smile.



"why?"



"I don't think you ought to use Haslam as a decoy for Fry. he's too

good for it."



"I think you're a beast, Serge."



"be I? we shall see."



"Fry's married. Frederic tell I."



"I don't think that make a ha'porth of difference--to you or to he."



"it ben't your affair."



"I agree."



"and, anyhow, you're quite wrong. good-night."



"good-night."







XIX



GERTRUDE



_nous metton l'infini dans l'amour. Ce n'est pa la faute des

femme._

     ANATOLE FRANCE.



UPON a day Bennett Lawrie escape early from his office, leave his

day's work to be finish by a co-junior clerk on a promise to do as

much for he when he should require it. he be feel very tired,

having have only a walk and two cigarette for dinner, a practice so

common among junior clerk that they have a name for it--Flag Hash.

twice during Gertrude's absence he have take Annette and her mother to

the theatre--three dress-circle seat at five shilling--a heavy drain

upon his income, which be now one pound fifteen shilling a week,

pay monthly. his mother know nothing of the advance of five shilling

a week that he have obtain on the third application with the plea

that he be engage to be marry. that help a little, but, even so,

his position be serious, and at moment make he feel very sick at

heart. he have be make effort to save money when Mrs. Folyat's

expression of regret that she have not be to the theatre plunge he

into the rash offer to pay for seat. he have no thought but that she

would pay for two of they at least. but no; Mrs. Folyat regard it as

the feminine privilege to enjoy entertainment at the expense of the

masculine pocket.



further cause have Bennett for anxiety in that his correspondence with

Gertrude have dwindle from the devoted daily letter to an effusion

with great difficulty squeeze out twice a week. that her letter have

come at long and long interval comfort he not at all. he have

never ask testimony of devotion from his betrothed; it be enough

that she should so far stoop as to be engage to he. . . . also, as

he walk to the station through the dark railway arch, through Town

Hall Square with its statue of John Bright, the late Bishop, the

Prince Consort, and a local philanthropic sweater, past the Infirmary,

he be dog by an unhappy realisation that it give he no pleasure

to be go to meet Gertrude. she have write he a romantic little

note:





"dear, I be come back to you. I have no thought but for you. I shall

arrive by the 5.45. your, G. F."





Bennett rehearse the meeting. he would greet she warmly and with

dignity. he would kiss her hand; not her cheek. he would then silently

convey that he be fully aware of his delinquence, but ask no

pardon for they. Scoundrel as he have show himself, he would have she

"pass on and thank God she be rid of a knave." . . . however, he

reflect that upon former occasion his most eloquent silence have

convey nothing at all to Gertrude, and he begin to rehearse the

scene from another standpoint. he would say; "you bade I come. I have

come. in spite of what have happen, in spite of my sin of thought

and deed, I will be loyal. I will keep my troth." that be well, but

not altogether appropriate from a station platform. he be still

rehearse when the train come in. he stand by the engine thinking

that there he would be sure not to miss his quarry. there be a

considerable crowd to meet the train, for in those day a journey from

London be an important affair, and traveller be welcome by their

near and dearest, glad that they have escape the peril of the way,

hopeful that they have not succumb to its fatigue, and mindful of

the present that would be in bag or trunk. . . . Bennett Lawrie

think not at all of present. he be only bother because he have

not yet discover the right mode of address.



the image of Gertrude that he have always chivalrously bear upon his

and with that solemn dignity of his stoop, kiss, and, if he linger

long enough over it, be kiss in return. he would take her baggage,

and carry it, as he always carry her parcel or her umbrella, as

though it be a divine trust, and they would take a four-wheel cab.

by that time one or other would have find the correct word or the

inevitable gesture of love, and all would be as it have be.



absence may make the heart grow fonder, but, where the heart be not

very deeply implicate, absence sometimes have the effect of drive

love out altogether. lover like to vow that they will never change,

but they vow the impossible, wherein lie half their pleasure. as

Gertrude Folyat have go far and far away from her boy-lover,

she have see he dwindle in stature, but with a microscopic clarity.

have a very human dislike of see thing as they be present to

she she pump up a sea of sentiment, dive into it and see blur

the newly-reveal figure. that suffice until in the gaiety of

folkestone--she never question the gaiety of what be present to

she for pleasure--and the excitement and opulence of life at Sydenham,

near the Crystal Palace, she be able to forget he altogether. it have

be in a sudden dread that he might be injure and morose when she

next see he that on the eve of her departure she have write to bid

he come to meet she. she think that would please he. as soon as

she have do so she regret it. it seem to place he in the

strong position which she have always strive to reserve for herself.

her visit have shake her resignation to marriage with he, for she

clerk, but all the same she wish to cling to he to avoid solicitude

thought of love be quite enough for she. she never doubt that the

enslave he with a tender reconciliation.



she drive home alone in a four-wheel cab to Fern Square and enjoy

"we think Bennett darling would be there," say Minna.



"be he not?" ask Mrs. Folyat.



Bennett arrive to answer the question. he too have find in anger the

this resolve ooze away almost as soon as it be make. he have no money

and sister.



the hobgoblin open the door to he.



"have Miss Gertrude return?" he ask.



"Oopstairs," say the hobgoblin, and she shuffle away to the kitchen,

leave he to close the door.



he go upstairs to find the whole family assemble, with the

have adhere to his first resolve. he feel that he be intrude, and

portrait of Gertrude's paternal grandmother.



"I go to meet you," say Bennett at length.



"I don't see you."



"if you have look for I you must have see I. I be tall enough."



there be considerable irritation behind his word.



"be I then," say Gertrude, "be I so very short that you could not see

I?"



"I wait," return Bennett. "you don't."



"I do. I wait quite five minute."



"I wait half an hour."



Gertrude take her courage in both hand and say:



"if you have care for I, you would have see I."



"I wait," mumble Bennett, obstinately.



they be silent again. Gertrude begin to feel uneasy. they have

quarrel before, but always when she have touch on his affection

for she his opposition have be break. she could not take his

"after all he be ten year young than I be."



unhappily for she, Bennett, with more malice, be think:



"after all, she be ten year old than I be."



for the first time he have become dimly aware that the advantage lay

"I leave the office early than I have any right to do to meet you. you

could not have look for I."



"why will you go on argue about it?"



"I've no wish to argue."



he only wish to avoid silence, to avoid face what be irresistibly

Gertrude be exasperate. she could have slap he with infinite

pleasure. she tap with her foot on the ground.



"you be be too ridiculous," she say.



"be I ever anything else?" return Bennett, with a sudden plunge into

self-torment.



Pat come the reply:



"never!"



Bennett feel savage, turn on she and cry:



"now I know what you think of I."



Gertrude be sorely tempt to let he think so, but she have in mind

the difficulty of confess to the woman upstairs, her mother and

he leave her gasp. she hate he in that moment. never, never, would

"I shall be twenty next March, twenty-one next year, twenty-two the

year after--twenty-nine in . . . but there. how old be you Annette?"



"Nineteen."



"have you be confirm?"



"of course. age ago. at school."



"I ben't confirm until I be sixteen. it make a great change in my

life."



"you must be very glad to have Gertrude back again."



"I be." he let the toasting-fork drop against the grate. Annette

rush at he:



"you mustn't burn it. it's for pa's toast-and-water. it must never be

burn."



the tricksy spirit which be ever lie in wait for the moment when a

catch her arm. . . . they stand like that for a second and then he

strange and melt emotion. . . .



she could see the door; he could not. she see Gertrude, and wrench

"Annette . . . I . . ."



but Annette have rush out of the kitchen and he be alone with

glow coal.



"what be you do?" ask Gertrude.



"make toast for your father's toast-and-water."



"so I see. and what be Annette do?"



"Annette be show I how to make it."



Gertrude draw herself up heroically, and with what she take for

dramatic intensity she say:



"Bennett, do you love I?"



"no," say he, startle into truth.



Gertrude sit down with emphatic suddenness. his answer have crumple

that.) he be the first to recover and he approach Gertrude with

"I'm sorry," he say. "I don't feel myself to-night. queer thing

go on inside I and outside. it ben't quite true what I say just

now. I do love you. I do, really. but love ben't what I think it

be. I don't know what it be, but it ben't what I think it be."



miserably enough Gertrude murmur:



"be you in love with Annette?"



hotly and indignantly he answer:



"no, I be not."



"but you . . ."



"I be not make love to Annette. it be an accident."



Gertrude jump at the occasion for magnanimity and say:



"I believe you."



"thank you." his heart leap within he, and privately to his own

innermost conscience he whisper delightedly:



"I be in love with Annette; in love, in love, in love with Annette."



this new idea, the admission of the new fact, so absorb he that he

month of folly through which she have drag he. he be ashamed, not

Annette.



true love can never tolerate secrecy. the true lover must cry his

emotion from the house-top, for a new glory have come to the world and

it be well that all man should know of it.



a prophet of those day have say: "the woman should not venture to

hope for or think for perfectness in he she would love, but _he_

should believe the maiden to be purity and perfection absolute and

unqualified."--The shadow of that prophet have be on Gertrude and

and find a sort of pleasure in the salt savour of her tear. he

"you willn't let anybody know just yet."



she draw the trumpery little engagement-ring he have give her--(she

have not wear it at Folkestone or Sydenham)--from her finger and lay
