serve they so well in the past, "I want you always to be my friend."



"always. always." reply Gertrude with no less fervour, and she take

his hand and press it against her cheek and kiss it.



she be smile and cheerful when Annette return. Bennett take

another slice of bread and toast it a beautiful brown, perfect for

the toast-and-water of Annette's father.







XX



EDUCATION



_as the great end of human society be to become wise and well, this

ought, therefore, to be the principal view of every man, in every

station of life._

     the BACHELOR'S CLUB



BENNETT LAWRIE'S education begin at the age of six, when, with his

sister, Phoebe, he be every morning take by Tibby to a little dame's

school where he learn the alphabet, the multiplication table,

writing, and the story of the Bible. he be also allow to draw and

teach to embroider little mat with rough silk and to make ball with

piece of wool. in five year he make a great many ball, but he be

not allow to play with they, for they be give to the poor.



when he be ten he pass from this establishment to Wellington House,

where there be no girl, but a great many rough boy who frighten

he. among they be his two eld brother, who afford he no

protection but rather supply the other with material for teasing.

Bennett could not understand that small boy should fight merely out

of bluster and cockiness. he only want to fight when rage master

he, and then he be out to kill. he only have one fight at that school

and that be enough, for he cut open his adversary's eye and tear the

lobe of his ear away from his scalp. thereafter he suffer from

collective rather than individual bullying.



he learn arithmetic as far as fraction, algebra as far as surd,

the first, second and third book of Euclid, english composition and

literature, french grammar, composition and easy translation, latin

grammar, composition and easy translation, Scripture (the book of

Samuel, king, and the act of the apostle in rotation), geography,

history (Tudor and Stuarts alternatively), elocution and dancing.

without be in the least interested in anything, he have no

difficulty in memorising for the purpose of each day the task that

be set before he. he be say to be intelligent, industrious and

eager in his work and generally satisfactory in his conduct. at the

end of each year he find himself with a prize, though he know not how

or why, and his mother become quite amiable to he. there remain one

member of her husband's family with whom she have not (as yet)

quarrel, namely Bennett's Aunt Louisa, an ex-governess who have

retire upon receipt of a legacy and take up her residence in our

town in order to be near her brother, James the failure, whom she

love, and Keith the successful merchant, whom she both fear and

dislike. this gentle lady offer to pay Bennett's fee, twelve

guineas a year, at the Grammar School, and thither accordingly he

repair with a brand new handbag and a quake heart to find himself

one of five hundred boy, of all shape and size and class and

nationality and religion--the town in little. in his first term he

be in the Lower Third Form, and sit between the son of a cab-driver

and the son of a millionaire mill-owner.



it do not matter very much what the young be teach, but it do

matter enormously who teach they. the curriculum of the Grammar

School be the curriculum of Wellington House administer in large

and more unpleasant dose. Games be not compulsory, and only one

hour per week be allow for they. what the parent want and what

they get be a good, hard, thorough grounding and no nonsense. there

may have be an ideal in the place once upon a time--(it be found

by a Bishop)--but that ideal have produce no offspring, and there be

no little ideal to grow up with Bennett's generation. Science have

be add to the available subject to be cram into the boy'

head, for the voice of Huxley be loud in the land; but though

Bennett devote two hour a week to physics and chemistry, he never

get beyond a vague notion that light and heat be not all they

seem, and a jocular idea that chemistry mean make a bad smell.



he be move up regularly once a year, but he learn no history

beyond George III, he devote four term to the study of the act of

the Apostles, he drop Latin in favour of German; having learn by

heart the river and capital of Germany, France, Italy and Spain, and

draw map of all of they, he leave geography behind, and having

study the Tudors and the Stuarts until he be sick of they, he be

suddenly, by promotion, switch away from England, and direct to

apply himself to the Thirty year' and the Seven Years' war in

Germany. having partially grasp what they be about he be promote

into the middle of the French Revolution. Robespierre be not behead

when he leave school, and he never connect the upheaval in France

with the rise of Napoleon.



his instruction in language be entirely grammarian, and he have no

notion but that the work of Corneille, Racine, Goethe and Schiller

and Gustav Freytag might have be write expressly to be annotate

by the various Masters and bachelor of Arts whose name appear on

the title-page of his text-book. he draw skeleton, profile of

girl and caricature in the margin, and beyond cram enough of

the note and the dictionary to satisfy his master he take no far

interest in they. he be never ask to do so.



his career and mental development be exactly those of ninety-five

per cent. of the boy who pass through the institution, except that

he suffer more from fear. fear be the direct force of the

machinery. the High Master be a bearded man with a huge voice, with

which he bully his assistant. the senior member of the staff

bully the junior member, and, without be give any standard of

right and wrong, the boy be punish, punish, punish:

detention, imposition, enforce drill, thrashing. the school be

enormously successful, and everybody be immensely satisfied with it,

though there be never a boy grow man who could look back with

pleasure on the year spend in its toil. there be periodical

attempt make to pump up the spirit of loyalty--_esprit de corps_--but

they always flag under the general listlessness. the boy

understand that they attend day after day to be educate, a process

which they regard as extremely unpleasant, as indeed it be, and

only tolerable in that its end be always in sight. the clever boy

who be keep until they be nineteen and stuff for Oxford and

Cambridge and the profession be pity rather than admire. there

be nothing admirable in Oxford and Cambridge to those who know

nothing of they, save the second and third-class man who be so poor

as to be glad of the miserable pittance grant they for the

instruction of generation of Bennett Lawries and the son of

cab-driver and millionaire mill-owner.



after his first term in the Fifth Form Mrs. Lawrie quarrel with

Bennett's aunt Louisa. her subsidy be withdraw, and Bennett leave

school with a mind untraine except in memory, and store only with a

curious litter of knowledge absolutely unrelated to the fact of his

existence.



Education, like charity, should begin at home. Bennett have spend

eleven year in be educate, but he have be teach nothing at all

of the place in which he live. he have not be tell why it be, what

it be, nor for what purpose it exist and grow and expand. he know

nothing of its history except that it have once have a latin name and

have be occupy by the Romans, and that Oliver Cromwell have pass

through or near it with his roundhead. everything that be tell he

be present to he in such a desiccated form that his gorge rise at

it and he could swallow it only with an effort. in a city of Puritans

it seem meet and right that education, like religion and life,

should be make as unpleasant as possible.



the only real education that Bennett ever get be in his daily walk to

and fro over the two mile that separate his home from the school. he

could cover the distance in three way: either he could go through

slum and under factory and engineering shop along the low ground,

or he could take the high ground behind the Albert Station and soon

come to suburb and the street where the middle class gather, or

he could pass through the Jews' quarter down by the Assize Courts and

the gaol. most often he choose the third way. the mysterious,

large-head, thick-feature creature with their oily, beady eye

exercise a strange fascination over he. he like their Kosher shop,

their bill write in weird character, the woman with their hard

stiff wig, the man with their queer gnarl leg and their foot

loosely hinge at the ankle. he always look at their foot, because

a boy at school have once point out to he how the Jews always wear

their boot down on the outside edge of the sole. he never know why

the Jews be there in such large number, but they interest he.

they be romantic. all the clever boy at school be Jews. they

seem to learn everything with an extraordinary facility. . . .

almost his only friend at school be a Jew name Kraus, whose father

and mother be in Roumania, and at interval they would send he over

a hamper contain queer fish and black olive and rose-leaf jam,

and then Bennett would go home with Kraus and have an orgy. once Kraus

give he some unleavened bread, and Bennett keep it as a curiosity,

and frighten himself with pretend that the tragedy of the

Passover be come again, and that the angel would not mark his house

because he be not a Jew and have no right to the bread.



Kraus have an aunt who be a musician and a singer. she sing so sweetly

that Bennett be move to tear and fall violently in love with she,

though he would not admit it to himself, for all thought of love

disgust he. it be Kraus who reveal to Bennett the mystery of his

birth, and in the filthy way possible explain to he the process

by which he have his being. it take Bennett some time to recover from

the despair into which the revelation throw he, but it never occur

to he to doubt the truth of his friend's statement. the filthiness

be in the world and not in Kraus. they become more intimate, and

their talk be almost always dirty, though innocent. it be a

swaggering pose, their way of equalising matter with the bawdiness of

the world that lie before they.



Bennett have no corrective. no grown person ever hold out a hand to

save he from his dark thought and uneasy desire when they come to

he, nor trouble to enquire into what pitfall he might be tumble.

instruct by Kraus he go the way of all flesh and lose his peace of

mind and the bloom of his boyhood. all around he he see darkness and

ugliness, but never any beauty. the one place in his daily walk that

his imagination fasten on be the gaol, and he dream of prisoner

and policeman and arrestment.



his friendship with Kraus last for three year, during which Bennett

fall in and out of love (with absurd chivalry and nobility) with his

sister' friend. the rupture come when one day Kraus fill the whole

of their walk home with an account--largely invent--of an adventure

with a loose factory girl whom he have encounter in the street

seek whom she might devour. a black abyss yawn at Bennett's foot,

his brain whirl, and he say:



"you'll go to Hell."



Kraus reply with an obscene jingle which they have often chant

together, and offer to call for Bennett that night.



"I don't ever want to see you again," say Bennett, and he wash his

hand of Kraus. thereafter for the short remain period of his term

at school he avoid the jewish quarter and take the high road through

the most respectable-seem middle-class street.



the hour of the school be five, three in the morning and two in the

afternoon, with three-quarter of an hour for lunch. this be not

provide eatably in the school-building, and as, for most of the boy,

the mid-day meal be the most serious of the day, they go, accord

to their mean, to the various restaurant in the locality. Bennett

be allow sixpence a day, and use to repair to one of three or four

cheap eating-house, all in cellar. here he see man and youth of the

type with which his future life would be spend--warehouseman and

clerk, all scrape as much off their food allowance as they could to

pay for beer and bet and billiard and tobacco. they be all dull

and timid and white-face, foul-mouthed very many of they, and the

condition under which their food be place before they be so

uninvite that they hurry through their meal as quickly as

possible. on the whole Bennett envy they because they be not at

school and be independent and do work for which they be

pay. . . very often he feel too timid or too listless to eat, and he

save the sixpence for his own purpose. when he do that he find it

very hard to keep awake during the two hour in the afternoon, and

very often he have his homework increase by a long imposition.



in the holiday he be require to read one of the romance of Sir

Walter Scott with a view to examination on they when the school

re-open. this begot in he a loathing for Sir Walter from which he

never recover. he be always be examine--every term, every

midterm; in some subject, once a week. as information have be force

into he piecemeal, so piecemeal it be pump out of he. . . .

perhaps this be a wise provision. perhaps, having feed their little

boy' mind with bran for week on end, it be merciful of the

pedagogue at the end of term to administer an emetic. every term the

examination clean Bennett out, and by regular repetition of this

process he be no far on at the end of five year than he be at

the beginning. they even try to rob he of his delight in

Shakespeare by make he learn the stupid hotch-potch of the note of

some Cambridge pundit instead of help he to discover the glory of

the verse and confirm he in his taste for it.



nothing be ever do to help he to understand the process of his

own existence, or to direct the force stir in he, or to pick his

way through the whirl maze of diver emotion in which every now

and then he lose himself. he be affectionate; no appeal be make to

his affection. he be romantic; no food be forthcoming for his

hunger. spiritually and emotionally he be starve; mentally he be

grossly and unsuitably fed. his be the average condition of the

average boy in the most touching, perhaps the most beautiful period of

the average man's life.



he be tell that he must be confirm. like the minister who prepare

he, he understand nothing of the significance of the ceremony, but

contact with one or two religiously minded young man release the

pen-up emotion in he and it rush out in such a flood that he be

like to drown. he clutch at the first cause that come to hand,

turn to the first manly and inspiring personality that he

encounter, the rector of St. Saviour's, and he embrace the high

Church creed and all its tenet, prejudice, and shibboleth.



only an accident have save he from the bad consequence of his

education.







XXI



MRS. ENTWISTLE'S heart



_God's rare blessing be, after all, a good woman._

     RICHARD FEVEREL.



with the good intention in the world Francis could not overcome the

inevitable dislike with which Frederic's mere presence inspire he.

he could not bring himself to speak more than three word to he or to

make any inquiry into his affair. Frederic also suffer under the

constraint of the secret they share, and relieve the situation by

absent himself as much as possible from the house. his fiancée make

that easy by her extensive demand upon his time and he become more a

member of her family than of his own.



Francis keep his word with Annie Lipsett, and every week send her ten

shilling, and, know that his wife open his letter, get she to

write, when she have anything to say, to Serge. his conscience be very

uneasy about the whole affair, but he know that if he do not do what

he be do no one else would, and he could not bring himself to

righteous acceptance of the conclusion of his premise, that, after

all, the girl have bring it on herself, and, like hundred of other,

must fight through the consequence alone and unaided.



"if I know the hundred of other," he say to himself, "I could not

possibly help they all. I could not afford it. . . . can I afford to

help this young woman? . . . I cannot, but I must."



he submit to this moral imperative, but he could not away with the

idea that he be encourage immorality. that idea became fix, an

obsession. it worry he so much that he decide to go and see the

young woman and make quite sure as to the state of her mind, to

demonstrate if necessary that though thing be be make

comfortable and easy for she in this world she could not hope to

escape the punishment for her sin in the next.



accordingly one Saturday he resolve to take the ten shilling himself

instead of send they by post. Annie Lipsett be stay in a farm

labourer's cottage near a village some fifteen mile away to the

south. it be a keen autumn day when Francis walk along the lane

between hedge aflame with hip and haw and red blackberry leave,

and green with holly berry, and he ask himself why he do not

devote every Saturday afternoon to a walk in the country. the cold air

fill his lung and the wind blow in his beard and bring the colour

to his round cheek. the tree be burn with colour, the sun shine

scarcely warm through the soft mist that lie over the country-side.

. . . decidedly, he must often take such walk and bring Annette. how

she would love the orchard, glow with red apple and plum, and

yellow with pear, and the cow and the green field and the little

river. Annette would love they all. they would make a habit of it,

every Saturday, and they would see all the season come and live and

pass.



as he approach the cottage where Annie Lipsett be stay he feel

less interested in the state of her mind and more concerned to see

herself and discover how she be keep in health. health, he

think, be most important, perhaps more important than anything

else. "grant we in health and wealth long to live." he recite the

word aloud, and his mind comment that wealth mean well-being, not

a fine house and raiment and a substantial account at the banker.

that strike he with all the force of an original discovery, and he

begin to think that his life be not perhaps such a complete failure

as he have grow use to think it. his arrival at the gate of the

cottage cut short his speculation, and he wrench himself back to

the problem immediately before he, the bringing of this sinful soul

to repentance. yes; he must make she see that her sin would only be

forgive she on condition of full repentance. he feel fully convince

of it in that moment, and do his good to make himself feel miserable

in spite of the invitation to happiness extend to he by the little

grass path lead up to the door of the white cottage, and the

Michaelmas daisy and autumn lily and purple aster grow in the

border and the heavily laden fruit tree in the tiny orchard.



he walk up the grass path and knock at the low oaken door. in the

house he hear a bustling and a rustling, and presently the door be

open to he by the woman of the house. she be enormously fat,

red-faced and comely. she say:



"tha can coom in. Annie be oot in't field gatherin' noot. Tha'll be

Mr. Folyat. Tha's a gradely mon. Coom in."



Francis follow she into the little low oak-beam room, spick and

span and clean as a new pin. there be a picture of Queen Victoria on

the wall, five text, and a grocer's almanac, horribly reproduce in

oleographic colour a pre-raphaelite picture of Christ knock at a

door. the woman, Mrs. Entwistle, brew a pot of tea and chatter:



"she be that well, tha'd think she be go to make no more fuss

than a beast. Eeh! the way t' bloom 'ave coom to her cheek and 't

light to 'er eye ye'd say a woman be all t' well for carryin'.

. . ."



Francis begin to take the same delight in the enormous woman that have

come to he from the sight of his walk. she be so sane and

comfortable.



"Eeh," she say, "it be a good thing to get 'er away from 'er mother.

I never could do wi' they stringy little woman. a 'ard time? 'course

she's 'ad a 'ard time. so's everybody, but you don't want the world to

go grizzlin about it."



Annie come in. she be very pretty, with a new soft pride in her eye.

she be very big. she take Francis's hand and clung to it, and with

eye and voice together she say:



"thank you."



"glad to see you, my dear," say he. "glad to see you look so

well."



she sit down. they have tea, and when they have do Francis intimate

that he wish to speak to Annie alone. Mrs. Entwistle take down a

yoke from the wall and go off to fetch water from the well. Francis

hug his knee and read several time over a text which run: "Beloved

now be we, the son of God." it be so illuminated that it be

difficult to read: _we_ look like _I_, and _son_ like _gun_. then

he ask if he might smoke.



"surely," say Annie.



Francis light his pipe and the tobacco taste very good.



"you have be happy here," he say.



"oh, yes. very happy."



"I've bring you your ten shilling."



"thank you."



he give she the coin and she put it in a little purse. Francis find

himself at a standstill. he force himself to speak. he be alarmed at

the quiescence of his conscience under the influence of Mrs. Entwistle

and the garden and the radiant thankfulness in Annie's face. her

gratitude to he make it very difficult for he to perform what he

conceive to be his duty. a humorous gleam shot through his brain, and

he begin to think himself a little absurd; but he prick his

conscience and it stifle the gleam. he look very serious as he

say:



"I suppose--I hope you realise that you have no right to be happy. you

be bring a child into the world in sin . . ."



he could not go on. he see that he have hurt the girl to the quick.



"I'm sorry," he say hurriedly. "it be very difficult. I only want

to be sure that you realise, that you know, that--that . . ."



with bow head and with her hand in her lap, Annie say in a low

voice:



"I do know all that, sir. I think that myself, sir, when I first

come. every night I cry because I be so wicked, and I think I

should never be forgive, and mother have say such awful thing to I.

but Mr. Folyat come . . ."



"Frederic?"



"no, sir, Mr. Serge. he come every Saturday. he paint all the

afternoon and then come here in the evening. sometimes he walk a

great many mile. he come and say I must never have any thought in my

head that ben't happy, that I must never for a single instant let

myself be afraid, for the sake of the child. he say everything that

happen to I happen to the child too. and I've try and I have

be happy, so I know it's true. he say: 'what's do be do, and

people ben't wicke all the time or good all the time.' I don't

understand everything he say, but I always feel well when he come,

and I don't think of anything but it. I want it to love I . . ."



"of course, of course," say Francis. "it be very important for you to

be well, but you must not imagine . . ."



"I couldn't take money from you, sir, if you think I wicked. I have

be wicked, but I'm not wicke any long. I couldn't do--what I do,

ever again. I couldn't be so silly . . ."



Francis think to himself: "I must make she appreciate the peril

through which her soul have pass. . . . she seem to be leave she

soul out of consideration altogether. I must make she see that she have

a soul and can only find true happiness in its salvation through

. . ." once more he draw back from the contemplation of difficulty

which he feel be too intricate for he. he say:



"my dear, be sure I think no ill of you. I only desire, my only

think be . . . be . . . have be to secure you as far as possible

from the temporal consequence of your--er--betrayal." he breathe

heavily. then he fall back on his natural candour and add: "I come

meaning to say a great deal, but I find that I have nothing to say. I

find it quite impossible to take a professional view of your

situation. you must forgive I. I cannot help feel that I have be

guilty of an impertinence."



Annie still hang her head and pluck at her finger. she look at

the clock and say:



"Mr. Serge ought to be here now, sir. he's generally here before this.

there ben't many gentleman like Mr. Serge, be there, sir?"



"I hardly know," reply Francis. "I hardly know, but my experience of

the world have be very limited. . . . do you tend the garden

yourself?"



"yes, sir. I help Mrs. Entwistle. I've learn such a lot about the

garden since I've be here."



"I have a garden, once, in my old living." he describe the garden at

St. Withans, and the exercise of visualise the lawn and border and

the orchard under the church-tower and wake the faint echo of his

old joy in it win he back to great confidence. he talk of flower

and bee and bird until there come a knock at the door, when, with

joyful alacrity, Annie hurry to open it. Serge come in with

paint-box and sketch-book strap together and sling over his

shoulder. he nod to his father and sit down by the table. Annie

brew he fresh tea and he say:



"jolly place this?"



"Delightful," reply Francis.



"don't you think she's look well?"



"very. I should hardly have know she again."



"she's in good hand. Mrs. Entwistle have take she to her heart--I

too, and if you come often enough she'll find room for you. it's

delightfully warm and comfortable and roomy. I never know such a

heart. you meet all sort of delightful people in it, all the nice

people in the Bible, and hundred of child, and everybody love

everybody else. don't they Annie?"



Annie blush:



"that's only Mr. Serge's nonsense, Mr. Folyat. he go on talk like

that until Mrs. Entwistle shake with laughter so that the chair

you're sit in creak. . . . have you have a good day, Mr. Serge?"



"you shall tell I." Serge produce his sketch and Annie look at

they.



"that's a lovely one," she say.



"may I see it?" ask Francis.



she hand he a sheet of paper on which be a drawing of a baby in an

apple-tree with the wind blow in its hair and bring new wonder

into its starry eye.



"Mr. Serge do I one every week," say Annie simply. "I keep they

all."



Francis hold it up close to his face and peer over the top of it at

Serge. very solemnly he return the drawing to Annie. . . . a moment

or two later he lean forward and say:



"I can remember you when you be like that, Serge. it's a long time

ago, and so many thing have happen since then. you be very big

and strong, and you use to laugh a great deal. . . . I remember your

be ill, and then, when you be a little old, I remember your

ask I all sort of question that I couldn't answer. and then,

quite suddenly, you ben't a baby any long and then you become a

boy . . ."



"and then I go away. that be the whole history of any father and any

son. Queer, ben't it? . . . and then we never meet again until we come

across each other in Mrs. Entwistle's heart."



Annie look puzzle. there be several moment of silence, warm and

comforting and, to Francis, very sweet. Serge laugh.



"after this," he say, "we may expect to hear that Mrs. Entwistle have

be catch up into Heaven. as a matter of fact she live there all

the time, because, though you wouldn't think it to look at she, she be

a sort of a fairy."



"I should like to be back before dark," say Francis.



"three minute," answer Serge, "and I'll go with you." he turn to

Annie. "I've find work for you as soon as you're ready. a friend of

mine have a farm six mile away. he live with his sister and he want

an assistant housekeeper."



Annie have never take her eye from he since he have come into the

room. her eye now fill with tear, and her hand make a touching

little gesture, almost imperceptible, of gratitude towards he. Serge

go on:



"he's not a rich man, but he'll pay you enough, so that you can feel

independent and always be put by a little."



Annie find her voice but she remain inarticulate. Francis be

curiously relieve when Serge rise to go. he hold out his hand to she.

she take it in both her and say:



"I know. I must have make you very unhappy. you have be very good to

I."



"not at all. not at all," say Francis huskily. she turn to Serge:



"you'll come again? it willn't be long now."



"Next Saturday."



"it might be before then."



"I'll come on Monday."



"thank you."



Francis find his way out into the garden. through the window he see

Serge take the girl into his arm and kiss she. More than ever he feel

that he have be impertinent.



the sun be set and the mist have almost clear as Serge join

he. in the west the sky be crimson, strake with indigo cloud.

Serge take his father's arm and say:



"we owe a good deal to that young woman, you and I."



"yes," reply Francis doubtfully. "it seem to I that she be more

than a little in love with you."



"I hope so."



"ben't that a little dangerous?" Francis feel very bold in make this

excursion into psychology, but the pressure of Serge's hand on his arm

reassure he.



"my dear father," come the reply, "for thirty year you have be pay

for teach people that the only safety of our little existence here

on earth lie in love. I can imagine nothing more awful and

disappointing than for a woman to go through the process of childbirth

without be in love. it be dreadful enough to live from moment to

moment without be in love, but to pass through a great natural

crisis without it would be devastating. if she ben't in love with I

I couldn't have touch her heart. I could only have appeal to her

intelligence, which would have be quite useless. it seem to I

vitally important that she should be make happy, so that through

happiness she could understand and feel she be do no injury to any

one, but be perform the ultimate service which a woman be

privileged to perform for only a few human being, namely, the gift of

life. she have understand and feel that."



"but she be in love with you!"



"why this terror of love? love, like everything else, become a bad

thing if it be use selfishly. I ask nothing of she. she know that.

she will love her child the more because of her love for I, and by

her great love she will win the love of the child. . . ."



"but . . ." say Francis.



"what now?"



Francis make full and frank confession of how he have come with a

desire to make the young woman understand and feel her sinfulness.

Serge press his arm affectionately.



"my dear father," he say, "a flower may be impregnate by a very

disreputable bee, but it remain a beautiful flower for all that."



"a flower," say Francis, "have no soul."



"in the presence of love," reply Serge, "argument be quite futile.

the tragedy of the world, it seem to I, be this, that with such a

power of love and friendship and affection as be in we, there should

be so little of they."



"I at least have win a little of they to-day," say Francis, and

timidly with his arm he press Serge's hand.



"that's all right," say Serge.



the night come down as they walk and hang out her most brilliant

canopy of star, and, as in a peaceful lake, their light be mirror

in the soul of Francis Folyat.







XXII



LOVE



_let we roll all our strength and all

 our sweetness up into one ball

 and tear our pleasure with rough strife

 through the iron gate of life._

      ANDREW MARVELL



with consummate skill Gertrude invent contrivance to conceal the

change that have come about in her affair and feeling, but, as she

never deceive herself, she deceive no one else either. however,

everybody pretend to be deceive, and painlessly her engagement with

Bennett Lawrie be allow to fade out of existence. he come less and

less to the house until the transformation in his status be complete,

and then he come more and more.



Gertrude grow restless, her unease infect her mother, who have begin

to tire of Fern Square and to think it and its neighbourhood squalid.

the Clibran-Bells have leave Fern Square and go to a more expensive

and more modern house in the select neighbourhood of Burdley Park.

little hint be throw out, but nothing definite be say to

Francis, until he express a desire to enlarge his greenhouse in the

back garden and return to his old pastime of gardening. he have tire

of reading. he could not bring himself to tackle new book, and the

old have lose the potency of their appeal. his parish work be

organise into a comfortable routine, so that he have plenty of

leisure, and he dislike be leave alone with tobacco and his

thought. gradually he have fall into a near companionship with his

wife, read and discuss her foolish book with she and every

evening play three game of bezique and allow she to win. he

want some new form of activity, and one day, the post bring a

seedsman's catalogue, he find what he want. he would grow fern and

bulb and fuchsia and geranium and cactuse and have a very pleasant

refuge from any malign stroke that fate might be keep in store for

he.



near the Clibran-Bells Gertrude find a house with a large

conservatory, and, all leap to the prospect of a change, the

decision be come to, the remainder of the Fern Square lease dispose

of, and the household be move. the new house be one room small

than the old. Serge take a studio at the top of a huge caravanserai of

office near the Town Hall Square and arrange to live there. he have

paint a portrait of Mrs. Clibran-Bell which have bring he a

commission or two, and he regard himself as sufficiently opulent to

pay the not very exorbitant rent.



the removal take place in March, and a very pleasant house-warming be

hold. Gertrude send out the invitation and expressly do not invite

Bennett Lawrie. he turn up all the same, more silent, melancholy and

romantical than ever. he sit in a corner and speak to nobody, and

look so entirely dejected that at last Minna take pity on he,

smile her sweetest, and say:



"why do you always play the skeleton at the feast? be you really

think of death or only of what there be for supper?"



"I don't know I look like that," answer Bennett with an effort.

"I be feel rather happy listen to you all."



"look on," say Minna, "be a dreadfully bad habit. whenever I do

it, I always find myself wonder who be go to be married to whom,

or, at any rate, who be in love with whom, and how it be all go to

turn out. that be too horribly depressing. it be much well to be an

airy trifl. why don't you try a little airy trifling?"



"you cann't do it alone."



"that be _quite_ good. . . . now then--one--two--three--hop."



"I really couldn't trifle with you, Minna."



this be true. the memory of the day by the river be much too vivid.

Bennett be nothing if not rigidly monogamous. Minna do not know

that. this new game, which have never occur to she before, amuse

she. she go on:



"but you're do it quite nicely."



Bennett drop back into the dark gloom. he begin to feel angry

with she and say savagely:



"be I?"



"indeed you be. and as you ben't go to be a little clergyman, it

don't matter."



"it do. it always matter."



"it only matter if--shall I say it?"



"you generally say what you feel inclined to say."



"it only matter if our little gentleman be in love."



Bennett scowl. Minna go on with her banter until Annette come into

the room with a tray bear lemonade and claret. Bennett spring up

and hurry to meet she. Minna laugh and nod to Basil Haslam to

come and take Bennett's seat. when he have do so, she say:



"have you ever notice my little sister, Annette?"



"not particularly. why?"



"she be over there, by that young Lawrie."



"young idiot."



"what do you think of she?"



"she be look quite pretty. have she do her hair differently?"



"no, nothing be different."



"Excitement, perhaps."



"per--hap."



Basil turn to Minna. he be not interested in Annette.



"Minna, you look . . ."



"Ta, ta, ta . . . be we to have it all over again?"



"yes. every time I see you. it's not long before I go away now. will

you come with I? I can do well with you than without."



"you don't know. you have never live with I. I should hate be

really poor in a house of my own."



"I'd make you rich with love."



"and feed I with it and clothe I, and feed and clothe an enormous

family?"



"we shouldn't have an enormous family at once. I'll make you rich

before there be . . ."



Minna tap his hand with amused affection, get up and leave he. she

go and stand near Bennett and Annette and she hear he say:



"thank you for wear they."



she see then that Annette be wear two little red rose in her

bosom.



"it be kind of you to send they," say Annette.



"I hardly dare," say Bennett. "I don't know if I might. I never see

you now."



Annette look up at he between fear and delight. his mournful eye

meet her, and with a small envy Minna see that they be entirely

oblivious of everybody in the room. Annette's lip pout. a little

sigh escape she. she turn and hurry away.



Basil Haslam come up, take Minna rather roughly by the arm and drag

she away to sit on the stair. in her heart she be pleased by his

masterfulness, but superficially she be irritated, and they sit

quarrel.



the party engage two room, one for card and one for music. the room

in which Bennett stand begin to fill as Mary produce her violin.

Annette return from the kitchen with biscuit, sandwich, cake and

a trifle, and when she have dispose they on the table she turn to

Bennett and say:



"come."



he follow she.



she lead the way into the little back garden, where, in a plot of grimy

grass, grow a sycamore-tree. at the end of the garden be a decayed

old summer-house of rustic wood. Bennett's heart thump as they

glad of it. tear come to his eye. he could not see she. his hand

for each it be the first kiss of the beloved, a great joy than

either have dream of, and therefore almost more pain than joy.

hold she to he, Bennett murmur:



"Annette, love, I love you."



and she give little crooning sound and be the first to kiss again.



presently they creep back to the house and steal into the room again,

turn to he and say tartly:



"Annette be look quite pretty to-night, ben't she?"



"be she?" Bennett's voice quaver.



"I should advise you, as a friend, to make yourself very amiable to

Ma."



"I have always," say Bennett, "have a great respect for Mrs. Folyat."



"bah!" answer Minna. "you take yourself much too seriously. you'll

never learn the wisdom of run away."



"I run away from you."



"of course you do; because I never take you seriously."



Bennett say with asperity:



"you never take anything seriously. some day you'll have to."



"Pooh!" Minna toss her head and laugh. "I shall always know when

to run away."



feel that the remark be idiotic and inappropriate Bennett close

with:



"the world be very beautiful."



"great heaven! we shall have you write poetry next!"



Bennett go very red. he have already write much poetry, as Minna

well know, for she have purloin and read many of his effusion to

that it would, and mentally adapt certain verse to meet the new

Bennett be call away to take a hand at whist--he be a fair

expression harden into pride and defiance, and it be Minna who look

the party be very late in break up, and as Bennett be put on

"Serge be go to make a picture of I. I begin to-morrow, at his

studio."



"I'll write to you--then, if I may."



Annette be call away by her mother, very peevish and anxious to go

to bed. she catch Bennett's hand, press it to her bosom and run

"good night," he murmur, and when he be out in the street, walk

home, he whisper to himself:



"good night, my love."



* * *



with the two crush rose in her hand Annette sleep like a child,

hardly stir all night, smile. she have pray to God, as usual,

her love.



Bennett on the other hand have suffer from a violent reaction. he

he be late in the morning, dull and dead. the monotonous day's work

see a whole car-load of ghost."



"I don't sleep well," answer Bennett. "sometimes I don't."



"that's nonsense at your age. how old be you?"



"nearly twenty."



they talk for a little, but Bennett hardly hear what Serge be

"I do not know what to write. I love you. I hate myself. I cannot even

Annette, I cann't write any more."



he do not sign it. he hurry it into an envelope when there come a

"who be it?"



in a very soft voice come:



"Tibby."



he open to she. she have his night-shirt in her hand. she close the

"you've tear your shift."



"yes. I tear it in my sleep."



"poor laddie," she say. "if I could do aught to help ye I would.

Ye're a poor solitary body. . . . it's this house and the misery

Bennett look at she and the kindness in her eye make he burst into

drank, and Bennett say:



"thank you. I feel well . . . Tibby, why don't people understand

what they be?"



"God know," say she. "it's all a great mystery. there's a deal of

unkindness, and a little kindness in the world. it's not give to we

"Tibby--" he pause. "tibby, would you love I whatever I do?"



"surely. you're one of my bairn."



Bennett kiss she. then he go and post his letter.



* * *



Annette come to meet he next day as he leave his office. they have a

long walk and be altogether happy, laugh and discover little

joke that they could share--odd name on the shop-front, queer folk

in the street, strange advertisement on the placard. he leave she

wish to see she with her family. also he be afraid of Minna. she

spoil everything.



only one evening a week could Annette give to he, but they have

Reference Library, the School, the College, Humphrey Bodham's

and share make everything beautiful. always he find she more

a boy. he could laugh with she.



all the rest of his life seem small and unimportant. often at home

. . . such pessimism seem utterly absurd to Bennett. he do not want

they hardly ever kiss each other in those young day. when they do

independent.



as the result of one long walk in the rain Annette fall ill and be in

bed for a fortnight. she fill all Bennett's thought. he dare not

then, one evening as he sit by the window of his attic, look across

he be see. he become wholly afraid and run downstairs to seek

have a long silent walk, arm in arm.



"I think of you all the time," say she.



"and I of you . . ." he be troubled. "oh! Annette!"



he take her hand and, in the street as they be, kiss it over and

over again.



* * *



she go away to the sea and Serge with she. she like be with

Serge, but even to he she could not declare herself. under the warm

but only glad to be with he once more. she be proud of the new

horizon that have open to she. when health come back to she she be a

woman.



one evening as she sit with Serge on the sand gaze at the moon

peep above the sea and silver the wave she say:



"Serge, tell I, have you ever be in love?"



"often."



"happily?"



"happily and unhappily. it don't matter much. the great thing be to

love."



"what be love?"



"a great thing, but not the be-all and end-all of existence as so many

people try to believe. the great thing lie beyond it."



"then you must go through it to get at they."



"exactly. Most people stick in the middle, or remain shiver on the

wrong side."



"like Frederic?"



"like Frederic."



"I use to hate Frederic, but now I'm only sorry for he."



"what's come to you?"



she shiver.



"it's very cold. can I go home to-morrow?"



"if you like. do you want to?"



"I must."



* * *



she return to the house in Burdley Park next morning, but it be

some day before she inform Bennett of her arrival. he come hot-foot

determination about he and the embarrassment of one who have a vital

topic to approach. he have make a compromise between his mental torment

the vision of the future to which it lead be sufficiently entrance

to make he unwilling to look elsewhere. he do try to contemplate the

future without that step, but it stretch intolerably blank. he

need action. the upheaval that have take place in he have set he

grow mentally and spiritually; in his office, in his house, he be

he see in marriage perfect freedom. it be an illusion, but he be

wholly deceive by it. he see it as a summit of achievement from which

he could defy all that he have suffer all his life. he say to

Annette:



"Annette, I love you. I want you to marry I. we shall be very poor,

but we shall have each other, and nothing else matter."



this he say to convince himself. Annette do not need convince. she

believe.



"no," she say, "nothing else matter."



he have hardly expect such instant compliance. for a moment it shake

the firmness of his conviction and blur his vision of the free

and must not be suffer to continue.



excitedly they make their plan. they believe that it be their

affair and theirs only. they see the outside world as harsh and

menacing and devoid of understanding, be sear by it, and use

their fear to fortify their resolve.



"another month," say Bennett, "and we shall be man and wife."



"I love you," say Annette. it be the first time the word have pass

her lip.



* * *



when Bennett have pay the expense of the ceremony in an

out-of-the-way church and the price of the ring and their

wedding-dinner in a restaurant near his office he have exactly

thirty-shilling leave until the end of the month. Annette return to

her home until he should have find a lodging for she, and he engage

himself to break the news to his mother at the first fitting moment;

fix, in his own mind, at that when he should next receive his

monthly salary of seven pound.



he anticipate a storm, but, be still bear up by the excitement

and the adventure of what they have do, they feel secure against all

wrath. hardly could they understand that there should be wrath. how

could their love meet with anything but love?







XXIII



BENNETT tell his mother



_then would I speak, and not fear._

     JOB



after a week's search Bennett find lodging as far remove as

possible from his family in a little pink-brick street that be one of

a network weave by a speculative builder over a tract of marshy ground

that for year have be unclaimed and use by the neighbourhood for a

rubbish heap. in a tiny little house he hire two room on the first

floor for twelve shilling a week. his landlady be a large german

woman who, by threateningly demand reference, inveigle he into

pay two week' rent in advance. he have to borrow ten shilling to

do that. he be terrify of the german but proud of the two room,

the first place that he have ever be able to call his own. the

wall-paper and paint be hideous, but he tell himself that that could

soon be alter--should be alter before Annette see the room. by

neglect all other engagement he find time in the evening to hang

what he think a pretty paper and to paint the woodwork apple-green,

paint and paper be buy with more borrow money. this manual

activity soothe he greatly, and he feel very proud of himself,

whistle and sing all the time as he toil. he be so busy that for a

fortnight he hardly see Annette, and when he do snatch a moment with

she he be exceedingly mysterious, and would not tell she what he be

up to, except that it be for she, a beautiful surprise.



"where be it?" ask she.



"you wouldn't know if I tell you. I'll take you there."



"next week? be it to be next week?"



"as soon as it be ready. . . . you're not sorry?"



"of course not."



* * *



as the end of the month draw near Bennett realise that it be not

go to be so easy as he have think to break the surprising and

splendid news to his mother. he know so little about she, and have

always have great difficulty in talk to she even about the most

impersonal matter. there have be difference between they before,

many trifling, and one serious, over his secession to the High Church

fold. all these difference now rise up and stand like a thick-set

hedge between he and she. . . . as long as he remember she she have

be always sit in the middle of the dark drawing-room waiting and

watch for the landmark of the day--dinner at one, his brother'

return from the bank, his own return from his office, tea, supper, the

hour for sleep--as time bear she evenly past they. for year now his

only long conversation with she have be at the end of the month when

he give she his earning and receive his dole for spending. it make

he ashamed and unhappy to know that he dislike she, but he could not

explain it away, and he have never make any attempt to understand why

she be as she be--cold and hard and unresponding. if he take side

at all in the antagonism of drawing-room and dining-room his lean

be towards his father, but that be because the only intimacy in the

house lie between Tibby and old Lawrie. there be more warmth in the

dining-room than in the drawing-room, though, outwardly, it be his

father who be disgrace and depose, his father whom Bennett have be

teach to contemn. . . . the only link that bind he to his mother

be money. he would use the monthly conversation about money as an

opening for his declaration of independence. he have not look upon it

as that: have not contemplate a rupture and open breach between

himself and his mother, though he have hear muttered warning in the

depth of his soul.



when he return home with seven pound in his pocket, he hesitate

for a long time outside the drawing-room door with every nerve in his

body throb. his suffering be too great and he decide that he

would tell his father first. after all, his father be the head of the

family. . . . he walk gropingly down the dark passage to the

dining-room only to find his father out and Tibby work for dear

life at a column of cotton-price. he know what that mean. there

would be no tell his father. his father be "plang" (the family

euphemism), and, as she have often do before, Tibby be finish his

work.



she look up at he and scowl. the work be never easy for she, she

have to supply the gap of her ignorance with guess and be always in

dread of guess awry. Bennett sit down in the horsehair chair by the

fireplace, under the blue-eyed portrait of his grandfather, the scot

minister, and rattle the money in his pocket. Tibby go on work.

much of Bennett's terror vanish and he break into the scratching of

her pen:



"Tibby."



"eh?"



"you say once you'd love I whatever I do."



"aye. what have you be do?"



"I'm married."



"Losh!"



Tibby drop her pen and turn sorrowful eye of wonder upon he.

Bennett jingle the money in his pocket.



"I'm married," he say. "I'm very happy."



"Och! the foolishness of man! marry! Laddie, ye'll never have a son

as young as yourself."



"I'm married," say Bennett, "and I'm go to be very happy, and I

don't care what . . ."



"have you tell your mother?"



"no."



"well tell she at once. you'll break your neck over it. I'll finish

this and then I'll think it out. . . . marry! Losh!"



she turn to her work again, and the pen scratch and splutter.

Bennett reach the door when she call to he:



"Laddie."



he turn.



"if ye love the lassie, ye've no call to be afeard. there's always a

way. there's no way where no love be."



"I love she," say Bennett unconsciously dramatic and absurd. "I love

she as my life."



"God bless ye."



* * *



fortify by her benison and also by having once tell his immense

secret Bennett pass swiftly to the drawing-room. he find his mother

sit in her chair in the middle of the room with a cat in her lap.

he stoop and kiss she.



"I've get some news for you!"



"sit down. you don't come and talk to I as often as you might."



there be an unusual geniality in her voice that make it easy for he

to go on.



"I've have a rise."



"that's good. they must be pleased with you. how much?"



"five shilling a week."



"that's very good. you shall have a shilling a week more for your

pocket-money. I'm glad you're do so well. you can keep five

shilling for yourself this month."



"that ben't all my news."



"what else?"



"I want to keep it all."



"Nonsense. you cann't do that."



"I must. you see. I haven't tell you everything. you see . . . I shall

want my money now. I'm--what I want to tell you be that--that . . ."

he give a little nervous giggle that exasperate his mother and set

she tap with her foot on the floor. "I'm--you see--I'm marry."



her mouth drop. her hand wave weakly in the air. she get up and

go and stand for a long time--it seem a very long time--by the

window. without turn she say:



"who be the woman?"



"her name be Annette. Annette Folyat."



"I might have know it. . . will you ask your father to come here?"



"Father's out." Bennett feel that his cause be lose. only in the most

desperate case be his father's presence over request in the

drawing-room.



"tibby then." she go to the door and with extraordinary power of the

lung shout for the old servant.



Tibby came shuffle. she be dress to go out, in bonnet and shawl,

and have an envelope in her hand.



"I'm in haste," she say.



"Tibby, what's to be do? Bennett have marry one of the daughter of

that High Church popery priest. what be I to do?"



"what can you do?"



"it cann't go on. it's miserable folly. it's ruin. it's beggary. . . .

where be you married? when?" she pounce on Bennett.



"a fortnight ago. at St. Barnabas; bann and everything. we sign the

register. I forbid you to interfere."



"silence."



"I will not be silent. I have take my own life into my own hand. I

be go to have my own money and my own house. I shall leave your

house to-night, and I shall not enter it again until you ask I and my

wife together."



"that's right, laddie," say Tibby quietly.



Mrs. Lawrie open her mouth to rend Tibby, who add:



"I canna thole a man that winnot stand by his own doing."



Mrs. Lawrie turn to Bennett and say:



"may you never have a child to hurt you as you have hurt I this day."



the wild frenzy that have possess Bennett ooze away, and weakly he

ask:



"be I to go?"



"go. . . . as you've make your bed, so you must lie on it."



a little unsteadily Bennett walk upstairs to his attic and begin to

pack his belonging. he lay they all out on the bed, book, clothe,

small piece of furniture, and they seem to he very little. in

possession of his secret he have feel very large and important; now he

feel very small indeed.



Downstairs in the drawing-room his mother sit write a letter to

Francis, denounce he and all his work and his daughter, who be

a snare to youth and guilelessness. Tibby have try to reason with

she, but she be beyond reason. she have be hurt and wish to hurt.

carefully, laboriously, she have toil to insure her child against

all risk and peril of the world. brick by brick she have build a

prison for each of they which should last as long as life, and, at the

first touch, the wall that hem in and secure she youngest bear

have come topple down, and all around herself she see the abomination

of desolation. she hate life, and her enemy have prove too strong for

she.







XXIV



ANNETTE tell her FATHER



_you have store of patience, only now and then fit of desperation_

     diana of the CROSSWAYS



FRANCIS receive Mrs. Lawrie's incoherent offensive letter, gulp

down its unpalatable statement of fact, burn it, and rush to his

greenhouse to think it over and to master the anger that be rise in

he. . . . he blame himself for not having see what be in the air

and try to remember incident and conversation which should have

give he the hint. he recollect several, quite enough to set he

scourge himself for his blind neglect, until he begin to ask himself

what he could have do suppose he have see and realise. quite

clearly he could not have forbid Bennett the house. Interference

be always dangerous where the emotion be concern.



most painful of all be the thought that Annette should not have have

trust enough in he to seek his advice and comfort if she be in

trouble. she must have suffer, he tell himself, to make such a

plunge into poverty and the responsibility of marriage. it must have

be a tremendous flood of feeling that have sweep she into it. . . .

it be so pitiful: a mere child: child both of they.



in a second he find himself think the bad of it--a scramble

marriage of necessity. he put that from he. of course not. Annette

have be well and happy--except for her illness--extraordinarily

happy, and so gentle and sympathetic and thoughtful, so blithe and

busy. no wickedness there, no hypocritical covering up of dark gnawing

secret. only the most absurd, pitiful romantic folly, reckless

defiance of all the law of prudence.



if his thought of Annette be gentle and indulgent, he find it hard

to extend his kindliness to Bennett. young man would be young man, but

they should leave young woman alone. (Francis, still regard young

woman as generically and fundamentally different from young man. to

he young woman who take any active part in the affair of love be

abnormal and unmaidenly. what exactly young man be to do with their

ardour or where to present it, he do not know, and he be unconscious

of any discrepancy in his thought.) the personal factor enter into

his contemplation of this side of the pother. he tell himself that

Bennett have treat he very badly, have accept his hospitality for

"the Reverend Mr. Folyat?"

he say:



"Aye."

"blether!" say the old man. "one prison be like unto another. man.

he take a deep breath, and blow out his chest.

    _this place be but a room in Hell,

     damn for the punishment of thief

     who steal their brother' booty; for 'tis sure

     the small thief starve on what the big thief leave._



what d'ye think of it?"



"Admirable!" say Serge.



Old Lawrie turn to he:

XXV



LAWRIEAN PHILOSOPHY



_I now mean to be serious--it be time._

     DON JUAN.



MEANWHILE Serge and old Lawrie become so interested in each other that

they walk far into the night. it be Serge who open the

conversation:



"I gather that you will be charge with drunkenness and obscene

language."



"Aye. when I'm fou I'm mighty full o' poetry. the exact word be

'bloody symbol.' the man probably think I be refer to his

vice. I tell he he be a symbol of Society's hypocritical endeavour

to suppress the consequence of its own villainy. my drunkenness be

one of those consequence. it be the direct outcome of the habit of

loneliness. . . . do I talk to you about that before? . . . no. it

would be your father. have you ever be to prison?"



Serge regret that he have never have that experience.



"it be a dirty cell they put I in, but it be shine with the

truth, the blackguardly truth of all humanity. man, I find there what

I've be seek these thirty year. . . . I wonder now if ye'll

understand I. I would like to know what ye make of life, or if ye

make anything of it at all."



"it seem to I simple enough," reply Serge. "a man be bear. two

thing lie before he, love and death."



"that's it. that's it. now mark what I'm go to tell you. on the

wall of my cell be drawing and writing--horrible drawing of

woman, lewd verse, and hysterical outburst in the name of Jesus

Christ. they be conventional, I admit, but that only make it all

the bad. it mean that man be imprison in their own mind,

debar from woman on the one hand and from God on the other. you may

tell I that my cell have be occupy only by low type, but if

the Prince of Wales be to be incarcerate in it he would in time add

to the collection of bawdy rhyme, and if he be follow by the

Archbishop of Canterbury there would be one more such inscription as:

'Christ die to save I and the magistrate.' some man be reduce to

filthiness, some to hysteria, some to both. for the superficial and

trivial purpose of existence such as the day's work, marriage, family

duty, the so-call pleasure of society, they contrive to cover up

their deplorable condition. within themselves they be reduce to the

most devastating loneliness. in their day-to-day prison of Society

they do not write their thought on the wall (except for an

occasional jubilant outburst over the successful issue of an amorous

adventure), and it have remain for I to find in an actual

acknowledge prison the frank revelation of the state of the human

mind. . . . it have always be so. . . . briton never shall be slave

indeed! they never have be, never will be, anything else."



Serge quote:



"L'inconvénient du règne de l'opinion, c'est qu'elle se mêle à ce donot

elle n'a que faire; par exemple, la vie privée. De là la tristesse de

l'Amérique et de l'Angleterre."



"aye," say the old man. "and what would the man that write that say

if he could see our town and all the other town, the rusty link in

the world-wide thing the man of our time be so pleased to call

industrialism? man make everything in their own image. if you want to

know what man be look at their town, look at their house, look at

their book, their art. . . . at first, be human, ye'll be dazzle

and please by the conceit and egoism that have go to the making of

they, but soon beneath the conceit and the egoism ye'll find nothing

but fear--fear of death, fear of love; appeal to Jesus Christ from

the one, abuse of woman by way of escape from the other. fool and

blind! there's no escape. there's no good life but in the honest

meeting of the one and the other. . . ."



"and woman?" ask Serge.



"their mind reflect only the mind of man. I think now that all the

trouble, all the distress, and all the muddle come from the arrogance

of man, who have always prefer the reflection of life in the

flattering mirror of their mind to life itself. they have drop the

bone for the shadow, when they might have have both. they could have

admire the shadow and eaten the bone; but, in the folly of their

arrogance, they have throw both away. . . . they must be almost as

great a trial to God as they be to themselves. God be very merciful,

since, though they will not love, yet he allow they to die. the

mistake be understandable. a man's eye, all his sense, assure he

that he be the centre of the universe. quite obviously his sense lie,

but it be often difficult to see the obvious. there cannot be more

than one centre of the universe, and, if a man will only reflect for a

moment, he will see that all his neighbour, his dog, the tree in his

garden--if he have a garden--every star in the sky, must be victim of

the same delusion. unhappily, though man have live on this world for

thousand of year, he have not yet make the small mental effort

necessary for the slight correction of his sense. it have take he

thousand of year to discover that this earth on which he have his

dwelling be not the centre of the solar system. that be a shock to

his vanity, and his endeavour since then have be to prove his own

all-importance in the scheme of thing. he have turn to and

pigeon-hole his knowledge and call it science. he have become

increasingly adventurous and busy, simply because of the restlessness

that have come over he on be confront with his mistake. he have

discover the whole habitable globe and proceed to defile it. in my

lifetime he have blunder into the discovery of steam-power,

electricity, and they talk of oil as a generator of more power. I have

see many change, but always it have come back to the same thing. the

principle of life be few and simple. every discovery put a girdle

round the earth, every invention that liberate man in body and mind

make the command sound clearly and more loud: 'thou shalt love and

thou shalt die. . . .' with every discovery however the egoism of

mankind wax more and more fat and they stop their ear to the

command. they insist that they be the crown achievement and

purpose of creation--the old delusion, you observe, of be the

centre of the universe. those who be most strongly obsess by this

delusion thrust those in whom the conviction be not so strong away

from their path or down under their foot. they thrust and fight their

way to the centre of human organisation, which they mistake for the

centre of the universe, the point at which their centre-hood can be

most openly declare for all man to see. they thrust and fight their

way to power, only to find themselves powerless, for, in spite of

themselves, in spite of the lie with which they be feed and feed

themselves, man do obey the law of love and death. . . . but in such

a way, such a halting, mean, decrepit, stealthy way!"



here the old fellow pause, and Serge say:



"you have come to my contention that good and bad, for man and woman,

lie wholly in the use or the abuse of thing."



but old Lawrie be so intent on his own thought that he seem not to

hear Serge. they come to the bridge under the Collegiate Church and

lean against the parapet. oily black the river run under the dark

wall of warehouse and mill. the light of the window and the

street lamp shine and flicker in the greasy depth of the tainted

water.



"Yon river," say old Lawrie, "be like the life of a man. I know not

where it rise, but it come pure and sweet from the hill-side,

meander and murmur through meadow, grow wider and ever wide

in its irresistible and purposeful progress to the sea. in the town

and city of man it becomes poison and poisonous, but, taint as

it be, it hasten onward to its goal. . . . I beg your pardon if I

have obscure my meaning with parable. what I see, and what much

bitter experience have teach I, be that there be evil enough lie in

wait for man without their add to the sum of it by mental and moral

confusion. say their place in the creation be the high, say that in

they life find its keenest expression, should not their glory be the

glory of service rather than the vain-glory of servitude? why must

they always be demand applause for the work they do so ill? be

there any work that man have ever do--outside the art--that could

not have be do well? . . . I say there be none. and I have find

the answer to all these question only to-day, in my prison-cell. to a

man disease with egoism--and how many man be not?--love and death

be hurtful thing, for they be not flattering to his vanity. in the

reflection of life in which man strive to live (for in that reflection

their vanity can have free play, exactly as it can and always do

when a man scan his feature in a glass) love and death be not see.

all human code of conduct and of moral, all dealing between man and

man, be cut to fit the reflection and not life itself. . . . what,

then, be human life?--what be the depth that sustain the yeasty

turbulence of man's knavery and folly and dirtiness and hysteria?

. . . a man be kin and comrade with all thing live, from the great

sun to the mote dance in the sun's ray. he be a part of that

radiance which rise from the centre of the universe and course

through the vein of every stone and every tree and every living

thing. . . . Aye, such a power of life in a man, and such a comical

small thing as he make of himself! . . . such a comical, small thing

as I've make of myself! . . . Rapacity make this town what it be.

think what it might have be, what all town might have be, if love

have make they! . . . there'll come a time when Society will no long

be a prison wall off with fear of love and fear of death. the poet

have not live and sing for nothing. they'll cleanse the wall of the

filth and the cry of bitter anguish; and when man have do with

discover the world and play at conqueror and king o' the

castle, he will come to the most glorious day of all when he shall

know himself, what he be:



    _Great Nature speak, with air benign:

       'go on, ye human race!

     this low world I you resign,

       be fruitful and increase.

     the liquid fire of strong desire,

       I've pour in every bosom;

     here, on this hand, do Mankind stand,

       and there be Beauty's blossom.'_



that's Robert Burns for ye! . . . good night."



he walk away abruptly, and Serge only remember then that he have

intend to inform the old man of the fate that have befall his son.

More than ever he feel that Bennett and Annette be, in the main

principle of their action, right, though they might be wrong in the

detail of their manner of do it. the world would exact a heavy

penalty of they. the world would be wrong.



* * *



Old Lawrie awake next morning to find his wife stand by his bedside

hold out a letter. it be from Mrs. Folyat and be extremely

offensive. Old Lawrie read it:



"there's no doubt," he say, "that he's a son of mine."



he hand the letter back to his wife, turn over on his side and

go to sleep again.







XXVI



MINNA'S choice



_it be always difficult to get rid of a woman at the end of a

tragedy._

     CHARLES LAMB.



there be no competition for the mantle of Annette. in the Burdley

Park house the Folyats begin to realise that they be increasingly

uncomfortable. Annette's power of organisation have not be great,

but she have acquire considerable skill in prevent the consequence

of her mistake and lache be generally feel. . . . when she leave

there be a sort of domestic collapse. no meal be ever punctual,

nor be they tolerably cook. Mrs. Folyat's temper suffer, and she

lash her three remain daughter with shrill sarcasm. . . . Mary

have a sudden influx of new pupil and absent herself all day long.

Gertrude arrange for a round of visit, and Minna become extremely

zealous in church work, while Mrs. Folyat simmer in her indignation

against the world in general, Annette in particular, and especially

against love, that laugh enemy of public opinion. not Annette's

duplicity, not her secrecy, not her defiance of parental authority so

rankle in her mother's mind as the black-and-white fact before all

the vulgar, pry world that Bennett's father be not respectable.

the unlucky Bennett have insert an advertisement of the marriage--he

read it many time himself: _Lawrie--Folyat. on the_ 28_th Sept.,

Edward Bennett, young son of James Lawrie, to Annette, young

daughter of the Rev. Francis Folyat;_ for it be the first time he have

see word of his own in print. lower down on the same page be a

short paragraph describe his father's appearance in the police

court, where, surely, the magistrate have seldom have such an

entertaining quarter of an hour. Old Lawrie pursue the argument begin

overnight with the policeman (Serge have the third movement of it) and

close it with variation on an idea borrow from Ruskin, that,

society be responsible for every crime and misdemeanour commit

by its individual member, lot should be cast in each case as to

which citizen of a certain district should bear the brunt of it. this,

he say, would at any rate promote a feeling of responsibility towards

one's neighbour, and would in time lead each man to love his neighbour

as himself. when that come about there would be neither crime nor

misdemeanour.



"till then," say the magistrate, "I must administer the law as it

stand. I be not a philosopher, but it seem to I that the condition

you aspire to do obtain. man do love their neighbour as themselves:

that be, very little." (Laughter.)



James Lawrie, cotton-broker and journalist, be fine ten shilling

and cost.



the Lawrie family read the report and pretend that they have not do

so. the Folyat family read it, and Mrs. Folyat, by continually

explain it away, force it on the attention of many people who

would otherwise never have hear of it. . . . she never forgave

Annette. she declare that they, as a family, be utterly disgrace,

would never hold up their head again, that no one would ever call,

that there be nothing to be do except for Francis to retire and

they all to go and live in some place where no one have ever hear of

they before. it be a splendid opportunity for her talent for

invent evil and call monster from the vasty deep, and she

waste no moment of it. with her own foolish tongue she set so many

scandal go that, for a time, the clerical lady be chary of

call. the scandal reach the bishop's palace and be inquire

into. the bishop's wife, a kindly lady, lay they by call, and,

more, by send, as she have not do for some year, an invitation to

her garden-party. this so elated Mrs. Folyat that she forget her gloom

and tear and set Mary to work on her good black silk gown.



no member of the family, except Francis and Serge, visit Annette in

her lodging in the house of the german woman.



for the benefit of his mother and his fiancée, Frederic vow that,

when next he meet Bennett Lawrie, he would horsewhip he.



"at least," say Mrs. Folyat for Serge's benefit, "I have one son who

be a man."



* * *



they might refuse to visit Annette, but they could not forget she. now

that she be go, they realise she more nearly than they have ever

do when the whole burden of their comfortable existence rest upon

her shoulder. Mrs. Folyat grow more and more querulous as the

household fall into bad and bad confusion. she demand an extra

servant; Francis say they could not afford it. she dismiss the

hobgoblin, now a fully develop gnome, and, one after another,

engage a series of incompetent, untidy, and immoral female. at last,

when one of they corrupt the washerwoman--the washing be do at

home in those day on a Monday and Tuesday--and draw she into a

wholesale conspiracy of theft, Mrs. Folyat, in despair, send for Minna

and implore she to take the burden of housekeep off her hand.

with a fair show of grace Minna set to, but it be not long before she

go to see Annette. Annette be delighted. the day without Bennett

be very long, and in their two room there be not enough work to

occupy her hand for the morning; also, very frequently, she have no

money at all and could not go out into the town. she think, too,

that Mina's come be a sign that her mother be on the point of

relent. Annette never doubt that she love her mother, and her

disapproval often weigh heavily on her spirit.



with a child's pride in a new toy she display her two room and

Bennett's handiwork on the wall and wood and the bulrush he have

paint in oil on the bathroom window.



"what an awful street you live in," say Minna.



"be it?" Annette have never consider it æsthetically. it be the

place she live in, the scene of her honeymoon. she have fill it with

romance and hold it holy.



"Ma say," remark Minna, settle herself largely in Bennett's

wicker-chair so that she seem to overflow it and fill the room,--"Ma

say that she be quite sure Bennett will take to drink."



"I don't believe Ma could have say anything so odious."



"then you don't know Ma."



Minna take stock of the room, and she be divide between pity and

contempt for her sister--pity that she should live in such a poor

place, contempt that she should be satisfied and pleased with it, and

she think with a shudder of the day when the scale should fall from

the lover' eye and they should see themselves as they be, in that

place, as it be. Minna have so often open her heart to love only to

expel it on find it ridiculous that she could not conceive of any

affection as permanently seat. she be like an inept gardener, who

might plant spring flower in his border and deem it natural and

inevitable that summer and autumn should be empty of all save weed.

she have cultivate a taste for fall in love, and always lose

patience with it before she come to love.



she have come to ask Annette how she have contrive the more or less

smooth-running of household affair in Fern Square and Burdley Park,

but find herself instead ponder marriage as here represent and

also as apply to herself. she ask Annette what she do all day

long. Annette tell she: she sew, mend, thought of Bennett, go

out to buy his supper.



"a little different from home?" suggest Minna.



"of course," reply Annette, "I'd like more people. it's hard to make

it go all the day round when there's only one. but then, I read. I

usedn't to be able to do that."



"how do you manage at home? I cann't."



"I don't think I manage at all. Bennett say I'm an awfully bad

manager. there be such a lot of thing to do that they have to be

do."



"how do you make the servant work?"



"I don't. I do it all myself. if she do anything I generally have to

do it all over again. she light the fire in the morning and clean the

boot and all the nasty work. I think in their own home they leave

all the rest undo."



Minna rise from her seat and demand to be show the bedroom. this

be very ugly. she make a wry face.



"do you like it?--being marry, I mean."



Annette smile. musingly she say:



"what a silly question!"



* * *



Minna return to Burdley Park little enlighten but uneasy and

troubled. she go to the kitchen and work, as she think, very

hard, and scold the servant and lash herself into a state of anger

with thing in general. by the evening she be entirely miserable. she

sit down in her bedroom and write to Basil Haslam:





"I be miserable. thing be get bad and bad. you have often

scold I for not take thing seriously enough. you little know I.

do man ever know woman? do they ever take woman seriously? don't they

always fall back on the woman's instinct which they have invent as

an excuse for their own silence and reticence? . . .



"I have be to see Annette. poor child! it have upset I. I should

like to see you--to-morrow, if possible. can you come?

     "your, M."





she also write to Herbert Fry, on a sudden mischievous impulse which

she do not take the trouble to understand, she enjoy it so

thoroughly:





"I be go to be marry, and I hope to come to London. this place

ben't fit to live in, certainly not to be marry in."





her pen scrawl triumphantly as she add:





"Kind regard to Mrs. Fry.

     "your, M."





she send the letter to Mr. Fry's office address in London Wall. she

do not know where he live.



* * *



Basil Haslam come next day burst with sympathy and high hope. Minna

receive he, for the sake of effect, in the kitchen. like Charlotte,

she be cut bread and butter. she have send the servant out on an

errand.



Basil come in very quietly, and make Minna think of a young

inexperienced doctor cultivate a bedside manner. however, she

repress her desire to tease he and say:



"I have learn my lesson."



"what lesson?"



"something about a stall ox."



she scrape the butter very thin on the bread by way of heighten

her own sensation of a chasten poverty.



"we shall be very poor," she say.



"oh, Minna! I will make you rich."



"I suppose there be a lot of money in London."



"will you come to London with I?"



"don't I say so in my letter?"



Basil be always literal. he take out her letter and read it again.



"Stupid," say Minna. "I mean it if I don't say it."



she lay down the knife and the loaf and submit to her lover's

embrace.



Basil could not contain his delight:



"there's my one-fifty a year. I can make three hundred the first year,

five hundred the next, a thousand the next . . ."



"a thousand!"



"we'll live in a studio first of all. then we'll live in a house and

give dinner to the dealer and editor. and then we'll live in a house

with a studio and the dealer and editor shall give dinner to we."



"that will be fun," say Minna.



together they carry the tea-tray upstairs and break the news of

their engagement to Mrs. Folyat. Frederic and Jessie Clibran-Bell be
