there. they have be conspire with Mrs. Folyat to bring about a

speedy wedding. with the assistance of Mr. Clibran-Bell Frederic have

be take work on his own account and have make fifty pound in a

year. Herbert Fry have assist he by let he act as his agent--on

condition that he have Frederic's agency work in London--and now there

be talk of his set up in an office of his own, if his father

could guarantee he one year's expense.



Mrs. Folyat set all this before Basil and Minna, and excitedly they

plan a double wedding in two month' time.



Mrs. Folyat see in this project the chance of wipe out the stain of

Annette's offence.



* * *



Francis be approach that very night. he be for wait. they could

sell no more of their Potsham house, or there would be no provision

for their old age. (he have already begin to think dimly of retirement

to the soft south and a garden.) Mrs. Folyat, however, have set she

heart on the plan. she wheedle, cajole, coax, scold, suggest

scheme after scheme, until Francis agree to sell his life-insurance

policy, but on condition that the proceed be divide equally

between his child with the exception of Leedham, who be married to

a wealthy portuguese widow, ten year his senior, in Rio . . . Mrs.

Folyat pounce on that, and next morning see to it that he begin to

take the necessary step.



twelve hundred pound be raise by this mean. Serge disapprove and

disclaim his share, so that the rest have two hundred and forty

pound each.



Frederic take an office near Serge's studio, engage two clerk, and

be regard as sufficiently establish to enter into the state of

matrimony.



there be an entertaining wedding. Bennett and Annette be invite

and formally take back into the fold. Basil and Minna Haslam go to

London to spend their honeymoon in the studio they have take in

Chelsea. Frederic and Jessie Folyat take a house next door but one to

James Lawrie's. there be many tear shed over the bride, and after

Mrs. Folyat have deliver herself of a sort of funeral oration _à la_

Bossuet, Minna whisper to Serge:



"Ma always do love a theatrical performance."



"'your son's your son till he get he a wife,'" say Mrs. Folyat to

Frederic, and then to Minna she complete the tag, "'your daughter's

your daughter the rest of your life.'"



it be a very exciting and a very happy day in the Folyat household.

Mrs. Folyat chatter all the evening. Mary and Gertrude say not a

word and go silently off to bed, so that Francis be compel to

escort his wife to her room and perform the innumerable little

service she require.



"don't the house feel empty?" she say.



Francis mumble.



"I'm sure Annette be go to have a baby."



"M-m-m-m."



"I think I be never go to be a grandmother."



"you always be impatient," say Francis in an unexpectedly loud

voice.



that be criticism, which she could not abide. Francis rue his

precipitancy. he be very unhappy about Frederic, but, he ask

himself, what could he do? what could he do? there be no doubt that

Jessie love Frederic, but do not that, in itself, the more

dangerously expose she to his folly and weak selfishness?



he hardly hear his wife's word as she went maunder on. in the

darkness he pray that all might be, as he try to believe, for the

good.







XXVII



GERTRUDE make the best of it



_De quel ravissement nous privent nos intempérance._

     JOUBERT.



when Annette's baby--a boy--be bear, Gertrude be the first to go and

see it. she take with she a woollen bonnet and a horn spoon.



having become capitalist with the enormous sum that have come to

Annette, they have leave their lodging for a little house in a row of

little house each of seven room and a scullery. they have a little

maid, who open the door to Gertrude. she be a tiny wizened creature

but very voluble. Gertrude be not a yard inside the house when she

have a full description of the baby, its layette, Annette's condition

and appearance, and the devotion of Bennett, who, she say, "never have

no eye for nothink 'cept 'be ugly little wife."



Gertrude be show upstairs, to find Annette sit up chatter to

an enormously fat woman, who be introduce to she as Mrs. Entwistle.

they be talk about Serge, of whom the fat woman express the

most glowing admiration.



the baby, a very little one, ugly and blotch, be hand to

Gertrude, and she be properly ecstatic over it. Mrs. Entwistle say:



"Eeh! ow I do 'ave to slap 'be little buttock to make 'Im cry!"



"Slap?" say Gertrude, rather horrified.



"Eeh! Miss, don't ye know that? well, I never. sometimes you 'ave to

fair leather into 'em."



Gertrude hold the baby in her arm and hug he close to her breast.

she be feel very mournful, and envy tug at her heart. she say:



"it's a very little house you live in."



"ben't it? but we love it. it's just big enough for the three of we."



"how--how be Bennett?"



"oh! he's very well, and he get more money now, though still very

little. I'm afraid we shall never have very much as long as he remain

in business, and if he leave it I suppose we should have nothing. but

we don't think about it--much."



"you must be very happy."



very mournfully Gertrude say this. she be disappoint. she have

fancy that when she hold Annette's baby in her arm she would feel

all kind of beautiful and exalted emotion. it be certainly pleasant

to feel its warmth, and to hold it, so helpless as it be, give she a

genial sense of protection, but she be want, hope for more than

that. and when Annette reply that she be very happy--she look it

too--Gertrude realise painfully that she be brutally indifferent.



the starving cannot rejoice with the well-feed.



Gertrude feel her life trickle away through her finger: bad of

all, though she be not conscious of it, her desire for life be

ebb away from she. all the bitterness, all the hunger, all the hard

envy in her heart she translate into one word: "Old." she say to

herself: "I be get old." . . . having come to a concise and

rounded thought she be prick by it into revolt, and she say

gently, at first, to Annette:



"I envy you. I remember you when you be a little girl. I have always

thought of you as little, so that I have hardly know you. . . . and I

must have always seem to you beyond your reach. now it be you who

be beyond mine. ben't it funny?"



she give the child to Annette, watch it blindly wriggle against

its mother's breast, and tear trickle down her nose on to the

counterpane. Annette be so engross in her boy that she do not

notice it, and Gertrude be at once ashamed of her tear, brush they

away, and angrily, in her heart, accuse Annette of selfishness. she

would have be so grateful only for a little pressure of the hand, a

little smile, something that would bid she come into the circle of

warmth, so radiant with the joy of the child. she be too timid, too

much take up with pity for herself, to force her way in. she dared

not assume that she would be welcome, for she be too conscious of her

own awkwardness.



she let slip the opportunity as she have spoil so many. the conflict

in her soul leave she bruised and sore, and she almost hate

Annette--Annette who have lie and cheat to take her lover. she

turn from her thwart emotion to sentimentality, rake over the

ashe of the past, and artificially reconstruct the ruse and

strategem that she suppose Annette have use to capture Bennett

during her absence. . . . with effusive cordiality she kiss Annette

and the baby and promise often to come and see it. a little

awkwardly--she be not always tactful--Annette explain that

Bennett's sister be to be the baby's Godmother. that give Gertrude

the handle she be seek, and she persuade herself that she have

deliberately be slight.



she go away almost without another word. on her way home she be

thrust by her fancied injury into contemplate her future. as

people always do when they contemplate the future, she lose sight of

the infinite gradation which lead from the point at which she stand to

the point on which her eye be fix, so that all her forward life

be present to her mental vision as acid, cold, bitterly assail

she without clemency. all her desire be to escape that future, and to

evade the phantom conjure up by her own mind--a mind very similar to

her mother's and also infect by it--and to do so in a way that

should, if ever so slightly, prick Annette's conscience . . .



idea be too often the gaoler of our soul, which, seek health

and freedom, grope out of prison, take counsel of the first-comer,

an idea whom we have fee'd with prejudice and cowardice to stand guard

over we. Gertrude, seek freedom from her home, from her own folly,

from herself, accost the first-comer, marriage, who, with a false

smile, open a door and clap she into another cell. this, be

large than the other, she take for a place wide open to the wind of

Heaven, and pass from querulous fear of the future to excitement in

the immediate view. to be sure, she only see four wall, but there be

more light on they, more air and mystery between she and they. . . .

above all, nowhere in her cell could she see the figure of her sister

Mary, whom she have begin to detest, nervously and irritably. . . .

Mrs. Folyat have grow more and more incapable. the work of the house

be divide between Gertrude and Mary. between the two there be a

grim struggle as to which of the two should make herself the less

indispensable to her mother. it be very certain, as both know in

their inmost heart, that if one of they be to be leave, that one

would remain for ever, with nothing to do save to turn the hour-glass

when the sand run out. Mary, be the weak of the two, be the

more good-natured, and it be for Mary that Mrs. Folyat most often

call when she drop her knitting-needle, or mislay her

spectacle, or lose her book by sit on it, or wish to play

Patience at some inappropriate hour. everybody say Mrs. Folyat be a

dear old lady. she like the character, clung to it and abuse it.

either Gertrude or Mary must be gobble up by her selfishness. both

Gertrude and Mary believe that their mother be a dear old lady. they

dream not that they be in revolt against she, but fancy--as it

seem more heroical to do--that they be at grip in a fearful

struggle with life. they be both very near hysteria, Gertrude, after

her visit to Annette, be the nearer.



* * *



there come to live near the town at this time Mrs. Bradby-Folyat, an

aunt of the Folkestone Folyats, an old lady of much wealth, whose

estate be continually be augment by legacy bequeath by

irascible Bradbys and Folyats who be sicken by the attention of

their legacy-hunt poor relation. Mrs. Bradby-Folyat leave she

relation alone, and the harvest of her wisdom be great. . . . be

a lady of strong character and almost masculine intelligence she have a

great fondness for the weak and almost idiotic Streeten Folyat, who

long ago have abandon his sheep-farm in Westmoreland and wander

from one profession to another, shed in each a portion of his

patrimony. between journalism and market-gardening he spend several

month with his aunt at Boynton and amuse himself in the town in

Frederic's company. occasionally he visit the house in Burdley Park.

. . . then he buy a small fleet of fishing-smack at Scarborough,

sell they after ten month at a heavy loss and return to Boynton.

his income have dwindle to four hundred. he buy house in our town

and be quickly embroil in a law-suit--his idleness make he

quarrelsome--and place the case in Frederic's hand. by sheer luck

Frederic win the case and delight the old lady at Boynton, who

insist on consider that he have save Streeten from ruin. she

invite Frederic and his wife to stay with she, and entrust he with

the management of her estate. Frederic be almost delirious at this

access of fortune, and calculate that if the old lady live for

another ten year he would make at least six thousand pound. he be

in debt--he could not amuse himself with Streeten for nothing--and he

borrow money from a friendly moneylender whose rate of interest per

cent. _per mensem_ seem reasonable and low.



when Frederic be not at Boynton Streeten be at Frederic's house, and

when Streeten be at Frederic's house there also be Gertrude.

Streeten be amazingly vain, a fop, and as eager to scan his feature

in the glass as a little boy just on the verge of adolescence, who be

begin to feel that the eye of the world be upon he. such man,

when no mirror be near, will turn to the near woman. if in she he

can see the faint reflection of himself, pat he will fall in love

with it. . . . there be not many mirror in Frederic's house.

Streeten turn to Jessie but see only Frederic, to Gertrude then, and

he see himself enlarge, heighten, dazzle. it be the most

bewildering reflection of himself that he have ever see, and at once

he be prostrate before it.



almost before he could realise what have happen he be pick up,

thrust into a frock-coat and silk hat, take to church, marry to

Gertrude, and pack off for a honeymoon to Ilfracombe. he be very

bored and savage. he want to be at Boynton or amuse himself with

Frederic.



it be one thing to steal glance at your own reflection when you think

no one be look, quite another to be married to it, though the

mirror tell its tale never so constantly.



it be too cruel, it be indecent, to write of Gertrude's honeymoon.







XXVIII



MOTHER and DAUGHTER



_life . . . be like love. all reason be against it, and all healthy

instinct for it._

     EREWHON revisit.



ON hearing of the capture of Streeten, celebrate in the most jubilant

strain by Mrs. Folyat, with a magnificently unjust comparison of her

new son-in-law with Bennett Lawrie, Minna write this letter to Mary:





"mother Bub love cut her lamb up into chop. do she fix he with

an eye? you know how she can bore into the back of a man's neck. I'm

almost sorry I miss the fun. I suppose she'll be well off than any

of we. we're beastly poor, the studio's always in a mess and we cann't

get it straight. London be amusing but awfully big and callous. it

make you feel that it never care whether you're there or not, and

the river be almost the most human thing in it. nobody seem to want

Basil's work. I suppose there be thousand of Basils all want to

do the same thing, and I suppose each Basil have a _I_ want a great

deal to eat and more pleasure and fun than be good for she, and not

care particularly how much of his soul he have to sell to give it

she. the Folyats have a certain charm, but we're all selfish--except,

of course, dear old Ma, who always would have it that we get our

wickedness from Pa. Mother Bub have the charm of a basilisk, or a

fly-paper. I hope she will come to London, as it will be nice to

borrow money from she.

     Dearest love, Mottle dear,

          M.



P.S.--Basil have just sell a drawing. sausage and mash for supper!

also beer!



P.P.S.--Did Bennett go to the wedding? how grateful he must have be

to streeten for step into his shoe.



P.P.P.S.--I wouldn't get married if I be you. and to think I might

have be a Countess! Willie Folyat live in London--the Hearl of

Leedham, if you please. I hear he's turn out a horrid little prig. I

know he would, I feel it in my bone. I have such clever bone.

          M. H."





Mary be magnanimous and keep the letter to herself. she be

essentially good-natured and bear no malice. she be amuse by Minna's

spite and do not believe a word she say. to her Gertrude be happy;

she be marry, therefore she love her husband and her husband love

she. it be impossible for Mary to take a detached view, to tell black

from white, good from bad. she be mentally short-sighted and her

pleasure lie entirely in sentiment. she love music as she love

nothing else in the world, but her pleasure in it be the pleasure of

rhythm. Harmony touch she not at all. she have a sort of nervous

sensitiveness which make she extremely shy and unresponsive. a kind of

island existence be her, and the island on which she dwelt be in a

perpetual fog. every sound that reach she from without--and little

else but sound do reach she--be blur. voice more easily move

she than action or the expression of a person's face. she have always

love her father because of his soft, gentle voice, and when Serge be

in the house she be animate and more quickly interested in what be

go on around she.



she accept her defeat by Gertrude docilely enough, give up the

majority of her pupil and much of her chamber-music and take up the

rein of the household. with only her father and mother to provide

for, it be fairly easy, and the expense be so much reduce that

she be able to pay more wage and to procure a well class of maid.

Mrs. Folyat take a dislike to the maid, and all her service have to be

perform by Mary herself.



Serge have fall into the habit of take supper with his family--his

father and mother and Mary--nearly every Sunday evening, and he be

exasperate by the petty attention which his mother be continually

demand. he have try many a time to find a way to the heart of this

curious, stupid, yet gentle and kindly sister of his, but he have

always find it impossible to set her thought move. she seem to

have almost lose the capacity of thinking, and she have so little sense

of humour that any blunt statement of fact hurt she as a direct

attack. she show in many way that she be fond of he, but it be

as a dog be fond, with a mute uncomprehending sympathy.



Serge fall back on action. whenever his mother, in the metallic tone

of her querulous mood, ask Mary to fetch her book from the other end

of the house, or to unravel her knitting when she have drop a

stitch, or to read to she because her eye ache, serge bustlingly and

rather ostentatiously forestall his sister. there be never any sign

that he have produce any effect on mother or daughter.



this go on for month. existence in the house in Burdley Park pass

smoothly and placidly, and Francis seem to be happy, as he have never

be, busy with his parish and greenhouse. he be silent for day

together, except that every now and then he would hum tunelessly to

himself, boom like a bumble-bee. he have every small joy that he

ask of the world and be content. he like the new generation of his

parishioner well than the old, they be not so dour, and

everything seem to he to be go well and happily. he see Frederic

very seldom, and Annette very frequently, and Minna and Gertrude be

regular correspondent. Serge have not for a very long time ask he

for money, and for the first time for many, many year his expense

and income be on good term with each other. good of all, Mrs.

Folyat have begin to see herself and her husband as a sort of Darby and

Joan, and sweeten her conduct to fit the character. everybody say

you might go far before you could find a more delightful old couple.

they achieve a sort of celebrity.



Mary too come in for her share of the general admiration, and her

devotion to her mother be by more than one tyrannous old woman

brandish over the head of a peevish and fade daughter. Mary recke

nothing of it, and it would have make no impression on she if she have.



one Sunday, when her mother have rather explosively demand she

spectacle, and Serge without a word have go down to the dining-room

for they, and without a word have give they to she, a new idea come to

Mary. she sit stupidly gaze at her mother and very slowly she begin

to think what would become of she if her father and mother be to

die. there be a loud-tick clock in the room, and it say with

remorseless insistency:



"I-shall-be-alone."



this be a very dreadful idea to she. she strangle it.



there be no getting rid of thought. if they be strangle their

corpse remain and rot in the mind, to its last detriment. this

idea remain in Mary's mind, cold and dead, and gradually poison

the sweetness of her nature.



a fix idea be a dead idea.



* * *



Mary's temper suffer, and she vent her spleen on her pupil to

such a degree that there come a time when she have only one left--the

young daughter of the sausage-machine manufacturing widower, all of

whose daughter she have instruct in turn in a polite mastery of the

violin. her bi-weekly visit to his house have become part of the

routine of his household, and she be part and parcel of its

furniture, be fit into the heavy dinner-party when there be a

gap. at other entertainment she be never omit and be as much

part of their colour as the fade good chair-cover that be take

out for the occasion or the japanese lantern which illuminate they.

she be useful, for she be always willing to play the piano for hour

on end when the young people want to dance. to the widower, who

never see she on other occasion, she be always associate with

gaiety.



one day he propose marriage to she, and she refuse he.



"be sure," he say, "that I shall always be your friend. and I shall

continue to hope."



he be really relieve at be reject, and retire to cogitate the

extraordinary impulse which have drive he to do such a thing. he feel

very uncomfortable, for the fiction with which he have surround his

dead wife have be shake.



Mary leave his house in a flutter. the man have always be kind to she,

he be an admirable father, and she have always respect he as a

solid man, though he be a little too northernly solid to be take

altogether seriously. his house be very comfortable, though ugly and

too reekingly prosperous, but the habit of year have make it a corner

of the equilateral triangle of her life. its atmosphere be altogether

different from that of her home and deliver she from monotony.



she only feel about his proposal of marriage be one of surprise.

she think of it only materially and be not signally disturb by it

until he send she an extraordinary letter in which he seek to

explain away his behaviour. in the course of it he cut deeply in his

contemplation of the marriage--having be marry--and have,

unwittingly, set his emotion stir. Mary's emotion respond, but

her modesty pluck they back. she do not, she tell herself, "love"

Mr. Hargreave, because, after all, he be only a common man who have

begin life as a boy in a smithy. it have be easy enough when she

think of marriage as a mere translation from one house to another,

but, quite clearly, he be ask for a personal relation, and from

that she shrank, chiefly because it be a change from the custom of

year. when she desire a concrete objection she fish out from the

confusion in her mind the prejudiced idea that she would be a

stepmother; bad than that, a stepmother to child to whom she have

be a pay instructress, child moreover who have, upon occasion,

be so cruel to she that she have be unable to retort upon they or

to maintain discipline. . . . she read Mr. Hargreave's letter once

more and see that she could not easily enter his house again, except

it be to leave her home.



she leave the matter for a day or two, do not reply, and fail to

attend for Violet Hargreave's violin lesson. a day or two more and she

receive another letter from Mr. Hargreave in which he completely

abase himself, and, in his desire to be kind and to palliate the

affront he conceive himself to have put upon she, wax tender and

be almost lyrical in her praise.



she try to write to he but could find nothing to say. tear roll

down and plop on to the paper, and she grow hot and impatient with

herself. clearly she must go to his house and reassure he. no soon

have she resolve on that, than she feel that she could not explain

herself, that he would renew his proposal, and she would have to say

either "yes" or "no." if "yes," then a thousand and one objection

would rise in her mind. if "no," then it would become impossible for

she ever to enter his house again.



she dry her eye and resolve that she would go there and then and

get it over. it be evening. she could ask for one of the Hargreave

girl, leave a piece of music; she be familiar enough at the house;

no one would suspect the undercurrent.



as she go downstairs her mother call to she. she could not find

"Johnny Ludlow" anywhere, and what have Mary do with it, and why be

she so careless?



"I be not careless," reply Mary. "you be read it yourself. you

must have leave it in your room."



"Johnny Ludlow" be find behind the cushion of Mrs. Folyat's chair.

Mary feel a gust of impatience as she give the book to her mother. she

sit down suddenly, and with a desperate gulp she say very quickly:



"Mr. Hargreave have ask I to marry he!"



"you, my dear . . . Mr. Hargreave! he must be nearly sixty!"



the word "sixty" chill Mary.



"fifty-one," she murmur.



"an old man like that . . . really he ought to be ashamed of himself.

he ought to be prepare himself for his eternal life instead of

thinking of a wife. . . . and with all those child too."



Mary's sense of justice be offend.



"a great many widower marry again for the sake of their child,

don't they?"



"but Mr. Hargreave have two grow-up daughter."



again there be a chilly catch at Mary's heart, and she have a lump in

her throat. she say:



"no one else have ever ask I to marry."



it need melodrama to move Mrs. Folyat; tragedy or tragi-comedy leave

her blank. she be in no mood for general consideration, for she be

think with cold practicability of the need of the moment. when she

think of the house without Mary it be as a place of absolute

silence. there be many evening when Francis say never a word; many

again when he sit alone in his study or work in his greenhouse, and

only come up just before it be time to go to bed. Mrs. Folyat have a

horror of silence. . . . Mary must not go, she think, Mary must not

go. she come swiftly to the point and ask:



"have you accept he?"



unreasonably, in the face of experience, Mary have be expect

sympathy; she so crave it. for a flickering moment she desire almost

viciously to lie, but she be hurt into truth.



"no," she say.



Mrs. Folyat sigh with relief and triumph.



"of course you couldn't," she say. "he be such a common man. . . .

let we play Bézique."



Mary fetch the card and they play until Francis come up at ten

o'clock. she let he take her hand and go downstairs to the kitchen

to brew her mother's chocolate. she have lose all interest in Mr.

Hargreave, and she feel nothing at all.



in her bedroom that night she find it quite easy to write to he. she

say that she trust he to understand that she could not marry he,

and that it would be good in the circumstance if he find another

teacher for Violet. he have (she continue) always be very kind to

she. she be very grateful to he, and would think well of he, but

her duty lie towards her father and mother.



so, without any ill-feeling she slip into the part design for she

by her mother.



as she be write to Mr. Hargreave, her mother say to Francis:



"my dear, what do you think? that horrid old Hargreave have actually

propose to Mary. of course she refuse he."



"Poor Mary," say Francis.







XXIX



discussion



_will kein Gott auf Erde sein?

 sind wir selber Götter._

      W. MÜLLER.



through the year Father Soledano have remain a fairly frequent

visitor at the house of the Folyats. his be the only really constant

intimacy that Francis enjoy, and it be base on the kinship of

their humour and their common taste for mental caricature. both

stranger to our town and dwelling outside its activity, they love to

foregather and burlesque its politic, its manner, and its worship of

money. Father Soledano go far than Francis and poke his fun at

english institution, though then he become malicious and Francis

could not see eye to eye with he. Francis have no politic save a

dislike for Mr. Gladstone and a distrust of Benjamin Disraeli and Lord

Salisbury, and he know too little of modern English literature to be

able to appreciate the priest's sarcasm at the expense of Carlyle,

Ruskin, George Eliot, or Robert Browning. he have never hear of George

Meredith, but he become almost angry when Soledano scoff at Dickens

and Thackeray. . . .



their discussion use to take place on Sunday evening in the study,

and it often happen that Serge be present. one Sunday night when,

as often happen, Soledano, hark back to the Manchester murder, he

launch out upon a violent assault upon England, and quote once more

word that be often upon his lip, word of the mother of Charles

Stewart Parnell:



"'the English be hate everywhere for their arrogance, greed, cannot,

and hypocrisy. they want we all to think they be so goody-goody. they

be simply thief.'"



"oh! come, come," say Francis, "not so bad as that. after all we have

give the world a good deal and show the way to other nation in

many thing."



"you have show the other nation how to steal."



"I don't think any nation, or any collective body of man who have

pool their sense of right and wrong need much instruction in that,"

say Serge. "it be simply a question of steal from a body of man

weak than themselves. man in the mass be abominable. there ben't

anything to choose between England and France, or Italy, or the new

German Empire or America. England have be more successful than the

rest and have therefore have more opportunity of do harm. . . ."



"good as well," put in Francis. "good as well."



"only incidentally and by accident," retort Soledano. "what I

contend be that you cannot have collective villainy and individual

virtue, collective bad action and individual good action."



"you cann't indict a nation," say Serge.



"I can and I do."



"then you be not so clever as I think. there be no such thing as

collective action, there be only the action of individual. you herd

man together so that they may carry out the will of individual, and,

as in the present condition of society, the most cunning and

cold-blooded and unscrupulous man survive to exercise their will over

the herd. what be produce by the herd be almost always bad, because

their effort be direct only towards base ideal. . . . in the long

run it may be a good thing to gather man together into huge masse for

the easy and more expeditious creation of wealth and the mean of

subsistence. I don't know. I cann't see into the future. but you and my

father know--who better?--how the poor be be grind down in this

town, and it must be the same in every other. what appal I be that

there be no sort of corrective to the base ideal of success and

accumulate wealth and what be call power except the blind revolt of

nature in man and woman--especially woman. there be absolutely

nothing. there be a certain number of artist in this town, man of my

own trade, but they all seem to be do their work from the dealer's

point of view, to produce a saleable article, and not for the sheer

delight of exercise a talent, without which the result cannot give

delight. the theatre be even bad: they be feed from London with

stupid replica of piece design to give the illusion of pleasure

rather than pleasure itself. the newspaper will soon be nothing but

advertising sheet. it will soon be impossible for any man to do his

work with any joy in it. it be bad enough when a man waste himself in

feed his own vanity, but when he be use only to feed the vanity of

another man then there be absolutely no hope for he. there might be

something say for an arrangement by which a man give a certain number

of hour of his day to joyless work, so that during the rest he can

take joy in other thing. but all these man who be do work in

which there be no reward at all be pay so little that they be

shatter by financial anxiety. they marry wife whom they cannot

afford to keep and produce child whom it be impossible for they to

feed and educate. . . . England be in a bad way, Father. it seem to

I rather unfair to attack she when she be down."



"the great Empire the world have ever see!" say Father Soledano

mockingly.



Francis look thoughtful. he light his pipe and say:



"I wish I understand what you be talk about, Serge."



"I want a corrective," answer Serge. "all this material organisation

may be a good thing in the long run, but spiritual health be every bit

as important as physical health--more. they're organise education

now, but towards no ideal save the base ideal of cunning unscrupulous

man--self-help, and all that. a man's life consist of only two

thing, work and love. at present love be wipe out of consideration

altogether, and work be regard as a damned unpleasant thing that have

to be stomach. at present a man must be either a slave or an

employer of slave, that is, a slave who be promote. if you promote a

slave to the condition of a free man he go bad, because he have the

soul of a slave and cannot live except under tyranny. if he escape

from the tyranny of a man he seek that of his own vice. . . . if you

educate man as slave they will be slave, just as your Loyola say,

Father--every child who pass through the jesuit' hand remain

theirs for ever. . . . you get revolt every now and then as in the

French Revolution and in 1848, but that be nothing but the desire of

the slave of poverty for the slavery of wealth."



"Christopher Sly," say Soledano, "will always be Christopher Sly. if

you be stupid enough you can stand anything. man be stupid. that be

the whole story. when you have say that you have say everything."



Serge bring his fist down on the table.



"I don't believe it. if, inspire by a base ideal, they can do all

that they have do, they can, when inspire by a noble ideal, the

simple and most beautiful of all, the ideal of a life of love and

work, do well yet and gain material well-being in justice through

spiritual health."



"bah!" say Soledano. "that be your english idealism. man can only

understand a base ideal. they be impel only by one

instinct--hunger. they be terrify of hunger and fight only to

protect themselves against it. all their other instinct, even the

instinct of reproduction, have to take their chance--a very poor one.

also my friend, your idealism be just a joke to woman. life be too

serious, too immediately appal for they, for they be just as

cruelly drive by their instinct of reproduction as they be by the

instinct of hunger."



"very well, then," say Serge, "drop the idealism and call it

practical good sense. concentrate on the instinct of hunger and the

instinct of reproduction and organise for the satisfaction of both."



"it be impossible. you be ask man to be intelligent. the English

will never be that."



Father Soledano say good-night to Francis and hold out his hand to

Serge.



"I'm come with you," say Serge.



"still unconvinced?"



"absolutely convinced that I be right."



they drive back in a cab to the priest's house in the asphalt

courtyard under the cathedral.



"will you tell I," ask Serge, "how you reconcile what you have say

this evening with what you say in your church?"



"I don't."



"can you go on?"



"like the rest of the world, I do what I be tell. if I examine and

scrutinise everything that I be tell to do I should do very little

of it. . . . on the whole we do good. we save a certain number of man

from sink into brutality, and to a certain number of other we give

an outlet for their emotion, which amount to the same thing."



"how much do you believe of what you tell they?"



"I have never examine my belief. like your father, I do what I be

tell to do. suppose I renounce my faith and the priesthood. my place

would be take by another. there be too many man, my friend, too many

woman, and life move both too slowly and too swiftly . . . what can

you do? you say that the good life consist wholly of work and love.

then work, my friend, and love. there be nothing to prevent you. I

also work, and I also love. very lovingly I despise man, because I

know they, as you, I think, do not."



"quite candidly, it seem to I cowardly and rather despicable to

teach man to believe in another life beyond the grave."



"life, as it be, must be make supportable."



"from within, not from without."



"you seem to be level an accusation at my Church, but you must be

just and observe that we do display, for the benefit of the man whose

soul be our care, a certain faith in the next life by renounce the

pleasure of this."



"you stifle an instinct. that seem to I as great a sin as abuse it

by excess of the pleasure derive from its satisfaction."



"I find your point of view interesting, but too naïve and simple. the

idea of original sin may be fanciful, it may have its origin in

oriental myth, but there be contamination from some source or other."



"simply from a wrong interpretation of life. I say it be possible for

man to understand life."



"it be quite impossible. they can only live it."



"then there be absolutely no meaning in all their activity, all their

invention, all their discovery . . ."



"I can see none. they be still the slave of hunger. when you appease

the hunger of the body there remain the hunger of the spirit."



"exactly, and I contend that in the hand of intelligent man the

machinery in their power could satisfy the bodily hunger of all man,

and set they free to find satisfaction for the hunger of the spirit.

. . . as I see it, it be towards that that the world be tend. there

will be a great deal of cruelty and oppression by the way, but there

will come a time when man's mastery of the world will be so great that

anything save the most elastic organisation will make life intolerable

for rich and poor alike. as you say, if you be stupid enough you can

endure anything. man be more intelligent now than they be fifty

year ago. they will be ten time as intelligent fifty year hence.

. . ."



"look at home, my friend. look at home."



"I do. and it be just that absurdly pathetic tragi-comedy that make

I scan the world to see what hope there be for future generation.

. . . you make the mistake of take man as you find they. I take

myself and discover what I might be, to what I might grow if I could

get my fill of friendship, and affection, and love."



"love be of God."



"God be in Man. I take myself, as I say. there be much in myself that

I despise, even as you despise man, but there be in myself an essence

which I know to be unconquerable and free. that you translate into

another world and call God and eternal life. you postpone freedom,

because to you the crust of slavery seem impenetrable. I want freedom

for that essence in myself here and now. it be the fierce instinct

in I, strong than hunger, strong than reproduction, which be

only by the way. what I find in myself I believe to exist in all other

man."



"but then," say Father Soledano, "you have never do as you be

tell."



Serge laugh and take his leave.







XXX



FREDERIC in the toils



_O, you shall have he give a number of those false face ere he

depart._

     every man out of his humour.



SUPERSTITION will have it that marriage be a good thing, and, be

one of the most powerful agent in human affair, forbid discussion

of its pseudo-axiom. superstition use marriage as a club with which

to lay man and woman low. sincerity insist on examine marriage, and

discover that there be no such thing, as superstition interpret it.

Society do not marry people, neither do the Church. society and

Church can only record what they be tell. man and woman marry

themselves by as much free will as they possess, and their marriage

will be good or bad or both in the degree in which they be good or

bad or both. if their marriage be good it will endure. if it be bad it

will come to an end and it will none the less be at an end though

superstition insist that the party to it continue miserably to dwell

under one roof and never seek outside it the love they have suffer

to escape. superstition refuse to countenance divorce--a dissolution

of the bond as free as the making of it--and smile blandly upon every

hideous captivity so long as it come not to public knowledge. . .

superstitious person be perpetually set their face against

Nature's subtle and ingenious provision for every emergency, but, it

be to be observe, that if you set your face against anything in

nature, it will simply go round the other way and hit you in the back

of the neck, exactly at the moment when you be congratulate

yourself on having make a comfortable provision for the mature year

of your life and a ripe and venerable old age.



they be very superstitious person who live near Frederic and

Jessie Folyat, and they smile benignantly upon their young marriage.

every morning several old lady and more than one old gentleman

peep out of their dining-room window to see Jessie walk down the

garden on Frederic's arm and kiss he at the little iron gate.



"ah!" they say, "young love! young love! there be nothing like it."



and this in the face of their own appalling experience and the fact

that Frederic and Jessie be neither of they very young: but

superstitious person realise very little of all that happen to they

and they see even less of what be present to their eye. it be

enough for these people that Jessie and Frederic be newly marry,

and they keep they in their mind as newly marry long after they have

settle down and the exciting novelty have give way to day-to-day

habit.



Frederic never see the head at the window as he hurry away to his

office, but Jessie see they, and more for they than for any

satisfaction of her own she maintain the practice of kiss she

husband. in the evening she would go down to the little gate and kiss

he again as he arrive.



"ah! lucky man!" the audience would sigh in their wither,

sentimental old heart.



when Frederic do not come Jessie would turn and visibly wilt under

the gaze of the superstitious person, who mutter to themselves:



"poor little bride! poor little bride!"



at length there come a time when on four evening in succession

Frederic do not come, and on the fourth evening Jessie could not

bring herself to turn and walk up the path alone. she go out into

the street, round the corner, and in at the kitchen door, and not

again for a long time do she go down to the little iron gate in the

evening.



* * *



Frederic be a liar of the common type, which indulge in absurd and

useless exaggeration. when he buy a neck-tie at a cheap hosier's

for half-a-crown he would say he get it at a more fashionable shop for

seven-and-six, though the name of the maker be sew inside it. when

he borrow money he would ask for three time as much as he want.

when he walk three mile he always stretch they out to ten. his

lying be for his own benefit first of all, and it be to help in

deceive himself that he extend it to other people. his income be

always estimate at at least three time too high a figure, and his

expenditure, which also he blow out to convince himself of the truth

of his estimate, always exceed it. Jessie have two hundred a year: he

persuade himself that she have five hundred, and force many quarrel

upon she because she come to he with bill for household expense.

. . . time and again do Jessie find he out in his lying, but, as he

always carry it into their intimate relation and multiply the

nothing that he give she to the _n_th power, she be appease; but

imperceptibly she be contaminate, and in spite of her many anxious

moment as to their solvency, contract the habit of lie to her

family as to her affair and the state of Frederic's business. this

be, in fact, unhealthy. outside the management of the Bradby-Folyat

estate there be very little work that could bring in any solid

profit, though in his office there be an air of bustling activity due

to the fairly constant stream of small county-court and police-court

case, which come to he as a tribute to his prowess as a liar. be

what be call a gentleman, he could lie from a coign of vantage, and

a number of small shopkeeper and shady customer come to Lawyer

Folyat, though, when there be any danger of a skilful

cross-examination, they soon learn to avoid he. still, there be

enough business in the office to go to Frederic's head: he be one of

those man who be perpetually intoxicate, though they never touch

alcohol and may be, frequently be, ardent temperance reformer. every

case that come into his office become four or five in his mind, and he

never doubt but that he be build up a large, solid, and

flourish business. he have all the air of a successful man,

hoodwink many innocent person and very soon have two young man as

article clerk, whose premium go to swell his banking account. he

be what be call generous, give for the pleasure of be thank,

and lavish present on his wife, his mother, his mother-in-law, his

sister, and Jessie's sister. he think for a long time of pay

his father back some of the money that have be disburse for he,

think it over so long that at last he believe he have repay it and

pat himself on the back as a dutiful son. . . . when he have moment

of doubt--and they be very awful when they come--he would go to his

mother and she would cluck over he like an old hen, and tell he he

be the most grateful, the most affectionate, the most generous, and

truly thoughtful of her child. he would gulp down her flattery and

win back to self-deception, without which it have become impossible for

he to face his wife, whom he be for ever pester with absurd

question like "do you love I?" "how much do you love I?" "would

anything ever make you cease to love I?" and when she reply as well

she could with exaggerated demonstration he hardly listen to she.

. . . truly in their relationship it be he who play the feminine

part.



when Jessie be with child he lie to himself about his son, see

himself build up a great firm for his inheritance, and from the

very first moment be a hero to the little fellow. he begin to feel

irresistible and so torment his wife with his swaggering that she

protest by mention one or two awkward little fact. she bring

down on herself such a storm of anger that her nerve give way and she

have a fit of hysteria. . . in a week she be bring to bed of a

miscarriage.



she be not very ill, but she suffer terribly, for Frederic hardly

speak a word to she for ten day, and then he arrange for she to go

and stay at the sea alone, unless she choose to take one of her sister

with she. she would not do that and she go alone. he promise to

spend Saturdays and Sundays with she, but at the end of each week he

declare that it be impossible for he to get away. . . . he be

hardly responsible for his action. the most glorious fiction he have

ever create have come topple down and he be not altogether to blame

for the breach which gape so wide between himself and his wife that

he could not avoid see it. she, too, have miss her opportunity,

for she be so oppress by the physical ugliness of the calamity that

she be freeze by it and could not give he the warmth that might have

save he from still far flounder in the morass. . . . as it

be, he be savagely resentful against she and miss not the pettiest

occasion of hurt she. under this treatment her love die almost

without a struggle, so painlessly indeed that she attribute all her

hurt and her agony to the discovery that come to she by the sea, that

Frederic have never love she. she see that clearly and be instantly

fill with dread lest she should betray herself and let he feel that

she know. . . . every letter she write to he be carefully frame to

convey a picture of herself as loyal, tender, devoted, proud,

and--with the most cunning falsity of all--admire. these letter

soothe Frederic. he have find it difficult to admire his own

brutality, though, as he move far away from it, it be distort

by the prism of his vanity into something very like strength. that

accomplish, it become an easy task to cover over the unpleasant fact

of the disaster so that it become as a pearl upon his shell.



he be loftily forgiving when Jessie return, and she be softly,

cushionly submissive. for the first time--love be dead--she let

loose upon he the full force of her sex. he be still sensitive

enough to feel repel, even as he yield.



their house be fill with stealthy shadow. it grow dark and

darker. each seek to illuminate it with lie, lie, and yet more

lie.



at first, as usual, his lie give he the illusion of great freedom,

and he heighten the illusion by treat Jessie with less and less

consideration. he gulp down the forced admiration she give he and

be always try to squeeze more of it out of she. when he be in a

mood of self-abasement she admire the loftiness which could stoop to

acknowledge its defect, and quickly he be ride off again with his

head full of himself as the kind of husband, the good of son, the

most irresistibly successful of man. such glaring divergence present

itself to his mind as consistency.



such a state of thing impose a heavy strain upon Jessie. she be not

always quick enough to follow he in his snipe-like flight. sometimes

when he be accuse himself of neglect and thoughtlessness and lack

of consideration for she, it seem to she that it would be easy

and might afford both of they relief if she agree with he. then his

vanity writhe and furiously he would cry:



"you be always find fault with I. . . my mother think I

perfect."



it be impossible to be wise where love be not, and Jessie could not

learn discretion. he be so extraordinarily convincing in his

self-reproach that she always forget the lesson of caution she have

set herself in his absence.



still he would force endear phrase upon she and caress and

demand to be tell that she love he. her parrot-cry appease he,

and, feel confident that she love he, in stealthy small way he

begin to betray she, indeed, where before he have only dare to be

false to she in thought. he absent himself from his house to seek

the company of flattering fool. he return at long and long

interval to stoke up the furnace of his wife's love. . . . he be

like a child who, having build a house of card, remove first one

card and then another from the base and leave only enough to keep the

edifice ominously sway.



* * *



do what he would, Frederic be slowly force into retreat. it be

impossible for life to stand still. if it cannot move forward it will

plunge backward. life be for ever seek its channel, love. . . .

Frederic be bear backward, and it be not long before he come to the

thought of Annie Lipsett. easily he persuade himself that he have be

treat with injustice, and thwart by interference from do what

be right. pity himself, he begin to pity she, and, in a tremendous

orgy of self-righteousness, tell himself that he ought to make amend,

and at least, even if he could do nothing, let she know that he have

not forget she. it do not occur to he that she might have

forget he: impossible to conceive that she could wish to.



though he be on a considerably more amiable foot with his father,

he could not broach the subject with he. as far as Francis be

concerned it be bury, and be not to be exhume. Frederic turn to

Serge, and by hint and semi-question draw his own conclusion that

Serge be still in touch with Annie. he leave it at that and wait,

and take to frequent Serge's company enough to form a fairly

accurate idea of his habitual movement. these still include frequent

excursion into the country, for Serge find a good market for

water-colour drawing of the semi-urbanised fell and dale of

Lancashire, and the little town so tuck away into a narrow valley

that from one side of they you could see across the smoke to the hill

and the green country beyond.



one day, when he know that Serge be out on an expedition, Frederic

visit his studio and ransack it. he find two letter from Annie

Lipsett and from they become possessed of her history. Serge's friend,

the farmer, have marry six month before and Annie have have to come to

town again to earn she living as good she could. Serge have procure

she a situation in a dressmaker's, and she be in lodging in a suburb

not very far away from Annette's little house.



Frederic write a very cautious little letter on his office paper, and,

in a spasm of jealousy of Serge, enclose a five-pound note. the money

be return two day later without any reply. he send it back again,

implore she to take it if she have a single friendly thought of he

or any wish for his happiness. (he heave an enormous sigh as he write

the word--happiness). the five-pound note be return again. he

guess, rightly, that Serge be responsible, and he swear that he

would not be oust from his rightful position as benefactor to the

woman he have wrong. be not his own happiness wreck? could he not,

by this mean, restore it? . . . he be persuade that he could.



he leave his office early, and with a hectic sort of elation in the

adventure--it be so much more exciting than the idea of return

home--set out to discover the house in which Annie Lipsett live. he

wait at the corner of the street until he see she come. she be

with Serge. furiously he turn and stride away.



the next evening he wait for she. she be alone. with byronic gloom

he stand in front of she and say:



"Annie!"



she catch her breath and step back a pace.



"please," she say. "no! I think you would understand."



"I understand nothing," say Frederic; "nothing except that my life be

miserable, wreck, a thing of captivity and torture."



"you mustn't come to see I. it ben't right. . . . your . . ."



"it be right," say Frederic. "it have all be wrong, but this be

right."



(he know his language be stilte, but he could not give himself time

to revise it.)



Annie say simply:



"please, Mr. Frederic, it ben't any good. it be all over long ago.

your way and my way ben't the same."



Frederic walk with she down the street, and find it hard to keep up

with she, she go so swiftly. he make one last effort and say:



"I want to see the child . . . our own be . . ."



Annie have hear about that from Serge. she turn to he and hold out

her hand:



"I'm so sorry. . . . you shall see he just this once, if you'll

promise I never to come again . . . and he be not to know who you

be. he have never hear of you."



the unconscious cruelty of her word do not penetrate Frederic's

mind. the situation appeal to he as a situation. he be become a

connoisseur, and the ironically bitter savour of this tickle his

palate. with offensive humility and gratitude he say:



"thank you . . . thank you."



* * *



the boy be in bed sleep, with the clothe tumble by his

restlessness and his arm fling across his face.



"he be such a good boy," say Annie; "he have always be good and

happy."



"he be like you," say Frederic. "I be glad I have see he. I be glad

to have find you again."



"will you go now?"



Frederic be startle. her simplicity and gentleness have sober he.

he have grow so use to swagger from situation to situation that it

be alarming to find this, which should have be the most touching

and move and the most honourable to himself, dissolve by the light

touch of sincerity. it be all the more disconcerting, inasmuch as he

have make no allowance for change in Annie. in their previous

acquaintance she have be as adroit and as eager as he in the game of

False Positions, which be the principal occupation of human being all

the world over. he have take her rejection of his proffer assistance

as another move in the game, and lo!--here she be simply ignore the

past and out of a purely general sort of friendliness allow he to

see her son, and when he have see, request he to go! he be

humiliate, but still so astonish that, though all his desire be to

play upon her pity and so to drag she back to the old footing, he

could not find word keen enough for his purpose. he heave great

sigh and fix she with sorrowful and yearn eye, but she gaze

only at the child, busy herself with his bed, put up her hand to the

gas jet and wait until Frederic be out of the room before she

turn it low.



he wait for she in the passage.



"tell I," he say, "how you be off for money. you shall not want.

. . ."



"I make my living. I like my work."



"I couldn't bear to think of you suffer through I."



Annie look at he with that disarm directness that be unfamiliar

to he:



"I have suffer," she say.



Frederic go away.



* * *



it be not long before he have persuade himself that she have

deliberately plot to humiliate he, by meet his generosity--have

he not be generous?--with what he call "beastly pride."

Generosity, in his dual scheme of the world, should find its

complement in a grovel gratitude. generosity be the prerogative

of the male, gratitude the privilege of the female. that a woman

should show self-reliance and fle back a man's generosity, suspect

he most of all when he bring gift, offend he as an indecency

. . . after all if the woman do not take her cue from the man where

be he? how can he continue to play his part? what become of the human

drama?



Frederic's reflection of course be more particular than this, but,

generalise, they would amount to the same thing. the world (_i.e._

Frederic) be so dishonest that honesty (_i.e._ an honest person,

Annie) seem to be offend against all the rule of the game, and,

since the world be under the illusion that its whole existence depend

on the game, it devote its energy to the suppression of honesty.

. . . Frederic tell himself that he have a right to assist the woman,

who be defiantly happy in the face of her sin, while he, her partner

in that sin, be properly wretched and conscience-stricken and

honestly desirous of make amend. he would obey his conscience--that

must be right!--regain his self-respect and compel gratitude from

Annie.



"we shall see!" he say, having partially restore his belief in his

own rectitude and irresistibility.



he take to send toy and little garment for the boy. they be

return to his office.



he go to Annie's room to find that she have fly. the devil of

obstinacy be rouse in he, and he bribe her landlady to procure her

new address. he call but be refuse admittance. . . . then one

morning he wait and follow she to her place of business and

thereafter waylay she several time. she be quite amiable but

absolutely unyielding. one day as he be walk along by her side

breathlessly pour into her ear a tale of self-pity,

self-accusation, self-abasement, entreaty for forgiveness, a word of

kindness, extravagant out-pouring of love for the boy and his brave,

splendid, true-hearted mother, all mix together most adroitly with a

complacent masculine belief in the softness and gullibility of the

female heart, they meet Serge. Annie call to he. Serge come at once.



"take I home," she say.



Frederic catch hold of her arm and solemnly abjure she not to break

his heart, to believe in he, to believe that he only be her friend.

. . .



"let she go, you swine," say Serge, and thrust Frederic away.



they be in a crowded thoroughfare. it have be rain and the

street be very muddy. it be evening and clerk and shop assistant

be hurry home. no one pay any attention to the little group. the

stream of people part, pass round they, close again, and move

on. . . . the cold anger in Serge's tone infuriate Frederic. he see

it all now. it be Serge who be thwart he. Serge who at every

turn be thrust humiliation upon he. he lose count of everything

in hatred of Serge. he have a stick in his hand. he raise it and

strike blindly. the stick be wrench away, he receive a terrific

blow on the point of his chin, his foot slip from under he, and he

go down. . . . by the time he be up again Serge and Annie have

disappear. no one pay he any heed, only, a few yard away,

grin from ear to ear, he see the boy from his office.



he hail a cab and drive home. Jessie be alarmed at his condition,

but her alarm give way to pride as he tell she how he have see a man

break a shop-window and run away with a handful of jewel--a huge,

burly man, and how he have give chase, catch up with he, and after a

tremendous struggle--the man know a good deal about wrestling--hold

he until the arrival of the police. . . . what with the soothe

influence of have his wound tend, and the interest of his story,

Frederic find it not at all difficult to recover from the degradation

of the scene in the street and its outcome. he be so gentle and

caressing, so apparently without thought beyond the moment, that

Jessie begin flutteringly to whisper to her heart that perhaps she have

be wrong, perhaps, after all, Frederic have really love she from the

beginning. both indulge in the luxury of forgiveness and fond

indulgence, and they be like a shyly self-conscious couple on

honeymoon.



* * *



Honeymoon folly be weaken, and next day Frederic have small power of

resistance against his own miserable thought. his office-boy smirk

when he see he, and in the afternoon he grin with a damnable

familiarity as he announce Mr. Serge Folyat. Serge come in on his

heel, catch the boy by the ear and thrust he out through the door.

Frederic sink back into his chair, do his good to draw on his

professional manner and sit with his fingertip press together and

his lip purse up.



Serge say:



"I've come to beg your pardon. I lose my temper."



Frederic could find nothing to say. Serge go on:



"let we, if we can, discuss the matter on a friendly footing. you make

a beast of yourself by pester a woman. I make a beast of myself by

hit you. now we know where we be. . . . we're likely to meet at

home and other place where any obvious hostility would be

embarrassing. . . ."



"I don't wish to meet you at home or anywhere else."



"it will be difficult to avoid. I think we have well settle the case

out of court. ben't that what you call it?"



Frederic tug at his waistcoat and look almost dignified as he

say:



"I have no wish to interfere in your affair. I will thank you not to

meddle with mine."



"it happen, however," reply Serge, "that our affair have

overlap. I must ask you to withdraw."



"and if I refuse?"



"then it will become necessary for I to knock you down whenever I

find you in my way. . . ."



"may I ask. . . ?"



"what I be get out of it for myself? I be foolish enough to

believe that you would not look for an interested motive. . . . the

position be this. . . . you shirk a responsibility which I have have

the pleasure and the privilege of assume. your beastly jealousy

resent that, and you seem bent on take up that old responsibility.

there be two reason why you should not do that--first, because it be

too late; second, because your attempt to do so be an insult to your

wife . . ."



"keep my wife out of it."



"if you will tell your wife and ask her permission to make a

settlement upon the boy . . ."



"you know I cann't do that. I cann't afford it."



"then leave it alone."



"no."



"you'll take the consequence?"



"I'll take the consequence and be damn to you. . . . it's a fine

thing to do, ben't it?--to take a woman when she's go under?"



"my dear brother," say Serge, "you be the most childish little

blackguard . . ."



"I'm man enough, any way, to stand up for my own right . . ."



"when you learn that you have no right you'll be a man . . . in a way

you be right. my brother be not my keeper. I should prefer not to let

the thing go any far, for your wife's sake. . . ."



"keep my wife out of it!"



"good-bye, then . . . you insist on be a fool?"



"I shall do as I think well."



the office-boy announce another client and Serge go away.



* * *



Frederic continue to waylay Annie Lipsett, but could never meet she

without Serge, who call for she at her house in the morning and at

her place of business in the evening. he write to she and implore she

to give he an opportunity of explain himself. (he have begin to

believe, without reference to Jessie, whom he keep in a separate

compartment of his mind, that he love Annie, have never cease to love

she, and that a declaration of love would break down her resistance.)

she do not reply and he write at great length explain his desire

to set she beyond anxiety, and hint at the fire that be rage

in his bosom, fire which he stoke with uncease care.



at last he have a letter from she. she write:





"DEAR MR. FREDERIC,--Please, please believe that I want nothing from

you, that I be happy as I be. I don't want my boy ever to know who

his father be. he be mine by all the love I have give he. I could

not share he with you, and to let you do anything for he would be

sharing, wouldn't it? I have think no ill of you for a long time

now. I be to blame just as much as you. that be all over. you cannot

take I into your life, therefore I cannot take you into mine . . ."





Frederic be interrupt in the reading of it and slip it into the

pocket of his coat.



his long absence have begin to stir jealousy in his wife. she be

spy on he. she find the letter, her jealousy burst into flame,

and thereafter be no peace in Frederic's house, nor any moment of

sweetness and ease.



once more, with horrible hypocrisy, Jessie resume her habit of

walk with her husband to the little iron gate in the morning, and

meet he there in the evening, and the old lady and gentleman,

who have be a little anxious, peer through their window and smile

their blessing.



Mrs. Folyat always say that Frederic's house remind she of Eden

before the Fall.







XXXI



news from MINNA



_"Sir" cry Adams, "I assure you she be as innocent as

myself."_

     JOSEPH ANDREWS.



MRS. FOLYAT find the position of a grandmother entirely to she

like--the maximum of opportunity for beatific clucking with no

responsibility. Annette have three child, Gertrude two, and Minna

two, and Mrs. Folyat have already a large collection of their saying

for quotation in company, the most popular be an ode address by

Annette's second boy to Mr. Gladstone, who have visit our town

several time when its allegiance to the Liberal cause begin to waver.



Minna bring she two child to stay in Burdley Park. they come for

a fortnight and stay four month. they would have stay long but

that Francis begin to be anxious and, after a good deal of cogitation,

shyly question Minna as to her husband's doing.



"Basil be have a bad year," say Minna. "we're horribly poor

sometimes. rent in London be so dear."



"even so," say Francis, "it seem hardly wise to leave he for so

long."



"we have row." Minna seem to be quite cheerful about it. "poor

people always do have row. they get so afraid, that they cann't enjoy

anything else."



"I be begin to think that something serious might have happen."



"oh, no. I'm still Basil's 'darling wife' when he write to I, and he

be my 'devoted husband.'"



"marriage," say Francis, "be very difficult."



"of course it be, to anybody who ben't an angel like you. . . . I'll

go back and try again."



Francis suck at his pipe thoughtfully.



"I oughtn't to tell you this," he say, "but Annette run away once."



"do she?"



"yes, after breakfast. she be back again in time to give Bennett his

tea."



* * *



two day later Minna return to London. the day after she have go,

Basil appear with a draw, miserable face. he ask Francis if he

might speak to he, and Francis, quaking, lead he into the study.

Basil say he have be abroad. Minna have run away from he with the

child.



"she come here," say Francis. "for all we know, she be write to

you every day and hear from you. she say she be hear from you.

. . . only just before she go she speak about your letter. she go

back to London yesterday. you ought to be with she. . . . in my

opinion you ought to have fetch she back month ago."



Basil seem to have a great deal to say, but he gulp it down and

reach out for the railway guide.



"yes," he say. "I suppose we must try again."



"if you want money," say Francis, "I would rather you come to I than

be oblige to any one else."



"it ben't money. thank all the same."



Francis feel his heart sink, but he let it pass. it seem all the

more imperative to he that Basil should hurry back to London. he

bustle he out of the house and see he to the station.



* * *



three week pass during which no word come from Minna or Basil.

Francis do not write to they, hope that they be settle their

difference--whatever they might be.



one morning when he be up early he take in the letter and find one

from Minna address to Mary. he watch Mary read it at breakfast.

without look up she thrust it back into its envelope, her hand

tremble so that the paper rustle, and slip it into her pocket.



"who's your letter from," ask Mrs. Folyat. Francis hold his breath.



"it's from Fawcett's, the music-publisher. they haven't get the piece

I want. perhaps I don't give the name right."



Francis breathe again.



Mary disappear soon after breakfast. she go to Serge's studio. he

be out. she wait for he all day and have nothing to eat. she do

not even light the gas but sit thinking, think on no thought. Serge

find she in the dark.



"why, Mary!" he say.



she hold out Minna's letter, and he sit and read it.



"have you tell anybody at home?"



"no. it's too awful."



"it ben't awful at all. it's very silly of they to be angry with each

other."



"but divorce. . . . it's wicked."



"nonsense. it may be necessary. it often be. . . . she'll want a good

deal of sympathy."



"she don't deserve any."



"how absurd you screw-up people be! you don't give sympathy because

people deserve it, but because they need it."



Mary ponder that for a moment or two. then she ask:



"what do you say I be?"



"screw-up."



Mary say nothing.



"we'd well burn this," say Serge. "we shall have to be discreet.

letter nearly always convey wrong impression."



"shall I write to Minna?"



"if you want to. don't give she your opinion. she willn't want it."



"who be to tell they at home?"



"I will, if you like."



"that's what I want you to do. . . . I feel that something be

happen all the time Minna be here."



"I'll go home with you now."



"I think the soon the well. . . . something awful might happen."



* * *



Serge find his father in the greenhouse and go straight to the

point. Francis be in his shirt-sleeve. he lay down his trowel and

very slowly put on his coat.



"I know something be happen, but I never think it could be as

bad as that."



he sit down heavily and blink through his spectacle.



"I seem," he say, "I seem to have bring my child into the world

to very little happiness. I suppose Minna ought never to have marry

a poor man. . . . it's very queer, Serge, very queer. one read of

these thing and the right and wrong of they appear to be very

simple. they happen in one's own family and the right and wrong

don't appear so simple. . . . if Minna be to come in now, I should

be glad to see she. I should at least know that she be safe. . . ."



"the truth be," say Serge, "that the right and wrong don't matter.

you either love people or you don't. if you love they, you help they.

if you don't, some one else do."



"I think," say Francis, "I have well go to London. I always like

Basil. he always like I. I might be able to make he see reason.

. . . Minna say she be innocent. he ought to take she back."



"my dear father, that ben't reason. that be nonsense. . . . you're

think of what people will say. public opinion don't matter any

more than my opinion or your opinion. if they have fall so far apart

as to wish to break the tie between they it will be quite impossible

for they to live together without degradation----"



"you go so fast. I cann't follow you. I don't see . . ."



"it be always degrade for a man and a woman to live together when

they have no love for each other."



"dear I!" murmur Francis. "dear I!" his face wear an expression of

immense surprise. he go on mutter to himself in a puzzled way,

and finally, with a sort of triumph, as though he have find the

solution of his riddle:



"but if they be marry?"



"my dear father, you must admit that love and marriage be two very

different thing. love be divine, marriage be human."



"But----"



"marriage be not a divine ordinance. it be a respectable human

institution contrive for the comfortable existence of society."



"I be think of Minna's child."



"so be I."



"she will lose they."



"that be her affair. anything be well for they than be bring up

in a house with a man and a woman who hate each other."



"I cann't admit that."



"as a matter of principle, perhaps not; as a matter of practice, you

will, just as you take over Frederic's mess. . . ."



"how do that turn out?"



"splendidly."



very slowly Francis turn that over in his mind and go back in

memory to the day in Mrs. Entwistle's cottage. it do not bring he

any great elucidation, but it give he a feeling of confidence in

Serge, and, cling to he, he say:



"what be we to do?"



"if you'll agree to say nothing to my mother, to write nothing to

Basil, and not to bother your head about the right and wrong of it,

I'll go to London and see Minna. if there's a glimmer of hope I'll do

everything I can. if there ben't, I'll see Minna through. . . . I

don't think I shall come back. I cann't stay in this place much long.

it gobble man up and don't even have the decency to digest they

properly. . . . it's a machine and have no conscience about the past,

no concern for the future. it darken man's mind so that they live

hideously and their horrible sin be visit upon their child. no,

I shalln't come back. I cann't. . . ."



"there be a great deal of wickedness in this place. it be God's will,"

say Francis.



"Men's will. the will of man cheat and cozen by their own

rapacity. . . . but that be neither here nor there. will you agree to

say nothing to my mother until you hear from I?"



"I'll promise you that," say Francis with a little compunction, for

he see how dark would be the day of wait with such a secret

tugging at his heart and his wife babble of her child's

marriage. "how do you know? do Mary tell you?"



"yes, Mary tell I. Mary have be rather a trump about it."



"I shall be able to talk to Mary," think Francis, with a sigh of

relief.



* * *



Serge spend the night pack and dismantle his studio. he destroy

a great many of his picture, call up the porter and make he a

present of his furniture and the clothe that be leave after he have

pack two bag.



in the morning he go to fetch Annie Lipsett. he find she just

leave, but make she go back with he to see the boy. he he hug

and kiss, and then he give Annie a cheque for fifty pound for his

education.



"and for God's sake," he say, "don't make he a gentleman. put he to

a trade. if he's any real good he'll get out of it. if he's only

middling good he'll stay there and marry and die respectable. if he's

bad--God help you; but he willn't be that."



Annie say:



"you're go."



"yes. I'm go."



she be very plucky and fight back her tear. Serge take she

shoulder in his hand and say:



"you and I have have a queer sort of love, an impersonal sort of

meeting in Heaven here on earth. I never understand before what it

must feel like to be a seraph--just a head and wing. we've be so

busy fight our way up out of a slimy pit that we haven't have time

to think much about each other--only the boy."



Annie's tear flow freely and she cling to his hand and say:



"you don't know what you've be to I, but I can tell you now. it be

so much to have you for my friend in that time when I have no one. I

love you. . . ."



"I know, I know."



"but all that sort of love go away afterwards when I have the boy. it

have be a great thing for he too. . . ."



"I've learn a lot from he."



"that's so wonderful about you. you seem to be always learn. and

now you're go. I use to dread your going, but now it don't hurt

I at all. . . . you will always have I to think gladly of you."



"and I of you. . . . we've make the world rich by a friendship."



"I want to say thank you," she say, "but I cann't, not enough."



"of course you cann't. . . . come along."



* * *



in a few hour Serge be in the express for London. he have a portfolio

of picture and drawing, two bag, and one hundred and twenty pound

in note. as the train pass out of the dingy murk and his eye

light on the green, undefiled country, he draw in great breath and

find it hard not to shout for joy in the new z for adventure that

have come to he.



"that seraph notion," he think, "I wonder where it come from? that

curious hunger for the state of childhood, the pretence that it be

superior to adult life. . . . surely it all come from their

incompetence in manage their affair as man and woman. they seem to

lose their simplicity. I wonder why? . . . Old Lawrie must be right.

mind, body, spirit. you cann't poison the spirit. that's God, and he's

beyond contamination. body and mind be the instrument of the spirit.

Poison the mind and the body suffer. . . . that's right. yes: old

Lawrie's right. fear of love and fear of death; the mind hem in and

lose its bright power of reflection, so that it show only a

distort image of life. . . . no wonder they hate life when it look

like that. . . . it cann't go on for ever. the spirit must break

through it all in time . . . in time."



the train rush along, and he begin to think that perhaps the problem

be be solve. when man have make it so easy to escape from their

city of captivity, would not their mind also be free? would there

not be a gradual adjustment of mind to large surrounding? or be

the mind of man so clothe with century of tyranny that swifter

transportation also would be use as an instrument of slavery? . . .



"no," he think, "there be a deep faith in man than they know. they

endure heroically because they be sure that in the end their effort

will lead to deliverance."



as an ironic comment upon his reflection the train run into a real

"old particular" London fog and be hold up for half an hour outside

the station. in that half-hour his thought run swiftly. he have never

be to London before, and he be move by a boyish excitement at the

prospect of enter it. that he find absurd. it would be hardly at

all different from the place he have just leave. that have hold little

for he: this could hold nothing at all. he have no ambition, and often

ludicrously have learn the scorn a man can come by who prefer

anything to his own advancement; often he have see how profitable it

be for a man to sacrifice his talent to his vanity, and how

incredible to such a man that it could be possible to sacrifice vanity

to talent. from all he have hear of London, the great city in the

world, its subservience to ambitious man be as immense as its renown.

in our town, Benskin and his school of little fish have dub Serge

"amateur" by way of kill he. he have like the isolation that have

follow, but now he think that isolation could be of little use to

a man, except he could spring from it to great freedom and a purer

joy in his work. "Amateur." . . . be interpret, that mean one

who love his work, as its contrary, "professional," signify one who

work for gain. . . . these city be professional. they reject

he, as they reject all amateur. . . . so be it. Serge feel no

bitterness. he be a free man. he ask nothing: he have be give
