much, first of all the power to enjoy. . . . he chuckle to think that

the only usefulness the suspicious world of professional man would

allow he lay--apparently--in succour female in distress.

Knight-errantry, once the lofty of profession, be descend into

the hand of the contemn amateur.



"at bottom," say Serge, "the difference between they and I be that I

take woman seriously and they don't."



* * *



his stay in London be short even than he have think it would be.

he visit Basil first, and find he work desperately, painting,

charcoal drawing, black-and-white, Christmas card, book

illustration, design for menu, chocolate box--all slipshod,

formal, but just neatly and obviously charming. through his tooth he

ask Serge what the hell he have come for and go on work. Serge

turn over a pile of drawing on the table by the window.



"Benskin would dote on you now. . . . how you must hate art to be able

to do they so well!"



Basil grunt. "I hate everything."



"you always be extreme."



Basil lay down his pen.



"do she send you?"



"no. she don't know I'm in London. I come to you first because I

think your point of view might be helpful when I come to tackle she.

I've get nothing to go upon except her letter to Mary, which ben't

particularly illuminate."



"it wouldn't be. it's just funny to she--just funny, do you hear? I've

implore she, on my knee I've beg she just to help I to

understand she, to give I some clue as to what it be that she really

want, to keep we from go to smash, and she just sit and listen

to I with that slow grin of her. . . . I frighten she, I think,

the last time, and the grin fade from her face, but she become as

hard as a stone. . . . she don't care. she don't care. and I think

she want to break I. . . . she haven't do it. do you hear? she

haven't do it!"



"do you weep?"



"I . . . I break down."



"ah! not a good way of convince she of your capacity to give she

what she want."



Basil stride angrily about the studio, wave his arm and shouting.



"it's not a bit of good. it's do now. . . . it's all over. it's

finish."



"it willn't be finish until you've done think about it. there

don't seem to be much prospect of that."



"I'm not go to discuss it with you."



"I don't want to. what be the fact? you've accuse she of

infidelity. who's the man?"



"Fry. . . . his wife's divorce he. that's evidence enough, ben't

it?"



"I'm not concerned with the evidence. I only want to know whether it's

necessary that there should be a divorce."



"she's leave I."



"I might persuade she to return."



"could you?"



"I might. . . ."



"I'll forgive she. . . . if she will come to I as a contrite woman.

. . ."



"that's slush. if you be go to spend your life in quarrel as

to which be really the magnanimous party, I shalln't stir a finger.

. . . do you want she?"



"if she . . ."



"if you want she, there can be no condition. . . ."



"but she . . ."



Serge see that it be hopeless. Basil be cling to his grievance,

nurse they, cherish they. they have become more precious to he

than his own happiness, than his wife, than the well-being of his

child. . . . still there be hope that on Minna's side there might

be magnanimity and generosity enough to uproot the thick-set hedge

with which Basil have surround himself.



* * *



Minna be in room in the Marylebone Road, near Madame Tussaud's. she

have a woman friend with she, a queer inanimate creature who look as

though she have step out of the waxwork--a model of Nell Gwynne.

Minna seem quite happy. she be lie on a sofa eat turkish

delight and read "Jane Eyre." she drop her book as Serge enter

and her friend glide away.



"I _be_ glad to see you," she say. "it's so dull. ben't it a beastly

business?"



"I've just be to see Basil."



"be he still weep?"



Serge ignore that question and ask she another.



"what's the trouble between you two?"



"Basil say I'm----"



"I know that, but that's only the outcome of the trouble."



Minna be interested. she sit up on the sofa with her hand between

her knee.



"how clever you be, Serge! no one else have ever think of that.

everybody else be quarrel as to whether I do or do not."



"do you?"



"no. that come long after the mischief's do. the trouble between

Basil and I be simply this. Basil want I to be a mother to he and

I cann't. People be simply sicken about mother. I'm a woman first

and a mother afterwards. be a mother grow out of be a woman.

. . . Basil want I to be a work of art in theory and a mother in

practice. I simply couldn't do it. . . . it's my own fault. I know

Basil be like that before I marry he. I have a sort of blind moment

when I think I could change he. you cann't change people. I cann't

change myself. . . . I ought to have leave he long ago, but Basil's

the sort of man you cann't leave. he cling. he play on your nerve

and make you frightened. he look at you with his big eye and seem

so helpless that you're afraid to leave he, and you don't like

hurt he. he simply _make_ you be a mother to he and then take

advantage of it, and thing go from bad to bad. . . . London seem

to frighten he, take away all his courage and his ambition. London's

too big for he. he want to be at the top of the tree all at once,

simply because he's afraid of the climb. . . . we should have do

well to stay at home."



"that wouldn't have make any difference."



"no, I suppose not. I be I and Basil be Basil and that's the whole

story, and it's just like a man of that sort to turn round and try to

kill you when you willn't let he cling to you any long."



Minna's voice become venomous.



"grievance again!" think Serge, and he see then how impossible be

his position. he could not tell Minna of Basil's willingness to take

she back upon condition. either of they or both must surrender their

grievance if anything be to be do. that seem to be extremely

improbable.



"you will not go back, then?"



"I'm quite willing to go back, if Basil----"



More condition! oh, the folly of insistence upon right! . . . Serge

drop the subject, accept the inevitable and ask:



"then it be to go on?"



"that rest with Basil."



"if he do not withdraw the petition I suppose you will not defend."



"I shall defend my honour if I have to spend my last penny on it. I'm

not go to have mud throw at I and say 'thank you' for it. I don't

trust Basil. he's a vindictive little beast. he's sure to say our

marriage be happy. . . . besides, I must think of the child."



"I wish you would."



"I do. their mother's honour be precious to they."



"personally," say Serge, "I would sell my honour for twopence."



"oh! you! . . . but then you don't care what anybody think of you."



"not a straw."



"then it ben't any good talk to you. you really be an immoral man.

. . . if Basil go for I, I shall go for he. you'd hold up the

other cheek, I know, but then you're not human. I tell my child

once to think before they strike, and Benny say, 'I do think, and

then I strike. . . .' I'm like that too. I'm not go to listen to

you. I'm not go back to Basil, I'm not go to lie down and let

he weep over my sin in public. he's a little beast and everybody

shall know that he's a little beast. . . ."



Minna have work herself up into a state of anger. she be hot and red

in the face with it, and look coarse and unpleasant.



Serge say to himself:



"no wonder knight-errantry be dead, since woman have take upon

themselves to be as stupidly selfish as man."



he make one last effort, and suggest that she should take the more

sensible course and leave it to Basil unoppose to set the cumbrous

machinery of the law in motion, if only for the sake of her father and

mother. to that Minna only reply with a brilliant but spiteful

caricature of Mrs. Folyat's state of mind as slowly she digest the

unpalatable truth that all marriage be not make in Heaven.



* * *



Serge write to Francis that night and tell he that there be no hope,

since both Minna and Basil be resolute to part. all that could be

look for be that they would injure each other as little as possible

in the process. so far as he could see, the pain of uprooting be

over. the pair be absolutely divorced. unhappily, they seem

determined to call down on each other the disapprobation of the world,

in their frenziedly childish desire to hurt each other. . . . Serge

beg Francis to make his mother take a reasonable, human view of it,

since Minna would need friendliness and assistance, and suggest that

he should come to an arrangement with Basil's family for the

maintenance of the child.



his letter end thus:



"good-bye, my dear father. I be your first disappointment, but in the

end you and I recognise each other. that be permanent. it will be

with I wherever I go, with you to the end of your life. you be of

those who believe that understanding be not give to we. your belief

must be a bitter comfort to you. I believe that man be rapidly come

to an end of their material activity so that soon they will be force

to find understanding or perish. . . . do you remember a night when

you and I watch the rest act an absurd play, and I say

involuntarily, 'round the corner'? modern life be theatrical.

everybody be play a part, because they be without understand.

life for modern man and woman be for ever round the corner because

they attempt to tackle their affair with the mind of child,

child who believe everything they be tell and examine nothing.

they play with everything. they can do nothing else. unhappily, life

be a serious business which yield its reward of joy only to

simplicity, sincerity, and purity, or, if you like the old trinity

well--faith, hope, and charity. the old belief be true--nearly all

that you preach, I mean; but from repetition they have become stale

and meaningless. they need restatement. . . . I be go back to the

sea, not because I believe that the 'great wide space of the

earth'--what a lot of twaddle be talk about them!--have a monopoly

of truth, but because I must move and keep move. it be in the air.

perhaps I feel it before other man. the salvation of human life lie

in movement, circulation. . . . more simply and less philosophically I

be go because it amuse I to go. I like pass through the world

salute the few man of courage and good heart whom one can find, and,

of such man, my dear father, I count you not the least."



Francis keep this letter and through his hour of torment often read

it. it let in air.







XXXII



the cutting of a KNOT



_fear, and the pit, and the snare, be upon thee, o inhabitant of the

earth._

     ISAIAH, xxiv. 17.



it be one of the most disconcerting phenomenon of existence that, when

passionate love have answer its purpose, it simply disappear,

leave its instrument wed by such truth as they have discover

in each other or divorce by the lie they have forge for each

other's delight. very rarely, however, be the issue so simple. the

bone-and-shadow business come into play here also, and most people

marry with very little passionate love and a great deal of careful

imitation of it, so that most marriage be strangle in their birth

with a very tangled web of lie.



it be so with Frederic and Jessie Folyat in their marriage, and they

be never so nearly unite as when jealousy come between they. their

marriage feast do coldly furnish forth the funeral of love, and over

love's dead body they quarrel. they have scene, hysterical

skirmish and almost as hysterical reconciliation. there be grim

sport in it all, a sort of fascination in the stealthy prying and

spying, each crouch and shrink in readiness for the other's

spring, the snarl bravado with which each dare the other to come

on, a little far, a little far, invite to a caress,

repel with a scratch blow; and all smooth-seem, veil,

polite, with polished air and graceful manner and feigned interest

and inquiry: a pooling of the common stock only to wrangle over the

division of it again. the gambling fever be in it. at any moment all

might be lose upon a throw, a little gain, a little loss, more gain,

more gain, a little more and the other might be beggar, the game

win. but neither dare let the game come to an end. when one be near

ruin, grey-face, anxiously glare for the turn of the card, the

other cheat and the game go on. it absorb both. neither could do

without it. it be a drug. their craving for it be agony; its

satisfaction a seem delight.



they be very skilful and cunning to let no trace of it appear on the

surface of their life. Frederic abandon all pursuit of Annie

Lipsett, he desert the company of his flattering fool, for these

thing trespass upon the field of his fevere sport. it be very

rarely that they go of their own accord to seek purchasable

pleasure. visit they pay, when politeness and discretion compel,

and everybody find they charming. they could be good company and

their talent be useful. they become popular and be much in

request to organise entertainment, bazaar, jumble sale, and such

function.



* * *



in his business Frederic become more cunning, quicker-witte, and his

reputation gain. his practice increase. his whole life be

concentrated on his home and his office. he grow lean and alert, but

he be always tired.



in the early day of his management of the Bradby-Folyat estate he have

borrow large sum of money. these debt he be able considerably to

reduce, and very soon there be no arrear of interest outstanding.

as he begin to feel himself on more solid ground his habit of

exaggeration lose much of its hold upon he, and man who have

previously avoid he begin to seek his company. many who have

dismiss he as a bore now come to see quality in he, and, as he

gain the acquaintance of a well class of man, the quality of the

work that pass through his hand improve.



over Minna's disaster he behave well. he explain the legal aspect

of divorce to his father, and by tell he what he hear man say

about it--man who have know Basil Haslam, man of the world--help he

to understand that there be less malice than idle curiosity in

gossip, that scandal be the thing of a day, and that sympathy be to

a great extent on Minna's side and altogether with her parent. . . .

Francis be not greatly comfort. he feel that the attitude of mind

of Frederic's "man of the world" be rather dirty, but he appreciate

the kindness, which be great than he have look for. he be not at

all easy, remember Serge's and his own attitude towards Frederic's

imbroglio, when Frederic rush up to town, pounce on Herbert Fry,

and insist on his marrying Minna. . . . as it happen, it be a

fatal step. Minna comply only because she think the marriage would

infuriate Basil (the horrible ordeal through which she have pass have

deprive she of all control), and Fry because he love she and because

his affair be more complicated than any one know save himself, and,

have to leave the country, he prefer to pass into exile with a

beloved companion. his life have come to ruin, and he think that to

have Minna for his wife would be a step towards reconstruction and

would help to blot out the past. . . . Frederic come back glow with

virtue and manly pride, feel that he have make an honest woman of

his sister.



Frederic's interview with Herbert Fry have seem to he a direct

triumph of right over wrong. it be the first time he have ever find

himself in the van of the big battalion, and it give he a feeling of

confidence that be almost exhilarate. he return to his wife, to

find that her suspicion of he be not abate, and convince himself

that she be cruel and unjust. he gamble in marriage more recklessly

than ever, and if before she have be anxious, now she be fill with

dread as she see that she be play a lose game. soon or later

he would have clear she out and the last tie between they would be

break. her dread paralyse she. only mechanically could she keep the

card flutter and the pot a-boiling.



* * *



Frederic lose his draw look. he be win, and be sure that the

luck would never turn again. feel immeasurably superior to Fry, who

have commit the unpardonable folly of be find out, and morally

on a different plane from Minna and the world of illicit love which

have spew she out to the scorn of all man (except man of the world,

who could wink at these thing), he fall back upon the cushion of

middle-class prosperity, thrust aside happiness as a thing to be

desire, and concentrate all his energy on money. he begin to

speculate--successfully at first, then unsuccessfully. in his early

day of practice it have hardly be worth while to keep his account

separate, and, as his business grow, he never trouble to reorganise

his book.



Mrs. Bradby-Folyat die. she have take a dislike to Gertrude and leave

Streeten Folyat only a thousand pound. Frederic receive twenty-five

pound to buy a mourning ring. that do not fret he. there be the

estate to be wind up, and the picking of the process would be rich.



the executor ask to see the account. Frederic make they up, but

they be find so slovenly and unsatisfactory that further inquiry

be institute and an accountant be call in--a precise, mince

little man who speak with a strong north-country accent, sniff, and

walk in and out of the office as though he be tread the aisle

of a chapel. he exasperate Frederic so that he go out of his way to

be rude to he. the accountant sniff and smugly turn the other

cheek. he be a week in the office and go away without say a

word.



Frederic receive a letter from one of the executor request he to

hand over all paper and security to another large firm of

solicitor. without comment a statement of account be enclose,

show a deficit in the Bradby-Folyat estate of six thousand pound.

every nerve in Frederic's body quiver and go hot and dry. he

lock the statement away and give order for the Bradby-Folyat

deed-box to be hand over to the representative of the nominate

solicitor upon their give a receipt for it. mechanically, with a

fevere concentration upon the figure as an occupation to keep

himself from think, he go into his banking account. he have three

hundred pound in cash. his share, which would have to be sell at a

loss, would realise another thousand. outstanding debt amount to

not two hundred. . . . his wife's money be her upon trust for her

child, or, fail her child, for her nephews and niece. all

the Clibran-Bell money be trust money. his father have none, only

enough to make a small provision for his old age and his wife and

Mary.



the executor call. he be polite--a barrister by profession, with

the most suave and urbane brow-beat manner. he suppose that the

numerous mistake could be rectify, and that where loss have

occur through incompetent investment the deficiency would be make

good. Frederic say not a word. he twiddle a little piece of paper

between his finger and his face be as white as the paper. the

executor draw his own conclusion and say:



"it be misappropriation and embezzlement. I have try to persuade

Batson's not to take proceeding, but they insist that it must go

before the Law Society. . . . you will be lucky if you get no bad

than be strike off the roll."



the word bit into Frederic's brain and go trickle down his spine.

the executor take his hat and leave he sit by his table still

twiddle the little piece of paper, with his face as grey as a

goose-feather. he sit very still for a long time stare at the piece

of paper in his hand. presently he let it fall, but still he sat

stare. . . . he hear his clerk go. the cashier bring the key of

the safe. he say good-night. Frederic say good-night, and be

startle at the sound of his own voice. the silence have seem to he

so inevitable, so final, surely eternal.



one think spring to life in his brain: "no one must know." that give

he a purpose and bring he to the need of action. at home he force

an amiable mood upon his wife. in the evening they call at Burdley

Park and take Mary to the theatre. they see her home after a merry

evening, and, in the high spirit, they call on the Clibran-Bells

and invite some of the family to come and play whist on the morrow.

Frederic smoke a cigar with his father-in-law and discuss the new

waterworks scheme and the police scandal which have lately set all the

town by the ear, a whole division having be discover to be

draw large profit from organised prostitution in a certain

district. many droll story be in circulation of constable catch

_in flagrante delicto_, and Frederic and his father-in-law laugh

heartily over they. at certain moment Frederic have a crazy desire to

pick his father-in-law up by the scruff of the neck and shake he:

there be something in his manner so ridiculous and undignified, and

his jocularity be so trivial and pointless. however, Frederic

continue to laugh, and old Clibran-Bell pat he on the shoulder

and tell he he be a good fellow, a very good fellow.



in the office next day Frederic tease and pester his clerk and

keep they all bustle, find errand for they to go, require

book from the Law Library, discover paper that be long overdue

and have to be fetch. seize with a wild, hilarious impulse, he make

out a whole series of bogus writ and send they to be stamp and

deliver. . . . when all the clerk have go he sit down and write to

Batson's, say that he have make further inquiry and have many

paper and much information to lay before they which have previously

be overlook, and he add that such deficiency as might remain

after final examination would be pay in full. this letter he post

himself. he return to his office, write a cheque for each of his

clerk, repay his article clerk their premium, lay an envelope

contain they on each desk, look round to make sure he have

forget nothing, lock the outer door, walk down to the bridge by

the Collegiate church and throw the key into the river.



at night, after the whist-party have disperse, he pretend that he

have paper to look into and send Jessie to bed. he sit by the fire

stare into the glow coal. it die down, but he make no effort to

keep it alight. he be exhaust. the assumed hilarity of the evening

have be too great a strain, and yet not strain enough. he be drive

himself to a collapse, but be fearful lest it should come too soon.



it be very cold. he shiver and crouch over the black grate. he

hear his wife's voice call he:



"ben't you come up to-night?"



"presently . . . presently."



when he judge that she would be asleep he creep upstairs, and in the

dark, to avoid wake she and also to avoid see she, he slip

into the bed by her side. all night he lie awake, cold, throbbing,

strain and start at all the small noise of the house.



at breakfast he chatter gaily over the newspaper. there be a

school board election toward, and a woman have offer herself as a

candidate for their division. he chaff Jessie and say he suppose

she would soon be want to vote for Parliament. Jessie be to spend

the day in town shop with her mother. he ask she to make sundry

small purchase for he, and they agree that they would have a crab

for supper.



he be rather a long time pack the little handbag he always take

with he to town. she go to remind he that it be get late and

find he with his hand in a drawer. he shut it hastily and ask she

to fetch his tobacco-pouch from upstairs. when she come down again he

be wait for she at the front door. she walk to the little iron

gate with he and they kiss. as he reach the kerb he turn to

look at she and see the old lady and gentleman at their window, and

he feel with a twinge of shame that for year he have be a spectacle

without know it. . . . he think Jessie look rather ill, tired,

old, and bony. it be absurd for they to kiss in public. . . .

everything seem absurd, fantastical, and unreal. the world be

present to his eye in sharp outline than he have ever see it

before. it be bathe in a cold grey light. it have nothing to do with

he. it be go on. he feel stationary. that his body be move be

nothing. his thought be not move. everything be absurd. the new

sharply outlined world, with its curious interwoven activity (he see

how they be dovetail), be move on. the world with which he have

be concern--the world in which he have be miserable, elated,

crestfallen, amused, disgusted--the world in which he have know

affection and companionship and spite and jealousy--be move

backward, sink from under his foot while he himself stand on the

verge of a nonsensical dawn that have its light from a set sun.

away from he, backward and forward, everything move fast and

fast, make he dizzy, intolerably dizzy, sick and cold with it.



he have intend to go to London, but at the station he see a sign

indicate a train for Plymouth. the name start out of the blurred

past and relieve he, a little restore his balance, and he see

clearly the scene of his boyhood--the grimy little office where he

have be article, the ship, the Hoe and the Sound. then all that too

slip away from he.



he take the train for Plymouth, and have a carriage all to himself as

far as Crewe. he sit stupidly stare out of the window. the train be

go so fast. why do everything move so fast? . . . he be very

tired. at Crewe a man enter the carriage. he have not think of

that. he must change. he must be alone. the train move on before he

could bring himself to stir. . . . with the presence of the man at the

other end of the carriage his mood change. out of the cold mist that

be upon he a desire rise and take possession of he. he do not

know what desire it be but it take the form of an itch to speak to

the man. he steal glance at he but his lip would not obey he. the

man say:



"it's a fine day."



Frederic agree that it be a fine day, and the desire in he fall

back into the void. the man be part of the absurd world that have

nothing to do with he, the world that go on, the trivial, silly

world. how trivial and silly everything be: the train be silly,

movement be silly; absurd and grotesque be the man's voice and his

idiotic comment on the day, and that be silly too. a day be but the

passage out of night into night. the whole world be nothing, move

out of nothing into nothing. some thing be clear because they do

not matter; other thing be blur because they have matter to

he, Frederic, who matter no long because he be stand still

and everything else be move. . . . his eye mechanically read the

legend--"To stop the train pull down the Cord outside the Window.

Penalty for Improper Use, £5."



"I could stop it," he think, "but it would only move on again.

everything move on again."



with that slight movement in his mind old habit reassert itself and

he begin to crave for self-pity, but the unfamiliar presence of the

man make that impossible. that habit of mind need the co-operation

of other habit. it be isolate and fall away again. . . . there have

be so much clear-cut action in the last few hour, action for a

purpose, action that could not be recall and therefore drive he on

to the fulfilment of the purpose.



"I must be alone," thump Frederic's mind, and the train-wheel take

it up, "alone, alone, alone."



he became exasperate with the presence of the other man. what right

have he in the world where there be nothing but Frederic, nothing at

all, an empty world where a man must at last make sure, make very

sure, that he be nothing?



the man get out at Bristol. it seem to Frederic that he have come and

go like a shadow. so little time, the emptiness of the world move

so swiftly, and through it Plymouth come near and nearer to he.

Plymouth appear to he as a sort of monster, a dog shadow that

have run after he for a long time, terrify he, to spring ahead and

come to meet he. and yet it be still behind. everything be two

thing, behind and in front; the contradiction make it nothing. . . .



he be freeze with terror. back, back, this way; no, that; now the

other. . . . "it will have I! it will have I! why? I be nothing. I

be nothing. it don't believe that I be nothing. . . . no, no. there

be nothing but myself, myself, myself. . . ."



he draw the revolver from his pocket.



"no, no. I will go back. I will go back."



he sit absolutely numb for a long time. suddenly he think of his

wife, coldly, clearly. he see himself in she. she be strong than

he. he must show she that he be the strong.



he think of his father and pass into a golden stream of peace. he

would go to his father. . . "I will arise and go to my father and say

unto he, 'Father, I have sin against Heaven and before thee and be

no more worthy to be call thy son.' . . ." his father would

understand as he have understand before. . . . but then he know that

all that his mother have think of he would be sponge away from her

mind, and he remember with what bitterness she have speak of Minna.



"too difficult," he think. "too many people."



the train gather speed down an incline. fast and fast. his

terror lest the journey should come to an end clutch he back into

the present. he must make haste. he must be the quick.



he have drop the revolver into his pocket again. now he fumble for

it. it catch in the lining, and he tug at it with feverish

impatience. . . . his heart leap on the report, which seem to come

from far away. . . . he feel nothing; less and less; out of the

swiftly move world he be sink downward, downward, gather

speed, fall, fall into nothing.



* * *



in the small hour of the morning Mr. Clibran-Bell ring furiously at

the bell of the house in Burdley Park. after many minute Francis come

down in his dressing-gown with a candle in his hand. Mr. Clibran-Bell

step into the hall.



"Frederic," he say, "have not be home all night. Jessie be at my

house. . . . my dear friend, my dear old friend . . ."



"have . . . something happen?"



"he's . . . he's at Plymouth. dead. they find he in the train, shoot

in the side."



Francis stand very still, his mind slowly grasp this new appalling

fact. tear trickle down his cheek into his beard.



"I shall go to Plymouth by the first train," say Mr. Clibran-Bell, "I

must get at the truth for Jessie's sake."



"yes. it be very good of you."



it be a long time after Mr. Clibran-Bell have go before Francis go

upstairs again. the candle burn itself out. his thought see-sawed up

and down.



"Frederic--dead. frederic--dead."



when he have grope his way back to his own existence, burden with

this new catastrophe, he say to himself:



"I cann't go on. . . . I cann't go on."



he see Frederic lying huddle in the corner of a railway carriage,

stranger to whom he be nothing find he. . . . then he think of

that other dead body that he have see long, long ago, the woman lie

in the little dark house, under the gutter light of the tallow

candle and the clement light of the moon. death violent, death

insistent, death that would not be shroud away or soften or make

seeming bless with word or tear. tear! death be too harsh. and

yet it matter nothing how it come about. it be always the same:

bitter, the bitter end to bitterness. all life be salt with the

savour of death. vain, vain the endeavour to sweeten it. sweetness and

corruption, be they not yoke-fellow? . . . word from the Bible

pass through Francis Folyat's mind: "I be the resurrection and the

Life," and again: "for behold, the day be come in which they shall

say, Blessed be the barren, and the womb that never bare, and the

pap which never give suck," and again in his bitter grief he turn

to the Book of Job:



"my day be past, my purpose be break off, even the thought of my

heart.



"they change the night into day: the light be short because of

darkness.



"if I wait, the grave be mine house; I have make my bed in the

darkness.



"I have say to corruption, Thou art my father: to the worm, Thou art

my mother and my sister.



"and where be now my hope? as for my hope, who shall see it?



"they shall go down to the base of the pit where our rest together be

in the dust."







XXXIII



the conclusion of the matter



_the vision of Christ that thou dost see

 be my vision's great enemy.

 Thine have a great hook nose like thine

 Mine have a snub nose like to mine._

      the EVERLASTING GOSPEL.



TWELVE middle-class Englishmen and an official sit in inquest on the

body of Frederic. they gaze shyly and uninterestedly upon it and then

hear the evidence to the effect that he be most happily married and

be without financial worry of any kind. . . . the verdict, in view of

the fact that the revolver be in the deceased's overcoat pocket, be

one of death by misadventure.



Francis learn the truth from Mr. Clibran-Bell. Mrs. Folyat be not

tell, neither be Jessie. queer thing be rumour, however, and

Mrs. Folyat begin to feel--not absolutely without foundation--that she

be look upon askance. she go into deep mourning and raise

Frederic to sainthood, and surround herself with relic from among

his personal belonging. she brood over the past and begin to piece

together her scatter memory. nothing take clear shape except, what

she have not see at the time, the long coolness between her husband

and her son, and she begin to charge and reproach Francis with it. by

vilify Francis she have the illusion that she be exalt Frederic.

she keep insist that Francis must be sorry now that her poor angel

be dead. Francis be remorseful. he be probe deeply and deeply

into the unillumine past, grope his way through tortuous

mole-gallery. the perpetual false deification of Frederic bother

he, his wife's voice, lachrymose and thin, din in his ear, be

an exasperation. he be busy, frantically busy, force his way with

all the strength of his nature out of the slough of despond into which

he have fall, and she seem intent on thrust he out of the

slough into a sea of treacly mud. at length, one day, when she have

raise Frederic a peg high in her idolatrous beatification, suddenly

the truth be wrench from he:



"can you not see that he mean to kill himself?"



"oh! Frank . . . !"



he could despitefully have bite his tongue out for having say it,

but, having do so, he owe it to she to go on. it might prove her

salvation. it might bring she back to he so that together they might

perceive and win to the way of brightness.



"he take the pistol with he in his pocket. he have no luggage with

he. he have lock the door of his office and pay up his clerk'

wage and the premium of his pupil."



"oh! Frank . . . oh! Frank!"



and Francis hope that she would turn to he and understand, but she

very anguish of sorrow she must turn to self-indulgence, and she move

from the luxury of worship to the luxury of self-accusation:



"we drive he to it. all of we. we never understand he."



she tell Jessie, who be prostrate by the knowledge, and Mr.

Clibran-Bell refuse ever to enter the Folyats' house again.



Francis pass through the very black hour of all after that. he

pray to his God but be not comfort; his mind would run only in

the harsh channel of the faith he have spend his life in teaching.

the God he find be a jealous God, a God of cruelty and vengeance and

punishment. in vain he tell himself that this be the just visitation

of sin. he could not believe it. all his spirit crave for the belief

in mercy, the live eternity, the life everlaste. he be hem in

by the habit of year, and long familiarity with thing sacred, all

the vocabulary of paradox that have flow so easily from his lip week

in, week out, year after year. he want the truth of it, but it be

all word, word, word, a rain of fine dust fall upon his

intelligence, blind his eye. he need that in his religion which

could square with and illuminate the fact of his existence, but ever

the darkness grow more impenetrable.



for three week he go on mechanically with his work, go blindly

through the ritual which he have fight so hard to establish, but

always when he come to the Benediction and commend the congregation

to the Peace of God, he know, could not away with the knowledge, that

there be no peace in his own heart, and he rebuke himself and call

himself Hypocrite.



he could not take refuge in self-torment. his need be too great. he

tell himself that he no long believe, and pray for help in his

unbelief. but there have always be faith in he. nothing have ever

shake it. his necessity lie in the fact that the symbol he have

always use be cheapen, worn, debase. his mind could not change.

it be definitely cast in the story of the Godhead in Man in the

person of Jesus of Nazareth, bear of the Virgin birth, persecute and

slay by the Jews to rise again in glory to the eternal salvation of

soul. . . . the teaching of this gospel should, if it have any

purpose, lead to noble life, a superb preparation for eternity. but

whither have it lead himself? to the small of small life, to the

ruin of two of his child, fall into the very snare against which

they have be warn with all the threat of eternal punishment and

Hell fire at the command of an appoint minister of the Christian

religion. . . . he try to look beyond his own family, to see what

effect the Gospel have have upon his parishioner and he could not

disguise from himself the pitifulness of their condition. to consider

the effect of the christian religion upon the history of the world be

too large an undertaking for he.



Serge have say that he be of those who believe that understanding be

not vouchsafe to we. what do he mean? . . . word haunt him:--"To

justify the work of Man to God," or be it "to justify the work of

God to man"? surely the last. the work of Man could not be justify.

he feel himself to be near the clue he be seek, but the effort to

follow it be beyond he. for he the only tie between Man and God be

Jesus Christ.



he read the Gospels, and soon give up try to unravel the hard

saying, but he read again and again every passage in which the word

Love and Mercy occur. they soothe he, and, read over and over

the gentleness of Jesus under persecution, he become soften and very

tender, and seek the company of child, his grandchild.



he rest for a fortnight and then take up his work, for one Sunday

only. all the old business of threaten and hector and denouncing

and hold the wrath of God back with prayer, and pile up mountain

upon mountain of sin to teach the love and mercy of the Gospel

through and after punishment, everlasting and relentless, be empty,

all sound and fury.



his conclusion be, not that the christian religion have become

theatrical, rhetorical, mechanical, inhuman and unjust, but that he

himself by his own life have become unworthy to administer it. like

many Christians, face with the difficult, almost (in these day)

impossible, task of distil the essential truth from its

accumulation of taint lumber, he take refuge, without see any

inconsistency, in the ascetic ideal, think that a life of absolute

chastity and poverty and abstraction from the thing of this world

would give a man the right to hurl thunder and the lightning of the

jewish Bible at his fellow man. and yet in his heart, as, latent in

the heart of all man, be the true faith in the ineffable love,



    _. . . che muove 'l Sole e l'altre stelle._



he could not disentangle this love, this spirit of man, from the

superstition of the age, and could not therefore let it freely move

his own existence. he tell himself that he have fail, that he ought

never to have enter the priesthood, that he be an old man and could

not change. no other course lie open to he than to retire.



he write to his Bishop to ask his leave, and, if it be grant, to

apply for a pension from the Diocesan fund.



never again do he conduct Divine service in any church.



* * *



he feel infinitely happy when he have do this, and a new brightness

come to Mrs. Folyat and Mary when they know they be to escape from

the town where they have come by so much suffering, and the numb

monotony of a rather idle existence in drab surrounding. they set

their face southward, for they have decide to live in Potsham, where

Francis have hold his first curacy. they be go to live in

Crabtrees, where Francis Folyat and Martha Brett have meet and love

each other so long ago, and all day long Francis would be busy in the

garden run down to the river, and all day long Martha would sit in

the gazebo and look out at the water, and see the tide come in, and

the heron fish, and the boat go sail by, all as it have be

long ago, peaceful and beautiful. . . . already, week before they

could go, the peace of it begin to fill the house in Burdley Park, and

the dark past slip away from they and Francis begin to feel the

richness of old age, when good and bad have be do, and the

fruit of reflection can be gather in.



often as he sat work in the greenhouse, or in the study turning

over his book--he have go back to the love of his early day,

Fielding and Don Quixote--Francis would think of Serge, and the day

when together they have walk away from Mrs. Entwistle's cottage. that

memory preoccupy he more and more, and he feel a desire to see

Annie Lipsett again before he go away. she write to he at long

interval to let he know that she have not forget. his feeling

about the episode have always be spice with the joy of forbidden

thing. it have be entirely separate from the rest of his life, and

yet, unknown to he, it have inform the whole of it, and, in his most

need, have give he the assurance of love and mercy which have uphold

he in the face of the doctrine and dogma of his church, even though

he have seem to himself to be uphold the Church by the sacrifice

of himself.



* * *



he find Annie Lipsett busy and thoughtful. she be go to be

married to an auctioneer who have be a lodger in her mother's house.

she have just have a letter from Serge in Ceylon and its friendliness

have remove her last anxiety.



"you see, sir," she say to Francis, "Mr. Serge find I when

everything be as complicated as that piece of lace, and he make it

all simple. and after that, be with he make one able to bear

everything, because one feel that, whatever it be, it would go away.

he use to say that be unhappy and dark in your mind be just the

same as be unwell in your body, and if it be take in time there

be always a cure for it. so funny he use to be about it. he be

always talk to I about the boy, and he use to say that I must

teach he nothing, because child be always right by themselves

until they begin to imitate grow-up people, and bad thing be easy

to imitate than good because they be grotesque, and grow-up people

have always to be learn good thing from child over and over

again."



"I have never forget that day when I come to see you."



"nor I, sir."



"we're go away, for ever. it be queer, but you be the only person

whom I really want to see before I leave. we have never seem to

belong to this place."



"I use to hate it too, but Mr. Serge make I laugh at it all. he say

it be just an accident, though I don't know what he mean by that. I

often don't really understand Mr. Serge, except about the boy, but

then I could see that everything he say be true."



"I hope you will be very, very happy."



Annie surprise Francis by put her arm round his neck and kiss

he. he return the kiss.



it be only some time after he have leave she that it strike he that he

have never once think of Frederic in connection with she. when he

call Frederic to his mind it be always as a graceful, impudent,

funny little boy. he have never know the man Frederic. Frederic have

never be a man.



* * *



even in our town the green of spring be show and the zestful wind

be blow upon the blacken house when Francis, his wife and Mary

leave upon their long journey to the south. gleeful and glad they be,

and the spring be in their heart and the keen adventurousness of

escape. after long captivity they be shake from their shoe the

dust of the hostile city, leave in its toil the sole hostage of all

their family, Annette, doom to the life of drudgery to which that

city condemn its woman, for, except they be bear in drudgery, the

son of its woman could never endure its service, nor would they be

fit for it.







XXXIV



nunc DIMITTIS SERVUM TUUM, DOMINE



_for mine eye have see Thy salvation._

     the SONG of SIMEON.



many wise man have laugh at the futility of thought and discard an

opinion as a worthless thing.



in the garden at Crabtrees Francis grow rose and delphinium and tall

hollyhock and all homely flower, and busily he tend his vegetable

and herb. he keep bee and grow skilled in their way. every day in

summer Mrs. Folyat sit in the gazebo, and in the winter she have she

own little drawing-room where the gossip would come in and take tea

over a great fire.



their living be very frugal, for their mean be small. only two

house besides Crabtrees be leave of Mrs. Folyat's inheritance.



outwardly Potsham be hardly at all change since the day when Francis

and his bride have set out on their honeymoon, but its glory be

depart. its fragrance and faint perfume of the high manner of an

old day be go. little boy whom they remember play

barefoote in the street call the Strand, down by the little dock

and the mud flat, have make fortune and dispossess little by little

the old gentlefolk. their son have go to the university and their

daughter have visit London. no long be the inhabitant of

Potsham gently little in a little place, but in a little place ape

the folly of great city. People and place be no long in

harmony. man and woman seem continually to be adjust themselves

to an outside standard. they be as sluggard who protest their

wakefulness. . . . but for Francis and Martha, Potsham be as it have

be in their youth, a place of sleep, of tranquil sleep attend by

pleasant dream of rose and blue water and warm fig ripen in the

sunlight mellow by the soft, moist air.



their golden wedding come, their diamond-wedding, and between the two

be but the drowsy hum of bell in a lofty tower. the hair of both

be snow-white, and Francis have his brush into two long ringlet

that fall down on to his shoulder on either side of his head. his

eye be bright and young, often twinkle with merriment behind his

spectacle, and people use to come and tell he funny thing to see

he enjoy the joke and chuckle down in his throat and shake all over

with his inward mirth.



Gertrude often come to stay with her two child, and upon a day she

arrive and never go away. Streeten have shed his capital bit by bit

in one profession after another until he have not enough leave to

support his family. then he disappear without a word and no trace of

he could be find.



every two year Annette use to come and bring with her one or more of

her child. like her mother, she have eight. she could never stay

long because Bennett would write every day and implore she to come

back. . . . when any of her child have be ill she use to send

they down, and they stay until Francis judge they well enough to

return, and that be never until their little pinch white face be

fill out and bake as brown as a bun. the second boy, Stephen, once

spend five month at Crabtrees. he be a very queer, silent little

creature, and he use to sit and stare at his grandparent and his

aunt. once, after dog Francis for two day and scrutinise he

in the most embarrassing way, he say:



"Grandpa, what be it make your eye so bright and blue, like the

sky?"



Francis chuckle and reply:



"my dear, they're little mirror and I polish they."



a great summer pass into a melancholy misty autumn, but on a rare

fine day, the sun warm the first sigh breath of winter, Stephen

Lawrie sit with a book in his lap under the siberian crabtree on the

lawn. his grandfather be dig in the vegetable garden near by,

when, look up, Stephen see he pitch forward and fall flat on his

face. it be as though he have be blow down.



the boy sit stare, stun by the heaviness of the fall. then he be

seize by the terror of it and rush scream away.



it be a stroke, and Crabtrees become a house of the sick. Stephen be

pack off home.



* * *



before the winter be out Francis seem to be quite well again, and

he be out and about and busy prepare for the spring. February be

hardly go when he be lay low again, this time never to rise. he

be partially paralyse and could not speak. for a long time his wit

be go. . . . slowly he creep back again into the existence of the

house. his spirit would not yield up his body to the earth.



Gertrude be his nurse, and very gentle with he. she be creep

about his room, think he asleep, with her shadow swinge to and

fro as she move. in a sudden, strangle voice, she hear he say:



"I can speak."



she turn to he, but he lie very still, and his face look pinch

and whiter than it have do. she be alarmed and sit up with he all

night. in the early morning he ask to see his wife. Gertrude fetch

she, and she came huddle and bunch up in shawl and flannel and sit

by his bedside. he move his hand a little and she reach out and

take it in her. he say:



"it have be a long time, but it have be a good time. it have not all

be good for you. I would be glad if you--if you could forgive I

. . ."



"oh! my dear, my dear. . . . the good . . ."



"I have always be afraid," he go on, and his voice gain in

strength. "I have always be afraid of say too much, and I have

say too little. . . . it have be good when we be old. you have

much to forgive."



Mrs. Folyat could only weep. Francis ask to be give his Bible and

the amethyst cross he have wear on sunday on his watchchain. they be

lay by his side and he take the cross in his hand. he say that

everything he leave be to go to Mary, but she be to help the other

when they need help. . . . then he tell his wife she must go away

and rest, for he desire to communicate for the last time and must

have a space in which to prepare himself. Gertrude aid Mrs. Folyat

out of the room.



it be all Fool's Day.



at nine o'clock Mary be have breakfast alone when Serge walk in.

she tell he, and he go up at once to his father's room. he stand by

the bed for a long time before Francis open his eye and see he.

his eye smile and he say:



"my son."



"Father."



"I be not of those who believe that understanding be not give to we,

for I come to understand. the beginning and the ending of all thing

be in God, and we may not question, nor idly interpret the beginning

and the end. we pass from dust to dust, but the spirit endureth for

ever, and in all thing in our passage the spirit move we. be it not

so?"



"it be so."



"and life be very good, to be round with a sleep."



"life be very good."



"surely I have not altogether fail my God, since I have know you."



"we have know each other. a man must die many time before his life

be do."



"so be it. . . . I shall sleep now."



Serge stoop and kiss his father's brow, and in a few minute he

be dead.



the END
